John Truby

Anatomy of Story

– 1 –

The Space and Time of the Story

Everyone knows how to tell stories. We all do it, every day. "You'll never guess what happened at work." Or: "Guess what I just did!" Or: "A guy walks into a bar and..." Throughout our lives, we see, hear, read, and tell thousands of stories.

The problem is telling a good story. When you want to become a specialist in the art of storytelling, or even make it your profession, you face immense difficulties. First, you must acquire a deep and precise understanding of a subject as vast and complex as possible. Then you must be able to apply this understanding to fiction. For most authors, this is the greatest challenge of all.

I want to be very precise about the various obstacles inherent in narrative techniques; it's the only way to overcome them. The first of these obstacles is the common terminology with which most authors think about their stories. Terms like "dramatic progression," "climax," and "denouement," which date back to Aristotle, are so broad and theoretical that they are almost meaningless. Let's be honest: they have no practical value for an author. Imagine you're writing a scene where your hero is hanging in the void, on the brink of dying from a fall. Is this a stage of the dramatic progression, the denouement, or the opening scene of the story? It could be any of these things, or all at once, but in any case, these terms don't tell you how to write the scene, or whether you should write it at all.

Classical narrative terminology is an even greater obstacle to acquiring good technique: its very conception of what a story is and how it works. As an apprentice author, the first thing you probably did was read Aristotle's Poetics. Aristotle is, in my opinion, the greatest philosopher in history. But his vision of storytelling, though powerful, is strangely narrow, focused on a limited number of plots and genres. It is also extremely theoretical and difficult to put into practice. That's why most authors seeking practical advice from Aristotle leave empty-handed.

If you're a screenwriter, you probably moved from Aristotle to a simpler vision of the story called the "three-act model." But this idea also poses problems because the three-act model, although much easier to understand than Aristotle, is extremely reductive and, in many ways, completely wrong.

This theory claims that every screenplay consists of three "acts": the first corresponds to the beginning, the second to the middle, and the third to the end. The first act is about thirty pages long. The third act is also about thirty pages. And the second act consists of about sixty pages. The three-act story is supposed to have two or three "high points" (of any kind). Got it? Good. Now try writing a screenplay with that.

We simplify this narrative theory, but in reality, not by much. It's obvious that such a reductive approach has even less practical value than Aristotle's. But there's worse: this theory tends to forge a mechanical vision of the story. The division into acts comes from the conventions of traditional theater, where the curtain is closed to signal the end of an act. This division into acts has no place in films, novels, short stories, or even, it seems to us, in many contemporary plays.

In short, the division into acts is external to the story. The three-act structure is a mechanical element imposed on the story, which has nothing to do with its internal logic—where the story should or should not go.

A mechanical vision of the story, like the three-act theory, inevitably leads to episodic storytelling. An episodic narrative consists of an accumulation of parts, like objects stored in boxes. The events of the story are discontinuous elements that are not firmly connected to build the narrative progressively, from beginning to end. The result is a story that moves the audience only sporadically, or not at all.

There is another obstacle to mastering storytelling, which is related to the writing process. Since many authors have a mechanical vision of what fiction is, they rely on a mechanical process to write. This is especially true for screenwriters, whom a mistaken idea of what makes a script marketable leads to writing scripts that are neither popular nor good. These screenwriters generally base their work on an idea that is just a slight variation of the story of a film they saw six months ago. They apply a genre to this idea, such as "crime," "romance," or "action," and fill in the boxes of characters and plot twists according to the codes of that genre. The result: the story is extremely generic, mechanical, devoid of originality.

In this book, I want to present things differently. My goal is to explain how a good story works and to outline the techniques necessary for its creation so that you can have every chance of writing one yourself. Some will say that it's impossible to teach the art of storytelling. I don't share this view, but I believe that to succeed, one must think about the story and define it in a new way.

In short, I will outline a practical poetics that will work equally well for writing a screenplay, a novel, a play, a serial, or a short story. I will:

• Show that a good story is an organic story—not a machine, but a body that evolves.

• Treat storytelling as a craft based on precise techniques that will help you succeed, regardless of the medium or genre you choose.

• Work on a writing process that will also be organic, meaning we will develop characters and a plot that will naturally evolve from your original idea.

The main challenge an author faces is overcoming the contradiction between the first and third of these tasks. You build your story from hundreds, even thousands of elements using a wide range of techniques. But despite this, the story must seem organic to the audience, who must perceive it as an individual thing that develops and builds towards a climax. If you want to master the art of storytelling, you must master this technique thoroughly, to the point where your characters seem to act on their own, doing what they have to do, while you pull the strings.

In this sense, we authors are very close to athletes. When you watch a great athlete, everything seems easy, as if their body naturally moves that way. But the reality is that they have mastered the techniques of their sport so well that this technical aspect has become invisible to the audience, who sees only the beauty.

THE STORYTELLER AND THE AUDIENCE

Let's start simply, with a very short definition of the story:

A storyteller tells an audience what someone did to get what they wanted and why they did it.

You'll notice that there are three distinct elements: the storyteller, the audience, and the story being told.

A storyteller is above all someone who plays. Stories are verbal games that the author plays with their audience (who doesn't keep score—that's the job of studios, TV channels, or publishing houses). The storyteller imagines the characters and actions. They tell what happens, presenting a series of actions that have been accomplished in a certain way. And even if they tell the story in the present (as in theater or cinema), the author summarizes all the events so that the audience feels it is a complete story, a unit.

But telling a story is not just inventing events or remembering past events. Events are just description. The storyteller must select, connect, and build a series of intense moments. These moments will be so exciting that the audience will feel like they are experiencing them themselves. Telling a story well is not just telling the audience what happened in a life. It's giving them the experience of that life. It's the essence of life, but conveyed in such a fresh and new way that the audience feels it is part of the essence of their own life.

Well-told stories allow the audience to relive events in the present, and thus understand the forces, choices, and emotions that drove the character to act as they did. Stories allow the audience to acquire a certain form of knowledge—emotional knowledge—what was once called wisdom, but they do so in an entertaining and diverting way.

As a creator of verbal games that allow the audience to relive a life, the author constructs a kind of human puzzle and asks the audience to try to understand it. The author creates this puzzle in two main ways: they give the audience certain information about their characters and hide certain information. These hidden pieces of information are absolutely necessary for the story to work. They tend to push the reader or viewer to try to understand who the character is and what they are doing. Consequently, they help them enter the story. When the audience no longer needs to try to understand the story, they cease to be an audience, and the story stops.

The audience loves both the emotional part (reliving a life) and the intellectual part (trying to understand the puzzle). Every good story must be composed of these two parts. But there is a whole range of forms of fiction, stretching from one extreme to the other, from sentimental melodrama to the most cerebral detective stories.

THE STORY

There are thousands, even millions of stories. But what makes each of them a story? What are the common points between all stories? What does the storyteller always reveal and hide from their audience?

KEY POINT: Every story is a form of communication that expresses the dramatic code.

The dramatic code, deeply rooted in the human mind, is an artistic description of the possibilities of a person's development. This code is also a process that underlies every story. The storyteller hides this process under particular actions and characters. But it is this code of development that the audience will ultimately retain from a good story.

Let's start by studying the dramatic code in its simplest form.

According to the dramatic code, transformation is fueled by desire. The "universe of the story" cannot be defined by the formula "I think, therefore I am" but rather by "I want, therefore I am." It is desire, in all its facets, that makes the world go round. It is what moves every conscious living being and gives them direction. Telling a story is exposing what a person wants, what they do to get it, and the price they have to pay to achieve their ends.

When the character is driven by desire, the story "walks" on two "legs": action and discovery. A character seeking to fulfill a desire undertakes actions to get what they want and learns how to best achieve it. When they learn new information, they make a decision and change their way of acting.

All stories follow this movement. But some forms of stories emphasize one aspect more than the other. The genre that relies most on action is the myth, as well as its modern version, the action story. The genres that rely most on discovery are detective stories and multiple viewpoint dramas.

A character seeking to fulfill a desire and encountering obstacles is forced to make efforts (otherwise, there is no story). And these efforts make them evolve. Therefore, the ultimate goal of the dramatic code, and of the author, is to present the transformation that has occurred within a character or to explain why this transformation did not occur.

The different forms of storytelling all have a specific way of expressing the transformation a human being undergoes:

• The myth presents the longest of all developments, from birth to death and from animal to divine.

• Plays generally focus on the moment when the main character makes a crucial decision.

• Films (especially in American cinema) present the minimal change a character must undergo while pursuing a limited goal with great intensity.

• Classic short stories generally trace the few events that led a character to have an insight.

• Serious novels generally deal with how a person interacts and evolves within a society, or accurately depict the mental and emotional process that leads to their transformation.

• TV series present a number of characters who, in a micro-society, strive to change simultaneously.

Drama is a code of maturity. Its central point is the moment of transformation, the impact, the moment when the person frees themselves from their habits, weaknesses, and ghosts of the past to transform into a more fulfilled and accomplished being. The dramatic code reflects the idea that human beings can become an improved version of themselves, psychologically and morally. And that's why people love it so much.

KEY POINT: A story does not present the audience with the "real world"; it presents them with the universe of the story. The universe of the story is not a reproduction of life as it really exists. It is life as human beings imagine it could be. It is a condensed and exaggerated human life so that the audience can gain a better understanding of how real life works.

THE BODY OF THE STORY

A good story describes an organic process that human beings go through. But it is also a living body in itself. The simplest children's story consists of many parts, or subsystems connected to each other and dependent on each other. Just as the human body consists of a nervous system, a circulatory system, a skeleton, etc., a story consists of different subsystems: characters, plot, sequence of twists, universe of the story, moral debate, network of symbols, weaving of scenes, and symphonic dialogues (all these points will be developed in the following chapters).

One could say that the theme, or what I call the moral debate, is the brain of the story. The main character is its heart and circulatory system. The twists are its nervous system. The structure of the story is its skeleton. The weaving of scenes, its skin.

KEY POINT: Each subsystem of the story consists of a network of elements that contribute to defining and differentiating the other elements.

No element of your story, including the hero, can function if you do not first create and define it in relation to all the other elements.

MOVEMENT OF THE STORY

To understand the movement of an organic story, one can refer to nature. Just like the storyteller, nature often connects different elements in sequences. The diagram below represents a number of distinct elements that must necessarily be connected to each other over time:

Nature uses a few basic patterns (as well as a number of variations) to connect elements in sequences, sequences that can be linear, zigzag, spiral, branching, or explosive. Storytellers use one of these patterns, or a combination of them, to connect the different elements of the narrative over time. The linear and explosive patterns are the two opposite extremes. With the linear pattern, things happen one after the other, as if they were following a straight line. With the explosive pattern, everything happens at once. The zigzag, spiral, and branching patterns are combinations of the linear and explosive patterns. Here's how these narrative patterns work.

The Linear Narrative

The linear pattern follows the evolution of a single character, from beginning to end, like this:

A historical or biological explanation of what happened is then necessary. Most American films are linear. They focus on a single hero, who seeks to fulfill a single desire, with great intensity. The audience watches how they try to achieve their goal and how they are transformed by it.

The Zigzag Narrative

The zigzag story has a winding path, seemingly without a particular direction. In nature, the zigzag is the shape of rivers, snakes, and the brain:

Myths like The Odyssey; comedic travel stories like Don Quixote; The History of Tom Jones, a Foundling; Adventures of Huckleberry Finn; Little Big Man and Flirting with Disaster; as well as many of Dickens' stories, such as David Copperfield, take the form of a zigzag. The hero has a desire, but this desire is not intense. They move across a wide territory randomly; and they encounter many characters from different strata of society.

The Spiral Narrative

A spiral is a path that coils around itself, heading towards a center:

In nature, the spiral is the shape of cyclones, horns, and shells.

Thrillers, such as Vertigo, Blow-Up, The Conversation, and Memento, often take the form of a spiral, where a character keeps returning to a single event or memory, and thus gradually explores it.

The Branching Narrative

Branching is a system of paths that extend from a few central points, dividing into increasingly smaller parts, as shown below:

In nature, branching is characteristic of trees, leaves, and waterways.

In a narrative, each branch generally represents in detail an entire society or a part of a society that the hero explores. Branching is found in highly developed fictions such as Gulliver's Travels and It's a Wonderful Life, or in stories with multiple heroes, like Nashville, American Graffiti, or Traffic.

The Explosive Narrative

An explosion presents multiple paths that extend simultaneously; in nature, the explosive pattern is found in volcanoes or dandelions.

As for scenarios, you can't show the audience several elements at once, even in a single scene, because you have to say things one after the other; thus, there are no truly explosive narratives. However, you can give the appearance of simultaneity. In cinema, this appearance is given through the technique of cross-cutting.

Narratives that (apparently) show simultaneous actions involve a comparative explanation of events. By seeing several elements at once, the audience grasps the key idea inherent in each element. Narratives of this type also focus on exploring the universe of the story, exposing the connections between the various elements of this world and showing how each character adapts, or does not adapt, to the whole.

Narratives that emphasize simultaneous actions generally use a branching structure. These include American Graffiti; Pulp Fiction; Traffic; Syriana; Crash; Nashville; Tristram Shandy; Ulysses by Joyce; Last Year at Marienbad; Ragtime; The Canterbury Tales; L.A. Confidential and Hannah and Her Sisters. Each of these stories represents a different combination of linear and simultaneous narrative, but each emphasizes characters coexisting in the universe of the story, as opposed to a single protagonist evolving from a starting point to an endpoint.

WRITING YOUR OWN STORY

Now let's move on to practice: what writing process will give you the best chance of creating a good story?

Most authors don't use the best process. They use the easiest one. A process that could be described in four words: external, mechanical, fragmentary, generic. There are, of course, many variations of this process, but they all work more or less the same way.

The author starts with a premise, a story idea that is a vague copy of an existing story. Or a combination of two stories they have creatively spliced together (or so they think). Aware of the importance of a strong main character, our author focuses almost all their attention on their hero. They "inflate" this character mechanically, piling on as many distinctive traits as they can, and they plan to make their hero change in the last scene. They design the adversaries and secondary characters separately from the hero and see them as less important. Consequently, these characters are almost always weak and underdeveloped.

As for the theme, our author almost completely avoids it, so that no one can accuse them of "trying to convey a message." Or they express it exclusively in dialogue. They set the story in a world that seems suited to their character, most often a big city, since most readers or viewers live in a big city. They don't try to use symbols; that would be too obvious and too pretentious.
He imagines a plot and a series of scenes based on a single question: and then, what happens next? He often sends his hero to various locations. He organizes his plot relying on the three-act structure that divides the story into three parts but does not connect the events beneath the surface. Consequently, the plot is episodic, with each event (or scene) appearing isolated. He complains of having "second act problems" and cannot understand why the story does not lead to a "punchy" climax that deeply affects the audience. Finally, he writes dialogues whose sole function is to advance the plot, with all the conflict concentrated on the present action. If he is ambitious, he ensures that the theme is expressed by the hero through dialogue, towards the end of the story.

While most authors use an approach that is external, mechanical, fragmented, and generic, the writing process we will work on can be described as internal, organic, interconnected, and original. We must warn you right now: this process is not easy. But I believe that this approach or its variants are the only ones that truly work. The other advantage is that they can be taught. Here is the writing process we will present in this book: we will study storytelling techniques in the order in which you will use them to build your narrative. More importantly: you will build your story from the inside out. This implies two things: (1) making your story personal and unique and (2) finding and developing what is original in your story idea. As the chapters progress, your narrative will develop and become more detailed, and each part will remain connected to all the others.

• The Premise We will start with the premise, that is, your entire narrative condensed into a single sentence. This premise will highlight the essence of your story, which will tell us how to develop your idea to get the most out of it.

• The Seven Key Steps of Narrative Structure The seven key steps of story structure are the major stages of your story's development and the hidden dramatic code beneath the surface. These seven structural steps should be conceived as the DNA of your narrative. By working on these seven key steps, you will give your story stable and solid foundations.

• Characters We will then create the characters, not from scratch, but by gradually defining them from your original story idea. We will connect and compare each character to all the others, so that they are all strong and well-defined. Then we will determine the function each of them must fulfill to help your hero develop.

• The Theme (or Moral Debate) The theme is your moral viewpoint, the way you think people should act in the world. But rather than making characters the spokespersons of a message, we will express a theme inherent to your story idea. And we will express this theme through the story's structure so that it surprises the audience and touches them at the same time.

• The Story Universe We will then create the story universe by designing it as an extension of your hero. The story universe will help you define your hero and present the audience with a physical expression of their development.

• The Network of Symbols Symbols are small packets of highly condensed meaning. We will define a network of symbols that will highlight different aspects of the characters, the story universe, and the plot.

• The Plot It is from the characters that we will discover the form the narrative should take; the plot will develop from your characters. By using the twenty-two steps of narrative structure (the seven key steps and fifteen others), we will define a plan in which all elements are connected beneath the surface and lead to a surprising but logically necessary conclusion.

• Weaving Scenes In the final step before writing the scenes, we will establish the list of all the story's scenes, with all plots and themes intertwining to form a tapestry.

• Constructing Scenes and Symphonic Dialogues Finally, we will write the story by constructing each scene so that it allows your hero's development. We will write dialogues whose function is not just to advance the plot—dialogues with a symphonic quality—by playing several "instruments" and levels at the same time.

I can promise you one thing: you will take great pleasure in this creative process as you watch your story develop before your eyes. Let's begin without delay.

QUESTIONS / ANSWERS

Some authors react negatively to the idea of structure because they think structure tends to restrict creativity and art. They believe one can very well write a first draft by immersing oneself in the scenario and creating the story as it unfolds. You advise planning the story and its entire structure before starting the first draft. If the result is comparable, what is the difference?

A: The idea that structure would harm creativity stems from the old romantic notion that art comes from divine intervention. And it is dangerously wrong. The reality is that art comes from technique. And the most important element of technique is structure.

The results of the two approaches are not comparable. With the first approach, you hit a wall after twenty to thirty pages. It is almost inevitable, and it is one of the hallmarks of amateurism.

Paradoxically, starting by structuring your story is far more creative than inventing as you go because it provides solid foundations on which you can rely to take creative risks. You know that the structure is there to tell you whether the creative leap you want to make will work or not. If you have started by determining the right general narrative structure for your story, each scene you write will move you forward on the right path for your hero.

The second approach also allows you to save a tremendous amount of time and effort in the writing process. For every hour you spend on preliminary structuring—your preliminary work on the story—you will save ten on the time you will then spend writing and rewriting. Do not make the mistake that too many authors make by thinking, "I'll fix it in the rewrite." They never do.

1. Peter S. Stevens, Patterns in Nature (Boston: Little, Brown, 1974), pp. 38-48.

– 2 –

The Premise

Michael Crichton does not have the deep characters of Chekhov nor the brilliant plots of Dickens. But he happens to be simply the best premise author in Hollywood. Take Jurassic Park, for example. Crichton's script likely started from this founding principle: "What would happen if we took the two greatest heavyweights of evolution—dinosaurs and humans—and forced them to fight to the death in an arena?" That's a script we want to read.

There are many ways to start the writing process. Some authors prefer to begin by dividing the story into its seven main steps, a method we will study in the next chapter. But most of them start with the shortest expression of the story as a whole: the premise.

WHAT IS THE PREMISE?

The premise is the story formulated in a single sentence. It is the simplest link between character and plot, usually consisting of an event triggering the action, an indication of the main character, and an indication of the story's outcome.

Here are some examples:

• The Godfather: The youngest son of a mafia family avenges the men who shot his father and becomes the new Godfather.

• Moonstruck: While her fiancé visits his mother in Italy, a woman falls in love with her future brother-in-law.

• Casablanca: A hardened American expatriate reunites with a former love, whom he eventually abandons to fight the Nazis.

• A Streetcar Named Desire: An aging beauty tries to get a man to marry her while enduring constant attacks from her sister's violent husband.

• Star Wars: As a princess is in mortal danger, a young man uses his fighting skills to save her and defeat the evil forces of a galactic empire.

For all sorts of practical reasons, a good premise is absolutely necessary for the success of your endeavor. First, Hollywood aims to sell films worldwide and capture a large portion of revenue on their opening weekend. That's why producers look for high-concept premises—films that can be reduced to a catchy descriptive sentence that audiences will instantly understand and that will drive them to the cinema.

Moreover, your premise is your inspiration. It's the light bulb moment when you think, "This would make a great script." And this enthusiasm gives you the perseverance needed to spend months, even years, laboriously writing.

Which brings us to another important point: for better or worse, the premise is also your prison. Once you decide to develop an idea, you must realize that there are hundreds of other potential ideas you won't write about. It is therefore important that you are satisfied with the particular universe you have chosen.

KEY POINT: What you have chosen to write is far more important than any decision you will make about how you will write it.

The last reason why it is necessary to have a good premise is that it will be the decision on which all other decisions you make during the writing process will be based. Characters, plot, theme, symbols—all of this stems from your story idea. If you fail at the premise stage, you won't be able to recover. When the foundations are faulty, no work on the upper floors can make the building stable. You may be excellent with characters, a master of plot, or a genius of dialogue, but if your premise is weak, nothing can save your story.

KEY POINT: Nine out of ten screenwriters fail at the premise stage.

If so many screenwriters fail at this stage, it's because they don't know how to develop their idea, how to unearth the treasure buried within it. They don't grasp that the greatest advantage of the premise is that it allows you to explore the entire story, as well as the many forms it can take before writing it.

The premise offers a good illustration of the dangers of ignorance. Most screenwriters know the importance Hollywood places on high-concept premises. What they don't know is that the marketing argument will never tell them what the organic story requires.

They also ignore the structural weakness inherent in any high-concept premise: these premises provide only two or three scenes, those just before and just after the twist that makes them unique. An average feature film has forty to seventy scenes. A novel can have double or even triple that. It is only by mastering the art of storytelling in its entirety that one can overcome the limits of high concept and successfully tell the whole story.

The first technique to unearth the treasure of an idea is to take your time. The beginning of the writing process indeed requires a lot of time. And we're not talking about hours, or even days. We're talking about weeks. Don't make the amateur mistake of starting with a nice premise and immediately writing scenes. You'll write twenty to thirty pages and find yourself in a dead end from which you cannot escape.

In the writing process, the premise stage is where you explore the overall strategy of your fiction—where you determine the whole and define the general shape and evolution of the story. You start from almost nothing to be able to continue. That's why the premise stage is the most experimental of the writing process. You fumble in the dark, exploring various possibilities to determine what works and what doesn't, what forms an organic whole and what doesn't fit in.

This means you must remain flexible, open to all possibilities. That's why it's so important to use a creative and organic method as a guide.

DEVELOPING YOUR PREMISE

During the weeks you try to define your premise, use these steps that will allow you to turn your starting sentence into a good story.

Step 1: Write Something That Could Change Your Life

The bar is set very high, but this is probably the best advice you will receive in your writing career. We have never seen anyone fail by following it. Why? Because if a story is so important to you, it is likely to be important to many readers or viewers as well. And when you finish writing this story, whatever happens, you will have changed your life.

You may be thinking, "I'd love to write a story that would change my life, but how can I know it will before I've written it?" It's very simple. Do something that most authors, quite strangely, never do: a little introspection. Most authors simply imagine a premise that is just a vague copy of someone else's film, book, or play. This premise seems to have commercial appeal, but it is in no way personal. The story that will result will never go beyond the general stage and will thus be doomed to failure.

To do introspection, to have a chance to write something that could change your life, you must collect information about who you are. And you must get this information out of you, place it in front of you, so you can study it with some distance.

Two exercises can help you. First, write a wish list, a list of everything you would like to see in the cinema or theater, or everything you would like to read in a book. It should be everything you find exciting and everything you find entertaining. You can note information about characters you have imagined, write down cool twists, or good lines that have crossed your mind. You can also list themes that matter to you or genres that have always attracted you.

Write all this on as many sheets of paper as necessary. You hold in your hands your personal wish list, so don't reject anything. Eliminate any thought like, "That would cost too much money." And don't try to organize your thoughts. Let one idea lead to another.

The second exercise is to write a list of premises. That is, a list of all the premises you have ever thought of. There may be five, twenty, fifty, or more. Again, use as many sheets of paper as necessary. The key instruction for this exercise is to express each premise in a single sentence. This will force you to be very clear about each idea. And it will allow you to see all your premises on one and the same medium.

Once your wish list and your list of premises are complete, lay them out in front of you and study them. Look for central elements that overlap. You may find certain characters or types of characters appearing multiple times, notice a particular tone recurring in your different lines, note that one or two types of stories (or genres) repeat, or that you often return to a particular theme, subject, or time period.

As you study your lists, the contours of what you like will begin to take shape. This will be, in its rawest form, your vision of things. You will have in front of you, on a piece of paper, what you really are, as an author and as a human being. Feel free to return to it as often as necessary.

Note that these two exercises are designed to open you up to the outside and to integrate what is already deep within you. They do not guarantee that you will ever write a story that will change your life. Nothing could guarantee that. But it is very likely that once this essential introspection exercise is completed, all the premises that come to mind will be more personal and original.

Step 2: Determine What Is Possible

If many authors fail at the premise stage, it is often because they do not know how to grasp the true potential of their story. This task requires both experience and technique. What you need to determine here is the direction your idea can take, how it can flourish. Do not limit yourself to a single possibility, even if it seems very good to you.

KEY POINT: Explore all the possibilities available to you. This will allow you to reflect on the many directions your idea can take and choose the best one.

To explore these possibilities, the first technique is to determine if something is promised by the initial idea. Some ideas indeed generate certain expectations, things that should happen to satisfy the audience if it turns out that this idea becomes a story. These "promises" can lead you to the best possibilities for developing your idea.

But there is another, more useful technique for determining what is possible from the initial idea: asking yourself, "What would happen if...?" This form of question leads to two distinct points: your story idea and your own mind. It helps you define what is allowed by the story universe and what is not. It also allows you to explore how your mind moves in this imaginary world. The more you ask yourself "What would happen if...?", the more you immerse yourself in this world, the more you flesh it out and provide it with details, and the more you make it convincing to the audience.

The important thing here is to let your mind run free. Do not censor yourself; do not judge yourself. Never tell yourself that one of the ideas that came to mind is stupid. "Stupid" ideas often lead to creative inventions.

To better understand this process, we will examine stories that have already been written and imagine what their authors might have thought as they explored the various possibilities of their premises.

WITNESS

(Earl W. Wallace and William Kelley, based on an original story by William Kelley, 1985)

A little boy witnessing a crime is a classic basis for a thriller. It promises exciting perils, intense action, and violence. But what if we pushed the story further to explore violence in America? What if we showed the two extremes of the use of force—violence and pacifism—by having the little boy move from the peaceful world of the Amish to the violence of the city? What if we then forced a man of violence, the hero cop, to enter the Amish world and fall in love? And then, what if we brought violence to the heart of pacifism?

TOOTSIE

(Larry Gelbart and Murray Schisgal, based on an original story by Don McGuire and Larry Gelbart, 1982)

The idea that immediately comes to the audience's mind is to be entertained by watching a man dressed as a woman. And you suspect they will want to see the character in as many comical situations as possible. But what if we tried to go beyond these useful but too obvious expectations? What if we exploited the hero's strategy to show how men experience love deep down? What if we made the hero a macho who is forced to take on the disguise—the one of a woman—that he least wants to wear but most needs to evolve? What if we intensified the pace and plot to tip the story towards farce by showing several men and women chasing each other?

CHINATOWN

(Robert Towne, 1974)

A man investigating a murder in 1930s Los Angeles promises all sorts of twists and surprises typical of a good detective story. But what if the crime took on increasingly significant dimensions? What if the detective started by investigating the smallest "crime" there is, adultery, and ended up discovering that the whole city was built on a murder? We could then reveal more and more to the audience, until we unveil the darkest secrets of American life.

THE GODFATHER

(Novel by Mario Puzo, screenplay by Mario Puzo and Francis Ford Coppola, 1972)

With a story about a mafia family, you promise ruthless killers and violent crimes. But what if we made the family head larger, if we made him the king of kings in America? What if the head of the dark side of America, as powerful in the criminal world as the president, found himself in official America? This man being a king, we could create a great tragedy, a Shakespearean rise and fall, the death of a king and his replacement by another. What if we turned a simple crime story into a dark American epic?

MURDER ON THE ORIENT EXPRESS

(Novel by Agatha Christie, screenplay by Paul Dehn, 1974)

A man killed in a train compartment right next to the one where a brilliant detective is sleeping promises an ingenious detective story. But what if we wanted to take the idea of justice beyond the classic arrest of the murderer? What if we sought to show the ultimate expression of poetic justice? What if the victim deserved to die and a natural jury of twelve men and women had been both his judge and executioner?

BIG

(Gary Ross and Anne Spielberg, 1988)
The story of a little boy who wakes up to find he has become an adult promises an entertaining fantasy comedy. But what if this comedy were set not in a distant, strange world, but in one any child could recognize? What if the hero were sent into a real child's dream, a toy factory, and allowed to leave with a pretty, sexy woman? And what if the story wasn't just about a child growing up physically, but also presented the ideal combination of man and little boy necessary for everyone to live a happy adult life?

Step 3: Identify the challenges and problems raised by the story

There are construction rules that apply to all stories. But each story also has its own set of specific rules or challenges. These are particular problems deeply rooted in the basic idea. You can't dodge them. And you shouldn't dodge them. These problems are landmarks that will help you discover your true story. You must tackle these problems head-on and solve them if you want to develop your story well. Most authors don't identify these problems, or they do so once the story is finished. That's far too late.

The trick is to learn to identify these problems at the premise stage. Of course, even the best authors can't identify all the problems at this stage of the process. But once you master the key techniques of characters, plot, theme, story universe, symbols, and dialogue, you'll be surprised at how easily you can pinpoint the inherent difficulties in any idea. Here are just a few of the challenges and problems raised by the following story ideas:

STAR WARS

(George Lucas, 1977)

In any epic, but especially in one set in space like Star Wars, you must quickly introduce a wide range of characters and then let them interact over a long period and in a vast space. You must make the futuristic story credible and understandable in the present. And you must find a way to create a transformation in a hero who is morally good from the start of the story.

FORREST GUMP

(Novel by Winston Groom, screenplay by Eric Roth, 1994)

How to turn forty years of historical events into a personal, coherent, and organic story? Here are some of the problems raised: creating a mentally delayed hero capable of leading the plot, having deep opinions, and experiencing a transformation, while finding a good balance between fantasy and genuine feelings.

BELOVED

(Toni Morrison, 1987)

The main challenge Toni Morrison faced was writing a story about slavery in which the heroine was not depicted as a victim. Such an ambitious story generates a number of problems that must be resolved: maintaining a coherent narrative line despite constant jumps between past and present, ensuring that events from a distant past speak to today's audience, advancing the plot despite characters who react more than they act, showing the psychic impact slavery had on those who lived through it, and demonstrating how its effects continue to be felt years after its abolition.

JAWS

(Novel by Peter Benchley, screenplay by Peter Benchley and Carl Gottlieb, 1975)

Writing a "realistic" horror story—in which characters fight against one of man's natural predators—raises many problems: designing a fair confrontation against an adversary with limited intelligence, creating a situation where the shark can attack often, and writing an ending that matches a duel between the hero and the shark.

THE ADVENTURES OF HUCKLEBERRY FINN

(Mark Twain, 1885)

The main challenge the author of The Adventures of Huckleberry Finn faced was enormous: how to present the moral—or more precisely immoral—structure of an entire nation in fictional terms? This brilliant story idea carries some major problems: using a little boy to lead the action; maintaining a brisk pace despite an episodic structure; and credibly presenting the acquisition of a great moral sense by a simple, not entirely admirable little boy.

THE GREAT GATSBY

(F. Scott Fitzgerald, 1925)

Fitzgerald's challenge was to present the corrupted American dream reduced to a competition for glory and money. His problems were equally imposing: creating narrative dynamism while the hero only helps someone else, engaging the audience with superficial characters, and turning a small love story into a metaphor for America.

DEATH OF A SALESMAN

(Arthur Miller, 1949)

Arthur Miller's main challenge was to turn the life of a little man into a great tragedy. The problems to solve were: mixing past and present events without confusing the audience, maintaining narrative dynamism, and putting some hope in a desperate and violent conclusion.

Step 4: Find a guiding principle

Once the promises and inherent problems of your story are defined, you must find a global strategy that will determine how you write your story. The guiding principle of your story is your global strategy formulated in a single sentence. This guiding principle will help you develop the premise into a solid structure.

KEY POINT: The guiding principle is what organizes the story as a whole. It is the internal logic of the narrative, which intrinsically cements the different parts so that the story is much more than the sum of its parts. It is what makes the story original.

In short, the guiding principle is the source of your story. And if you want to make your story original and effective, it is the most important factor to consider. This guiding principle can be a symbol or a metaphor (called "central symbol," "great metaphor," or "extended metaphor"). But it is often something broader than that. The guiding principle follows the fundamental process that will unfold throughout the story.

Guiding principles are difficult to pinpoint. And it must be said that most stories don't have one. Because they are standard stories told in a generic way. This is the difference between the premise, which is found in all stories, and the guiding principle—which is only found in good stories. The premise is concrete; it tells the facts. The guiding principle is abstract; it is a deeper process that develops within a story told in an original way. Formulated in one line:

Guiding principle = story process + original development

Imagine you are an author seeking to present the inner workings of the Mafia in America, as hundreds of screenwriters and novelists have done before you. If you are really good, you end up finding this guiding principle (for The Godfather):

Using the classic fairy tale strategy of explaining how the youngest of three brothers becomes the new "king."

It is important to understand the guiding principle as the "synthetic idea," the "constructive cause" of the narrative; it is the guiding principle that, internally, makes the story an individual unit and makes it different from all other stories.

KEY POINT: Find your guiding principle and stick to it. Take the time to find one and keep it in mind throughout the writing process.

Let's take the example of Tootsie to study how the difference between the premise and the guiding principle can manifest in a real script.

• Premise An actor who can't find work disguises himself as a woman and lands a role in a TV series, before falling in love with one of the actresses on the team.

• Guiding principle Forcing a macho man to live in a woman's skin.

How to determine the guiding principle from the premise? Don't make the mistake most authors make at this stage. Instead of looking for a unique guiding principle, they choose a genre, impose it on their premise, and then try to force the story to respect the highlights (the events) of that genre. The result? A mechanical, generic, and unoriginal fiction.

To find your guiding principle, you will need to study the simple and short premise sentence you have before you. You must, like an investigator, "detect" the shape of your story from its premise.

This does not mean that there is only one unique guiding principle per idea, that everything is already fixed or predetermined. From your premise, you can glean all sorts of guiding principles or forms from which you can develop your story. Each will offer you different possibilities to say things; and each will bring you different problems that you will have to solve. Again, there are techniques that can help you.

For example, you can find a guiding principle using the metaphor of the journey or voyage. Huck Finn descending the Mississippi on a raft with Jim, Marlow sailing into the "heart of darkness," Leopold Bloom from Ulysses moving through Dublin, Alice falling down the rabbit hole before entering the topsy-turvy Wonderland—each time using the journey metaphor to organize the organic process of the story.

Notice how the use of the journey in Heart of Darkness provides a guiding principle to a very complete work of fiction:

The journey of a narrator going up a river into the jungle is the line that simultaneously leads him to three different destinations: to the truth about a mysterious, apparently immoral man; to the truth about the narrator himself; and backward to barbarism, to the moral "heart of darkness" that resides in every human being.

A simple symbol can sometimes serve as a guiding principle. This is the case with the red A in The Scarlet Letter, the island in The Tempest, the whale in Moby Dick, or the mountain in The Magic Mountain. You can also connect two great symbols in a single process, such as the green of nature and the black of coal in How Green Was My Valley. Units of time (day, night, four seasons), the narrator's point of view, or any specific way the story evolves can also serve as guiding principles.

Below are some examples of guiding principles drawn from books, films, and plays, from the Bible to "Harry Potter." Observe how they differ from the premise.

MOSES IN THE BOOK OF EXODUS

• Premise When an Egyptian prince discovers he is actually Hebrew, he leads his people out of Egypt and slavery.

• Guiding principle A man who doesn't know who he is struggles to restore his people's freedom and receives new moral laws that will define him and his people.

ULYSSES

• Premise A day in the life of an average man in Dublin.

• Guiding principle A modern odyssey through the city, where in the space of a single day, a man finds a father, who in turn finds a son.

FOUR WEDDINGS AND A FUNERAL

• Premise A man falls in love with a woman, but she is already engaged, and at their next meeting, he himself is engaged.

• Guiding principle Friends experience four utopias (the weddings) and one nightmarish moment (the funeral), while each of them searches for the ideal partner.

THE "HARRY POTTER" SERIES

• Premise A boy discovers he has magical powers and enrolls in a school for wizards.

• Guiding principle A wizard prince learns to become a man and a king by spending seven school years in a school that trains wizards.

THE STING

• Premise Two small-time con artists swindle the rich man who killed their friend.

• Guiding principle Tell the story of a con in the form of a con and fool both the opponent and the audience.

LONG DAY'S JOURNEY INTO NIGHT

• Premise Family members must face the mother's addiction.

• Guiding principle As the day passes and night falls, all family members confront the mistakes and ghosts of their past.

MEET ME IN ST. LOUIS

• Premise A young woman falls in love with the boy next door.

• Guiding principle Depict the evolution of a family over a year through events that occur in each of the seasons.

COPENHAGEN

• Premise Three people recount contradictory versions of a meeting that changed the course of World War II.

• Guiding principle Use Heisenberg's uncertainty principle outside the context of physics to explore the ambiguous morality of the man who discovered it.

A CHRISTMAS CAROL

• Premise Three ghosts visit a miserly old man, who thus regains the Christmas spirit.

• Guiding principle Follow the rebirth of a man by forcing him to observe his past, present, and future on Christmas Eve.

IT'S A WONDERFUL LIFE

• Premise As a man is about to commit suicide, an angel shows him what the world would be like if he had never been born.

• Guiding principle Reveal the power of the individual by showing what a town, and a nation, would have been like if a single man had never lived.

CITIZEN KANE

• Premise Tell the life story of a wealthy media magnate.

• Guiding principle Use multiple narrators to demonstrate that one can never truly know a man's life.

Step 5: Determine the best character induced by your idea

Once your guiding principle is determined, it's time to focus on the hero.

KEY POINT: Always tell the story of your best character.

"The best" does not mean "the most likable." It means "the most fascinating, challenging, and complex," even if this character is not particularly likable. If you must tell the story of your best character, it's because that's where your attention, and the audience's, will inevitably go. You will want this character to lead the action.

To determine which is the best character induced by your basic idea, ask yourself this essential question: which one do I adore? You can find the answer to this question by asking yourself a few others: do I want to see him act? Do I like the way he thinks? Do the challenges he faces interest me?

If you don't find any character you adore in your basic idea, move on to another idea. If you find one but it's not the main character, immediately modify your premise so that he takes on that role.

If the basic idea you want to develop seems to induce multiple protagonists, know that you will have as many narrative lines as main characters. You must therefore find the best character for each narrative line.

Step 6: Identify the central conflict

Once you have chosen the character who will lead your story, you must determine what it is essentially about. That is, identify the central conflict of the story. To determine this central conflict, ask yourself the question: "Who fights whom for what?" and answer it in a succinct sentence.

The answer to this question will give you the subject of your story, as all the conflicts in the narrative will essentially stem from this specific problem. The next chapter will develop the subject of conflicts in sometimes complex ways. But it is absolutely necessary that you always keep this formulation of the conflict in a single sentence, as well as that of your guiding principle, in sight.

Step 7: Identify the unique sequence of cause-and-effect relationships

Every good organic story has a single chain of cause and effect: A leads to B, which leads to C, and so on until Z. This is the backbone of your story, and if you don't have a backbone or, conversely, you have too many, your narrative will collapse (we will talk later about stories with multiple heroes).

Let's say you have found the following premise:

A man falls in love and fights his brother for control of a vineyard.

You will notice that it is a divided premise, with two cause-and-effect relationships. However, it is easier to identify the problems of the premise and find solutions when this premise is a unified sentence. When you have written an entire book or script, you feel that the problems of the narrative are set in stone. But when you have only written a single sentence, it is still possible to make a simple modification, which allows, for example, to transform a divided premise into a unified premise, like this:

For the love of a woman, a man fights his brother to take control of a vineyard.

The trick to finding the unique path of cause and effect is to ask: "What is my hero's main action?" Your character will undertake many actions throughout your story. But there must be one action that dominates, that unifies all the other actions he undertakes. The cause-and-effect relationship is nothing other than this action.

To take an example, let's return to the premise of Star Wars:

While a princess is in mortal danger, a young man uses his fighting skills to save her and defeat the evil forces of a galactic empire.

By forcing oneself to describe the film in a single sentence, one comes to understand that the action that unifies all the other actions of Star Wars is "using his fighting skills."

Now let's take the example of The Godfather, an epic book and film. Again, if one follows the process of reducing the story to a single sentence, one can clearly discern the main action:

The youngest of a mafia family takes revenge on the men who shot his father and becomes the new Godfather.

Of all the actions Michael undertakes in the story, the one connected to all the others, the main action, is revenge.

KEY POINT: If you develop a premise with multiple characters, know that each narrative line must have a unique sequence of cause-and-effect relationships. And that all narrative lines must come together to form a broader and more global backbone.

In The Canterbury Tales, for example, each pilgrim tells a story that has an individual backbone. But these stories are told by a group—a microcosm of English society—traveling to Canterbury.

Step 8: Determine the transformation possibilities of your hero

After the guiding principle, the most important thing to find from your premise is the fundamental transformation of your hero. This is the factor that gives the most satisfaction to the audience, whatever form the story may take, even if this transformation is negative (as in The Godfather, for example).

The transformation is what your hero experiences at the end of his confrontation. To simplify to the maximum, this transformation can be represented using a three-part equation:

F X A = T

F represents weaknesses, both psychological and moral; A is the confrontation to accomplish the main action of the story; and T is the transformed person.

In the vast majority of stories, a character with weaknesses struggles to obtain something and emerges from this confrontation transformed (positively or negatively). The basic logic of the narrative is as follows: how does the confrontation to achieve the main action (A) lead the character to change, to move from F to T? You will notice that A, the main action, is the pivot of the equation. If forced to struggle to obtain something, a character with certain weaknesses will necessarily be transformed.

KEY POINT: The main action must be the action most likely to force the character to confront his weaknesses and change.

This is the basis of any story because it is the basis of human evolution. It is difficult to pinpoint how humans evolve, but it is what you, as an author, must express above all else (unless you choose to explain why evolution did not occur).

To achieve this, it is best to start from the main action and then determine the opposites of this action. This is what will allow you to find out who your hero is at the beginning of the story (his weaknesses) and who he is at the end (the transformation he has undergone).

You can follow these steps:

1. Write your premise sentence (know that this premise may be modified once you have discovered your hero's transformation).

2. Determine the main action your hero undertakes throughout the story.

3. Make F (the psychological and moral weaknesses of the hero) and T (his transformation) the opposites of A (the main action).

It is crucial to go to the opposite of the main action; this is the only way a transformation can occur. If your hero's weaknesses are similar to the main action he will undertake during the story, he will only deepen these weaknesses and remain the same.

KEY POINT: Find several possibilities for weaknesses and transformations.

Just as there are several possibilities for developing the premise, there are several possibilities concerning the weaknesses and the transformed person your hero will become. Let's say, for example, that your hero's main action is to become a criminal during the story.

To find possible weaknesses and transformations, you must reflect on this main action and determine its opposites. You will notice that each weakness and each transformation is a possible opposite of the main action.

• A shy and uptight man joins a gang of criminals and ends up divorcing.

F – initial weaknesses: shy and uptight man

A – main action: joins a gang of criminals

T – transformation: ends up divorcing

• A contemptuous and uptight banker joins a gang of criminals and ends up helping the poor.

F – initial weaknesses: contemptuous and uptight banker

A – main action: joins a gang of criminals

T – transformation: ends up helping the poor
• A very shy man joins a gang of criminals and ends up completely drunk.

F – initial weaknesses: very shy man

A – main action: joins a gang of criminals

T – transformation: ends up completely drunk

All these examples show you the possible transformations you can find from the initial premise of a man who becomes a criminal.

Let's put this technique into practice to study two familiar stories.

STAR WARS

• Premise: While a princess is in mortal danger, a young man uses his fighting skills to save her and defeat the evil forces of a galactic empire.

F – initial weaknesses: naive, impetuous, lacks focus and self-confidence

A – main action: uses his fighting skills

T – transformation: self-esteem, gains a place among the happy few, becomes a confirmed fighter

Luke's initial weaknesses do not evoke the qualities of a fighter at all. But pushed to constantly use his warrior skills, he strengthens and becomes a confident fighter for good.

THE GODFATHER

• Premise: The youngest of a mafia family avenges the men who shot his father and becomes the new Godfather.

F – initial weaknesses: cautious, conventional, isolated from the rest of the family

A – main action: revenge

T – transformation: tyrannical, becomes the uncontested leader of the family

The Godfather is a very good example to explain why it is necessary to go to the opposite of the main action to determine the weaknesses and transformation of the hero. If, from the beginning of the story, Michael were presented as a vengeful man, by avenging the men who shot his father, he would remain absolutely the same. His character would not have experienced any transformation. But what if he started the story opposite to revenge? A cautious, conventional man isolated from the rest of his family, who carries out revenge, can end up tyrannical and become the uncontested leader of his family. This is an absolutely radical transformation. But also a totally credible transformation.

Know that the ideas you find using this technique will only correspond to possible transformations for your story. Working on the premise, especially when it comes to characters, is always provisional work. Do not hesitate to think about other transformations as you progress in the writing process. We will explore this determining narrative element in more detail in the next two chapters.

Step 9: Determine the hero's moral choice

The central theme of a story is often crystallized by a moral choice the hero must make, usually towards the end of the story. The theme is your opinion on how to act in the world. It is your point of view on morality, and it is one of the main reasons you write your story.

The best way to express the theme is probably through the structure of the story, through what we call the moral debate. This is where you, the author, defend your vision of life, not with philosophical arguments, but through the actions of characters seeking to achieve a goal (for more details, refer to chapter 5, "The Moral Debate"). The most important step in this debate is undoubtedly the final moral choice you will leave to your hero.

Many authors make the mistake of leaving their hero with a false choice. By false choice, we mean a choice between something positive and something negative. For example, forcing a hero to choose between going to prison and dating the pretty girl. The answer is obvious.

KEY POINT: To make a real choice, your hero must either select one of the two positive possibilities available to him or, but this is rarer, avoid one of the two negative possibilities (as in Sophie's Choice) that present themselves to him.

Make sure to make the two options as equal as possible, one should seem only slightly more attractive than the other. The classic example of choosing between two positive options is the one that opposes love to honor. In A Farewell to Arms, the hero chooses love. In The Maltese Falcon (as in almost all detective stories), the hero chooses honor.

Again, note that this technique will allow you to find possible moral choices. The choice that comes to mind at this stage may very well change completely when you start writing the story. This technique is only meant to force you to start thinking about your theme, in practical terms, from the beginning of the writing process.

Step 10: Think about the audience's reception

Once your work on the premise is done, ask yourself one last question: is this story summarized in one sentence original enough to interest people other than myself?

This is the question of popularity, of commercial appeal. You will need to be inflexible when answering it. If, looking at your premise, you realize that the only people who will be interested in your story are you and your immediate circle, we strongly advise against using it as a base.

You must write first for yourself; write what is close to your heart. But you must not write only for yourself. Authors often make the big mistake of falling into the "either/or" trap: either I write what is close to my heart, or I write something that will sell. This is a false distinction, born from the old romantic idea that one must write in a garret and suffer for one's art.

You may one day come up with an idea that you absolutely must write. You may also have a very good idea and not know if it will interest the public. But never forget that even if you will have many ideas in your life, very few of them can be developed into real stories. You should always try to write something that is close to your heart and that you believe should interest an audience. The things you write must have special meaning for you. But know that when writing for an audience, it is much easier to do what you love.
Q: Can you give some examples from film and television that illustrate the difference between the premise and the guiding principle?

A: These examples illustrate not only the difference between the premise and the guiding principle but also the many ways you can find this guiding principle.

FOUR WEDDINGS AND A FUNERAL based on the development of the story's universe

• Premise: A man falls in love with a woman, but she is already engaged, and at their next meeting, he himself is engaged.

• Guiding Principle: Friends experience four utopias (the weddings) and one nightmarish moment (the funeral), while each of them searches for the ideal partner.

MENTALIST based on a character contrast

• Premise: A so-called medium uses his observational skills and manipulation talents to solve criminal cases.

• Guiding Principle: Series creator Bruno Heller sums it up as: what if Sherlock Holmes and Angelina Jolie had a son?

In this example, the premise and the guiding principle provide the audience with a strong character contrast that generates a strong contrast in the plot. The hero will use this contrast and the combination of Sherlock Holmes' mind and Angelina Jolie's charm to solve criminal cases.

Result: the guiding principle intrigues. It draws us into the story. We wonder: how will he do it? And it provides the story with strong narrative dynamism not only for the first season but for the entire series.

BREAKING BAD based on a character transformation sequence

• Premise: When a chemistry teacher discovers he has terminal cancer, he starts making and selling methamphetamine to fund his medical care and secure his family's future after his death.

• Guiding Principle: Series creator Vince Gilligan explained the underlying process of his premise by stating that he wanted to take his main character "from Mr. Chips to Scarface." From the kind English schoolteacher to the deadliest and most ruthless crime boss.

Gilligan then expressed this character transformation in structural terms: he wanted to take the character "from protagonist to antagonist."

This was not only an excellent guiding principle but also a revolutionary one. Before Breaking Bad, American one-hour TV series (as opposed to miniseries or TV movies) were designed to last forever. This was never the case, of course, but if they were designed with this in mind, it was so that TV networks could profit as much as possible during their airing period and then have as many episodes as possible to sell in syndication. This is the main reason why no characters in dramatic TV series, especially not the main character, ever have an epiphany. Never. Because once the main character has experienced a profound personality change, the series is over. And the golden goose disappears.

But you will notice with Breaking Bad's guiding principle that the entire series is built on the hero's personality change, which is at the very least negative. This means that the series was not designed to last forever but rather to have a defined duration, which Gilligan estimated to be between five and seven seasons. It lasted five.

While this limited duration was a source of disappointment for many loyal viewers, it was also one of the keys, if not the key, to the series' genius. Since the unique and negative character transformation was included from the start in the series' guiding principle, each episode and each season had to be structured based on this transformation. The plot always stems from the character. It seems to us that there is no greater principle of storytelling than this.

Each episode thus follows Walter White's gradual transition from Mr. Chips to Scarface, from Dr. Jekyll to Mr. Hyde. The writers were able to link the episodes so that each season led to a shattering climax.
For the hero, the confrontation is an intense and painful experience. This trial leads him to a significant realization about his true nature. The quality of your story will largely depend on the quality of this realization. To create a good realization, you must first know that this stage, like that of need, has two aspects, one psychological and the other moral.

In a psychological realization, the hero removes the mask behind which he lived and sees himself as he truly is for the first time. This removal of the mask is neither passive nor easy. It is indeed the most active, difficult, and courageous act the hero has accomplished in the story.

Do not have your hero bluntly state what he has learned. That would be moralizing, not subtle enough, and would tend to repel the audience. It is better to suggest the hero's thoughts through the actions he takes that lead him to the realization.

BIG

Josh understands that he must leave his girlfriend, abandon his life in the toy factory, and become a child again if he wants to have a life full of beautiful things and love when he grows up.

CASABLANCA

Rick forgets his cynicism, regains his idealism, and sacrifices his love for Ilsa to become a freedom fighter.

CHINATOWN

Jake's realization is negative. After Evelyn's death, he mutters: "As little as possible." He seems to think that his life is not only useless but also destructive. This character has also hurt someone he loved.

DANCES WITH WOLVES

Dunbar discovers a new reason and way of living thanks to his wife and his beautiful Sioux Lakota family. But ironically, the Lakota way of life is about to disappear, making Dunbar's realization both positive and negative.

If you have given your hero a moral need, his realization must also be moral. The hero not only sees himself in a new light; he reflects on the appropriate way to act towards others. The hero realizes that he was wrong, that he hurt people, and that he must change. He can then prove he has changed by undertaking a moral action.

TOOTSIE

Michael discovers what it truly means to be a man – "When I was a woman, I was a better man with you than I ever was with another woman when I was a man. I just need to learn to stay like that without a dress" – and he apologizes for hurting the woman he loves. You will notice that even if the hero directly states what he has learned, he does so in a clever and funny way, avoiding any form of sermonizing.

THE ADVENTURES OF HUCKLEBERRY FINN

Huck realizes he was wrong to think of Jim as an inferior being and declares he would rather go to hell than tell Jim's master where he is.

From a structural point of view, the stage most closely related to realization is that of need. These two stages define the transformation the hero will undergo (we will study all this in more detail in the next chapter). The need is the beginning of the transformation the character undergoes. The realization is the endpoint of this transformation. The need marks the hero's immaturity at the beginning of the story. It is what he lacks, what hinders him. The realization is the moment when the hero develops, blossoms as a human being (unless this realization is so painful that it ends up destroying him). It is what he has learned, what he has gained, which will allow him to live a better life in the future.

NEW BALANCE

With the new balance, everything returns to normal, and all desire disappears. That being said, there is now a major difference. Following the trial he has undergone, the hero has moved to a higher or lower stage. A fundamental and immutable transformation has occurred within him. If the realization was positive – he understood who he really was and learned to live in harmony with the world – the hero moves to a higher stage. If it was negative – he understood that he had committed a horrible act, an expression of immense inner weakness – or if he is unable to achieve it, the hero is destroyed.

Let's now study some examples of the hero's ascent.

DIE HARD

John fought the criminals, saved his wife, and reaffirmed his love.

PRETTY WOMAN

Vivian left the world of prostitution and now lives with the man she loves (who, of course, happens to be a billionaire).

THE SILENCE OF THE LAMBS

Clarice stopped Buffalo Bill, became an excellent FBI agent, and apparently defeated the ghosts of her past.

The following examples, on the contrary, present the fall of a hero:

OEDIPUS REX

Oedipus blinds himself when he learns he has killed his father and slept with his mother.

THE CONVERSATION

The hero discovers he has been involved in a man's murder and ends up in shock, desperately ransacking his apartment to find bugs.

VERTIGO

The hero drags the woman he loves to the top of a tower to make her confess to a murder, then watches in horror as this woman, overwhelmed by guilt, accidentally falls into the void.
As Bourne approaches his enemies at the Blackbriar headquarters in New York—the eye of the vortex—the pace of the attacks against him quickens, until he jumps from a building just as Vosen tries to kill him.

INCEPTION

Cobb and his team face intense attacks from all directions, at every level of the dream, with an increasingly rapid pace as the van nears the water.

Cobb's main adversary is the CEO, whose mind is filled with powerful defenders repelling any invasion, including an express train and an avalanche, with overwhelming force. Other adversaries include the aggressive memory of Cobb's deceased wife and the world of limbo, from which Cobb might never escape.

Q: How do you write a good story about a character whose essential nature is to have no clear desire? Don't you think that most of the time we are tossed around by daily life and that a clear desire doesn't exist? Shouldn't the story also speak to this for the audience to relate?

A: Some authors believe it's unrealistic for a character to have a single, strong desire. Over a lifetime, this is quite true. But stories aren't real life. They represent a particular moment in a character's life where they confront, or are confronted by, an existential problem and either overcome it or fail. In a story, you isolate this segment of the character's life, where their essence is revealed in action.

Viewed this way, the line of desire is merely the medium through which the author structures the story of this confrontation. It doesn't matter if the character has no goal during all other parts of their life.

AMERICAN BEAUTY

At the beginning of American Beauty, Lester, the main character, is the quintessential reactive man. He's pushed around by his wife, daughter, and boss. He admits his position as a loser. He's an idle man in every sense. And the audience feels he has been this aimless man for almost his entire adult life.

But that's not where the story lies. The story isolates the part of Lester's life where he acquires a line of desire, namely the part where he falls head over heels for his daughter's very pretty best friend. It's the pursuit of this passion, this "limit" situation, that allows him to become the best version of himself, even if he dies shortly after.

Q: Why is it so difficult to write a story about a person without a goal?

A: If you still want to write the story of a character without a goal—in other words, a goal-less hero—know that it's always possible. But let's be clear about the structural challenge you'll face: you'll be forcing yourself to write a story without a backbone, a task akin to entering a boxing ring with one hand tied behind your back.

Without a goal, you can't introduce an external adversary eager to prevent the hero from reaching the goal. Without an adversary, you can't create continuous conflict. Without a goal or conflict, you can't create high stakes—why it's important for the hero to win or lose—which means it will be difficult to engage the reader or viewer. And worst of all, your goal-less character will remain without a goal almost until the end of the story, that is, until the moment of realization, where they overcome their greatest flaw.

Q: So, what should I do with the nine-tenths of my story where my hero has no goal?

A: First, ask yourself again if you really want to tell this story. If so, the best technique for telling the story of a goal-less hero is this: postpone the desire.

Initially, your main character is a reactive hero with no desire other than to survive their oppressive environment. As the story progresses, they become an active hero driven by a strong desire.

Q: How do you execute this technique?

A: It's simple.

1. Initially, deprive the hero of strong desire.

This tends to intensify in the audience's eyes the reality of the hero's weakness, need, and problem. Without a goal to set the story on track, you force the audience to emotionally experience the hero's flaws, pain, and horror of the situation.

2. Assign the hero a stronger and more specific line of desire as they gain more power and the story progresses.

A PROPHET

(screenplay by Thomas Bidegain, Jacques Audiard, Abdel Raouf Dafri, and Nicolas Peufaillit, 2009)

Many French films have used this technique, but none better than A Prophet. In the opening scene, 19-year-old Malik enters prison. He has no knowledge of the rules and hierarchy of this extremely dangerous place. His only goal is to survive, and he doesn't even know how to go about it. You'll notice that survival is a very weak and reactive goal. It's the weakest of all narrative goals.

By delaying the introduction of an active and external goal, the screenwriters force the audience, as well as the hero, to dive into the horror of his experience. Malik and the audience feel the terror of deadly dangers that can arise from anywhere, at any time.
