After being forced to commit a murder by the "king" of the prisoners, Malik learns to decipher and study the workings of this king's mafia operations. The more he learns about the system's workings, the more his goal becomes active and precise.

He moves from merely wanting to survive day-to-day to wanting to become the head of the criminal empire.

This technique has also been used in a very different genre from the crime film, for The Class, which is a drama based on a true story.

Q: Are there other problems that authors inevitably face when they start creating their story?

A: The second biggest problem is that the main structural steps don't evolve from the first step, which is the hero's weakness and need. The result is that the story violates what we consider the cardinal rule of storytelling: the plot must come from the character.

A plot without character is an abstract puzzle for the audience to solve. A character without plot is a medicine the audience must swallow to learn to become better. None of this produces a satisfying story.

Q: What do you mean by "the plot must come from the character"?

A: Several things. But first and foremost, it means that the plot comes from the hero's weakness and need. In other words, the actions a hero takes on the surface to achieve an external goal ultimately result in a transformation within the hero themselves. If the author doesn't establish this link between character and plot and doesn't present events in the plot that will eventually force the character to change (or fail in their efforts), the story will lack emotion and personal resonance for the audience.

Weakness and need are the source of every story, and their resolution is the raison d'être of every story. In a good narrative, all major structural steps are organically connected to each other, but most importantly, they stem from the hero's original flaw.

Q: How do you ensure that the plot comes from the hero's weakness and need?

A: Through the technique of weakness as the primary cause of the story.

Always make weakness and need the primary cause of your story and ensure it drives the narrative logic from beginning to end. If the line of desire is the track the story follows, as well as its backbone, desire should always serve the step of weakness and need. And the same should apply to all other structural steps of the story.

To tell a good story, the most important criterion of all is the connection of all major structural steps to that of weakness and need (to tell a popular story, the most important criterion of all is narrative dynamism, with a clear line of desire).

Q: How do you implement this technique?

A: You must realize each of these connections in your story:

1. Connect weakness and desire.

Of all the desires you can use in your story, the best is the desire that comes from your hero's major weakness. In other words, in their quest for the goal, the hero is forced to finally confront their flaw, which they either overcome or fail to overcome.

This is first and foremost how the plot (desire) comes from the character (weakness). Your story thus becomes both popular and of quality.

BREAKING BAD

A good TV series uses the seven steps to structure each episode (including the first pilot episode), each season, and the entire series. If Breaking Bad was one of the best dramas in television history, it's mainly because all its major structural steps evolve from the primary cause, the hero's weakness and need, and are connected to it.

Weakness and need: Walter White is a timid man who has never accepted that his former partners arranged for him to lose his shares in a very successful chemistry company. Deep down, he believes he is a genius who has never been recognized for his true worth, and he is determined to stop at nothing to gain that recognition.

Desire: To manufacture and sell methamphetamine to pay for his cancer treatment and secure his family's future after his death.

Using his exceptional gift for chemistry, Walt succeeds so well in manufacturing and selling drugs that he becomes known as Heisenberg and kills anyone who gets in his way.

Like in The Godfather, Walt's desire doesn't push him to overcome his weakness, as he is not a character who evolves. His desire exacerbates his weakness until he dies from his corruption.

2. Connect weakness and adversary.

The adversary, who competes with the hero for the same goal, should be the person most capable of attacking the hero's major weakness. And they should attack this weakness while trying to defeat the hero in the pursuit of this goal.

Every time the adversary attacks, they should hit the hero's flaw, press where it hurts the most, and make the situation terrible for them.

Adversaries: Walt's brother-in-law, Hank Schrader, who is a Drug Enforcement Administration agent; his wife, Skylar, who opposes his desire to sell drugs; several criminals, who compete with Walt in the drug trade; and his associate, Jesse, who is both an ally and an adversary, and who argues with him about how the business should be run.

As a DEA agent tasked with ending methamphetamine trafficking, Hank is trained to track Walt and is led to do so. As Walt's wife, Skylar attacks his most intimate vulnerabilities, including his love for his family and his desire to protect them. Other dealers are much better equipped than the chemistry teacher to win a turf war. Jesse knows Walt's secret and takes advantage of the fact that Walt sees him as a surrogate son.

3. Connect weakness and plan.

The plan is only indirectly connected to the weakness, as it is the strategy the hero will use to defeat the adversary and achieve the goal. But every time their plan fails, the hero must be forced to do some introspection, consider their weaknesses, change, and become stronger.

Plan: Manufacture and sell crystal meth without the police, especially his brother-in-law, and his wife finding out.

Each step Walt takes to build his drug business requires him to lie to his wife and son and kill more or less innocent people. With each setback, he becomes more cunning and ruthless.

4. Connect weakness and confrontation.

The confrontation is not just a violent and intense conflict. It must be the hero's worst nightmare, a nightmare specifically designed for this particular character. Of the entire story, the confrontation must be the most intense test of the hero's flaws and skills.

Confrontation: In the final episode of the series, Walt sets up an ambush to kill all the neo-Nazis who murdered Hank, kidnapped Jesse, and stole his money.

Walt's determination to prove he's a genius and settle all his scores leads him to create an ingenious attack plan, which he has no chance of surviving.

5. Connect weakness and realization.

The realization is the moment when the hero understands how wrong they have been, about themselves and their behavior towards others.

Realization: Like in a classic tragedy, Walt realizes too late. Because of his pride and oversized ego, he is trapped on a train heading straight to hell.

6. Connect weakness and new balance.

Everything then returns to normal. But the hero has either overcome their weakness and evolved or failed to do so and fallen.

New balance: Walt dies, but he has settled all his scores. Jesse escapes.

Walt's pride and genius slowly drew him into a trap of corruption and violence from which he couldn't escape.

1. In French in the text (N.d.T.).

– 4 –

Characters

Tootsie was a huge success because the film's main character, played by Dustin Hoffman, was dressed as a woman. True? False. What makes this character funny, and what makes the whole story work, is a network of characters that helps define the hero and allows him to be funny. Look beneath the polished surface of Dustin Hoffman's dress, and you'll see that every character in the story is a particular version of the hero's central moral problem, namely how men mistreat women.

Most authors have a very poor approach to the hero. They start by listing all their distinctive features, tell their story, and then somehow make them change at the end. This technique cannot work.

We will work on a different process, which we believe will be much more useful to you. Here are the different steps:

1. We will start by focusing on the main character, but by observing all the characters together and considering them as different parts of a network of interconnections. We will distinguish each of them by comparing them to others based on their place in the story and their archetype.

2. We will then try to individualize each character by relying on theme and opposition.

3. We will then focus on the hero, "building" them step by step to make them a complex and fleshed-out personality that will appeal to the audience.

4. We will create the adversary in detail, as they are the most important character after the hero and, in many ways, the key to defining the hero.

5. Finally, we will study the different character-related techniques that allow for building conflict throughout the story.

THE CHARACTER NETWORK

The biggest mistake authors make when creating their characters is thinking of the hero and all other characters as distinct individuals. The hero is alone, in their bubble, with no connection to others. The result: not only is the hero weak, but the adversary is a giant with feet of clay, and the secondary characters are even weaker.

This gross mistake is exacerbated in scripts that emphasize the high concept premise. In these stories, the hero seems to be the only person that matters. But ironically, this extreme emphasis on the hero, rather than defining them more clearly, tends to make them appear as a bland marketing tool.

To create good characters, they must be conceived as different parts of a network where each helps define the other. In other words, a character is often defined by what they are not.

KEY POINT: When creating a hero or any character, the most important step is to compare and connect them to all others.

Every time you compare a character to your hero, you force yourself to distinguish your hero in new ways. And you also begin to conceive secondary characters as fully-fledged human beings, as complex and as important as your hero.

All characters connect to each other and define each other based on four points: their role in the story, their archetype, the theme, and the opposition.

Character network based on their functions in the story

Every character must serve the story's purpose, which is defined by the guiding principle (see chapter 2, "The Premise"). Every character has a specific role, a function to fulfill to allow the story to achieve this purpose. Director Peter Brook, speaking about actors, raises an important point for authors seeking to create characters:

[Brecht] insisted that all actors must serve the action of the play. [...] If [each actor] saw themselves in relation to the whole play, they would understand that not only do their overly detailed mannerisms oppose the play's needs, but also that too many undue characteristics could harm them and make their own appearance less remarkable1.

Even if the audience is mostly interested in the nature of the transformation that has occurred within the hero, you can only show them this transformation if each character, including the hero, plays their role in the team. Let's now study the function of the main types of fictional characters.

Hero

The most important character is the main character, or hero. This is the person who has the central problem and leads the action to solve this problem. The hero decides to seek a goal (desire) but possesses certain weaknesses and needs that tend to prevent them from succeeding.

All other characters in the story represent opposition, alliance, or a combination of both, with the hero. Moreover, the story's twists are largely produced by the ebb and flow of opposition and friendship between the various characters and the hero.

• Hero in Hamlet Hamlet

Adversary

The adversary is the character who seeks to prevent the hero from achieving their desire. The adversary must not merely put obstacles in the hero's way. That would be mechanical.

Remember that the adversary must desire the same thing as the hero. This means that the hero and the adversary must come into direct conflict throughout the narrative. This notion of conflict is often missing from stories. That's why it's important to seek the deepest conflict that opposes the hero to their adversary.

The relationship between the hero and the adversary is perhaps the most important of the relationships between characters. Thus, by working on the conflict that opposes them, one also allows the broader themes and stakes of the story to unfold.

Moreover, the adversary is not necessarily a character the hero hates. The hero may love or hate them. The adversary is simply the person who stands opposite. They may be a more sympathetic character than the hero, more moral, and even their friend or lover.

• Main adversary in Hamlet King Claudius

• Second adversary Queen Gertrude

• Third adversary Polonius, the king's advisor

Ally

The ally is there to help the hero. They also serve as a spokesperson, allowing the audience to better understand their values and feelings. Generally, the ally's goal is the same as the hero's, but occasionally the ally has a goal of their own.

• Ally in Hamlet Horatio

False Ally

The false ally is a character who seems to be the hero's friend but is actually an adversary. Creating such a character is one of the best ways to strengthen opposition and plot twists.

The false ally is always one of the most fascinating and complex characters in the story, as they are generally torn by a dilemma. By pretending to be the hero's ally, the false ally ends up feeling close to the hero. And so, while trying to defeat them, they often end up helping them win.

• False allies in Hamlet Ophelia, Rosencrantz, and Guildenstern

False Adversary

On the surface, this character fights against the hero, but they are actually an ally. The false adversary is not as common a character as the false ally because they are not as useful to the author. The plot, as we will see in chapter 8, stems from opposition, and particularly from opposition hidden beneath the surface. Allies, including those who initially seem to be adversaries, cannot offer us the same degree of conflict and surprises as an adversary.

• False adversary in Hamlet None

Secondary Character

The secondary character is perhaps one of the most misunderstood characters in fiction. Most authors conceive them as the hero of the subplot—for example, the girlfriend in a detective story. But that is not the role of a true secondary character.

The secondary character has a very specific function in the story, and again the comparative method comes into play. The subplot should be used to compare how the hero and the secondary character face the same problem. It is through contrast that the secondary character highlights the traits and dilemmas of the main character.

Let's take a closer look at Hamlet to better understand how to create a true secondary character. One could say that Hamlet's problem, reduced to a single sentence, is to avenge the man who killed his father. Now, it so happens that Laertes' problem is also to avenge the man who killed his father. The contrast lies in the fact that one murder was premeditated while the other was the result of a mistake and a passionate, impulsive act.

KEY POINT: The secondary character is generally not an ally.
The secondary character, like the ally and the adversary, provides an opportunity to define the hero through comparison and to advance the plot. The ally helps the hero achieve their main goal. The secondary character follows a path parallel to that of the hero and achieves a different result.

• Secondary character in Hamlet: Laertes, son of Polonius

Let's now try to break down some well-known stories to better understand how characters can be illuminated by contrast.

THE SILENCE OF THE LAMBS

(Novel by Thomas Harris, screenplay by Ted Tally, 1991)

This is the story of a new FBI recruit named Clarice who is searching for a serial killer known as Buffalo Bill. On the advice of her boss, Jack, she decides to seek help from another serial killer already in prison, the infamous Hannibal "the Cannibal" Lecter. Hannibal initially appears hostile but eventually provides Clarice with training far superior to anything she could have received at the FBI.

• Heroine: Clarice Starling

• Main adversary: Buffalo Bill, the serial killer

• Second adversary: Dr. Chilton, the prison director

• False ally: None

• Ally: Jack, her superior at the FBI

• False adversary: Hannibal Lecter

• Secondary character: None

AMERICAN BEAUTY

(Alan Ball, 1999)

American Beauty is a dramatic comedy set in the suburbs. Lester's main adversaries are within his family: his wife, Carolyn, and his daughter, Jane, both of whom despise him. Lester soon falls for one of his daughter's friends, Angela. But since Lester is a married man and Angela is a teenager, she becomes a new adversary. Living close to Lester is the rigid and conservative Colonel Frank Fitts, who disapproves of Lester's lifestyle. Brad, Lester's colleague, tries to get him fired.

After blackmailing his company for better severance pay, Lester begins to live life as he pleases and finds an ally in Ricky Fitts, his neighbor, who sells him cannabis. Ricky and his father, Frank, are also secondary characters. Lester's central problem is determining how he could live a meaningful life in a highly conformist society that values only appearance and money. Ricky reacts to the military and dreary atmosphere of his home by selling drugs and spying on others with a camera. Frank represses his homosexual desires by imposing a strict discipline on himself and others.

• Hero: Lester

• Main adversary: Carolyn, his wife

• Second adversary: Jane, his daughter

• Third adversary: Angela, Jane's friend

• Fourth adversary: Colonel Frank Fitts

• Fifth adversary: Brad, his colleague

• Ally: Ricky Fitts

• False ally: None

• False adversary: None

• Secondary characters: Frank, Ricky

TECHNIQUE: TWO MAIN CHARACTERS

There are two popular genres or story forms that seem to involve the presence of two main characters: the love story and the friendship story. The friendship story is actually a combination of three genres: action, love, and comedy. Let's now try to understand how the character network functions in these two story forms, based on the role each protagonist plays in the narrative.

Love Stories

To create two equally well-developed characters, you must adhere to a number of imperatives throughout the character network. The function of the love story is to present to the audience the value of an alliance between two equal beings. The central concept of love stories is particularly profound. Love stories tell us that one cannot become a true individual alone. One can only become a unique and authentic individual by allying with another. It is through the love of the other that each can flourish and become their true self.

Expressing this beautiful idea with an appropriate character network is not an easy task. If you try to write a love story with two main characters, you will have two backbones, two lines of desire, two paths that the story will attempt to follow. It is therefore important to ensure that one of the characters is slightly more central than the other. You must define the needs of both characters at the beginning of the story, but you must give one of them the main line of desire. Most authors assign this thread to the man because, in our culture, it is the man who is supposed to court the woman. Thus, the best way to make your love story original is probably to give the line of desire to the woman, as in Moonstruck, Broadcast News, or Gone with the Wind.

When you assign a character the line of desire, you automatically make them the more powerful of the two characters. In terms of narrative functions, this means that the beloved, the person who is desired, is actually the main adversary, not a second hero. Generally, the character network is completed by creating one or more external adversaries, such as family members who oppose the union. There may also be other suitors seeking to win over the hero or the beloved, allowing for a comparison of different versions of desired and desirable men or women.

THE PHILADELPHIA STORY

(Play by Philip Barry, screenplay by Donald Ogden Stewart, 1940)

• Heroine: Tracy Lord

• Main adversary: Dexter, her ex-husband

• Second adversary: Mike, the reporter

• Third adversary: George, her socialite fiancé

• False ally: Dinah, her sister

• Ally: Her mother

• False adversary: Her father

• Secondary character: Liz, the photographer

TOOTSIE

(Larry Gelbart and Murray Schisgal, based on an original story by Don McGuire and Larry Gelbart, 1982)

• Hero: Michael

• Main adversary: Julie

• Second adversary: Ron, the director

• Third adversary: John, the doctor in the series

• Fourth adversary: Les, Julie's father

• False ally: Sandy

• Allies: George, Michael's agent; Jeff, his roommate

• False adversary: None

• Secondary characters: Ron, Sandy

Friendship Stories (Buddy Stories)

The strategy of using the relationship between two friends as the foundation of a character network is as old as the story of Gilgamesh and his great friend Enkidu. There is also the more unequal but highly edifying partnership of Don Quixote and Sancho Panza, the dreamer and the realist, the master and the servant.

The friendship strategy essentially allows you to split the hero into two roles, showing two different approaches to life and two kinds of talents. The two characters are "married" into a team so that the audience can see their differences but also understand how these differences actually allow them to function better together, so that the whole is not just the sum of its parts.

As in the love story, one of the two characters must be more central than the other. This is generally the thinker, the schemer, or the strategist, because this character develops the plan and triggers the line of desire. The friend is a kind of double of the hero, similar in many ways but also different.

Structurally, the friend is both the main adversary and the main ally of the hero. He is not a second hero. But keep in mind that the opposition between the two friends is almost never serious or tragic. It generally takes the form of rather jovial bickering.

Generally, the character network is completed with at least one external, dangerous, and permanent adversary. And since most friendship stories use a journey with mythical value, the friends encounter many secondary adversaries along the way. These characters are most often people the two friends do not know, and they follow one another at a rapid pace. Each of these adversaries should represent a part of society that hates them or seeks to separate them. This technique is perfect for quickly defining secondary characters and distinguishing them from one another. It also allows for deepening and broadening the scope of the friendship story by defining various aspects of society through their relationship with the two main characters.

One of the most important elements of this character network is the fundamental conflict between the two friends. There must be a stumbling block in their relationship, a problem that constantly interferes. This allows for constant opposition between the two main characters while most other adversaries are strangers who appear and disappear immediately.

BUTCH CASSIDY AND THE SUNDANCE KID

(William Goldman, 1969)

• Hero: Butch

• Main adversary: Sundance

• Second adversaries: The railroad chief, E. H. Harriman (who never appears) and his hidden henchmen, the posse led by Joe Lefors

• Third adversary: The Bolivian police and army

• False ally: Harvey, who questions Butch's position in the gang

• Ally: Etta, Sundance's girlfriend

• False adversary: Sheriff Ray

• Secondary characters: None

TECHNIQUE: MULTIPLE HEROES AND NARRATIVE DYNAMISM

While all popular genres have a single hero, there are stories that do not belong to a genre and have multiple heroes. Remember that in Chapter 1, we studied how stories unfold, with linear action and simultaneous action at opposite extremes. To create a sense of simultaneous narrative movement, the simplest way is to have multiple heroes. Instead of following the development of a single character (linear), the story compares the actions that several heroes undertake simultaneously. The risk is presenting so many characters at once that the story is no longer a story; it loses narrative dynamism. Even the most simultaneous stories must have some linear quality, with events following one another in time.

To succeed with a multiple-hero story, you must apply the seven steps of narrative structure to each main character – weaknesses and need, desire, adversary, plan, confrontation, realization, and new equilibrium. If not, your character is not a main character; the audience does not see them go through the inevitable stages of any development.

Be aware that having multiple heroes automatically slows down narrative dynamism. The more characters you present in detail, the more likely you are to see the pace of your story slow down.

However, there are certain techniques that can accelerate the narrative dynamism of a multiple-hero story:

• Have one character emerge throughout the story so that they appear more central than the others.

• Assign all characters the same line of desire.

• Make the hero of one narrative line the adversary of another narrative line.

• Connect the characters by making them all examples of a single subject or theme.

• Use suspense (cliffhanger) at the end of one narrative line to switch to another line.

• Bring distant characters together in one place.

• Reduce time. The story can, for example, take place over the course of a day or a night.

• Have characters occasionally meet by coincidence.

Here are some examples of multiple-hero stories that use one or more of these techniques: American Graffiti, Hannah and Her Sisters, L.A. Confidential, Pulp Fiction, The Canterbury Tales, La Ronde, Nashville, Crash, and Smiles of a Summer Night.

TECHNIQUE: ELIMINATING SUPERFLUOUS CHARACTERS

Superfluous characters are the primary cause of a fragmented story. The first question you must ask yourself when creating a character is: "Does he or she fulfill an important function in the overall story?" If not – if he or she does not provide more texture or color – you should consider eliminating them entirely. Their limited value would probably not justify the time they would take in the narrative line.

CHARACTER NETWORK BY ARCHETYPES

Contrasts and connections between characters are also established through archetypes. Archetypes are fundamental psychological models of personality; they are the roles a person can play in society, the essential ways of interacting with others. As they are common to all human beings, archetypes cross cultural boundaries and have universal reach.

Using archetypes as the foundation for creating your characters generally gives them weight very quickly because each type expresses a fundamental model that the audience recognizes, manifesting both in the character and in their interactions with society.

An archetype has a deep resonance for the audience, evoking strong feelings. But for the author, it is a somewhat crude tool in their repertoire. If the archetype is not enriched with details, it can quickly become a stereotype.

KEY POINT: Always specify the archetype to individualize it while respecting the unique character of your character.

Following the work of psychologist Carl Jung, many writers have explored the meaning of archetypes and the connections between them. For fiction writers, the most important concept when talking about archetypes is probably the notion of shadow. The shadow is the negative tendency of the archetype, a psychological trap one can fall into when playing the role of this archetype or obeying it.

We will now present the different major archetypes and their shadows and study them practically so that you can use them to create a story. We will therefore consider the different archetypes based on their beneficial role and the probable weaknesses each may have in a story.

The King or Father

• Strength: Governs his family or people with wisdom, insight, and determination so that each member can thrive and succeed.

• Inherent weaknesses: May force his wife, children, or people to conform to a set of strict and oppressive rules; may completely cut himself off from the emotional field of his family or kingdom; or may seek to force his family or people to live only for his pleasure.

• Examples: King Arthur, Zeus, The Tempest, The Godfather, Rick in Casablanca, King Lear, Hamlet, Aragorn and Sauron in The Lord of the Rings, Agamemnon in The Iliad, Citizen Kane, Star Wars, Stanley in A Streetcar Named Desire, American Beauty, Willy Loman in Death of a Salesman, Fort Apache, Meet Me in St. Louis, Mary Poppins, Tootsie, The Philadelphia Story, Othello, Red River, Howards End, Chinatown.

The Queen or Mother

• Strength: Weaves a cocoon of care and protection in which her children or people can thrive.

• Inherent weaknesses: Her need to protect and control can become tyrannical; she may play the guilt or shame card to keep her children close to her and ensure her own comfort.

• Examples: Hamlet, Macbeth, Hera, Stella in A Streetcar Named Desire, Elizabeth, American Beauty, The Lion in Winter, The Glass Menagerie, Long Day's Journey into Night, and Adam's Rib.

These archetypes can be male or female:

The Wise Old Man, Mentor, or Teacher

• Strength: Transmits his wisdom and knowledge so that individuals can improve their daily lives and societies can evolve positively.

• Inherent weaknesses: May force his disciples to think in a certain way or make his own apology rather than that of the ideas he should defend.

• Examples: Yoda in Star Wars, Hannibal Lecter in The Silence of the Lambs, The Matrix, Gandalf and Saruman in The Lord of the Rings, Wuthering Heights, Polonius in Hamlet, Homais in Madame Bovary, Miss Havisham in Great Expectations, Mr. Micawber in David Copperfield, and The Iliad.

The Warrior

• Strength: Is the physical champion of good.

• Inherent weaknesses: May live by the harsh principle of "Kill or be killed"; may believe that everything weak must be destroyed and thus become the champion of evil.

• Examples: Achilles and Hector in The Iliad; Luke Skywalker and Han Solo in Star Wars; The Seven Samurai; King Arthur; Thor; Ares; Theseus; Gilgamesh; Aragorn, Legolas, and Gimli in The Lord of the Rings; Patton; Die Hard; Sonny in The Godfather; A Streetcar Named Desire; The Great Santini; Shane; Platoon; Sundance in Butch Cassidy and the Sundance Kid; Terminator and the Alien saga.

The Magician or Shaman

• Strength: Has the power to make deep reality visible, inaccessible to the senses, and can balance and control the hidden forces of nature.

• Inherent weaknesses: May manipulate deep reality to enslave others and destroy the natural order.

• Examples: Macbeth, the "Harry Potter" series, The Phantom of the Opera, Merlin, Star Wars, Chinatown, Vertigo, Gandalf and Saruman in The Lord of the Rings, A Connecticut Yankee in King Arthur's Court, The Conversation, and detectives like Sherlock Holmes, Hercule Poirot, and Nick Charles in The Thin Man.

The Trickster

The trickster is a derivative of the magician archetype and is extremely popular in modern fiction.

• Strength: Uses cunning, confidence, and the power of words to achieve his ends.

• Inherent weaknesses: May become a compulsive liar interested only in himself.

• Examples: Odysseus in The Odyssey, Men in Black, Beverly Hills Cop, Crocodile Dundee, Volpone, Loki in Norse mythology, Iago in Othello, Indiana Jones, Home Alone, Catch Me If You Can, Hannibal Lecter in The Silence of the Lambs, Br'er Rabbit, Butch in Butch Cassidy and the Sundance Kid, Sergeant Bilko in The Phil Silvers Show, Michael in Tootsie, American Beauty, Verbal in The Usual Suspects, Oliver Twist, Vanity Fair, Tom Sawyer, and The Adventures of Huckleberry Finn.

The Artist or Clown

• Strength: Defines excellence for his people, or conversely, shows them what doesn't work; shows them beauty and a vision of the future, or that a beautiful appearance can hide stupidity and ugliness.

• Inherent weaknesses: May become a true fascist insisting on the notion of perfection; may create a world where everything can be controlled; or simply, by criticizing everything, may no longer value anything.

• Examples: Stephen in A Portrait of the Artist as a Young Man or Joyce's Ulysses, Achilles in The Iliad, Pygmalion, Frankenstein, King Lear, Hamlet, the sword master in The Seven Samurai, Michael in Tootsie, Blanche in A Streetcar Named Desire, Verbal in The Usual Suspects, Holden Caulfield in The Catcher in the Rye, The Philadelphia Story, and David Copperfield.

The Lover

• Strength: Provides the attention, understanding, and sensuality the other needs to become a happy and fulfilled person.

• Inherent weaknesses: May lose himself in the other or force the other to remain in the shadows.

• Examples: Paris in The Iliad, Heathcliff and Cathy in Wuthering Heights, Aphrodite, Romeo and Juliet, Etta in Butch Cassidy and the Sundance Kid, The Philadelphia Story, Hamlet, The English Patient, Kay in The Godfather, Camille, Moulin Rouge, Tootsie, Rick and Ilsa in Casablanca, Howards End, and Madame Bovary.

The Rebel

• Strength: Has the courage to detach from the masses and take action to fight against a system that oppresses the people.

• Inherent weaknesses: Often does not provide or cannot provide a better alternative and merely destroys the system or society.

• Examples: Prometheus, Loki, Heathcliff in Wuthering Heights, American Beauty, Holden Caulfield in The Catcher in the Rye, Achilles in The Iliad, Hamlet, Rick in Casablanca, Howards End, Madame Bovary, Rebel Without a Cause, Crime and Punishment, Notes from Underground, and Reds.

Here is a simple but effective example of a character network highlighting the contrast between archetypes:

STAR WARS

(George Lucas, 1977)

Luke (+ R2-D2 + C-3PO)

(prince-warrior-magician)

Darth Vader

(king-warrior-magician)

Han Solo (+ Chewbacca)

(rebel-warrior)

Princess Leia

(princess)

INDIVIDUALIZING CHARACTERS IN THE NETWORK

Once the oppositions between your essential characters are defined in the character network, you must transform the archetypes into real individuals. But again, you cannot create these unique individuals in isolation, from scratch, as if they all merely coexisted in the same story.

You must create your hero, your adversary, and your secondary characters by comparing them to each other, but this time first based on theme and opposition. We will study the theme in detail in Chapter 5, "The Moral Debate." But we must now take a closer look at some of the essential concepts of the notion of theme.

The theme expresses your point of view on how to act in the world, expressed through the actions undertaken by the characters in the story. The theme cannot be a subject, such as "racism" or "freedom." The theme is your moral vision, your idea of what is good or bad in terms of action, and it is unique to each of the stories you write.

KEY POINT: To individualize your characters, start by finding the moral problem at the heart of the premise. Then deploy the various possibilities of the moral problem throughout the story.

You deploy these various possibilities through opposition. In practice, you must create a group of adversaries (and allies) that forces the hero to confront the central moral problem. And all adversaries must be variations on the theme, each facing the same moral problem differently.

Let's now see how to put this crucial technique into practice.

1. Start by writing what you think is the central moral problem of your story. If you have studied the techniques of the premise, you should have already determined this problem.

2. Compare your hero to all other characters, and all characters to each other, based on these parameters:

• Their weaknesses

• Their need – both psychological and moral

• Their desire

• Their values

• Their power, status, and skills

• How each of them faces the central moral problem of the story
3. To make these comparisons, start with the most important relationship in any story, that of the hero and their main adversary. In many ways, this adversary is the key to creating your story because they represent the most effective way to define your hero, but also because they can help you create an excellent network of characters.

4. After comparing the hero to their main adversary, compare them to their other adversaries, then to their allies. Finish by comparing the adversaries and allies among themselves.

Remember that each character must present a different approach to the hero's central moral problem (variations on a single theme).

Let's look at some examples to better understand how this technique works.

TOOTSIE

(Larry Gelbart and Murray Schisgal, based on an original story by Don McGuire and Larry Gelbart, 1982)

Tootsie is an excellent example that demonstrates how you can start from a high-concept premise and create an organic story. It is a classic illustration of what is called switch comedy, a premise technique that suddenly reveals to the hero that they have changed (switch), becoming something or someone else. There are hundreds of switch comedies, a genre dating back at least to Mark Twain, who was a master of it.

But the vast majority of switch comedy authors fail miserably. This is because most of them ignore the major weakness of the high-concept premise: it only provides two or three scenes. The screenwriters of Tootsie, however, mastered the art of storytelling; they succeeded in creating a network of strong characters, each individualized through comparison with the others. As in all high-concept stories, Tootsie includes the two or three funny switch scenes, where Dustin Hoffman's character, Michael, dresses as a woman for the first time, auditions, and triumphantly meets his agent at the restaurant.

But the screenwriters of Tootsie created much more than two or three funny scenes. In working on the story, they began by giving Michael a central moral problem: how a man should behave towards women. The hero's moral problem is that he must learn to act properly with women, especially the one he falls in love with. The screenwriters then created several adversaries, each a variation on the theme of how men treat women or how women allow themselves to be treated by men. For example:

• Ron, the director, lies to Julie, cheats on her, then justifies himself by saying he would have hurt her even more if he had told her the truth.

• Julie, the actress Michael falls in love with, is beautiful and talented but lets men, especially Ron, take advantage of her and lead her by the nose.

• John, the actor playing the doctor, is a womanizer who uses his fame and status in the series to misbehave with the actresses he works with.

• Sandy, Michael's friend, has such a poor opinion of herself that when Michael lies and cheats on her, she apologizes.

• Les, Julie's father, falls in love with Michael (disguised as Dorothy) and treats him with the utmost respect when courting him with flowers and invitations to dance.

• Rita Marshall, the producer, is a woman who has repressed her femininity and attraction to other women to gain a position of power.

• Michael, disguised as Dorothy, helps the women in the series stand up to the men and gain the respect and love they deserve. But when Michael is dressed as a man, he courts all the women at once: he pretends to be attracted to Sandy, but only to try to keep Julie away from Ron.

GREAT EXPECTATIONS

(Charles Dickens, 1861)

Dickens, a great master of storytelling, is renowned for his networks of characters. His most instructive novel is undoubtedly Great Expectations, which probably features the most accomplished network of characters.

The distinctive mark of the character network in Great Expectations is the double pair of characters: Magwitch and Pip, Miss Havisham and Estella. The two protagonists of each pair have a fundamentally identical relationship—that of mentor to disciple—but this relationship is experienced very differently in each pair. Magwitch, the former convict, secretly gives Pip money and freedom, but not a sense of responsibility. Miss Havisham's excessive control over Estella, and her bitterness towards men, ultimately turn the young girl into a woman too cold to be capable of love.

VANITY FAIR

(William Makepeace Thackeray, 1847)

Thackeray referred to Vanity Fair as "a novel without a hero," by which he meant a novel without a heroic character worthy of emulation. All his characters represent variations of predatory animals trampling each other for money or power. This is what makes the entire network of characters in Vanity Fair unique. Note that the choice of this network is the most important means by which the author expressed his moral viewpoint and made it original.

Within the network, the main contrast between characters is that which opposes Becky and Amelia. Both have very different ideas about how a woman should find a husband. Amelia is immoral out of sheer stupidity, while Becky is immoral through calculation.

THE HISTORY OF TOM JONES, A FOUNDLING

(Henry Fielding, 1749)

The History of Tom Jones illustrates the immense impact that the choice of character network has on the hero. This comic "picaresque" novel features a very large number of characters. This great social factory generates multiple simultaneous but shallow actions. In comedies of this type, we discover the nature of the characters by watching them act foolishly and/or maliciously.

And this rule also applies to the hero. By making Tom a foolish innocent and basing the plot on false information about his identity, Fielding imposed limits on himself regarding the importance of awareness and depth he could give his hero. Tom still has a central moral problem—remaining faithful to his great love—but he has very little responsibility.

CREATING YOUR HERO

Creating on paper a main character that appears human is a complex task that involves going through several steps. Like a great painter, you must build your character in overlapping layers. You are more likely to succeed if you start from the outside, that is, from the network of characters. Whatever the nature of the character network you have built, it will have a strong impact on the hero that emerges from it, and it will guide you when you start detailing this main character.

Creating your hero, step 1:

The characteristics of a good hero

The first step is to ensure that your hero meets the requirements of any hero in any story; all of which relate to the function of the main character: they lead the story from beginning to end.

1. Your main character must always be fascinating

A character who drives the action must necessarily attract and hold the audience's attention at all times. There should be no downtime, pauses, or pointless filler (nor metaphors that state the obvious). From the moment your hero becomes boring, the story stops.

The best way to attract and hold the audience's attention is undoubtedly to make the hero mysterious, showing the audience that this character is hiding something. This tends to force the reader or viewer not to remain passive and to actively participate in the story. They should be able to say: "This character is hiding something, and I want to find out what it is."

2. The audience must identify with the character, but not too much

"Identify" is a term that many people throw around, but few know how to define. We think that readers or viewers must identify with the hero to become emotionally attached to this character. But what does that really mean?

People who think characters are created by accumulating characteristics also think the audience identifies with distinctive marks such as social origin, job, clothing, standard of living, race, and gender. Nothing could be further from the truth. If identification were based on such distinctive marks, no one could identify with anyone, as characters have too many characteristics for a reader or viewer to share them all.

A reader or viewer identifies with a character based on two elements: their desire and the moral problem they must face—in short, the desire and the need, the first two of the seven major steps of narrative structure. It is the desire that drives the story as the audience wants the hero to succeed. The moral problem is how one should behave with others, a problem the audience wants the hero to resolve.

Be careful, however, that the audience does not identify too much with the character: they would then be unable to step back and see the transformation and evolution that have occurred in them. Once again, Peter Brook's advice to actors provides excellent lessons for authors:

When [the actor] considers himself in relation to the whole play [...], he takes a different look at the characteristics he shares or does not share with the character, and ultimately, he makes different decisions than he would have if he always thought that "identification" with the character was all that mattered.

We will explain in Chapter 8, "The Plot," how to create distance between the audience and the hero at the most appropriate moment in the story.

3. The audience must feel empathy for the hero, not sympathy

Everyone talks about the need to make the hero sympathetic. Having a sympathetic hero (compassion) can be considered positive as the audience will want them to achieve their goal. Because the audience participates in the storytelling.

But it should be noted that some of the most memorable heroes in literature and cinema are not at all sympathetic. And they remain fascinating nonetheless. There are also stories where the hero is initially sympathetic but then begins to act immorally—becoming unsympathetic—when they lose a battle to their adversary. But the audience does not close the book or leave the theater for all that.

KEY POINT: What matters is that the audience understands the character, not necessarily that they approve of everything they do.

Feeling empathy means being interested in someone and understanding them. Thus, to hold the audience's attention—even when the hero is not sympathetic or acts immorally—you must show what motivates the character's actions.

KEY POINT: Always explain why your hero acts as they do.

Showing the reader or viewer why the character chooses to do what they do allows them to understand the cause of the action (empathy) without necessarily approving the action itself (sympathy).

Showing the hero's motivations to the audience does not mean showing them to the hero themselves. Often, at first, the hero is unaware of the true reason driving them to achieve the goal and only discovers their true motivation at the end of the story, at the moment of realization.

4. Your hero must have a moral and psychological need

The most memorable characters all have a moral and psychological need. Remember the difference between the two: the psychological need affects only the hero; the moral need involves learning to act correctly towards others. By giving your hero a need that is both moral and psychological, you increase the character's impact on the story and, in turn, increase the emotional power of the story.

Creating your hero, step 2:

The transformation of the character

"Character transformation," also known as "character arc," "character development," or "range of transformation," refers to the character's development throughout the story. This is undoubtedly the most difficult but also the most important of all the steps in the writing process.

"Character development," like "identification," is one of those expressions that everyone uses but no one seems able to define. Let's return for a moment to the standard technique of character creation, where the author imagines an isolated person and then tries to list all the characteristics they could be endowed with. The author tells the story of this person and makes them undergo a transformation at the end. This is what we call "the school of small change." A change is inserted in the last scene, and presto, the character is transformed. This technique does not work, which is why we will try to explore another one.

The self expressed as a character

Before discussing the true transformation of the character and the techniques to create it, we must first get an idea of what the self is, since it is this self that will be transformed. And for this, we must ask: what is the role of the self in a narrative?

A character is a fictional being, created to simultaneously show that a human being is completely unique in an unlimited number of ways, yet remains always a human being, endowed with characteristics we all share. This fictional self is then put into action, in space and time, and compared to others to show how a person can lead a good or bad life and how they can continue to grow throughout their existence, even after they have stopped physical growth.

Unsurprisingly, there is no monolithic conception of the self in the history of fiction. Below are some of the most important views on the self. In fiction, the self can be:

• An individual unit of personality, internally governed with an iron hand. The self is clearly separated from others but seeks to fulfill its "destiny." This is what the self was born for, what it was endowed with its various talents and abilities for. This idea of the self is very common in myths, whose heroes are often warriors.

• An individual unit composed of many often contradictory desires and needs. The self has a strong desire to connect with others and sometimes even to encompass another. This conception of the self is found in a wide variety of stories, but particularly in the work of modern playwrights such as Ibsen, Chekhov, Strindberg, O'Neill, and Williams.

• A series of roles played by the person according to what society demands at the moment. Twain is perhaps the most famous representative of this view. His switch comedies A Connecticut Yankee in King Arthur's Court and The Prince and the Pauper were created to demonstrate that people are largely determined by the position they occupy in society. And even in The Adventures of Huckleberry Finn and Tom Sawyer, Twain emphasizes the power of the roles we play and how society often dictates our being.

• An accumulation of images so unstable, porous, malleable, weak, and inconsistent that it can metamorphose into something completely different. Kafka, Borges, and Faulkner are the greatest representatives of this sense of the self. In popular fiction, this self is found in horror stories, particularly those dealing with vampires, cat people, and werewolves.

If these various notions of the self present significant differences, the goal of character transformation and the techniques to achieve it are relatively similar for each of them.

KEY POINT: Character transformation does not occur at the end of the story; it occurs at the beginning, or more precisely, it is made possible from the beginning by the way you prepare it.

KEY POINT: Do not conceive your main character as a fixed and determined person whose story you will then tell. You must think of your hero as a range of transformation, a range of possibilities, from the very beginning. You must determine the range of transformation from the start of the writing process, otherwise, the hero will not be able to undergo their transformation at the end of the story.

We cannot emphasize enough the importance of this technique. If you manage to master the range of transformation, you will win the "game" of storytelling. If not, no matter how much you revise and rewrite, you will never achieve a correct result.

Also, consider this simple rule: the narrower the range of transformation, the less interesting the story; the wider the range, the more interesting the story, but the greater the risk, as characters do not change much in the limited time frame in which they appear in most stories.

But what exactly does "range of transformation" mean? It refers to the "different possibilities of self" of the character, possibilities defined by their understanding of themselves. Character transformation is the moment when the hero finally becomes who they must be. In other words, the main character cannot suddenly become someone else (except on rare occasions). The main character completes a transformation process that occurs throughout the story and allows them to become themselves more deeply and precisely.

This process by which the hero becomes themselves but more deeply can seem ridiculously ethereal, which is why it is often misunderstood. We will therefore be very precise: your character can undergo several transformations throughout your story, but not all will be "character transformations."

For example, you can present a character who starts poor and ends rich. Or a peasant who becomes king. Or an alcoholic who learns to become sober. All these evolutions are transformations. But not all these transformations are character transformations.

KEY POINT: A true character transformation involves questioning and modifying their fundamental beliefs, leading the hero to adopt new moral behavior.

A character's self-knowledge is based on their beliefs about the world and themselves. What they believe they must do to lead a good life and what they believe they must do to get what they want. In good stories, as the hero seeks to achieve their goal, they are forced to question their deepest beliefs. Pushed to their limits, they discover what they truly believe in, the values that must guide their actions, and then act according to this new moral code.

If authors have been able to express different versions of the self, they have also used different strategies to express character transformation. We wrote in Chapter 1 that the story "walked" on two "legs," action and discovery. We can say that, generally, in the long history of fiction, we have moved from stories almost exclusively based on action—with myth, the audience learns simply by taking example from the hero's actions—to narratives placing much more emphasis on discovery, where the audience's main concern is first to determine what exactly is happening, who these characters really are, what the true nature of the events occurring is, before drawing conclusions on how to lead one's life.

These discovery-based stories are found in authors like Joyce, Woolf, Faulkner, Godard, Stoppard, Frayn, and Ayckbourn, as well as in films as diverse as Last Year at Marienbad, Blow-Up, The Conformist, Memento, The Conversation, and The Usual Suspects.

For readers or viewers, character transformation in discovery-based stories does not simply involve watching the hero gain a piece of self-understanding at the end of the story. The audience must truly participate in the transformation by becoming several characters throughout the story, not just by experiencing their different points of view, but also by having to determine which point of view they are sharing.

The possibilities for character transformations are limitless. The development of your hero depends on their starting beliefs, how they question them, and how they transform them at the end of the story. This is how you can make your story unique and truly yours.

But some types of character transformations are more common than others. We will now study some of them, not because you must necessarily use them in one of your stories, but because understanding them will help you better master this major technique.

1. From child to adult Also known as a coming-of-age story, this type of transformation has, of course, absolutely nothing to do with a child physically becoming an adult. You may think this is an obvious fact, but many authors of coming-of-age stories make the mistake of defining their character's development through their first sexual experience. Although this experience can be tragic or amusing, it has nothing to do with character transformation.

A true coming-of-age story presents a young person questioning their fundamental beliefs and modifying them before acting according to this new moral code. This type of transformation can be observed in stories such as The Catcher in the Rye, The Adventures of Huckleberry Finn, David Copperfield, The Sixth Sense, Big, Good Will Hunting, Forrest Gump, Scent of a Woman, Stand by Me, Mr. Smith Goes to Washington, and The Life and Opinions of Tristram Shandy, Gentleman (which is not only the first coming-of-age novel but also the first anti-coming-of-age novel!).
2. From Adult to Leader

During this transformation, a character who is only concerned with finding the right path for themselves realizes they must also help others find that path. This type of transformation can be observed in Matrix, Saving Private Ryan, Elizabeth, Braveheart, Forrest Gump, Schindler's List, The Lion King, The Grapes of Wrath, Dances with Wolves, and Hamlet.

3. From Cynic to Committed

This development is actually a specialized form of the previous one. But in this specific case, the character starts as someone who is only interested in themselves. They are detached from the rest of society and only think about their well-being, freedom, and money. By the end of the story, this hero has understood the importance of doing good in the world and has joined society as a leader. Stories like Casablanca and the character of Han Solo in Star Wars are good examples of this transformation.

4. From Leader to Tyrant

Not all character transformations are positive. In stories of this type, a character who helps others find the right path ends up forcing others to follow their own path. Many actors fear playing this type of transformation, thinking it might make them appear villainous. Yet, this development can give rise to excellent dramas. L.A. Confidential, A Few Good Men, Howards End, Red River, The Godfather, and Macbeth are good illustrations.

5. From Leader to Visionary

Through this transformation, a character who initially helps others find the right path eventually understands how society as a whole must evolve and live its future. Examples of this type can be found in religious stories and some creation myths.

Many authors use the story structure of Moses to depict this transformation. In Close Encounters of the Third Kind, for example, we find an ordinary man, Roy, who has a vision on a mountain. He climbs the mountain and, at its peak, sees the future of the universe in the form of a giant spaceship.

But beware! If you want your character to become a visionary, you must overcome a major problem: you must define the nature of their vision. Most authors who attempt to tell this type of story are quite surprised when they reach the end to discover they have no clear vision of how they would like society as a whole to act in the future. So, at the moment of final realization, they show their character a white light or beautiful images of nature.

This cannot work. The character's vision must be a clear and precise moral vision. Moses' Ten Commandments are ten moral laws. Jesus' Sermon on the Mount corresponds to a series of moral laws. If you have no idea of this kind, do not embark on writing this type of story.

6. Metamorphosis

In horror and fantasy stories, fairy tales, and some intense psychological dramas, the character undergoes a metamorphosis, that is, an extreme transformation. The hero truly becomes another person, an animal, or even an object.

This radical transformation requires a self that is initially weak, fractured, or devastated. If positive, this development presents an act of extreme empathy. If negative, it marks the complete destruction of the old self, trapped in the new.

In horror stories such as The Wolf Man, Wolfen, and The Fly, the transformation of the human into an animal marks a total surrender to sexual passion and predatory instinct. We observe the regression of man returning to his animal roots.

There are only rare examples of characters who metamorphose in the opposite direction, from beast to human. King Kong is undoubtedly one of these characters, when he falls in love with the young woman played by Fay Wray and dies to stay with her. "It was beauty killed the beast," declares the producer in his more predatory style. The wild child of Mad Max 2 is an animal child who learns to become human by observing Mad Max, but also ends up becoming the leader of his tribe. In Gilgamesh, Enkidu, the animal man, becomes human after being pushed to sleep with a woman.

In Kafka's Metamorphosis, a novel that could be called a "switch tragedy," the traveling salesman Gregor Samsa discovers one morning that he has been transformed into an insect. This is a rare example of character transformation occurring at the beginning of the story, with the rest of the narrative devoted to the experience of being an insect (reportedly, the height of alienation).

For extreme transformations of this type, the use of a symbol is absolutely necessary. Refer to chapter 7 to understand how to attach a symbol to a character.

Creating a Character Transformation in Your Story

Now that we know how character transformation works in storytelling, the question remains: how to construct this transformation in your story?

In chapter 2, dedicated to the premise, we explored the technique of going to the opposite of the main action of the story to get an idea of the possible transformations the hero can undergo. Remember that The Godfather works this way:

• Premise: The youngest son of a mafia family avenges the men who shot his father and becomes the new Godfather.

F – initial weaknesses: cautious, conventional, isolated from the rest of the family

A – main action: revenge

T – transformation: tyrannical, becomes the undisputed head of the family.

Then, in chapter 3, we saw how to use the seven structural steps for the character to lead the plot and simultaneously experience a profound change. We will now explain in more detail the techniques for creating the character transformation that will serve as the foundation for your story.

When we talked about building the transformation earlier, we used the verb "build" deliberately because this transformation must literally form the framework of your story.

KEY POINT: Always start from the end of the transformation, that is, the realization; then go back to the beginning and determine the starting point of the transformation, that is, the hero's need and desire; and finally, imagine the development steps that lie between this beginning and this end.

This is one of the most effective techniques in fiction writing. Use it, and you will progress remarkably. If you must start from the endpoint, it is because every story is a journey of learning (which may or may not be accompanied by a real journey) undertaken by the hero. As with any journey, before taking the first step, you must know the final destination. Otherwise, you go in circles or wander aimlessly.

By starting with the realization, the end of the transformation, you ensure that each of your character's steps brings them closer to this end. There will be no treading water, no filler, no unnecessary things. This is the only way to make the story organic (with an internal logic), to ensure that each step of the journey is well connected to all the others and that this journey builds up.

Some authors fear this technique: they imagine it is restrictive and forces them to write schematically. The truth is that this technique provides more freedom because it guarantees a certain security. Wherever you are in the story, you know your final destination. You can therefore take risks and try to tell events that might seem like digressions but actually bring you more creatively to where you need to go.

Remember that the realization is made possible at the beginning of the story. This means that a good realization consists of two parts: the realization itself and its preparation.

The moment of realization must have the following qualities:

• Be sudden to have maximum dramatic impact on the hero and the audience.

• Create an emotional explosion in the audience, who shares the realization with the hero.

• Bring new information to the hero, who must realize, for the first time, that they have lied to themselves and hurt others.

• Prompt the hero to act immediately according to their new moral code, to prove that the realization has indeed occurred and has profoundly transformed them.

The preparation for the realization must meet the following criteria:

• The hero must be a rational person, capable of recognizing the truth and discerning right from wrong.

• The hero must be hiding something from themselves.

• This lie or illusion must hurt the hero deeply.

You may have noticed what may seem like a contradiction: a rational person who lies to themselves. This may indeed seem contradictory, but it is what happens in reality. We all suffer from this. The greatest power of fiction is undoubtedly to show that a human being capable of reasoning brilliantly and creatively is also susceptible to confusion and self-enslavement in illusion.

TECHNIQUE: THE DOUBLE REVERSAL

To achieve a character transformation, the most common technique is to provide the hero with a need and a realization. The hero then questions their fundamental beliefs, which they modify before acting according to a new moral code. And as readers or viewers identify with the hero, they learn what the hero learns.

But a problem remains: how to express your own moral point of view as an author on what is right or wrong if it is distinct from that of the hero? The author's and hero's points of view are not necessarily the same. And you may also want to express the transformation by giving it more complexity and emotional impact than the standard method allows.

There is thus another type of realization, quite unique, that I call "double reversal." This more advanced technique involves giving a realization to both the hero and their adversary. Each character learns something from the other, and instead of a single vision of things, the audience receives two different points of view on the appropriate way to act and live in the world.

Compared to the standard method of single realization, the double reversal technique has two advantages. First, by using the comparative method, you present the audience with the appropriate way to be and act with more subtlety and clarity. It's like the difference between mono and stereo sound. Then, the audience is not confined to the hero's point of view. They can more easily step back and consider the whole picture, reflecting on the story's ramifications.

To create a double reversal, follow these steps:

1. Give the hero and their main adversary a weakness and a need (the hero's and adversary's weaknesses and needs do not necessarily have to be similar).

2. Make the adversary human: they must be capable of learning and changing.

3. During the confrontation, or just after, give a realization to both the hero and the adversary.

4. Connect the two realizations: the hero must learn something from the adversary, and the adversary must learn something from the hero.

5. Your moral point of view will correspond to the best of what both characters have learned.

The double reversal is a very powerful technique but quite uncommon. Most authors do not create adversaries capable of having a realization. If your adversary is evil, fundamentally and totally bad, they can never discover they were wrong. An adversary who pounces on people to rip out their hearts and make them their snack cannot realize they need to change.

Quite unsurprisingly, love stories provide the best uses of the double reversal. Love stories are designed for the hero and the loved one (the main adversary) to learn from each other. Good examples of double reversal can be found in films such as Kramer vs. Kramer; Adam's Rib; Pride and Prejudice; Casablanca; Pretty Woman; Sex, Lies, and Videotape; Scent of a Woman; and The Music Man.

Once your hero's realization is determined, you must return to the need. Creating the realization first gives a notable advantage: it automatically determines the hero's need. If the realization is what the hero learns, the need is what the hero does not yet know but must learn to live better. Your hero must understand the great illusion dominating their life to overcome the great weakness that spoils it.

Creating Your Hero, Step 3: Desire

The third step in creating a strong hero is to build their line of desire. In chapter 3, we compared this step to the backbone of the story. To create a strong line of desire, keep these three rules in mind:

1. The story must have only one line of desire, which gradually grows in importance and intensity. If your story has multiple lines of desire, it will lose all cohesion. It will literally go in two or three directions at once, and this lack of narrative drive will leave the audience disoriented. In good stories, the hero has only one main goal, which they pursue with increasing intensity. The story moves faster and faster, and the narrative drive grows stronger.

2. The desire must be precise—and the more precise, the better. To ensure your line of desire is precise enough, ask yourself if there is a specific moment in your story when the audience learns whether the hero has achieved their goal. In Top Gun, we know the hero has failed in his desire to win the top prize at flight school when the director awards it to someone else. In Flashdance, we know the heroine has fulfilled her desire to join the dance school as soon as she receives a letter informing her.

Some authors say things like: "My hero's desire is to become independent." Let's apply the rule of the specific moment: when can you say a person becomes independent in life? When they leave home for the first time? When they get married? When they get divorced? There is no specific moment when you can say a person becomes independent. Dependence or independence is more related to need and offers little in terms of desire.

3. The desire must be fulfilled—if you think it should be—towards the end of the story. If the hero achieves their goal in the middle of the story, you must either stop the story or create a new line of desire, in which case you have mixed two stories together. By extending the line of desire almost to the end of the story, you give your narrative a unity that ensures enormous narrative drive.

The line of desire in each of the following films meets these three criteria:

• Saving Private Ryan: find Private Ryan and bring him back alive.

• The Full Monty: make a lot of money by dancing naked in front of a room full of women.

• The Verdict: win the trial.

• Chinatown: determine the identity of Hollis's mysterious killer.

• The Godfather: avenge the men who shot Vito Corleone.

Creating Your Hero, Step 4: The Adversary

I am not exaggerating when I say that it is the adversary who defines the hero and determines your entire story. Of all the relationships between characters in the network, the most important is that of the hero and their main adversary. This relationship determines how the entire story is constructed.

That's why, as an author, you must love this character: they will help you in many ways. From a structural point of view, it is always the adversary who holds the key because it is through them that the hero learns. It is only because the adversary attacks the hero's major weakness that the hero is forced to consider it and evolve.

KEY POINT: To have a good main character, they need a good adversary.

To better understand the importance of this principle, imagine the hero and the adversary are tennis players. If the hero is the best player in the world and the adversary is a casual player, the hero will hit a few balls, the adversary will try to return them in vain, and the audience will quickly get bored. But if the adversary is the second-best player in the world, the hero will be forced to bring out their best shots, the adversary will counterattack with spectacular shots, and the audience will be thrilled.

This is exactly how good stories work. The hero and their adversary push each other to greater heights.

The story can only unfold once the relationship between the hero and their adversary is defined. If this relationship is well-defined, the story has every chance of working. If this relationship is poorly defined, the story will almost certainly fail. We will therefore study the elements you need to create a good adversary.

1. Make the Adversary Necessary

The most important characteristic of a good adversary is that they must be necessary to the hero. In structural terms, this has a very precise meaning: the main adversary is the person most capable of attacking the hero's major weakness. And they must attack it relentlessly, constantly. The necessary adversary forces the hero to overcome their weakness or destroys them. In other words, the necessary adversary allows the hero to grow.

2. Make the Adversary Human

"Human" does not just mean that they are a person, as opposed to an animal, object, or phenomenon. A human adversary is as complex and interesting as the hero.

From a structural point of view, this means that a human adversary is always, in some way, a "double" of the hero. Some authors have used this concept of a double—also called a doppelgänger—to create certain characters. But this is a broader technique, one of the first principles to apply to create a hero-adversary pair. The concept of a double provides a number of points on which the hero and adversary can be compared, better defining each other by contrast:

• The double-adversary has certain weaknesses that lead them to behave poorly towards others or commit actions that prevent them from leading a better life.

• Like the hero, the double-adversary has a need based on this weakness.

• The double-adversary must desire something, preferably the same goal as the hero.

• The double-adversary must have a lot of power, high status, or great skill to put the hero under pressure, allow for a final confrontation, and give success (or failure) more significance.

3. Give the Adversary Values Opposed to Those of the Hero

The actions of the hero and the adversary are based on a set of beliefs or values. These values represent each character's point of view on what makes life worth living.

In good stories, the adversary's values conflict with those of the hero. It is through this conflict that the audience determines which lifestyle is superior. The success of the story largely depends on the quality of this opposition.

4. Put a Convincing but Erroneous Moral Argument in the Adversary's Mouth

An evil adversary is a fundamentally bad being, and therefore mechanical and uninteresting. In most real conflicts, there is not really right and wrong, good and bad. In well-written stories, both the hero and the adversary think they are right, and both have good reasons to believe so. Both are wrong, but in different ways.

The adversary tries to justify their actions from a moral point of view, just as the hero does. Good authors detail the adversary's moral arguments, making them strong and convincing but ultimately erroneous (we will explain how in the next chapter, "The Moral Debate").

5. Make the Hero and Adversary Similar in Some Aspects

The contrast between the hero and the adversary is only interesting if it opposes two beings with strong similarities. Each of them then symbolizes a slightly different approach to the same problem. And it is in the similarity that the determining and instructive differences become clear.

By giving the hero and their adversary certain similar traits, you also prevent yourself from conceiving a completely good hero and a completely bad adversary. You should never think of the hero and adversary as extreme opposites. They are rather two possibilities in a range of possibilities. The conflict between the hero and the adversary is not a conflict between good and evil but one between two characters who have weaknesses and needs.

6. Place the Adversary in the Same Location as the Hero

This goes against common sense. When two people dislike each other, they tend to go in opposite directions. But if this is the case in your story, you will have great difficulty building the conflict. The right technique is to find a natural reason why the hero and their adversary remain in the same place throughout the story.

The character of Hannibal Lecter in The Silence of the Lambs offers a good example of how the adversary acts on the hero. Ironically, in this film, Lecter is not a true adversary. He is actually a false adversary, a character who seems to be Clarice's adversary but is actually her greatest friend. We like to think of Lecter as an evil Yoda; the training he gives Clarice, though brutal, is far more instructive than anything she could have learned at the FBI Academy.
But in his first meeting with Clarice, Lecter perfectly illustrates, through mise en abyme, the role of the adversary: to constantly attack the weaknesses of the heroine until she either fixes them or collapses. Clarice visits Lecter in his cell to obtain information about the serial killer Buffalo Bill. After a promising start, she overestimates her chances and insults Lecter's intelligence. He counterattacks:

LECTER: Agent Starling, do you think you can dissect me with this blunt little tool?

CLARICE: No. I thought your knowledge...

LECTER: You're very ambitious, aren't you? Do you know what you look like with your cheap handbag and shoes? Like a rube. A well-scrubbed, hustling rube with little taste. Good nutrition has given you some length of bone, but you're not more than one generation
Blanche: Beauty, appearances, good manners, refinement, kindness, Stella.

Stanley: Strength, power, women, sex, Stella, his friends.

Stella: Stanley, her marriage, Blanche, sex, her baby.

Mitch: His mother, his friends, good manners, Blanche.

• Similarities between the adversaries and the heroine

Stanley: Blanche and Stanley are different in many ways, but they share a deep understanding of the world, which Stella lacks. They are both very skilled at making calculations and recognizing this ability in each other.

Stella: Stella shares Blanche's past, the time when they both lived in the charming and graceful world of the old Southern aristocracy. Stella and Blanche also share a need for love and kindness.

Mitch: Mitch appeals to Blanche's taste for good manners and courtesy. He appreciates her refinement and the last remnants of her beauty.

• Power, social status, and skills

Blanche: Blanche has lost her social status. She clings desperately to her ability to please men through her looks and charm.

Stanley: Stanley is the leader of his circle of friends. He is also very skilled at getting what he wants, especially from Stella.

Stella: Stella has no power, except what Stanley is willing to grant her. But she is very skilled at pleasing Stanley.
