Mitch: Mitch has little power over his circle of friends or the world. He is a natural follower.

• Moral problem and justification

Blanche: Blanche believes her lies hurt no one and are her only chance at achieving happiness.

Stanley: He believes Blanche is a prostitute who tried to deceive him. He thinks he is doing the right thing and protecting his friend Mitch when he reveals Blanche's past to him.

Stella: Stella is not intelligent enough to realize she has participated in a process that is destroying her sister.

Mitch: Mitch believes that a woman who has acted like a prostitute can be treated like a prostitute.

• Variations of secondary characters on the heroine's weakness and moral problem Eunice and Steve are married and live upstairs. They argue about infidelity. When Eunice leaves the home, Steve catches up with her and brings her back.

• Four-corner opposition

Blanche

[artist]

Stanley

[warrior-king]

Stella

[mother]

Mitch

[none]
The connection of two symbols presents the same advantages as a journey: the symbols are the two opposing poles of a moral sequence. This technique is generally used to signify a decline in morality. But it can also mark an upward movement. The author of Heart of Darkness used the technique of the journey, but also that of the two symbols, to express his thematic line. Implicated by the very title of the work, the two symbols, the dark heart and the center of moral darkness, suggest an investigation into human depravity.

There are other guiding principles—units of time, use of a narrator, or a particular way of unfolding the story—that can also help you clarify your thematic line. We will now return to the guiding principles of stories we discussed in Chapter 2 (The Premise) to reflect on the thematic lines they can generate.

MOSES IN THE BOOK OF EXODUS

• Guiding Principle A man who does not know who he is struggles to free his people and receives new moral laws that will define him and his people.

• Thematic Line A man who takes responsibility for acting for his people is rewarded with a divine vision that dictates how he and his people should live in the future.

ULYSSES

• Guiding Principle A modern odyssey through the city, where in the span of a single day, a man finds a father, who in turn finds a son.

• Thematic Line A true hero is a man who endures the trials of daily life and shows compassion to those in need.

FOUR WEDDINGS AND A FUNERAL

• Guiding Principle Friends experience four utopias (the weddings) and one nightmarish moment (the funeral), while each searches for the ideal partner.

• Thematic Line When you find true love, you must commit with all your heart.

THE "HARRY POTTER" SERIES

• Guiding Principle A wizard prince learns to become a man and a king by spending seven school years in a school that trains wizards.

• Thematic Line When you have the chance to possess great talents and powers, you must become a leader and sacrifice for the good of others.

THE STING

• Guiding Principle Tell the story of a con in the form of a con and outwit the opponent and the audience.

• Thematic Line You can allow yourself a few small lies and cons when trying to destroy a bad man.

LONG DAY'S JOURNEY INTO NIGHT

• Guiding Principle As the day progresses and night falls, all family members confront the mistakes and ghosts of their past.

• Thematic Line You must face your own truth and that of others and then forgive.

MEET ME IN ST. LOUIS

• Guiding Principle Represent the evolution of a family over a year through events that occur in each season.

• Thematic Line Sacrifice for the family is more valuable than efforts for personal glory.

COPENHAGEN

• Guiding Principle Use Heisenberg's uncertainty principle outside the context of physics to explore the ambiguous morality of the man who discovered it.

• Thematic Line It is always very difficult to determine what drives us to act and to know if our actions are good or bad.

A CHRISTMAS CAROL

• Guiding Principle Follow the rebirth of a man by forcing him to observe his past, present, and future on Christmas Eve.

• Thematic Line You are happier when you give to others.

IT'S A WONDERFUL LIFE

• Guiding Principle Reveal the power of the individual by showing what a town, and a nation, would have been like if one man had never lived.

• Thematic Line A man's wealth is not measured by the money he earns but by the friends and family members who love him.

CITIZEN KANE

• Guiding Principle Use multiple narrators to demonstrate that one can never truly know a man's life.

• Thematic Line People who try to force others to love them end up alone.

DIVIDING THE THEME INTO OPPOSITIONS

The thematic line is your moral debate condensed into a single sentence. Once this line is defined, you must express it in dramatic terms. To do this, you must divide it into a set of oppositions, then link these thematic oppositions to the hero and their adversaries during their confrontations.

Three techniques can help you divide the thematic line into dramatic oppositions: assign the hero a moral decision, make each character a variation on the theme, and put the characters' values in conflict.

The hero's moral decision

The hero's moral development begins with the moral need at the beginning of the story and ends with a moral decision at the end, following a moral awakening. This thread, that of the fundamental moral lesson you wish to express, provides the framework, the moral fabric of your story.

To express the hero's moral line in dramatic terms, the classic strategy is to assign them a moral imperfection from the start and then show how their rage to defeat the adversary brings out the worst in them. In short, the hero must be capable of the worst before revealing the best. Slowly but surely, they realize that their central moral problem boils down to a choice between two ways of acting.

No matter how complex the characters' actions are throughout the story, the final moral decision boils everything down to a binary choice. That's it. The moral decision is thus somewhat the funnel's end for your theme. The two options are the two most important moral actions available to your hero. They therefore provide you with the major thematic opposition of your story.

This major decision usually comes right after the moment of moral awakening, which makes the hero aware of the choice they have to make. On rare occasions, the choice comes first, and the hero's awakening only recognizes that they made the right or wrong choice.

KEY POINT: Since the culmination of the moral line is the final choice, you must start by determining the moral oppositions using this choice.

• Casablanca: When Rick reunites with Ilsa, his former love, he has the option to use two exit visas to escape with her and return to America. But Rick chooses to fight the Nazis rather than give in to his love for Ilsa.

• The Maltese Falcon: Private detective Sam Spade discovers that Brigid O’Shaughnessy killed her lover. When the police come to him, he has to choose between justice and the woman he loves. He chooses justice.

• Sophie's Choice: Sophie tells an American writer about her past as a prisoner in Auschwitz concentration camp. Upon her arrival, she had to choose between two negative possibilities: which of her children will she let the Nazis kill? (One could argue that this is not a real choice.)

• The Iliad: During the final confrontation, Achilles kills Hector, the great Trojan warrior, then drags his body behind his chariot. He allows Hector's father, Priam, to retrieve his son's body so he can have a decent burial.

• Vertigo: Scottie discovers that his lover, Madeleine, helped a man kill his wife. At the end of the story, his moral decision comes before the awakening. He decides not to forgive Madeleine and is destroyed when he realizes he made the wrong choice and, by that choice, killed the woman he loved.

Characters as variations of the theme

Once you have determined, by examining the hero's final moral choice, the deepest moral opposition of the story, you must detail it through the network of main characters by making each of them a variation on the theme. Here's how to make this technique work:

1. Review the hero's final moral decision and the work you did on the premise to be clear about the central moral problem the hero must face.

2. Ensure that all main characters face the same moral problem, each doing so in a different way.

3. Start by comparing the hero to their main adversary, these two characters embodying the basic moral opposition you will detail in your story. Then compare the hero to their other adversaries.

4. Throughout the story, each of the main characters must engage in a moral debate in the dialogue to justify their actions towards the goal (a good moral debate is conducted mainly, but not exclusively, through structure. We will explain how to write moral dialogue in Chapter 10, "Building Scenes and Symphonic Dialogues").

TOOTSIE

(Larry Gelbart and Murray Schisgal, based on an original story by Don McGuire and Larry Gelbart, 1982)

Tootsie tells the story of an actor who disguises himself as a woman to get a role in a TV series. But he falls in love with an actress from the series, and several men, who take him for a woman, are attracted to him.

The hero's central moral problem is determining how men should behave with women in love. Each adversary and ally represents a possible behavior towards women or a reaction to that behavior.

L.A. CONFIDENTIAL

(novel by James Ellroy, screenplay by Brian Helgeland and Curtis Hanson, 1997)

In L.A. Confidential, three police inspectors investigate a massacre. These three characters are three heroes who must face the central moral problem of administering justice. Bud is a cop who enforces the law alone, assuming the role of judge and executioner. Jack has forgotten why he became a cop and arrests people for money. Ed wants to bring the guilty to justice, but he has gotten caught up in the political game of justice and now aims for the top of the profession. The other main characters represent other variations on the theme of justice corruption.

DANCES WITH WOLVES

(novel and screenplay by Michael Blake, 1990)

Dances with Wolves recounts the exploits of a U.S. Army officer at the end of the 19th century. Gradually, this man will embrace the Sioux way of life, a people he initially considered his enemy.

The hero's central moral problem is how to behave towards another people, another culture, towards animals and nature. Each adversary and ally has a different approach to this problem.

The characters' values in conflict

Using your network of characters, you can now put each main character's values in conflict with those of their rivals, who seek to achieve the same goal.

1. Assign your hero and each of the other main characters a set of values. Define values as deeply held beliefs about what constitutes a happy life.

2. Try to give each character a set of values.

3. Ensure these sets of values are as different as possible.

4. Consider that when your hero and their adversaries fight to achieve the goal, their values must come into direct conflict.

IT'S A WONDERFUL LIFE

(short story, The Greatest Gift, by Philip Van Doren Stern, screenplay by Frances Goodrich, Albert Hackett, and Frank Capra, 1946)

Disgusted with living in a small town ruled by a tyrant, George Bailey is about to commit suicide when an angel appears to him and shows him what the world would have been like if he had never lived.

The hero and the adversary fight for the town they live in based on very different values:

• George Bailey (Bedford Falls): Democracy, decency, kindness, work, importance of the average worker

• Mr. Potter (Pottersville): Tyranny, money, power, survival of the fittest

THE CHERRY ORCHARD

(Anton Chekhov, 1904)

In The Cherry Orchard, a family of poor aristocrats returns to their land to try to save it from bankruptcy.

The different characters fight for control of the land. The focal point of the competition is the value of the cherry orchard. Madame Ranevsky and her family love it for its immense beauty and the childhood memories attached to it. Lopakhine loves it only for its practical and financial value; he wants to cut down the cherry trees to build small houses he could rent.

• Madame Ranevsky: True love, beauty, past

• Lopakhine: Money, social status, power, practicality, future

• Varia: Work, family, marriage, practicality

• Trofimov: Truth, learning, compassion, higher love

• Ania: Her mother, kindness, higher love

FIELD OF DREAMS

(short story, Shoeless Joe, by W. P. Kinsella, screenplay by Phil Alden Robinson, 1989)

Field of Dreams is an American version of The Cherry Orchard where the land ends up "winning." In this story, the competition revolves around the value of the farm Ray has turned into a baseball field.

• Ray: Baseball, family, passion for dreams

• Mark: Money, practical use of the land

With characters representing variations on the same theme and value oppositions, you can use the four-corner opposition technique explained in Chapter 4. You will then need a hero, a main adversary, and at least two secondary adversaries. This technique can provide organic unity to the most complex story. Each of the four main characters can represent a fundamentally different approach to the same moral problem, and each can express an entire system of values without the story fragmenting into a complicated mess.

KEY POINT: Your moral debate will always seem simplistic if you use a binary opposition, like good versus evil. Only a network of moral oppositions (such as the four-corner opposition) can give the audience the impression of moral complexity that is the hallmark of real life.

You will notice that these three techniques allow you not to impose the theme on the characters but rather to express it through the characters. This ensures the story does not come across as preachy. You will also notice that the story has more depth because the opposition between the characters is not solely based on the plot, not limited to a competition for the same goal. Entire ways of life are at stake. The emotional impact on the audience is therefore enormous.

THE THEME VIA STRUCTURE

The moral debate does not mean that your hero and their adversary engage in a verbal debate about morality from the first scene. The moral debate is a debate of actions that must be created by showing the hero and the adversary using certain means to achieve an end. This is how you weave the theme into the story's structure rather than imposing it on the audience in an unsubtle way through dialogue.

Moreover, here is a major principle of storytelling: structure is not just a vehicle for content; it is content. And this content is much more powerful than what your characters say. And this principle applies nowhere as precisely as in the theme.

In a good story, the narrative structure converges towards the end, while the theme grows in the audience's mind. The question is: how can a convergent narrative structure cause the theme to amplify? One could schematize structure and theme when they ideally align as follows:

At the beginning of the story, you put the hero and their adversary in opposition. But the resulting conflict is not intense, and the audience does not yet know how each other's values conflict. They therefore have almost no idea of the story's theme.

Towards the middle of the narrative, the conflict between the hero and their adversary intensifies, hence the convergent structure. In this conflict, the opposition of values gradually becomes apparent. Consequently, the theme begins to amplify. However, for most of a good story, the theme remains largely hidden; it develops slowly in the audience's mind, and it is not until the end of the story that it strikes them with all its force.

The point of convergence of the narrative structure is the confrontation, which is immediately followed by the awakening and the moral decision. During the confrontation, the audience sees not just which force, but also which set of values prevails over the other. The audience's understanding of the theme then quickly amplifies. During the awakening—especially if it is a moral awakening—the theme amplifies even more. And this growing movement reaches its peak with the moral decision. Since the theme has been expressed through the structure, readers or viewers feel it emerges from the depths of their soul, rather than being imposed on them like a tedious sermon.

We will now examine in detail how the moral debate is expressed through the structure from the beginning to the end of the story. We will start with the basic strategy of expressing the moral debate, then we will study some variations.

Moral Debate: Basic Strategy

• Values The hero begins the story with a set of values and beliefs.

• Moral Weakness At the beginning of the story, the hero harms others in some way. They are not an evil being; they act out of weakness or ignorance of how to behave with others.

• Moral Need The hero must overcome their moral weakness and learn to behave correctly with others to evolve and live better.

• First Immoral Action The hero almost immediately commits an action that hurts others. The audience then understands that they possess a basic moral imperfection.

• Desire The hero sets a goal for which they are willing to sacrifice everything. This goal pushes them into direct conflict with an adversary who has different values but the same goal.

• Narrative Dynamism The hero and their adversary undertake a series of actions to achieve their goal.

• Immoral Actions Towards the beginning and middle of the story, the hero is generally dominated by their adversary. Desperate, they begin to commit immoral actions to prevail.

Criticism: Other characters criticize the hero for the means they use.

Justification: The hero tries to justify their actions. They may discover the truth about their situation and the harm they have done at the end of the story, but not now.

• Attack by an Ally The hero's best friend strongly reproaches them for their methods.

• Obsessive Dynamic Galvanized by new revelations about how to prevail, the hero becomes completely obsessed with the goal and seems almost ready for anything.

• Immoral Actions The hero's immoral actions intensify.

Criticism: Other characters also intensify their criticisms.

Justification: The hero vehemently defends their actions.

As the story progresses, the different values and ways of life represented by the hero and their adversary become clearer through action and dialogue. There are four moments at the end of the story when the theme explodes in the audience's mind. These moments are the confrontation, the awakening, the moral decision, and a structural stage we have not yet discussed: the thematic revelation.

• Confrontation This is the final conflict that determines who will achieve the goal. Regardless of the winner's identity, the audience learns which values and ideas are superior to others.

• Final Action Against the Adversary The hero may commit one last action—moral or immoral—against the adversary just before or during the confrontation.

• Moral Awakening The ordeal of the confrontation brings about an awakening in the hero who understands they were wrong about themselves, others, and how they must now act towards others. The audience identifying with the character, the awakening gives the theme all the force it deserves.

• Moral Decision The hero chooses between two ways of acting. This choice is proof of their moral awakening.

• Thematic Revelation In great stories, the theme reaches the peak of its impact on the audience at the moment of thematic revelation. The thematic revelation is not limited to the hero. It rather touches the audience who thus intuits how they should act and live in the world. This intuition goes beyond the characters' limits and affects the audience in their own lives. With the thematic revelation, the audience sees the "complete design" of the story, all the ramifications of its meaning, on a larger scale, no longer limited to the characters.

You understand that it is important to find a balance in terms of power between the hero and their main adversary not only for the network of characters and the plot but also for the moral debate. If the hero is too strong or too kind, the adversary will not be able to test them and make them commit moral errors. If the hero is too simple and naive, the adversary will be like a spider weaving a web from which the hero cannot even imagine escaping. The hero will become a victim, and the adversary will appear as an evil being.

Portrait of a Lady, by Henry James, is a masterpiece in many respects, but this novel suffers from an imbalance in terms of power, and the moral debate suffers. Isabel Archer lives in illusion throughout the story, even when she makes her final moral decision, that of helping Pansy, who cannot be helped. This sweet but naive woman must face the born schemer Osmond, a being whose talent for devising traps is matched only by his determination and the sadistic pleasure he takes in it.

TECHNIQUE: MORAL DEBATE = PLOT

The first thing that can make a story moralizing is an imbalance between moral debate and plot. You can express the moral debate through the narrative structure, organize it perfectly, and highlight it with subtle moral dialogues, but if your plot is not rich enough to support it, it will necessarily sink into the most boring sermonizing.

The plot, as we will see in Chapter 8, is a complex choreography that intertwines the actions of the hero and their adversaries in a way that surprises the audience. And it is this element of surprise, of magic, that prevents the moral line from sinking and gives it punch.

Let's take the example of The Verdict to illustrate the basic strategy of the moral debate in a story.

THE VERDICT

(novel by Barry C. Reed, 1980; screenplay by David Mamet, 1982)
• Beliefs and values of the hero At the beginning, Frank values alcohol, money, and opportunism.

• Moral weakness Addicted to alcohol and lacking self-respect or future plans, Frank would do anything for money.

• Moral need To act justly towards others rather than using them to make money.

• First immoral action To drum up business, Frank crashes a funeral pretending to be a friend of the deceased.

• Desire To win the trial and secure the damages his clients need to start over.

• Narrative drive Frank takes several actions to get an expert doctor to testify for his clients and to find the nurse who was in the operating room.

• Immoral action Frank reassures the victim's sister, Sally, while negotiating a settlement between two hundred and two hundred fifty thousand dollars, allowing him to pocket a third without having to go to trial.

Critique: None.

Justification: Frank is an alcoholic who has lost all self-esteem along with a sense of justice and morality. He thinks: why risk a trial rather than the financial certainty of a settlement?

• Moral awakening Early in the story, Frank sees his client, the victim, in a vegetative state, and realizes he must act justly if he doesn't want to lose himself forever.

• Moral decision Frank risks losing his share of the money by refusing the settlement and insisting on going to trial to ensure justice is served.

• Thematic revelation It is only by acting justly that one can save one's life.

• Attack by an ally The main attack comes not from Mickey, Frank's fellow lawyer, but from his clients. When they learn he refused the settlement without consulting them, they accuse him of incompetence and prostitution.

Justification: Frank tells them he will earn them much more by pleading their case than by accepting the settlement offer. But while he argues money, the real reason he refused the settlement was his desire for justice.

• Obsessive drive He is determined to find the nurse who was in the operating room.

• Immoral action Through cunning, Frank gets a woman to talk about the nurse and learns she will not testify for the opposing side.

Critique: None.

Justification: Frank believes he must find the nurse if he wants to win the trial.

• Immoral action Frank breaks into the woman's mailbox to find the nurse's phone number.

Critique: None. Frank acts in secret.

Justification: This is Frank's last chance to defend a just cause.

• Immoral action Frank hits his girlfriend, Laura, when he discovers she was paid by the opposing side to reveal information about his case.

Critique: Laura does not criticize because she feels guilty herself.

Justification: Frank, who loves this woman, felt utterly betrayed.

• Confrontation Frank questions Dr. Towler about the time the patient ate. The nurse, Kaitlin, states that the victim ate not at 9 o'clock but an hour after admission. She explains that Dr. Towler did not read the admission form and asked her to change the 1 to a 9 if she didn't want to be fired. Master Cancannon, the opposing lawyer, reads a precedent regarding the inadmissibility of a copy as evidence. The judge agrees and dismisses the nurse's entire testimony.

• Final action against the adversary Frank commits no immoral action during the trial. He simply pleads with conviction and intelligence.

The Verdict is a perfect example of how to use moral debate in a story, but this example presents a notable exception, a very instructive exception. The hero has a powerful moral awakening when he realizes what his client has suffered: she is in a coma due to the fault of two doctors, and he had intended to abandon her for the lure of profit. He makes his moral decision when he refuses the settlement to champion justice in court, even if it doesn't earn him a penny.

However, the awakening and decision occur barely twenty-five minutes into the film. This tends to diminish the power of the moral debate because, from that point, the moral danger the hero was in is averted. The audience still enjoys the suspense of wondering whether the hero will win the trial. For Frank remains an insecure and alcoholic lawyer. But they know Frank has learned to act justly, and that is now what he does.

The moral debate is more powerful when it is more dramatic. This means, among other things, delaying the awakening and moral decision as long as possible in the story, as close to the end as possible. Make sure readers or viewers keep in the back of their minds as long as possible this little question: "Will the hero do what is necessary, and will he do it in time?"

THE ILIAD

(Homer)

The moral debate of The Iliad is based on the basic strategy of the hero's slow decline and then his rise at the moment of awakening. But The Iliad offers an important variation by repeating this sequence twice.

The first sequence of decline and rise occurs over the first three-quarters of the narrative. The hero, Achilles, begins the story in a state of entirely justified anger against his main adversary, Agamemnon, who has taken the woman rightfully his. But his excessive pride (his moral weakness) leads him to act immorally, overreacting by refusing to fight, which results in the deaths of many of his comrades.

Until the middle of the story, Achilles' anger, which is very selfish, seems increasingly unjustified. But when his friend Patroclus dies, Achilles realizes his mistake, reconciles with Agamemnon, and returns to battle. This is the first awakening and moral decision.

The moral debate repeats itself more intensely but more concisely in the last quarter of the story: Achilles' anger against his second adversary, Hector, is initially justified, but Achilles declines morally when this anger leads him to desecrate Hector's corpse by dragging it behind his chariot around Troy. Hector's father, Priam, begs for his son's body to be returned. Achilles then has a second, even deeper awakening, realizing the superiority of compassion over vengeance, and decides to let Priam take back his son's body for a proper burial.

VARIANTS OF THE MORAL DEBATE

The basic method has several variants. You may feel the need to use multiple types of moral debates in your story, but as we will see later, combining forms is a risky endeavor.

1. Good versus Evil

In this minor variant of the moral debate, the hero remains good and his adversary bad throughout the story. This approach is particularly common in myths, action stories, and melodramas, which are simple moral tales with easily identifiable characters. The moral sequence unfolds as follows:

• The hero has a psychological weakness but is fundamentally good.

• His adversary is morally corrupt and may even be evil (intrinsically immoral).

• During the competition for the goal, the hero makes mistakes but never acts immorally.

• The adversary, on the other hand, performs many immoral actions.

• The hero wins the goal simply because he is good. When the two sides of the moral angle meet, it is the good hero who wins the "game" of life.

Examples of this type of moral debate can be found in The Matrix; City Slickers; Field of Dreams; Crocodile Dundee; Dances with Wolves; The Blues Brothers; Star Wars; Forrest Gump; Stagecoach; Places in the Heart; Terminator; The Fugitive; The Last of the Mohicans; Shane; and The Wizard of Oz.

2. Tragedy

Tragedy follows the basic method but alters its final part: at the beginning of the story, the hero is given a fatal flaw, and an awakening comes too late, near the end. The sequence works as follows:

• The community is in crisis.

• The hero has great potential but also a great flaw.

• The hero enters into conflict with a powerful or skilled adversary.

• The hero is obsessed with the goal and performs several questionable or immoral actions to achieve it.

• The conflict and competition bring out the hero's flaw, which worsens.

• The hero has an awakening, but it comes too late to prevent his downfall.

The key to this strategy is to emphasize the awareness of what could have been, on wasted potential, while showing that the hero's actions are his responsibility. The wasted potential is the key element to gaining the audience's sympathy while avoiding making him a victim, as his fatal flaw makes the hero responsible. The audience is saddened by the waste made even more tragic by the fact that the hero has his awakening... just a little too late to save him. But even if the hero dies or falls, the audience remains deeply moved by his emotional and moral success.

You will notice that this strategy marks a significant shift from classical Greek drama: the hero's fall is not the inevitable result of the action of immense and impersonal forces; it is the consequence of the hero's own choices.

Here are some examples of classic tragedies: Hamlet, King Lear, Seven Samurai, The Bridge on the River Kwai, Nixon, The Thomas Crown Affair, The Age of Innocence, Wuthering Heights, Vertigo, Amadeus, Le Morte d'Arthur, American Beauty, Touch of Evil, and Citizen Kane.

WUTHERING HEIGHTS

(novel by Emily Brontë, 1847, screenplay by Charles MacArthur and Ben Hecht, 1939)

Wuthering Heights is a love story written as a classic tragedy. The moral debate follows several threads: the destructive actions of the two main characters. And since this debate is based on the tragedy strategy, these characters are devastated by a terrible sense of responsibility, feeling guilty for what they have done to each other.

Cathy, the heroine, is not an unloved girl manipulated by a man. She is a woman who loves deeply, a love that "can only be found in heaven," but she deliberately abandons it for a man who will bring her wealth and comfort. Initially, she is in love with Heathcliff, and this love is mutual, but she refuses to live with a man who looks like a poor beggar. She wants to "dance and sing in the beautiful world."

When she returns from her stay at Edgar Linton's home, Heathcliff, her main adversary, criticizes her for staying so long. She defends herself by saying she had a lot of fun there. Then she hurts Heathcliff by ordering him to take a bath so he won't embarrass her in front of her guest (Edgar).

But Cathy immediately recovers from her moral fall when Edgar asks how she can bear to live under the same roof as Heathcliff. Furious, she replies that Heathcliff was her friend long before him and that if he wants to speak ill of him, he should leave. Edgar leaves, and Cathy tears her pretty clothes, runs to join Heathcliff on the rock where he waits, and asks for his forgiveness.

The moral debate crafted by Brontë through Cathy reaches its peak when the young girl explains to her servant that she plans to marry Edgar while Heathcliff listens secretly in the next room. It is then Nellie, the ally, who will lead the critique. She asks Cathy why she loves Edgar. Cathy replies that it's because he is handsome and pleasant and will one day be rich. Then, when Nellie asks about Heathcliff, Cathy says it would be degrading to marry him.

The powerful moral debate crafted by Brontë is supported by dialogues perfectly in harmony with the rhythm of a brilliant and highly emotional plot. Devastated, Heathcliff leaves, but only Nellie can see him. Shortly after, Cathy changes her mind and says she is not in love with Edgar. She dreams of being cast out of heaven and finding herself on the moor, and she sobs with joy. She says she only thinks of Heathcliff, but he seems to take pleasure in being cruel to her. Yet Heathcliff is much closer to her than she thinks. Cathy and Heathcliff are soulmates. After a heartbreaking awakening, Cathy says: "I am Heathcliff." When she discovers that Heathcliff heard her when she said it would be degrading to marry him, she runs out and cries her love in the storm. But it is too late.

This is the moment Brontë chose to radically alter the course of the tragic moral debate by literally reversing the roles and giving the lead to Heathcliff. Thus, Heathcliff, the new hero, returns and attacks cruelly as a love found in heaven must when it is scorned for something so grossly earthly.

Heathcliff is a rebel who, like Achilles, initially has a desire for revenge that seems justified. Brontë uses the "return of the man" technique: she brings back a rich and sophisticated Heathcliff, reminiscent of the Count of Monte Cristo. Readers or viewers are then overwhelmed by a sense of triumph, so much so that they don't even need to understand how the character could have undergone such a transformation. The man is back, finally armed with the weapons everyone would dream of having in such a situation. The reader or viewer thinks: "It's possible – I could have done it." then "And there, I would have taken my revenge."

With the audience clearly on Heathcliff's side, Brontë reverses the moral debate by pushing her hero too far. For even losing such a great love in such an unjust way does not justify marrying the sister and sister-in-law of his enemies out of pure revenge. Observing the innocent love that lights up Isabella's face, Edgar's sister, as she walks into the trap set by Heathcliff, is heartbreaking. It is a perfect illustration of the effect a good moral debate should produce.

These scenes between Cathy and Heathcliff are ordinary versions of those of kings and queens at war. It is Lear raging on the moor. If the concept of heavenly love is particularly credible, it is thanks to the ferocity of the attacks the two protagonists launch at each other. It is pure savagery that can only be explained by the extreme love they have for each other.

At the end of the film adaptation, Heathcliff attacks Cathy one last time, and it is a justified attack, even though Cathy is on her deathbed. He does not seek to comfort her. She is afflicted by his tears. She begs him not to break her heart. But he replies that she broke it herself: "What right did you have to sacrifice love for that poor something, that whim you felt for him?" Nothing in the world could have separated them. But she did it herself, he explains, by indulging in debauchery like a greedy child. Cathy begs for his forgiveness, and they kiss.

In the novel, Heathcliff goes too far, this time well beyond the bounds, by trying to destroy the Linton dynasty. That is why this section was cut from the film adaptation, which is a far superior work to the novel in terms of storytelling. In the novel, from this attack, the organic story of Cathy and Heathcliff is fundamentally over, Heathcliff only adds to the revenge and Brontë to the moral debate, just because she excels at it.

KING LEAR

(William Shakespeare, 1605)

In King Lear, Shakespeare crafts a more nuanced moral debate than those typically found in classic tragedies. The key to this technique is creating two "heroes": Lear, the main character, and Gloucester, the secondary character. Both Lear and Gloucester begin the play with moral flaws, then decline throughout the story, have a moral awakening, and eventually die. But their deaths lack the noble character of, for example, Hamlet's. The audience does not feel that the world's order has been restored, that all will be well in the kingdom of Denmark.

Shakespeare preferred to shift the focus to the elemental immorality of man and the amorality of nature. First, his two heroes, Lear and Gloucester, make the same moral mistake and die cruelly. A king experiencing a tragic fall gives pause. Two kings experiencing a tragic fall is proof of a moral blindness that seems endemic to the human race.

Then, Shakespeare has Cordelia, the only morally good character in the play, killed in a particularly cruel manner. It is true that Edgar, a good but primarily foolish man, has defeated his wicked brother and the two pests, Lear's daughters. But in this overwhelming devastation, there is little left to find value in a life turned toward good. In the famous last line of the play, Edgar says: "We that are young shall never see so much, nor live so long." In other words, in a world populated by immoral beings, despite immense suffering, a man can live intensely, but at what cost! For an aging Shakespeare, this is all the nobility one can expect from the human race.

3. Pathos

Pathos is a moral debate that reduces the tragic hero to an ordinary man and touches the audience by presenting the beauty of perseverance, lost causes, and the inevitability of failure. The main character has no awakening, not even too late. He is incapable of having one. But he never stops fighting, he struggles to the end. This moral debate can be outlined as follows:

• The hero has a set of outdated or rigid beliefs and values.

• The hero has a moral need; he is not a victim.

• The goal he sets is out of reach, but he doesn't know it.

• His adversary is far too powerful for him. It may be a system or a set of forces the hero cannot understand. This adversary is not evil. It is simply impersonal or indifferent and very powerful.

• The hero undertakes a series of immoral actions to win and refuses to listen to the warnings and criticisms of his allies.

• The hero does not achieve the goal. The adversary wins an overwhelming victory, but the audience understands it was not an equal confrontation.

• The hero ends up desperate: he is a broken man with no awakening and dies of grief, or – this is what the moral decision is reduced to – decides to commit suicide.

• The audience feels a deep sense of injustice and sadness at the death of this little man who has no idea what destroyed him. But they admire the beauty of the confrontation and failure and the hero's refusal to admit defeat.

The moral debate of pathos can be found in Don Quixote; A Streetcar Named Desire; many Japanese films such as Ikiru; Death of a Salesman; Hedda Gabler; The Conversation; McCabe & Mrs. Miller; Falling Down; M; The Apu Trilogy; Madame Bovary; The Magnificent Ambersons; The Cherry Orchard; Dog Day Afternoon; and Cinema Paradiso.

4. Satire and Irony

Satire and irony are two different things but often go hand in hand. Satire is a comedy that mocks beliefs, particularly those on which a society is based. Irony is a form of narrative logic where a character gets the opposite of what they sought to obtain. When used for the entire story, irony becomes a framework that connects all the actions of the story and expresses a philosophical view of how the world works. Irony also creates a perplexed tone that encourages the audience to laugh at the relative incompetence of the characters.

With the ironic-satirical form, the moral debate is crafted by creating a constant contrast between a character who thinks he is a moral man – by supporting the beliefs of his society – and the consequences of his beliefs and actions, which are invariably immoral. The main steps of the ironic-satirical debate are as follows:

• The hero lives in a clearly defined social system. Very often, a character partially or completely explains the values on which the system is based.

• The hero firmly believes in this system and is determined to climb its ranks. He sets a goal related to ambition or love.

• An adversary, who also firmly believes in this system and its values, seeks to achieve the same goal.

• As the characters fight for the goal, their beliefs lead them to undertake stupid and destructive actions.

• The debate of actions, in the middle of the story, builds on a series of juxtapositions that highlight the contradiction between characters who claim to act morally by expressing the highest ideals of society, and the disastrous consequences of their actions.

• The confrontation brings out the pride and hypocrisy of both parties.

• The hero has an awakening that usually leads him to question the value of his belief system.

• Often, the hero, or a secondary character, undermines the awakening, proving it has not been fully assimilated.

• The hero undertakes a moral action that is good in itself but generally has no impact on the stupidity or destructiveness of the system.

• A new friendship or love is formed, suggesting the couple will form a better microcosm but will have little effect on society as a whole.
The ironic-satirical debate is used in Pride and Prejudice; Emma (and its modern version, Clueless); American Beauty; Wedding Crashers; Madame Bovary; The Cherry Orchard; The Graduate; M*A*S*H; The History of Tom Jones, a Foundling; Waiting for Guffman; The Player; Being John Malkovich; Down and Out in Beverly Hills; The Prince and the Pauper (and its modern version, Trading Places); La Cage aux Folles; The Importance of Being Earnest; Private Benjamin; Dog Day Afternoon; Victor Victoria; Shampoo; Bob & Carol & Ted & Alice; and Lost in America.

EMMA

(Jane Austen, 1816)

Jane Austen's works are models of ironic-satirical moral debate, and Emma is probably her greatest achievement. Here is the moral sequence of this classic satire:

• Emma is a stubborn, insensitive, and self-satisfied young woman who constantly seeks to play matchmaker.

• Her first goal is to marry off Harriet, an orphan.

• Firmly believing in the class system but mistakenly thinking Harriet comes from a more affluent background than she appears, Emma convinces her to refuse the marriage proposal of the farmer Robert Martin.

• She also convinces Harriet to try to marry the vicar, Mr. Elton, whose social status is more advantageous. But in this process, Emma inadvertently leads Mr. Elton to think that she herself is attracted to him.

• The result of these well-intentioned but immoral actions is that Harriet has refused the proposal of a good man and Mr. Elton declares his love for Emma, only to have his heart broken when he discovers she is not in love with him after all.

• At a ball, Mr. Elton, who has just married another woman, embarrasses Harriet by refusing her a dance. But Harriet is saved by Mr. Knightley, who invites her to take a turn on the floor.

• Frank, a man passing through the county, saves Harriet when confronted by unpleasant characters on the road. Emma mistakenly thinks Frank is Harriet's new suitor, although he is of a higher class than she is.

• During a picnic, Emma flirts with Frank, though she is not attracted to him. This visibly angers another character in the social group, the pretty Jane.

• Emma also publicly humiliates the talkative but kind Miss Bates. Mr. Knightley takes Emma aside and reproaches her for her insensitivity.

• When she learns that it is Mr. Knightley, not Frank, whom Harriet has set her sights on, Emma is shocked: she realizes she herself is in love with Mr. Knightley. Moreover, she becomes aware that she has been arrogant and naive in meddling in matters that do not concern her and bitterly regrets having prevented Harriet from marrying Robert Martin.

• Mr. Knightley declares his love for Emma and agrees to move in with her so she can continue to care for her father. In the novel, but not in the film, the typical comedy-ending marriage and Emma's great realization are shaken by a paradox: if Emma is able to marry Mr. Knightley, it is only because her father fears chicken thieves and wants a young man in the house.

In this story, the main ironic-satirical debate is carried by Emma's efforts to find a good match for Harriet. Through this means, Austen exposes a system based on class differences and women's extreme dependence on men. Her heroine, Emma, supports this system but lacks intelligence and discernment. With great subtlety, Austen goes further in questioning the system by making the farmer, whom Emma believes to be of a lower social class than Harriet, a good and estimable man.

The moral debate progresses through a series of negative consequences generated by the ideas and actions of the matchmaking heroine. Austen reinforces the debate with two scenes of social humiliation and immorality that form a parallel. The first is where Harriet is embarrassed by Mr. Elton, who refuses to dance with her, before Mr. Knightley comes to her rescue. The second is where Emma cruelly snubs Miss Bates during a picnic. Again, Mr. Knightley plays the role of moral corrector by reproaching Emma for her insensitivity.

It should be noted that Austen, in these decisive scenes, argues for a deeper morality based not on social status but on what is good and proper in the relationship of one human being to another. It should also be noted that Austen avoids a moralizing tone by giving these scenes a strong emotional charge. The audience is pained to see Harriet snubbed and Miss Bates publicly humiliated. And they are pleased when Mr. Knightley does the right thing: saving a defenseless young woman or reminding our heroine when she is being cruel.

Emma and Mr. Knightley's marriage is a reaffirmation of the system insofar as both characters have a fairly high and relatively similar social status. This system, and the values on which it is based, will not change at the end of the satire. But the union of the two protagonists subtly shakes it. Emma and Mr. Knightley come together not because they are of the right social class but because Emma has evolved and become better, and because Mr. Knightley is a moral man, regardless of social status.

5. Black Comedy

Black comedy plays on the logical—or more precisely illogical—nature of a system. This elaborate form of storytelling is designed to demonstrate that annihilation is not so much the result of an individual choice (as in tragedy) but of a fundamentally destructive system that traps individuals. The key to the moral debate is to prevent the hero from having an awareness, so as to give it even more impact to the audience. The moral debate in black comedy unfolds as follows:

• Many characters coexist in an organization. Someone explains in great detail the rules of the system and its logic.

• Many characters, including the hero, seek to achieve the same goal, which generally involves killing someone or destroying something.

• All firmly believe in this goal and think that what they are doing is perfectly sensible. In reality, their actions are completely illogical.

• The opponents, who are also part of the system, also seek to achieve the goal and justify themselves with precise arguments.

• A wise character, usually the ally, constantly demonstrates that the characters' actions make no sense and will inevitably lead to disaster. He plays the role of the chorus, but no one listens to him.

• All characters, including the hero, use extreme, even criminal, means to achieve the goal.

• The characters' actions lead to death and destruction.

• The confrontation is intense and destructive, with each character still thinking they are right. Most characters die or go mad.

• No character, not even the hero, has an awareness. But it is so obvious that the hero should have had this awareness that the audience has it instead.

• The characters who are not dead are horribly mutilated but immediately resume their efforts to achieve the goal.

• At the end of the most positive black comedies, the wise character contemplates the system with horror and may even leave it or try to change it.

This form is extremely delicate to achieve. For the moral debate of black comedy to work, the hero must first be made sympathetic. If not, the comedy becomes an abstraction, an intellectual essay, with the audience able to distance themselves from the characters and feel morally superior to them. The readers or viewers must be absorbed by the story so that they can suddenly discover that they are fundamentally these characters and not beings superior to them.

Apart from creating a sympathetic hero, the best way to emotionally engage the audience in a black comedy is to have the hero passionately explain the logic of their goal. Authors who wish to add a touch of hope to this pessimistic form can offer the only sane character an alternative to this madness, as long as it is detailed.

Among the stories that use the black comedy debate are Goodfellas, Network, Wag the Dog, After Hours, Dr. Strangelove, Catch-22, Heathers, Brazil, and Prizzi's Honor.

COMBINING DIFFERENT TYPES OF MORAL DEBATES

Although they are self-sufficient forms, different types of moral debates are not necessarily exclusive of each other. In fact, some high-level authors use an excellent technique of combining different types of moral debates in a single story. Joyce's Ulysses begins with the very simple good/evil debate found in most myths and deepens it with a much more complex ironic-satirical approach. The Cherry Orchard is a combination of pathos and ironic-satirical debate.

In its attempt to mix tragedy, black comedy, and ironic satire, American Beauty shows us how difficult it is to combine these forms. Although brilliant in many respects, the story never fully achieves its potential as a tragedy, black comedy, or satire. If the different forms of moral debates are self-sufficient, it is for a specific reason: they work in very different ways and have a very different emotional impact on the audience. To assemble them naturally requires total technical mastery.

Other examples of combined moral debates can be found in Madame Bovary, The Adventures of Huckleberry Finn, and Dog Day Afternoon.

THE UNIQUE MORAL VIEWPOINT

At the most advanced stage of moral debate in storytelling is the author capable of creating a unique moral viewpoint, in the sense of singular. In The Scarlet Letter, for example, Nathaniel Hawthorne develops a triangular opposition between characters that tends to defend a natural morality based on true love. With Ulysses, James Joyce created a natural religion and everyday heroism by sending a "father" and a "son" on a one-day journey through Dublin. These are large-scale moral debates, but they are not only moral debates. The expertise and artistry of these writers are also reflected in their networks of characters, their plots, the universe of their stories, and their symbols, which are as vast and precise as their moral debates.

A unique moral viewpoint is also found in some blockbusters. If you think it is the special effects that made these films big successes, you are mistaken. In Star Wars, George Lucas made a modern amalgam of Western and Eastern morality by combining a Western hero, a sort of Zen knightly order, and a morality called "the Force." The moral debate is, of course, much less elaborate than in The Scarlet Letter or Ulysses. But the intention is there, and in its conciseness, it contributed to the universal success of the films in this saga.

Similarly, The Godfather does not just portray the Mafia world in 1940s America. This film also presents a moral system based on the modern economy and a new form of warfare. The lines "I'm going to make him an offer he can't refuse," "It's just business, it's nothing personal," and "Keep your friends close, but your enemies closer" constitute the catechism of a modern American version of Machiavelli's The Prince. Like Star Wars, The Godfather treats morality succinctly. But note that the attempt to present a moral system is one of the major causes of the success of these stories when it is successful.

THE MORAL DEBATE IN DIALOGUE

It is primarily through narrative structure that a moral debate is constructed in a story. But it is not the only way. Dialogue must also be used. When structure is left to do most of the work, it allows dialogue to do what it does best, namely providing subtlety and emotional power.

We will explain in detail how to write moral dialogue in Chapter 10, "Building Scenes and Symphonic Dialogues." For now, let's look at the best places to insert this type of dialogue in the story.

Very often, the moral debate is expressed in the dialogues of the scene where an ally reproaches the hero for having undertaken immoral actions to achieve their goal. The ally explains to the hero that they are wrong. The hero, who has not yet had an awareness, defends their actions.

To highlight the moral debate through dialogues, one can also rely on the conflict between the hero and the adversary, at any point in the story, although it is easier to do so during a confrontation scene. A typical example of a moral debate occurring during a confrontation is found in the scene from The Hustler that opposes Eddie Felson to his former manager, Bert. In It's a Wonderful Life, the moral debate between the hero and the adversary occurs earlier in the story when George prevents Potter from taking over the family building and loan business. The great advantage of an early moral debate between the hero and their adversary is that it indicates to the audience what values are truly at stake and allows the drama to develop.

Finally, one can insert moral dialogue in a scene where the main adversary justifies their actions from a moral standpoint, even though they are wrong. This type of scene is the hallmark of very good authors because the adversary's moral arguments play a crucial role in the overall moral debate of the story.

An evil adversary is a character who is fundamentally bad, and therefore mechanical and uninteresting. In most real-life conflicts, good and evil, right and wrong, are not clearly defined. In good stories, both the hero and the adversary believe they are right and have reasons to believe so. They are also both wrong, although not in the same way.

By giving your adversary strong (though erroneous) arguments, you avoid the simplistic pattern of the good hero versus the bad adversary and give depth to this character. And since a hero is only as good as the character they fight against is interesting, you also give depth to your hero.

An excellent example of a moral debate with the adversary is found in The Verdict when attorney Concannon, the opposing lawyer, tells the woman he hired to spy on Frank: "We're paid to win." In A Few Good Men, Colonel Jessep justifies ordering the murder of a marine by presenting himself as the last bastion of a territory surrounded by barbarians. In Shadow of a Doubt, brilliantly written by Thornton Wilder, Uncle Charlie, a serial killer, terrifyingly justifies murdering widows by presenting them as large animals that "drink money, eat money... And what do you do to animals when they become too fat or too old?"

The key to success is not to make the adversary a "straw man," a character who seems formidable at first glance but is actually hollow. Never give your adversary obviously weak arguments. Give them the best arguments you can find. Give them reason on certain points. But also ensure there is a fatal flaw in their logic.

DEFINING THE MORAL DEBATE – WRITING EXERCISE NO. 4

• Guiding Principle Start by turning the guiding principle of your story into a thematic line. This thematic line should correspond to your viewpoint on what constitutes good and bad actions in this story summarized in a single sentence. When working on the guiding principle, focus on the key actions and their moral consequences.

• Techniques for Thematic Line Think of all the techniques, such as symbols, capable of condensing your moral viewpoint into a sentence or summarizing the unique structure you will give to your story.

• Moral Choice Write the determining moral choice your hero will have to make towards the end of the story.

• Moral Problem Revisit your work on the premise and formulate in a single sentence the central moral problem your hero will face throughout the story.

• Characters Seen as Variations on the Same Theme Starting with the hero and the main adversary, explain how each character confronts the central moral problem differently.

• Conflicting Values List the key values of each of the main characters and explain how these values will come into conflict as each character seeks to achieve the goal.

MORAL DEBATE

Detail the moral debate you will develop through the story's structure using the following sequence:

• Beliefs and Values of the Hero Reformulate the essential beliefs and values of your hero.

• Moral Weakness What is the main weakness of your hero in their behavior with others?

• Moral Need Towards the end of the story, what must your hero learn about the right way to behave and live in the world?

• First Immoral Action Describe the first action of your hero that will hurt another character in the story. Ensure this action stems from the hero's major moral weakness.

• Desire Reformulate the precise goal of your hero.

• Narrative Drive List all the actions your hero will undertake to achieve their goal.

• Immoral Actions When these actions are immoral, why are they so?

Critique: Write the critique or critiques the hero will receive after one or more of their immoral actions.

Justification: How does the hero justify each of their immoral actions?

• Attack by an Ally Explain precisely the main moral attack led by the ally against the hero. Again, write the hero's justification.

• Obsessive Dynamic Explain when and how your hero becomes completely obsessed with victory. In other words, at what point does your hero decide they are willing to do almost anything to win?

• Immoral Actions While obsessed with victory, what immoral actions does your hero undertake?

Critique: Describe the critique your hero will eventually face following these actions.

Justification: Explain how your hero justifies their methods.

• Confrontation During the final confrontation, how will you show that one set of values—those of the hero or the adversary—is superior to the other in this case?

• Final Action Against the Adversary Before or during the confrontation, will your hero undertake a final action—moral or not—against the adversary?

• Moral Awareness What will your hero have learned, or failed to learn, by the end of the story? Ensure the answer to this question relates to how to behave with others.

• Moral Decision Towards the end of the story, must your hero choose between two ways of acting?

• Thematic Revelation Try to find a narrative event other than the hero's awareness through which you will express your vision of how people should change in their behavior.

Let's take the example of the film Casablanca to better understand how the moral debate works.

CASABLANCA

(play, Everybody Comes to Rick’s, by Murray Burnett and Joan Alison, screenplay by Julius J. Epstein, Philip G. Epstein, and Howard Koch, 1942)

• Guiding Principle A marginalized former freedom fighter reunites with a lost love but chooses to resume the fight when that love returns to him.

• Thematic Line Even the greatest love must be sacrificed in the fight against oppression.

• Moral Choice Rick must choose between staying with the woman he loves and fighting dictatorship in the world.

• Moral Problem What balance to find between personal desires and sacrifice for the good of society as a whole?

• Characters Seen as Variations on the Same Theme

Rick: For most of the story, Rick only cares about himself, uninterested in the world's problems.

Ilsa: Ilsa tries to do the right thing but ultimately, love is too strong for her.

Laszlo: Laszlo is willing to sacrifice anything, including his love, to fight against fascism.

Renault: Renault is a true opportunist who only cares about himself and money.

• Conflicting Values

Rick: Self, honesty, his friends.

Ilsa: Loyalty to her husband, love for Rick, fight against the Nazis.

Laszlo: Fight against the Nazis, love for Ilsa, love for humanity.

Renault: Women, money, power.

MORAL DEBATE

• Beliefs and Values of the Hero Self, honesty, his friends.

• Moral Weaknesses Cynicism, selfishness, cruelty.

• Moral Need Stop caring only about oneself at the expense of others. Reintegrate into society and become a leader in the fight against fascism.

• First Immoral Action Rick accepts the transit letters from Ugarte even though he suspects they come from murdered couriers.

• Second Immoral Action Rick refuses to help Ugarte escape the police.

Critique: A man tells Rick he hopes he won't be around if the Germans come for him.

Justification: Rick replies that he sticks his neck out for nobody.

• Desire Rick wants Ilsa.

• Narrative Drive Rick often attacks Ilsa while trying to win her back. He also undertakes several actions to keep the transit letters to sell or use them for himself.

• Immoral Action When Ilsa returns after the club closes, Rick refuses to listen to her and calls her a tramp.

Critique: Ilsa doesn't verbally criticize but gives Rick a cold look as he leaves.

Justification: Rick doesn't seek to justify himself.

• Attack by an Ally Throughout the story, it's Rick's main adversary, Ilsa, who reproaches him the most. However, Rick's friend, Sam, the bartender, keeps advising him to stop clinging to his lost love. And Rick replies: "If she can take it, I can take it. Play it, Sam [our song]."

• Immoral Action In the market, Rick makes advances to Ilsa and asks her to lie to Laszlo and come back to him.
Critique: Ilsa reproaches Rick for no longer being the man she knew in Paris and tells him she was married to Laszlo before meeting him.

Justification: Rick does not try to justify his words and simply says he was drunk the night before.

• Obsessive dynamic Initially, Rick seeks to hurt Ilsa to avenge the pain she caused him. It is only later in the story that he becomes obsessed with the idea of helping Ilsa and Laszlo escape.

• Immoral action Rick rejects Laszlo's proposal regarding the letters and tells him to ask Ilsa for explanations.

Critique: None.

Justification: Rick wants to hurt Ilsa.

• Immoral action Rick refuses to give the letters to Ilsa.

Critique: Ilsa explains to him that this cause is more important than personal feelings and that it is also her fight. If Rick does not give her the letters, Victor Laszlo will die in Casablanca.

• Justification: Rick explains that he now only cares about himself.

• Immoral action Rick tells Ilsa that he will only help Laszlo escape. His last lie to Ilsa—making her believe they will leave together—is actually the starting point of a noble action, namely saving Laszlo and Ilsa.

Critique: Renault says he would do the same in Rick's place, which, given the character's nature, is not a compliment.

Justification: Rick does not seek to justify himself. He must deceive Renault into believing he plans to leave with Ilsa.

• Confrontation Rick asks Renault to call the airport, but Renault calls Major Strasser. At the airport, Rick points a gun at Renault and tells Ilsa to leave with Laszlo. Rick tells Laszlo that Ilsa has been faithful to him. Laszlo and Ilsa board the plane. Strasser arrives and tries to stop the plane, but Rick shoots him.

• Final action against the adversary Rick no longer commits immoral actions. He shoots Strasser, but given the global situation, this murder is justified.

• Moral awakening Rick understands that his love for Ilsa is not as important as the help he can provide to Laszlo in his fight against Nazi domination.

• Moral decision Rick gives the letters to Laszlo, urges Ilsa to leave with him, and tells Laszlo that Ilsa loves him. Then he leaves to join the Free French Forces.

• Thematic revelation Renault's surprise change at the end of the story, when he decides to join Rick in his fight (a classic example of a double reversal), generates a thematic revelation: everyone must play a role in the fight against fascism.





QUESTIONS / ANSWERS


Q: When you say that every story conveys a moral point of view, do you mean that there should be a moral message behind every story? I rather feel that morality goes against storytelling and tends to produce bad stories.

A: You've just pointed out the biggest mistake authors make regarding the theme: they express it directly in their dialogues, which could be called "on-the-nose dialogues." They send a message, they moralize to the audience. This indeed goes against storytelling and produces bad stories.

To understand the importance of the moral dimension of a story and the right way to express it, one must first distinguish between "moral" and "moralizing." Moralizing writings express, usually through direct commandments or a morally perfect hero, a narrow and simplistic way of acting towards others. Conversely, the moral dimension of a writing appears whenever a character acts towards another or upon themselves. When I say that the best stories convey a moral point of view, it should be understood in this broader sense of the word "moral," which I also distinguish from the strictly psychological point of view.

Every story presents a psychological dimension, as it shows how human beings behave. And some authors convey in their story a strictly psychological theme that can, in itself, be of interest. But these kinds of stories tend to have less impact on the audience. They are internal and non-dramatic, sometimes focused on a single person and their alienation from the world.

Drama is the conflict between at least two people and their beliefs, their values regarding the best way to live. When two people argue, the story always takes on a moral dimension. It deals with what these characters want and what they are willing to do, their actions for and against others, and the moral cost of their conduct in pursuit of what they want.

This is why a good story always expresses the author's point of view on values and the goodness or badness of action. But in a good story, this point of view is not moralizing; it is not a commandment primarily expressed through dialogue, with a hero who is a paragon of virtue. It is a debate of actions, a complex exploration of what constitutes a good action and the difficulty of accomplishing it, expressed through the narrative structure as a deeply flawed character takes steps to achieve their goal.

The ability to express a nuanced moral point of view through narrative structure is one of the hallmarks of a great author. It requires mastery, technique, and the ability to tell the story with art. It is not easy. But the effort is worth it.



Q: I feel that most American films always tell the same story of good versus evil. Is this the framework you advise us to work within? Isn't it too narrow and caricatural?

A: You absolutely should not work within this framework. In my opinion, the opposition of good versus evil is the worst moral debate one can find in a story, a debate almost always "moralizing." No human being is entirely black or white. If you start your story on a good vs. evil opposition, you stop the moral debate before it can even begin, as you cannot explore, through conflict, the unique combination of personal flaws and social forces that drives a character to harm another. The evil character is a "black box," inherently bad, and therefore indecipherable. This type of character is nothing more than a machine for committing bad actions, which means, paradoxically, that they cannot be held responsible for their actions.

You are right to say that many American films, if not most, only repeat the story of good versus evil. This is because Hollywood mainly seeks to tell myths and action stories, often with superheroes and supervillains, that appeal to a wide global audience, especially teenagers and young adults. These are stories that tell the success of a savior, providing viewers with the satisfaction, which they may never find in their own lives, of accomplishing something great. But even among Hollywood myth/action films, the best are those that make the adversary more complex and nuanced than the simplistic villain.

Conclusion: whatever story you write, you must always go beyond the simple opposition of good versus evil to make your hero complex both psychologically and morally. Your story will be all the better for it.





– 6 –


The Story's Universe



Joyce's Ulysses and the "Harry Potter" series are good illustrations of one of the keys to the art of storytelling. On the surface, these two works could not seem more different. Ulysses, a complex, adult, and intellectually stimulating story, is often considered the greatest novel of the 20th century. The "Harry Potter" series are entertaining fantasy stories intended for children. But their authors understood that creating a singular world, unique to their story—and organically linked to the characters—was as essential to the quality of their storytelling as the characters, plot, theme, and dialogues.

Claiming that "Cinema is a visual medium" is extremely misleading. While it is true that films allow us to see stories on a screen and witness incredible special effects that no other medium can offer, the "visual" element that truly affects the audience is the story's universe: a complex and detailed network in which every element has narrative significance, is a physical expression of the network of characters, and particularly of the hero. This key principle applies to cinema as well as to any narrative medium.

You will notice that, in this area, storytelling expresses real life by being the opposite of real life. In life, we are born into a world that already exists and to which we must then adapt. In good stories, it is the characters who come first, before the author conceives the world as an infinitely detailed manifestation of these fictional beings.

T. S. Eliot calls this "the objective correlative." But whatever name you give it, when you create the universe of your story, you begin to add that richness of texture that is one of the hallmarks of excellent storytelling. A good story is a tapestry woven from many threads organized to produce a powerful effect. It is true that one can tell a story without adding the texture of the story's universe. But this is a major loss.

It is important to know that the physical world of the story acts for the author as a "condensor-extender." You have very little time to place a significant amount of elements: characters, plot, symbols, moral debate, and dialogues. You must therefore rely on techniques that allow you to condense meaning into the limited space and time you have. The more meaning you condense into the story, the more it will expand, extend in the audience's mind, its different elements bouncing off each other in a nearly infinite variety of ways.

Gaston Bachelard, in The Poetics of Space, explains "the drama of human dwellings." Meaning is implanted in all kinds of forms and spaces, from shells to houses to drawers. Bachelard raises a crucial point for the storyteller: "The two spaces, intimate space and exterior space, constantly encourage each other in their growth." You will notice that Bachelard speaks of organic storytelling: when you create the right world for your story, you plant certain seeds that will germinate and grow in the hearts and minds of readers or viewers and touch them deeply.

To summarize this part of the writing process: you start with a story that fits into one sentence (the seven steps) and a set of characters. Then you create the forms and spaces that will express these narrative elements, and these forms and spaces will have the desired effects on the hearts and minds of readers or viewers.

It seems that the meaning found in physical forms and spaces is deeper than culture and education; it seems to belong to the human spirit. Hence the depth of its impact on the audience. The different elements of the story's universe thus form a new set of tools and techniques that you can use to tell your story.

Translating the narrative line into a physical universe, which thus provokes certain emotions in the audience, is not an easy task. For it requires speaking two languages—the language of words and the language of images—and making them match exactly throughout the story.

But how to apply these techniques to the creation of your story's universe? You just need to follow these steps (the first three concern the creation of the story's space and the last two the creation of the story's time):



1. We will once again start by relying on the guiding principle, the cement of the story. It will allow you to define the "arena" in which your story will unfold.

2. We will then divide this arena into visual oppositions based on the existing oppositions between characters.

3. Then we will detail this world using three of the four major building blocks—natural settings, man-made spaces, and technology—that constitute the story's universe, and we will emphasize what these forms and spaces mean to the audience.

4. We will then connect the story's universe to the overall development of the hero and apply the fourth building block of the story's universe: time.

5. Finally, we will delve into the development of the story's universe through the narrative structure by creating seven visual steps.





FINDING THE STORY'S UNIVERSE

IN THE GUIDING PRINCIPLE


Since the story's universe is part of the story, it must begin by returning to the core of the narrative, that is, the guiding principle. The story's universe, like the premise, characters, and theme, takes shape from the guiding principle.

For many reasons, it is more difficult to find the story's universe in the guiding principle than to find the premise, characters, and theme. As we have already mentioned, the narrative and the "visual" are two very different languages. But one can learn a language. The problem, in reality, is that the guiding principle and the story's universe operate in opposite ways.

Typically, the guiding principle describes a linear narrative movement, that of a single main character who develops. The story's universe is everything that surrounds the characters simultaneously. In other words, it offers simultaneous elements and actions.

To connect them, take the raw sequence of the narrative line you found in the guiding principle and deploy it three-dimensionally to derive the story's universe. Again, start simple: study the guiding principle and see if you can find a single visual idea that expresses the narrative line.

To see things more concretely, let's revisit the guiding principles of the stories we discussed in chapter 2 (The Premise), and this time try to describe the universe of these stories in a single sentence.



MOSES IN THE BOOK OF EXODUS

• Guiding principle A man who does not know who he is struggles to restore his people's freedom and receives new moral laws that will define him and his people.

• Theme line A man who takes responsibility for acting for his people is rewarded with a divine vision that dictates how he and his people should live in the future.

• Story's universe A man leads his people through a wild world until the truth is revealed to him atop a mountain.



ULYSSES

• Guiding principle In a modern odyssey through the city, over the course of a single day, a man finds a father, who in turn finds a son.

• Theme line A true hero is a man who endures the trials of daily life and shows compassion to those in need.

• Story's universe A city, where for twenty-four hours, each of its parts is the modern version of a mythical obstacle.



FOUR WEDDINGS AND A FUNERAL

• Guiding principle Friends experience four utopias (the weddings) and one nightmarish moment (the funeral), while each of them searches for the ideal partner.

• Theme line When you find true love, you must commit wholeheartedly.

• Story's universe The utopian world of marriage and its rituals.



THE "HARRY POTTER" SERIES

• Guiding principle A wizard prince learns to become a man and a king by spending seven school years at a wizarding school.

• Theme line When you have the chance to possess great talents and powers, you must become a leader and sacrifice for the good of others.

• Story's universe A wizarding school in a giant magical medieval castle.



THE STING

• Guiding principle Tell the story of a con as a con and fool both the adversary and the audience.

• Theme line Small lies and cons are permissible if they serve to destroy an evil man.

• Story's universe A fake workplace in a seedy city during the Great Depression.



LONG DAY'S JOURNEY INTO NIGHT

• Guiding principle As the day passes and night falls, all family members confront the mistakes and ghosts of their past.

• Theme line One must face their own truth and that of others and then forgive.

• Story's universe The dark house, full of cracks where family secrets can be hidden.



MEET ME IN ST. LOUIS

• Guiding principle Depict the evolution of a family over a year through events occurring in each season.

• Theme line Sacrifice for the family is more valuable than efforts for personal glory.

• Story's universe The large house that changes nature with each season and each change experienced by the family living there.



COPENHAGEN

• Guiding principle Use Heisenberg's uncertainty principle outside the context of physics to explore the ambiguous morality of the man who discovered it.

• Theme line It is always very difficult to determine what drives us to act and to know if our actions are good or bad.

• Story's universe The house in the form of a courtroom.



A CHRISTMAS CAROL

• Guiding principle Follow the rebirth of a man by forcing him to observe his past, present, and future on Christmas Eve.

• Theme line One is happier when giving to others.

• Story's universe A 19th-century London accountant's office and three different homes—one rich, one middle class, and one poor—anchored in the past, present, and future.



IT'S A WONDERFUL LIFE

• Guiding principle Reveal the power of the individual by showing what a town, and a nation, would have been like if a single man had never lived.

• Theme line A man's wealth is not measured by the money he earns but by the friends and family members he loves.

• Story's universe Two different versions of the same small American town.



CITIZEN KANE
