• Guiding principle Use multiple narrators to demonstrate that one can never truly know a man's life.

• Theme line People who try to force others to love them end up alone.

• Story's universe The mansion and isolated "kingdom" of an American titan.





THE STORY'S ARENA


Once you have your guiding principle and your one-sentence description of your story's universe, you must define an arena that marks the physical boundaries of this world. The arena is the basic space of all drama. It is a unique and unified place, enclosed in some way. Everything within the arena is part of the story. Everything outside is not.

Many authors, especially novelists and screenwriters, mistakenly think that because they can go anywhere, they should. This is a grave mistake because if you break the unity of the story's arena, the drama literally dissipates. When there are too many arenas, stories become fragmented and inorganic.

The rule of a single arena is, of course, easier to follow in theater, where one benefits from the natural advantage of the stage setting, delimited by the curtain. Cinema and novels have a larger arena, making a unified space all the more necessary for the unfolding of the drama.





Creating the Arena


We are not trying to push you to adhere to the rigid "Aristotelian unity of place" that claims all action should take place in a single location. There are four main ways to create a single arena without destroying the diversity of locations and actions essential to any good story.





1. Create a general space, then alternate and condense


To achieve this method, describe the broadest field of the story towards the beginning of the narrative. Start with the story's broadest world and the wall that separates it from everything else. Then, as the story progresses, focus on the smaller worlds within the arena.

The general space can literally cover large areas: the vast plains of the West, an entire city, a galaxy, or an ocean. But it can also delimit a small territory: a village, a house, or a bar.

This technique is found in Casablanca, Alien, Spider-Man, L.A. Confidential, The Matrix, Death of a Salesman, A Streetcar Named Desire, Mary Poppins, Groundhog Day, Sunset Boulevard, Nashville, Blood Simple, Meet Me in St. Louis, The Great Gatsby, Shane, Star Wars, and It's a Wonderful Life.





2. Have the hero undertake a journey in a generally uniform area, which develops along a single line


At first glance, this method seems to contradict the idea of a single arena, and it does indeed when not used wisely. If many travel stories seem fragmented, it is because the hero travels through many very different and disconnected places, each giving the feeling of being an isolated episode.

A sense of a single arena can only be created if the area in which the hero travels has remained fundamentally the same. Like a desert, an ocean, a river, or a jungle. But even then, try to make the journey a single, easily identifiable guiding line, and present the area simply, from beginning to end, to give it a sense of unity.

Good illustrations of the single guiding line journey can be found in Titanic, The Wild Bunch, The Blues Brothers, Jacques Tati's Trafic, and The African Queen's Odyssey.





3. Have the hero undertake a circular journey in a generally unified area


This method works much like the previous one, except that in the end, the hero returns home. The advantage of the single guiding line that gives the audience a sense of a unified and oriented path is lost. But by returning to the starting point, home, the hero's transformation is highlighted in contrast to this world, which has not changed.

The circular journey is the basis of works such as The Wizard of Oz, Joyce's Ulysses, Finding Nemo, King Kong, Don Quixote, Big, Heart of Darkness, Beau Geste, Adrift, Deliverance, The Adventures of Huckleberry Finn, Field of Dreams, and Alice in Wonderland.





4. Take the hero out of their element (fish out of water)
First, place your hero in an initial arena. Spend enough time there to clearly show all the talents he possesses that are specific to this world. Then launch him into another world—without lingering on the journey—and show that the talents the hero used in the first world, while seemingly unsuitable, actually work just as well in the second.

This approach is used in Beverly Hills Cop, Crocodile Dundee, Black Rain, and to a lesser extent, but still notably, in Witness and Dances with Wolves.

Strictly speaking, stories of heroes out of their element take place in two different arenas. This is why, unsurprisingly, they often feel like two-part stories. What cements them is that the hero uses the same talents in both places. So much so that the audience ends up thinking that even if the two arenas seem very different, they are fundamentally identical.

One of the keys to this technique is not to linger in the first arena. It is only the springboard for the main story, which unfolds in the second. Once you have presented the talents the hero uses in the first arena, it has fulfilled its function.

Oppositions within the arena

Characters are not created to fill a story universe, however fabulous it may be. The story universe is created to express its characters, particularly its hero, and to let them express themselves.

Just as you have defined the network of characters by dramatizing the oppositions that exist between them, you must define the story universe in your unique arena by dramatizing visual oppositions. To do this, you must return to the oppositions between characters and their values.

Review your network of characters and examine all the ways these characters confront each other. Pay particular attention to conflicts of values, as it is primarily for reasons of values that the main characters fight. From these oppositions, you will begin to see visual oppositions also emerge in the physical world.

Study these visual oppositions carefully and try to find three or four that can become central. Let’s now examine some examples to better understand how these visual oppositions stem from the oppositions between characters.

IT'S A WONDERFUL LIFE

(novella, The Greatest Gift, by Philip Van Doren Stern, screenplay by Francis Goodrich, Albert Hackett, and Frank Capra, 1946)

It’s a Wonderful Life is structured so that the audience can see two different versions of the same town. You will notice that this important element of the story universe, the town, is a direct expression of the fundamental opposition between George Bailey and Mr. Potter. And each version of the town is a physical manifestation of the values of the character it represents. Pottersville is the result of the tyranny and unlimited greed of one man. Bedford Falls is the consequence of democracy, honesty, and kindness.

SUNSET BOULEVARD

(Charles Brackett, Billy Wilder, and D. M. Marshman Jr, 1950)

The central opposition of Sunset Boulevard revolves around the modest screenwriter Joe Gillis—who still believes in good work under the veneer of the money chase—and the aging rich movie star Norma Desmond. This opposition is visually translated: Joe’s cramped apartment vs. Norma’s decrepit mansion; Los Angeles, a modern, sunny, open city vs. a dark gothic house; youth vs. old age; modest outsiders trying to break through vs. the grand, secure, but cruel movie studio; ordinary workers of the entertainment industry vs. the aristocracy of movie stars.

THE GREAT GATSBY

(F. Scott Fitzgerald, 1925)

In The Great Gatsby, the main oppositions are Gatsby and Tom, Gatsby and Daisy, Gatsby and Nick, and Nick and Tom (note the four-cornered system of oppositions). Each of these characters represents a different version of the modest Midwesterner who came east to make a fortune. The first opposition of the story universe is therefore the plains of the Midwest to the tall towers and elegant villas of the East Coast. Tom is a nouveau riche, but less "new" than Gatsby. There is thus an opposition among the rich of Long Island, an opposition between well-established East Egg, where Tom and Daisy live, and the equally wealthy but more modern West Egg, where Gatsby lives. Moreover, Tom and Daisy’s villa is described as opulent but classic, while Gatsby’s and the way he uses it are depicted as the height of flashy bad taste.

Gatsby made his fortune through his illegal bootlegging activities while Nick is an honest and hardworking stockbroker. Nick rents Gatsby’s small guest house from where he can watch the superficial parties organized by the hero. Tom is a violent man who cheats on his wife. Fitzgerald thus creates a contrast between Tom’s villa and his mistress’s gas station. The author creates a final opposition of sub-worlds when he depicts the valley of ashes, the hidden waste of the great capitalist engine represented by New York and Long Island. In a final thematic explosion, he compares New York, the pinnacle of American "civilization," to the city as it was before its development, still full of promise, "the green breast of a new world."

KING KONG

(James Creelman and Ruth Rose, 1931)

The main contrast in King Kong is that between producer Carl Denham and the immense prehistoric beast, Kong. The main opposition in the story universe is thus between Manhattan Island, a man-made, over-civilized but also extremely cruel world, where Denham, the image maker, is "king"; and Skull Island, the extremely cruel state of nature where Kong, the master of physical strength, is king. Within this main opposition is a triangular contrast of sub-worlds that opposes New Yorkers, Skull Island villagers, and prehistoric jungle animals, each group involved in a different form of survival struggle.

DANCES WITH WOLVES

(novel and screenplay by Michael Blake, 1990)

Throughout the story of Dances with Wolves, the central opposition of characters and values evolves, and with it, the main visual oppositions. Initially, the hero, John Dunbar, wants to participate in the construction of the American frontier before it disappears. The first opposition of the story universe is between the Civil War, in the east, where the nation has been corrupted by slavery, and the vast plains of the Wild West, where America’s promises have retained their freshness. Within the world of the western plains, the apparent value conflict opposes the white soldier, Dunbar, who believes in building the American nation, to the Lakota Sioux, who seem to be savages seeking to destroy it.

But Michael Blake describes the sub-worlds in a way that shakes this apparent value opposition. Dunbar’s cavalry outpost is a rat hole, devoid of life, a muddy scar that disfigures the landscape. The Sioux village is a small utopia, a circle of tipis by the river, with grazing horses and playing children. As the story progresses, Blake shows that the true value opposition lies between the expansionist American world that treats animals and Indians as objects to be destroyed, and the Indian world that is in harmony with nature and treats each human being according to their deep qualities.

L.A. CONFIDENTIAL

(novel by James Ellroy, screenplay by Brian Helgeland and Curtis Hanson, 1997)

In L.A. Confidential, the main opposition between characters is at first glance between the police and the murderers. In reality, it is between police investigators who believe in different versions of justice and a murderous police captain and a corrupt district attorney. That’s why the first visual opposition presented by the voice-over is that of a City of Angels, an apparent utopia vs. a racist, corrupt, and oppressive Los Angeles. This essential opposition is subdivided when the three main investigators are introduced: Bud White, the "real" cop, who confuses justice with vengeance; Jack Vincennes, the "cool" cop, who supplements his income by acting as a technical advisor for a TV police series and who arrests people for money; and Ed Exley, the smart cop who knows the political rules of the justice game and intends to use them to achieve his ambitions. The story stages this opposition of characters and values through different sub-worlds by contrasting the rich, white, and corrupt Los Angeles that committed the crime with the poor, black Los Angeles to which it is attributed.

DETAILING THE STORY UNIVERSE

The visual oppositions and the story universe itself are detailed by combining three main elements: territory (natural settings), populations (man-made spaces), and technology (tools). The fourth element, time, corresponds to how the original world you created develops over the course of the story, something we will discuss a little later. Let’s start by studying the natural settings.

Natural settings

Never choose the natural setting of your story at random. For a reader or viewer, each setting carries a multitude of meanings. As Bachelard says, "a psychologist of the imagination [...] realizes that the cosmos shapes man, transforms a man of the hills into a man of the island and the river [...] that the house remodels man." You must know some of the possible meanings of various natural settings, such as hills, islands, and rivers, to determine if one of them could be a good expression of your premise, characters, and theme.

The ocean

In the collective imagination, the ocean is divided into two distinct places: the surface and the depths. The surface is the quintessential two-dimensional landscape, a flat expanse as far as the eye can see. Therefore, the surface seems abstract while being completely natural. This flat abstract surface, like a vast chessboard, intensifies the sense of competition, the game for survival played on the largest scale.

The depths of the ocean embody the quintessential three-dimensional landscape, where all creatures, light, live at different levels of depth. This feeling of weightlessness, of floating, is very often present when the human mind imagines a utopia, which explains why the depths of the ocean have often hosted imaginary utopian worlds.

But the depths of the ocean are also a terrifying tomb, an immense impersonal hand that quietly seizes everything on the surface, living or not, to draw it into the infinite darkness of the abyss. The ocean is this immense mouth where ancient worlds, prehistoric creatures, secrets of the past, and old treasures it has engulfed await discovery.

Among ocean stories are Moby Dick, Titanic, Finding Nemo, Twenty Thousand Leagues Under the Sea, The Little Mermaid, Atlantis, The Sea Wolf, Master and Commander, The Odyssey of the Submarine Nerka, Mutiny on the Bounty, The Hunt for Red October, Jaws, and The Yellow Submarine.

Space

Space is the "up there" ocean, an infinite expanse of dark nothingness that hosts an unlimited diversity of other worlds. Like the depths of the ocean, space is three-dimensional. Like the surface of the ocean, space seems both abstract and natural. As everything moves in the shadows, although they are individual and unique, they are also reduced to their most essential quality. There is "the spaceship," "the human being," "the robot," and "the alien." Science fiction stories often use the form of myth not only because myth involves a journey but also because myth is a form of story that explores the most fundamental human distinctions.

As the setting of space carries the promise of an unlimited diversity of other worlds, it is a place of endless adventures. Adventure stories always have to do with a sense of discovery, novelty, the unheard-of, which can be both exciting and terrifying. At this point in human history, literature, and cinema, space is the only natural setting where the promise of unlimited adventures still makes sense (the ocean is also largely unexplored territory. But as we struggle to imagine a true community inhabiting it, the ocean is only the setting of a human world in fantasy).

Space is the setting for science fiction stories such as 2001: A Space Odyssey, Dune, the "Star Wars" saga, Blade Runner, Apollo 13, Forbidden Planet, many episodes of The Twilight Zone, the "Star Trek" series and films, and the "Alien" saga.

The forest

The main narrative quality of the forest is that it constitutes a natural cathedral. The tall trees, with their leaves above us that protect us, evoke a wise old man who assures us that, whatever problems we face, they will resolve with time. It is the place where contemplative people take refuge and where lovers hide.

But the intense intimacy provided by the forest can also be ominous. The forest is where people get lost. It is the place where ghosts and past lives hide. It is where hunters stalk their prey, and these prey are often human. The forest is less wild than the jungle; the jungle can kill anyone at any time. The forest, when it accomplishes its frightening work, first causes mental damage. It kills more slowly than the jungle but is just as deadly.

The forest is the natural setting of many fairy tales as well as stories such as Sleepy Hollow, The Lord of the Rings, the "Harry Potter" series, Return of the Jedi, Shrek, Excalibur, As You Like It, A Midsummer Night’s Dream, Song of Solomon, The Wizard of Oz, John McCabe, The Wolf Man, The Blair Witch Project, and Miller’s Crossing.

The jungle

The jungle is the state of nature. Its first impact on the imagination is a feeling of suffocation. Everything seems to grab you. The jungle gives the audience a sense of nature’s superiority over man. In this environment, man is reduced to the state of a beast.

Ironically, this primal place is also one of the two natural settings that express the theory of evolution, this modern theory of change.

The world of the jungle is found in the "Star Wars" saga; the Tarzan stories, including Greystoke; King Kong; The African Queen; Jurassic Park and The Lost World; The Emerald Forest; Aguirre, the Wrath of God; Mosquito Coast; Fitzcarraldo; The Eyes in the Trees; Heart of Darkness and Apocalypse Now.

The desert and ice

The desert and ice have always been places of agony and death. Even stories struggle to flourish there. The desert and ice seem completely impersonal in their brutality.

For something valuable to emerge from them, strong personalities must have come to harden and flourish in isolation. A rare example of a world of ice depicted as a utopia is found in Mark Helprin’s novel Winter’s Tale, which presents a village whose sense of community is strengthened when winter cuts it off from the rest of the world and freezes its lake, on which villagers enjoy all sorts of winter entertainments.

Deserts and ice worlds play an important role in the "Star Wars" saga, Fargo, Lawrence of Arabia, Beau Geste, Dune, The Ballad of Cable Hogue, The Searchers, She Wore a Yellow Ribbon, Once Upon a Time in the West, The Wild Bunch, The Sheltering Sky, The Gold Rush, and The Call of the Wild.

The island

The island is an ideal setting for creating a story in a social context. Like the ocean and space, the island is both highly abstract and completely natural. It is a miniature version of the planet, a small patch of land surrounded by water. The island is, by definition, an isolated place. That’s why, in stories, it is often the laboratory of the human race, a paradise or a solitary hell, a place where a special world can be built and where new forms of life can be created and tested.

The isolated and abstract nature of the island explains why this setting is often used to depict utopias or dystopias. And more than the jungle, the island is the ideal place to stage the workings of evolution.

Among the stories that use the island as a central setting are Robinson Crusoe, The Tempest, Gulliver’s Travels, The Incredibles, King Kong, Treasure Island, The Mysterious Island, The Island of Dr. Moreau, Lord of the Flies, Cast Away, the TV series Lost. The best use of the island in the history of storytelling is probably found in the series Gilligan’s Island.

In many ways, the island offers perhaps the most complex narrative possibilities of all natural settings. Let’s try to understand how to best exploit the island world. Note that the best way to use the inherent meaning of this natural setting is through narrative structure.

• Take the time, at the beginning, to present the normal society and the role the characters play in it (need).

• Send the characters to an island (desire).

• Create a new society based on different rules and values (desire).

• Make the relationships between the characters very different from those in the original society (plan).

• Through conflict, show what works and what doesn’t (opponent).

• Have the characters test new things when they encounter a problem (twist or realization).

The mountain

The highest of all places has become, in the collective imagination, the place of greatness. It is where strong men go to prove themselves—usually through isolation, meditation, discomfort, and direct confrontation with nature. The mountain top is the world of the natural philosopher, the great thinker who must understand the forces of nature to live in harmony with them, and sometimes control them.

Structurally, the mountain, the highest place, is associated with realization, the most mental of the twenty-two steps of narrative structure (see Chapter 8, "Plot"). Twists-revelations are moments of discovery and are essential to transform the plot and push it to a "higher," more intense level. Again, the mountain setting creates a direct relationship between space and person, in this case, between height and understanding.

This direct relationship between space and person is also found in the negative expression of the mountain, which is often described as a place of hierarchy, privilege, and tyranny, usually inhabited by an aristocrat who rules from above over the common people.

KEY POINT: The mountain is generally opposed to the plain.

The mountain and the plain are the only two main natural settings that form a visual contrast. Thus, authors often use the comparative method to highlight the essential and contradictory qualities of each.

The mountain world plays an important role in the story of Moses, the Greek myths of the gods of Mount Olympus, many fairy tales, The Magic Mountain, Lost Horizon, Brokeback Mountain, Batman Begins, The Snows of Kilimanjaro, A Farewell to Arms, The Deer Hunter, The Last of the Mohicans, Dances with Wolves, Shane, The Shining, and several other horror films.

The plain

This flat surface is wide open and accessible to all. Unlike the jungle, which is oppressive, the plain is completely free. That’s why it is, in many stories, the place of equality, freedom, and the rights of everyone. But this freedom comes at a cost and does not mean the absence of conflict. Like the surface of the ocean, this extremely flat expanse takes on an abstract character that highlights the fight or struggle for survival.

As for the negative aspects, the plain is often considered the place where mediocre people live. Unlike the happy few, who reside at the mountain top, the multitude of average people live with the rest of the herd, below. Unable to think for themselves, they need to be led, which usually leads to their destruction.

The plain is the natural setting of most westerns, including Shane and The Big Country, as well as Days of Heaven, Dances with Wolves, Lost Horizon, The Snows of Kilimanjaro, A Farewell to Arms, Blood Simple, and Field of Dreams.

The river

The river is an extremely powerful natural setting, perhaps the most powerful of all those that can be used in storytelling. The river is a path, making it a perfect physical manifestation for mythical stories whose structure is based on the journey.

But the river is more than just a path. It is a road that leads to something or away from something, reinforcing the feeling that the journey is an organic line of development, not just a series of episodes. In Heart of Darkness, for example, the hero travels up the river, delving deeper into the jungle. The line of human development linked to this journey leads from civilization to the hell of barbarism.

In The African Queen, the hero makes the journey and the reverse process by going down the river, to emerge from the jungle. His development begins in a jungle, an infernal landscape of death, isolation, and madness, and moves towards the human world of commitment and love.

The river marks a physical, moral, and emotional passage in The Adventures of Huckleberry Finn, Deliverance, Heart of Darkness and its adaptations, Apocalypse Now, A River Runs Through It, and The African Queen.

Beware of visual clichés! With natural settings, it is very easy to fall into the trap of commonplaces: "My hero is going to have a great realization? I’ll send him to the top of a mountain." Use natural settings only if they are essential to your story. And above all, use them in an original way.
Weather Conditions

Weather, like natural settings, can be used as a powerful physical representation of the characters' intimate experiences or to evoke strong feelings in the audience. Below is a list of classic correlations between weather and emotions:

• Lightning and thunder: Passion, terror, death

• Rain: Sadness, solitude, boredom, comfort

• Wind: Destruction, desolation

• Fog: Impenetrability, mystery

• Sun: Cheerfulness, amusement, freedom, but also hidden corruption under a pleasant appearance

• Snow: Sleep, serenity, almost inexorable death

Again, it is important to avoid repeating these classic correlations. Instead, try to use weather in a surprising or ironic way.

Man-Made Spaces

Man-made spaces are even more useful than natural spaces because they have the power to solve one of the most difficult problems an author faces: how to represent a society? In narratives, all man-made spaces are condensers-extenders. Each is a physical expression, a microcosm of the hero and the society in which he lives.

The author's problem is to represent this society on paper so that the audience can understand the deep relationship that unites the hero with others. Below are the main man-made spaces that can help you in this endeavor.

The House

For the narrator, the house is the very first of man-made spaces. The house is the first enclosure of the person. Its original physical elements define the development of the person's mind and the well-being of that mind in the present. The house is also the home of the family, which is the central unit of social life and the central unit of drama. Thus, all fiction authors must carefully consider the role the house can play in their story.

For your characters and your audience, no place can be more intimate than the house. But this house is also filled with visual oppositions that you must know to express its full dramatic potential.

Security vs. Adventures

The house is, first and foremost, the great protector. "In every dwelling, even in the castle, finding the initial shell is the first task of the phenomenologist." In other words, "always, in our reveries, the house is a great cradle. [...] Life begins well, it begins enclosed, protected, all warm in the bosom of the house."

The house may initially be the shell, the cradle, or the nest of the human being. But this protective cocoon is also what makes its opposite possible: the house is the solid foundation from which we venture out to discover the world. "The house [...] breathes. It is armor clothing and then it extends to infinity. In other words, we live there alternately in security and in adventure. It is a cell and it is a world." Very often, in stories, the first step of the adventure, the idea of adventure, occurs at the window. A character looks outside through the eye of the house and may even hear the whistle of a train calling them. And they dream of leaving.

The Ground vs. the Sky

The second opposition represented by the house is that of the ground and the sky. The house has deep roots. It rests against the earth. It tells the world and its inhabitants that it is solid and trustworthy.

But the house also rises towards the sky. Like a proud miniature cathedral, it wishes to offer the best, the "highest," to its inhabitants. "All strongly terrestrial beings – and a house is strongly terrestrial – remain subject to the attraction of the celestial, aerial world. The well-rooted house likes to have a branch that is sensitive to the wind, or an attic that can hear the rustling of leaves."

The Warm House

The warm house is large (though it is generally not a mansion). It has enough rooms, nooks, and crannies for each of its inhabitants to thrive in privacy. It should also be noted that the warm house carries within it two opposing elements: the security and comfort of the shell and the diversity made possible only by its size.

Authors often intensify the warmth of the large, diverse house by using the technique known as "domestic hum." This is Pieter Bruegel's technique (used in paintings such as The Hunters in the Snow and Winter Landscape with Skaters and a Bird Trap) applied to the house. In the domestic hum, all the different individuals of a family engage in their respective activities. Individuals and small groups may gather for a particular moment, then return to their affairs. This is a perfect representation of community on the scale of the house. Each person is both an individual and a part of the family, and even when each character is in a different part of the house, the audience feels a special connection linking them together.

The large, diverse house and the domestic hum can be found in stories such as You Can't Take It with You; Meet Me in St. Louis; My Father and Us; The Cider House Rules; Pride and Prejudice; The Magnificent Ambersons; The Royal Tenenbaums; Steel Magnolias; It's a Wonderful Life; the TV series The Waltons; David Copperfield; How Green Was My Valley; Mary Poppins and The Yellow Submarine.

The power of the warm house relies partly on the childhood memories, real or imagined, it evokes. Audience members lived in a large, comfortable house when they were young, and if some of them lived in a slum, they can always look at the large warm house and see in it the childhood they would have dreamed of having. This is why the warm house is so often used in memory stories, such as Jean Shepherd's A Christmas Story, and it is also why American authors so often use dilapidated Victorian homes with many gables and pleasant nooks set in old-fashioned settings.

The bar can be considered a variant of the house. Like it, it can be warm or terrifying. In the TV series Cheers, the bar is a utopia, a community where "everybody knows your name." The regulars are always in the same place, always making the same mistakes, and always maintaining the same strange relationships with each other. The bar is also a warm place simply because it is a place where no one has to change.

CASABLANCA

(play, Everybody Comes to Rick’s, by Murray Burnett and Joan Alison, screenplay by Julius J. Epstein, Philip G. Epstein, and Howard Koch, 1942)

The universe of the story played as important a role in the success of Casablanca as in the most elaborate fantasies, myths, and science fiction stories. And everything is centered around the bar, Rick's Café Américain.

What makes the universe of Casablanca – the bar – unique and gives it immense power over the audience is that it is both a dystopia and a utopia. It is in this bar that the king of the underworld has taken up residence.

Rick's Café Américain is a dystopia in that everyone wants to escape Casablanca, and everyone spends their time here waiting, endlessly, to be able to leave. But there is no escape. It is also a dystopia in that everything is easy money and bribes, a perfect expression of the hero's cynicism, selfishness, and despair.

But this bar is also a fabulous utopia. Rick reigns there as a master, a king surrounded by dozens of courtiers who pay him homage. The café is a large warm house full of nooks and crannies occupied by all sorts of characters. Each of them knows their place and appreciates it. There is Carl, the waiter, and Sacha, the bartender; Abdul, the bouncer; Émile, who runs the casino; and Rick's sidekick, Sam, the musician. And there is also Berger, the old-fashioned Norwegian shadow fighter, waiting to come under Laszlo's command. This bar is also the perfect hiding place for the letters of transit, which remain under Sam's piano lid.

In a world of contradictions, this warm house is the home of fashion and trends, embodied by King Rick, impeccably dressed in his white dinner jacket, always courteous and witty, even under the threat of the Nazis. But it is also a world that lives at night, and its king has his dark sides. He calls the two murdered messengers the "honorable dead." This king is Hades.

By creating a closed world that is both dystopia and utopia, the screenwriters of Casablanca have shaped a Möbius strip universe that never ends. Rick's Café Américain will always be open every night. People will continue to seek refuge there; the captain will continue to gamble and take advantage of women; the Germans will continue to make arrogant intrusions. This café is one of those timeless places that make great stories, and it continues to exist because it is a reassuring haven where everyone appreciates the role they play.

Far from being limited to being the place where everyone seeks to obtain an exit visa, Rick's bar, in distant Casablanca, represents the ideal community that no spectator wishes to leave.

The Terrifying House

Opposite the warm house, the terrifying house is generally a house that has gone from being a cocoon to a prison. In stories that have used it well, if the house is terrifying, it is because it is a manifestation of the character's weakness and need. This house is a materialization of the hero's greatest fear. In the most extreme cases, the character's mind is so corrupted that the house is also in ruins. But it remains a prison.

In Great Expectations, Miss Havisham is a slave to her own dilapidated villa because she has chosen to martyr herself on the altar of her unrequited love. Her mind has been corrupted by bitterness. And her house is a perfect image of her mind. In Wuthering Heights, the house is a horrible prison because it is where Cathy abandoned true love and because Heathcliff's bitterness drove him to commit horrible acts against its inhabitants in Cathy's name.

Horror stories place so much importance on the haunted house that it has become the quintessential setting for this form. Structurally, the terrifying or haunted house represents the power of the past over the present. The house itself becomes a weapon seeking to avenge the sins committed by ancestors. In such stories, the house is not necessarily a decrepit, creaky mansion with slamming doors, moving walls, and dark secret passages. It can be the simple suburban houses of Poltergeist or A Nightmare on Elm Street, or the grand mountain hotel of The Shining. At the top of this mountain, the isolation and the hotel's past sins do not lead the hero to higher thoughts; they drive him insane.

When the terrifying house is a large Gothic building, it is often inhabited by a family of aristocrats. These men live off the work of others, who often reside below, in the valley, simply because they are not "well-born." The house is either too empty for its size, meaning there is no life in its structure, or filled with expensive but outdated furniture that oppresses due to its number. In the end, the family is annihilated, and if the story is taken to the extreme, the house burns, devours its inhabitants, or collapses on them. Examples of this type can be found in The Fall of the House of Usher and other Poe stories, Rebecca, Jane Eyre, Dracula, The Innocents, The Amityville Horror, Sunset Boulevard, Frankenstein, Long Day's Journey into Night, and some stories by Chekhov and Strindberg.

In more modern stories, the terrifying house is a prison because it is neither large nor diverse. It is small and cramped; its walls are thin or completely absent. The family is crammed inside; therefore, there is no community, no isolated and comfortable corners where each person would have enough space to become who they truly are. In these houses, the family, the basic unit of drama, is a source of constant conflict. The terrifying house is like a pressure cooker; its inhabitants cannot escape, and it eventually explodes. Examples include Death of a Salesman, American Beauty, A Streetcar Named Desire, Who's Afraid of Virginia Woolf?, Long Day's Journey into Night, The Glass Menagerie, Carrie, Psycho, and The Sixth Sense.

The Cellar vs. the Attic

Within the house, the main opposition is that of the cellar and the attic. The cellar is a basement, a hell. It is the tomb of the house, the place where corpses, dark memories of the past, and terrible family secrets are buried. But they do not stay buried for long. They are just waiting to return, and when they succeed in making their return, in the living room or the bedroom, they usually destroy the family. These cellar ghosts can be horrible, as in Psycho, or amusing, as in Arsenic and Old Lace.

The cellar is also the place where plots are hatched. Plots come from the darkest part of the house and the darkest part of the mind. The cellar is thus the natural workplace of criminals and revolutionaries. This technique is used in Notes from Underground, The Lavender Hill Mob, The Silence of the Lambs, and M.

The attic is sloped and cramped, but it is at the top of the structure, where the house meets the sky. When inhabited, the attic is the place where great thoughts and great works of art, still unknown to the world, are created (Moulin Rouge). The attic also has the advantage of providing height and perspective. Its inhabitants can look out their tiny window and observe the Bruegel-like community scenes unfolding below in the street.

The attic, like the cellar, is also a place where things are hidden. As the attic is the "head" of the house, these hidden things, when terrifying, relate to madness (Jane Eyre, The Haunting). But more often, they are positive things, like treasures or memories. A character discovers an old chest in the attic that opens a window to their past or that of their ancestors.

The Path

Among the different man-made places, the opposite of the house is the path. The house encourages us to snuggle within it, to live a moment out of time, to feel comfortable, to feel at home. The path encourages us to go out, to explore, to change. The house implies a simultaneous story, everything happening at the same time. The path implies a linear story, each thing happening along a guiding line of development.

George Sand wrote: "What is more beautiful than a path? It is the symbol and image of an active and varied life." The path is always thin. It is a fine line, the simplest and most stripped-down mark of man, surrounded by the wild, cruel, and impersonal world. The path therefore requires courage. But it offers an almost infinite diversity of perspectives regarding the traveler's future. The path, whatever its width, carries within it the promise of a destination worth reaching.

Myths are centered on this fundamental opposition between the house and the path. The classic myth begins at home. The hero embarks on a journey, encounters many adversaries who test him, and returns home after learning what he already was deep inside. The myth tends to show that the house is not well used. The hero has not created his unique self in this safe place or has felt oppressed there. The path has forced him to test his skills. But the hero of the myth does not become a new man on the path. He must return home to become aware of what he has always been, but in a deeper way.

TECHNIQUE: THE VEHICLE

If travel stories often seem fragmented, it is either because they take place in too many different arenas or because the hero encounters a succession of adversaries along the way. This is why the vehicle in which the hero travels is one of the keys to the functioning of this type of story. The rule is simple: the larger the vehicle, the more unified the arena will appear. And the larger the vehicle, the easier it will be to bring in adversaries. They will then be recurring adversaries who, with the hero, will create a unified arena within the vehicle itself.

Among travel stories that use large vehicles, there are Titanic and Ship of Fools (ship), Murder on the Orient Express and Night Train to Munich (train), and Almost Famous (bus).

The City

The largest of man-made microcosms is the city. It is so large that it breaks the boundaries of the microcosm and becomes sprawling. The city consists of hundreds of buildings, millions of people. And yet, it is a unique life experience that must somehow be translated into narrative terms.

To codify the vast field covered by the city, authors often reduce the city to a smaller microcosm. The most popular of these is undoubtedly the institution. An institution is an organization with a unique function, boundaries, set of rules, hierarchy of powers, and mode of operation. The metaphor of the institution transforms the city into a highly organized military operation where a large number of people are defined and related to each other solely based on the role they play in the whole.

Very often, authors who describe the city as an institution create a large, unique building with many floors and rooms, including a vast hall with hundreds of perfectly aligned desks. The city as an institution can be found in The Hospital, American Beauty, Network, Double Indemnity, The Incredibles, and The Matrix.

TECHNIQUE: COMBINING CITY AND NATURAL SETTINGS

To find a metaphor for the city, fantasy uses an approach that is the opposite of that of the institution. Instead of enclosing the city in a highly controlled organization, fantasy opens the city by imagining it as a kind of natural setting like a mountain or a jungle. The advantage of this technique is that it makes the sprawling city a unit with distinctive signs that the audience can recognize. But – more importantly – it also allows for the maximum exploitation of the city's immense potential – positive or negative.

The Mountain City

The mountain peak is a frequently used natural metaphor to represent the city, especially very vertical cities like New York. The tallest towers and highest mountains are the residence of the wealthiest and most powerful people. Middle-class people live in medium-sized towers while the poor crawl in low-ceilinged slums at the foot of the mountain. Highly stylized crime fantasies, such as "Batman," often use the mountain metaphor.

The Ocean City

To represent the city, the ocean is an even more powerful natural metaphor than the classic but predictable mountain. With this metaphor, the author usually starts with the rooftops, which are depicted so that the audience feels like they are floating on waves. Then the story "dives" below the surface to follow several threads, or characters, who live on different levels of this three-dimensional world and are generally unaware of the presence of other people "swimming" in this sea. Films as different as Under the Roofs of Paris, Wings of Desire, and The Yellow Submarine take great advantage of this ocean metaphor.

The ocean is also the ultimate metaphor when depicting the city in its most positive aspect, as a playground where different individuals can live in freedom, originality, and love. In fantasy, the main way to achieve this goal is to literally make the city dwellers float. This gives them the power to fly. But it should also be noted that when characters float, ceilings become floors, nothing is closed, and people can experience the ultimate freedom that comes from imagination. Floating is a metaphor that represents the hidden potential within the triviality of the city; when one looks at the predictable world with a new perspective, suddenly, everything becomes possible.

In realistic films that treat the city as an ocean, the floating effect is created by the camera's eye. At the beginning of Under the Roofs of Paris, for example, the camera glides over the rooftops and then dives below the "ocean" surface, entering through an open window while "swimming." After observing a few characters for a moment, it exits, still "swimming," through a window, before slipping into another to capture a new group of characters. All of this is part of the story structure, created by the screenwriter and designed to evoke a sense of vast community within the immense ocean of the city.

MARY POPPINS

(books by P. L. Travers, screenplay by Bill Walsh and Don Da Gradi, 1964)

Mary Poppins is a story based on the ocean city metaphor. Mary floats down from the sky to begin her stay with the Banks. The neighbor, a sea captain, observes her from his roof (the deck of his "ship") with his first officer. Mary teaches the children that one can float when they love spending their days laughing. And Bert and the chimney sweeps dance on the rooftops, which they call the "sea of enchantment." Full of energy, they waddle on the waves (the gables) and defy gravity until the captain fires a cannonball. The chimney sweep then disappears below the ocean surface and waits for it to be time to dance again.

The Jungle City
The jungle city is the opposite of the ocean city. There, the three-dimensional aspect of the city is not liberating; it is a source of death—enemies are hidden everywhere, and a deadly attack can come from anywhere at any moment. Cities of this type are generally dense and humid, their inhabitants depicted as animals differing only in their ways of killing. This metaphor has been used in many detective stories, to the point that it has become a cliché. Among the stories that use the jungle city metaphor more originally are Pépé le Moko (the Casbah of Algiers), Spider-Man (New York), Batman Begins (Gotham), The Jungle (Chicago), Blade Runner (Los Angeles), M (Berlin), and King Kong (New York).

The forest city

The forest city is a positive version of the jungle city. With this technique, buildings are miniature versions of the city, more human; it's as if people lived in trees. The city resembles a neighborhood or village flanked by impersonal towers. The city depicted as a forest generally goes hand in hand with a utopian vision of the world, where characters enjoy the benefits of bustling urban life while also experiencing the comfort and coziness of home among the trees. This technique is used in films such as You Can't Take It with You and Ghostbusters.

GHOSTBUSTERS

(Dan Aykroyd and Harold Ramis, 1984)

Ghostbusters is a teenage adventure film set in New York. At the beginning of the story, the three "musketeers" are professors at a warm university that evokes a city. They study paranormal phenomena, which allows them to conduct all sorts of wacky experiments with pretty girls. They then create a business that lets them earn large sums of money by wearing funny uniforms, driving a customized ambulance, using super gadgets, and living in a firehouse. For these boys, the firehouse is the ultimate clubhouse. They sleep together in a dormitory, where they dream of sexy girls, and when called for a job, they slide down the "tree trunk," or pole, and embark on wild adventures.

Miniatures

A miniature is a shrunken society. The miniature is chaos theory applied to storytelling; it presents the audience with different levels of order. The order of the universe, which is too difficult to grasp because we cannot see the world as a whole, is clarified when the universe is shrunk.

In stories, all man-made spaces are forms of miniatures. The only difference is the scale. The miniature is one of the fundamental techniques of the storytelling universe because it is an excellent condenser-expander. By its very nature, it does not present things one after the other, successively. It shows many things at once in all the complexity of their relationships.

The miniature can have three different functions in a story:

1. It allows the audience to see the story universe as a whole.

2. It allows the author to express different aspects, or facets, of the same character.

3. It allows for the display of power, and often tyranny.

The classic documentary by Ray and Charles Eames, Powers of Ten, illustrates well how the miniature works in storytelling. From a distance of one yard, we observe a couple picnicking on a lawn. A second later, we see the same couple from ten yards, then a hundred yards, a thousand, ten thousand, etc. The perspective increases to the power of ten until we can only observe space from an inconceivable height. The perspective quickly tightens on the couple, then reverses the course of the power of ten by diving deeper and deeper into the microcosmic world of cells, molecules, and atoms. Each perspective presents a complete sub-world, an order of things that explains, in a condensed way, how the world works.

In storytelling, the miniature fulfills the same function. But it does not merely factually show how the different pieces of the story universe fit together. It shows what matters. "In the miniature, values condense and enrich."

CITIZEN KANE

(Herman J. Mankiewicz and Orson Welles, 1941)

Citizen Kane is a story built on the miniature. In the opening sequence, Kane, on his deathbed, drops a glass paperweight representing a cabin in the snow that shatters on the floor. It is a miniature of Kane's childhood, his lost childhood. Then comes a newsreel about Kane, which is a miniature of his life, told from a distant and pseudo-historical perspective. The newsreel presents Kane's estate, Xanadu, which is a miniature of the entire world recreated behind walls to satisfy Kane's personal pleasure and need for domination. Each miniature presents the audience with a very important image of this rich, lonely, and sometimes tyrannical man. But at the same time, these multiple miniatures evoke one of the story's themes: you can never truly know someone, no matter how many perspectives and narrators you use.

THE SHINING

(novel by Stephen King, screenplay by Stanley Kubrick and Diane Johnson, 1980)

In The Shining, Jack Torrance, procrastinating on his writing work, sees in miniature the vast labyrinthine garden behind the hotel. Leaning in for a better look, taking the "God's perspective," he discerns the tiny figures of his wife and son navigating it. The miniature here is a foreshadowing (a kind of temporal miniature) that announces the moment when he will attempt to murder his son in the maze at the end of the story.

From large to small / from small to large

Changing the size of characters is an excellent way to draw attention to the relationship these characters have with the story universe. It creates a true reversal in the minds of readers or viewers, who are forced to look at the characters and the world in a radically new way. The audience is suddenly compelled to question the underlying principles of what they considered given: the very foundations of the world are now completely different.

If fantasy exists, it is partly because this genre allows us to look at things as if we were seeing them for the first time. More than any other narrative technique, shrinking the character is an excellent way to achieve this effect. When a character shrinks, they regress and become a child again. On the negative side, they feel a sudden loss of power and may even be terrified by their own surroundings, now massive and dominating. On the positive side, the character, like the audience, is amazed to look at the world with fresh eyes. "The man with the magnifying glass [...] is childhood rediscovered. It gives the botanist the child's magnifying gaze. [...] Thus the tiny, narrow gate if there ever was one, opens a world."

At the moment of reversal, the underlying principles of the world suddenly escape the audience. And yet, this world remains intensely real. Suddenly, the everyday becomes sublime. In Honey, I Shrunk the Kids, the lawn becomes a terrifying jungle. In Fantastic Voyage, the human body becomes a magnificent but monstrous space. In Alice in Wonderland, the heroine's tears become an ocean in which she nearly drowns. For King Kong, the subway is a giant snake, and the Empire State Building the tallest of all trees.

When a character shrinks, they immediately acquire a heroic character. Jack climbs the beanstalk to fight a giant, and it is his brain, not his muscles, that he must use to win the fight. Ulysses does nothing else when he defeats the Cyclops by clinging to the bellies of his sheep and telling him that the one who blinded him is named Nobody.

Other examples of stories with tiny characters or shrunken characters include Gulliver's Travels, Stuart Little, Thumbelina, The Borrowers, Tom Thumb, The Mouse and the Motorcycle, and The Incredible Shrinking Man.

Enlargement is always less interesting than shrinking because it tends to limit the possibilities for subtlety and intrigue. The monstrously large character becomes the proverbial elephant in a china shop. Everything is pure physical domination. That's why in Wonderland, Alice is only giant at the beginning of the story, when her body fills the house to the point of overflowing. The wonderful aspect of Wonderland would quickly be trampled if Alice were as large as the fifty-foot woman. This is also why the best part of Gulliver's travels to Lilliput is the first, where Gulliver is still the slave of the six-inch-tall Lilliputians. When Gulliver, as a giant, stands above the factions, he defends the abstract thesis that all conflict between nations is absurd. But in essence, the story has already stopped. Nothing can happen without Gulliver's permission.

Wonderful fantasy, Big is an apparent exception to the rule that enlargement is less interesting than shrinking. But Big is not the story of a man who becomes a giant among small people. Big is another example of a man becoming small, or more precisely, a child in a man's body. All the charm of the story comes from the character played by Tom Hanks, who has the appearance of an adult but retains the personality, spirit, and enthusiasm of a little boy.

Passages between worlds

If your story's arena includes at least two sub-worlds, you have the opportunity to use an excellent technique, the passage between worlds. Passages generally appear when two sub-worlds are extremely different. This technique is most often used in fantasy when a character must move from the everyday world to the fantastic world. Classic examples of passages include the rabbit hole, the keyhole, and the mirror (Alice in Wonderland, Through the Looking-Glass), the cyclone (The Wizard of Oz), the wardrobe (The Chronicles of Narnia: The Lion, the Witch, and the Wardrobe), the painting and the chimney (Mary Poppins), the computer screen (Tron), and the television set (Pleasantville, Poltergeist).

The passage fulfills two main functions in the story. First, it literally sends your character from one place to another. Then, and more importantly, it acts as a kind of decompression chamber that allows the audience to transition from realism to fantasy. It explains to the audience that the rules of the story universe are about to change radically. It's a bit like saying, "Relax. Don't try to apply your normal conception of reality to what you're about to see." And this is essential in the highly symbolic and allegorical form that is fantasy, as its underlying themes are based on the importance of looking at life with fresh eyes and finding new possibilities in the most ordinary things.

Ideally, the character should move slowly through the passage. A passage is a universe in its own right. Your passage must therefore be filled with things and characters that are both foreign and inherent to your story. Let your character linger there. Your audience will thank you. The passage from one universe to another is one of the most popular narrative techniques. If you manage to create a unique passage, you will have already accomplished half the journey to success.

Technology (tools)

Tools are extensions of the human form that multiply what we are capable of. They are a fundamental way for characters to connect with the world. Every tool is a part of the identity of the character who uses it and reveals not only how their personal power has been magnified but also their abilities to manipulate the world and maneuver within it.

Technology is very useful in genres that emphasize the story universe the most, such as science fiction and fantasy, and in some highly ambitious stories that place the hero within a vast social system. As for science fiction, since you, the author, create the world, the specific technology you invent must highlight the elements of the human condition that concern you the most. And since all great science fiction stories deal with the author's vision of universal evolution, the relationship between humans and technology is always crucial. In fantasy, a tool such as a magic wand is a symbol of the character's self-mastery and an indication of the good or bad use they make of their knowledge.

In stories where characters are trapped by a system, tools symbolize how that system exerts its power. This is particularly true in stories of modernization, where an entire society moves to a more complex and advanced stage from a technological standpoint. The Magnificent Ambersons, for example, presents the consequences of the automobile breakthrough. In Cinema Paradiso, the cinema is demolished to make way for a parking lot. In the classic anti-western The Wild Bunch, set in the last days of the American frontier, aging cowboys discover the first automobiles and machine guns. In Butch Cassidy and the Sundance Kid, another great anti-western, there is an extraordinary scene where an imaginative bicycle salesman makes a series of sales pitches to people hesitant to join his group.

But tools can also be useful in story forms that do not seek to explore the world as a whole. Action stories, for example, always emphasize the hero's ability to transform everyday objects into weapons or use them to defeat their enemies. In drama, tools are everyday objects so common that they are almost invisible. And yet, technology (or sometimes its absence) also helps define the character and their place in the world. In Death of a Salesman, Willy Loman brings home a $70 commission but owes $16, which he leaves on the refrigerator. His son Happy gives him $50 at Christmas, but he needs $97 to repair the water heater, and he has postponed the car's maintenance. Willy is always "bogged down in the machine."

CONNECTING THE UNIVERSE TO THE HERO'S GENERAL DEVELOPMENT

The first step in building your story's world is to identify the key visual oppositions based on the characters and their values. The second step is to examine the endpoints of your hero's development.

This process is similar to the one we used for character creation. We start by sketching a network of characters because each character, through contrast and similarity, helps define the others. Then we focus on the hero. We examine all their possibilities for transformation, starting with the endpoint (the realization) and then going back to the beginning (weakness and need, desire), before filling in all the structural steps between these two points. We proceed this way because every story is a learning journey undertaken by the hero, and as an author, you must know the end of this journey before taking the first step.

To detail the story universe, simply reproduce the same process. We have already discussed some of the most important visual oppositions of the story universe when we studied the character network. We must now focus on the hero's general transformation to define what the world will look like at the beginning and end of the story.

In the vast majority of stories, the hero's general transformation corresponds to a passage from enslavement to freedom. If this is the case in your story, the visual world will probably also need to move from enslavement to freedom. Here's how the hero's and the universe's general evolutions correspond to each other:

If a character is enslaved, it is primarily due to a psychological and moral weakness. If a universe is enslaving (or liberating), it is because of the links between its three major elements—territory (natural setting), populations (man-made spaces), and technology (tools)—and their impact on the hero. It is the unique way you combine these elements that will define the nature of the universe you create.

– Beginning (enslavement): When the relationship between territory, populations, and tools is unbalanced, populations think only of their own survival, reduced to animals fighting for scarce resources or cogs entirely devoted to the smooth functioning of the great machine. It is a world of enslavement, even a dystopia, or hell on earth.

– End (freedom): When the relationship between territory, populations, and tools finds a balance (which you must define), there is a community where individuals can become who they are while being supported by others. It is a world of freedom, even a utopia, or paradise on earth.

In addition to enslavement and dystopia, freedom and utopia, there is another type of world that can be created for the beginning or end of a story: the apparent utopia. This universe looks perfect, but the perfection is just a facade. Beneath the surface, it is actually corrupt, rotten, and enslaving. Everyone tries to put on a good face to hide a moral or psychological disaster. This technique is used at the beginning of L.A. Confidential and Blue Velvet.

The important thing when creating these different types of universes is to connect them to your hero. In the vast majority of stories, there is a direct relationship between the hero and their world. An enslaved hero, for example, lives in a world of enslavement. And when they free themselves, they often create a world of freedom.

KEY POINT: In most stories, the world is a physical expression of your hero's personality and how it develops.

With this technique, the world helps you define your main character through the story's structure. It reveals the hero's needs, values, desires (both good and bad), and the obstacles they must face. And since in the vast majority of stories, the hero is initially, in some way, enslaved, you must focus on enslavement.

KEY POINT: Always ask yourself how the world of enslavement is an expression of your hero's major weakness. This world must embody, highlight, or accentuate your hero's weakness, or even represent it in its worst possible form.

Detective stories and thrillers, for example, often create a close connection between the hero's weakness—when it exists—and the city's underworld, the world of enslavement where the hero operates.

VERTIGO

(novel by Pierre Boileau and Thomas Narcejac, screenplay by Alec Coppel and Samuel Taylor, 1958)

The world of Vertigo highlights the hero's psychological weakness in the opening scene. While pursuing a criminal on the rooftops of San Francisco, Scottie slips and hangs by his fingertips five stories above the ground. He looks down and is seized by vertigo. His partner falls and dies trying to save him, causing a guilt in Scottie that will haunt him for the rest of the story. The storytelling technique that highlights the hero's weakness repeats a little later when vertigo prevents Scottie from climbing a tower to stop the woman he loves from committing suicide. This technique is indeed the main strength of Vertigo's story: the murderer uses the detective's own weakness—vertigo—to get away with impunity.

Creating a world of enslavement to express or accentuate the hero's weakness is also useful for drama or melodrama.

SUNSET BOULEVARD

(Charles Brackett, Billy Wilder, and D. M. Marshman Jr, 1950)

In Sunset Boulevard, the hero's weakness is his penchant for money and the good things in life. And of course, he quickly finds himself in a dilapidated mansion with an aging movie star, ready to spend money on him on the sole condition that he fulfills all her desires. Like vampires, the star and her mansion feed on the hero and regain their youth while he sinks into a gilded enslavement.

A STREETCAR NAMED DESIRE

(Tennessee Williams, 1947)

A Streetcar Named Desire offers a perfect example of how the world of enslavement can, at the beginning of a story, express the hero's major weakness. Blanche is a fragile and delusional woman seeking refuge in an imaginary world, a world of romance and pretty things. But she finds herself trapped in a cramped, stiflingly hot apartment with her sister and her brutal brother-in-law. Instead of giving her the illusion of romance, this hell, and its simian king, Stanley, will press on her until she cracks and collapses.

CASABLANCA

(play, Everybody Comes to Rick’s, by Murray Burnett and Joan Alison, screenplay by Julius J. Epstein, Philip G. Epstein, and Howard Koch, 1942)

Casablanca is a love story whose initial world, a world of enslavement, constantly highlights Rick's weakness. Every corner of his fabulous bar, the "Café Américain," reminds him of the love he lost in the romantic city of Paris. This club is also a place where business is conducted, possible only because Rick bribes a corrupt French police captain. All the magnificent objects in this bar tend to remind Rick that he has fallen deep into cynicism and self-centeredness while the world desperately cries out for leaders.

Fantasy is another story form where the correspondence between the world of enslavement and the hero's weakness plays an important role. At the beginning of all good fantasies, the hero is situated in a trivial world where their psychological and moral weakness originates. It is because of this weakness that the hero is unable to see the true potential of the place where they live, nor their own, and it is what drives them to explore the fantastic world.

FIELD OF DREAMS

(novel, Shoeless Joe, by W. P. Kinsella, screenplay by Phil Alden Robinson, 1989)
INTO THE DREAM

In "Field of Dreams," the hero, Ray, lives on a farm in Iowa near a town that wants to ban gambling. He builds a baseball field on his property, despite other farmers thinking he's crazy and his brother-in-law wanting to capitalize on the farm's practical and monetary value. Ray's need is to do something he's passionate about and to accept his father's illness. The construction of this baseball field—which will bring the ghost of baseball star Shoeless Joe Jackson—creates a utopian world that allows Ray to experience one last great moment of communion with his father.

MARY POPPINS

(books by P. L. Travers, screenplay by Bill Walsh and Don Da Gradi, 1964)

In "Mary Poppins," the home is a restrictive place governed by a father very attached to rules—his god is a clock. The apparent main character, Mary Poppins, is what we call a traveling angel, "practically perfect in every way." Therefore, she has no weaknesses. She is actually the agent who reveals to others their true potential, as well as the negative potential of their world of servitude. The children are rebellious in a self-destructive way and have no idea of the wonderful world of enchantment just beyond their door in London, as well as in their own minds.

The father, who is the main adversary, has an even greater weakness than his children. He sees the world as a workplace, and although he doesn't enter the worlds of fantasy, he benefits from his children's stays there and the presence of the magical nanny. In the end, the father's workplace world becomes a place where he can fly a kite with his children.

Among other traveling angel comedies that present a similar link between the hero and the world of servitude are "Crocodile Dundee"; "The Music Man"; "Amélie"; "Chocolat"; "Good Morning, Vietnam" and "Stop the World, I Want to Get Off."

The Parallel Development of the Story's Universe and the Hero

You'll notice that so far, all the major elements of the story—the premise, the guiding principle, the seven steps, the characters, and the moral debate—correspond and maintain links with each other, creating an organic unity with a very rich texture where everything works together. This is the orchestration that is so essential to the success of storytelling.

At the beginning of the story, all these elements are woven together and express the same thing. The hero lives (usually) in a world of servitude that highlights, amplifies, or exacerbates his major weakness. He then faces the adversary most capable of exploiting this weakness. In Chapter 8, you'll see that another element of the beginning, the "specter," can also express the hero's weakness.

The connection between the hero and his universe begins with the hero's servitude and continues throughout the character's arc. In most stories, as the hero is an expression of the world and the world an expression of the hero, they both develop together. And if the hero doesn't change, as in most of Chekhov's works, the world doesn't change either.

Let's now study some classic examples of transformations, contrasts, or non-transformations of the hero and his world throughout the story.

Hero: From servitude to freedom through even worse servitude

World: From servitude to freedom through even worse servitude

The hero begins the story in a world of servitude. He fights to achieve his goal and falls throughout the story's progression. Then, through awareness, he fulfills his need and becomes free in a world that also liberates thanks to his actions.

This pattern is found in episodes 4 to 6 of "Star Wars," in "The Lord of the Rings," "The Verdict," "The Lion King," "The Shawshank Redemption," "Life is Beautiful," and "David Copperfield."

Hero: From servitude to death or even worse servitude

World: From servitude to death or even worse servitude

At the beginning of stories of this type, the main character is enslaved by his own weakness or by a world that oppresses him. Because of the cancer that gnaws at the hero's soul, the world that depends on him is corrupted. As he seeks to achieve the goal, the hero has a negative awareness that destroys him and the world that relied on him. Or he is crushed by a world of servitude that he is unable to understand.

Among stories of this type are "Oedipus Rex," "Death of a Salesman," "A Streetcar Named Desire," "The Conversation," "The Conformist," "Sunset Boulevard," "Three Sisters," "The Cherry Orchard," and "Heart of Darkness."

Hero: From servitude to death or even worse servitude

World: From servitude to freedom through even worse servitude

With this approach, used in some tragedies, the connection between the hero and the world is broken at the end of the story. The hero has an awareness, but it comes too late to free him. However, before dying or falling, he makes a sacrifice that frees the world after his departure.

This pattern is found in "Hamlet," "The Seven Samurai," and "A Tale of Two Cities."

Hero: From servitude to death or even worse servitude through temporary freedom

World: From servitude to death or even worse servitude through temporary freedom

This technique involves bringing the hero into a sub-world of freedom towards the middle of the story. It is the world in which the character should live if he realized the true potential of his self. But because he fails to do so or because he discovers the rightness of his world too late, the hero ends up destroyed.

This sequence is reproduced in "The Wild Bunch," "The Treasure of the Sierra Madre," "Butch Cassidy and the Sundance Kid," and "Dances with Wolves."

Hero: From freedom to servitude or death

World: From freedom to servitude or death

Stories of this type begin in a utopian world where the main character is happy but vulnerable to attack or change. The hero's flaw, or the arrival of a new character, which alters the balance of social forces, causes the decline of the main character and his world until the final fall.

This pattern is found in "King Lear," "How Green Was My Valley," and the Arthurian stories such as "Le Morte d'Arthur" and "Excalibur."

Hero: From freedom to freedom through servitude

World: From freedom to freedom through servitude

Again, the hero begins the story in a world of freedom. An attack then occurs from outside or within the family. The hero and his world decline, but the hero eventually overcomes the problem and creates an even stronger utopia.

This approach is used in "Meet Me in St. Louis," "Amarcord," and, to a lesser extent, "Cinema Paradiso."

Hero: From apparent freedom to true freedom through total servitude

World: From apparent freedom to true freedom through total servitude

At the beginning of stories of this type, the world seems to be a true utopia. But it is actually an extremely hierarchical and corrupt place. The characters fight cruelly to win, and often many of them die. The hero eventually overcomes the corruption and creates a fairer society. Or he is simply the last survivor.

"L.A. Confidential," "Jurassic Park," "The Magnificent Ambersons," and "Blue Velvet" are good examples of this type of story.

A brilliant variant of this pattern is found in "Goodfellas," a story that combines two genres: gangster film and black comedy. The script moves from the apparent freedom of the gangster community to total servitude for the hero and death for all his friends.

THE TIME OF THE STORY'S UNIVERSE

Now that the story's universe is connected to the hero, we must study how this world itself can develop. Time is the fourth major element—after natural settings, man-made spaces, and technology—that you must use to build the world of your story.

Before studying the many ways to express time through the world—or more precisely to express the world through time—we must discuss two common mistakes made by authors regarding time.

Mistakes of the Past and the Future

The mistake that can be called the "mistake of the past" is quite common in historical fiction. The idea is that a historical fiction author depicts a different world, which has its own set of values and moral codes, and therefore, we should not judge the characters of these works by our standards.

The mistake of the past comes from the erroneous idea that a historical fiction author is first and foremost a historian. However, a storyteller always writes fiction. He uses the past as a pair of glasses through which the audience can see themselves more clearly today. It is therefore perfectly absurd not to judge characters from the past; they are shown to us only to better allow us to judge ourselves by comparing ourselves to them.

This comparison works in two ways. Negatively, it presents the dominant values of the past that continue to hurt people today. This is the case with Puritan values in Nathaniel Hawthorne's "The Scarlet Letter" and Arthur Miller's "The Crucible." Positively, the author can present past values that are still valid and to which he believes we should return. "She Wore a Yellow Ribbon," for example, glorifies duty, honor, and loyalty, values that reigned in a military outpost in 1870s America.

The mistake that can be called the "mistake of the future" is quite common in science fiction stories. Many authors think of science fiction as a prediction, an idea of what will happen in the future, what the world will really look like. This is what we saw at the end of 1983 when everyone debated the points on which George Orwell was right or wrong about 1984.

The mistake is to think that stories set in the future are about the future. They are not. When a story is set in the future, it gives its audience another pair of glasses that allows them to abstract from the present to better understand it. One of the major differences between science fiction stories and historical fiction is that stories set in the future are less about values than about the forces and choices we face today and the consequences of those choices if they are ill-advised.

The true time of a story is "natural" time. This time is linked to how the world develops and in turn favors the development of the story. Among the best techniques of natural time are the seasons, holidays, the single day, and the countdown.

The Seasons

The first technique of natural time in the story is the cycle of the seasons and the rituals that go with it. To apply this technique, you must set the story, or a particular moment in the story, in a season. The seasons, like natural settings, carry a meaning that the audience understands and that says something about the hero and the world.

When you push things further and present the change of seasons, you offer the audience a strong and detailed expression of the hero's or the world's flourishing or decline.

When you cover all four seasons, you tell the audience that you are moving from a linear story, which deals with a certain type of development, to a circular story, which deals with how things ultimately remain the same. You can present this positively or negatively. Positive circular stories generally rely on the links that unite man to the earth. Humans are animals and are happy to be so. The cycle of life, death, and rebirth is natural and worthy of celebration, and much can be learned by studying the secrets that nature reveals at its slow and steady pace. This is how Thoreau uses the seasons in "Walden."

Negative circular stories generally emphasize man's servitude—as an animal like any other—to the forces of nature. It is quite risky to use this method because it can quickly become tiresome. The weakness of many nature documentaries comes from the plot, which almost always follows the cycle of the seasons, becoming predictable and boring. An animal may give birth in spring, hunt and be hunted in summer, mate in autumn, and face food shortages in winter. But one thing is certain, this animal will give birth again in spring.

To connect the seasons to the narrative line, the classic method—executed with great finesse in "Meet Me in St. Louis" and "Amarcord"—is to establish a direct link between each of the seasons and the drama by following this pattern:

• Summer: Characters coexist in a state of vulnerability and turmoil or in a world of freedom that is susceptible to attack.

• Autumn: Characters begin their decline.

• Winter: Characters are at their lowest.

• Spring: Characters overcome their problem and rise again.

You can use these classic connections or, to avoid clichés, deliberately seek to deviate from them. A character can, for example, decline in spring and rise in winter. By altering this logical sequence, you will short-circuit the audience's expectations and tend to assert that humans, while part of nature, are not enslaved to its rules.

Holidays and Rituals

Holidays, and the rituals that mark them, can also allow you to give meaning to your story, pace it, and present its development. A ritual is a philosophy translated into a set of actions that repeats at defined intervals. Thus, any ritual is already a dramatic element in itself, comprising strong visual elements, that you can insert into your drama. Holidays expand the scope of the ritual to the nation, allowing you to express the political as well as personal and social significance of the ritual.

If you wish to use a ritual or holiday in your story, you must first examine the philosophy inherent in this ritual and determine whether you agree with it or not. You can, in your narrative, decide to support or reject this philosophy, in part or in whole.

A CHRISTMAS STORY
