(screenplay by Jean Shepherd, Leigh Brown, and Bob Clark, 1983)

THE GREAT AMERICAN FOURTH OF JULY AND OTHER DISASTERS

(novel, "In God We Trust, All Others Pay Cash," by Jean Shepherd, screenplay by Jean Shepherd, 1982)

Humorist Jean Shepherd is an expert at constructing stories around a holiday. He begins by combining a holiday with a narrator who remembers his family, allowing him to present the audience with a utopia of childhood through which each viewer can nestle in the real or imagined memory of a happy life within the family. The notion of a holiday creates a temporal passage that projects the viewer into his childhood. Shepherd achieves this effect through a voice-over narrator who recounts the funny things that happened each year during the holiday. His little brother, for example, who always wore a ski suit too big for him. Or his father who, every year, bought a gift that made his mother furious. There was always negotiation with the neighborhood bullies. And the time Flick got his tongue stuck on the flagpole?

Shepherd supports the philosophy of the holiday, not directly or from a religious point of view, but by pretending to mock it, laughing at the silly things people did at that time each year. But these silly things are also a source of well-being for him, especially because they happened every year and because the people, in his memory, will never grow up. That is the power of the perennial story.

When using this technique, it is important to understand the relationship between the ritual, the holiday, and the season in which this holiday occurs. Then you must orchestrate all these elements to express a transformation within the hero or the world.

HANNAH AND HER SISTERS

(Woody Allen, 1986)

"Hannah and Her Sisters" illustrates well how a holiday can be connected to a story and present a character transformation. In this film, the holiday in question is Thanksgiving. The celebration of this exclusively American holiday dates back to colonial times. Thanksgiving represents the beginnings of a nation and the formation of a community giving thanks for the fruitful harvest and the beginnings of America. But Woody Allen does not use Thanksgiving to structure the story and provide the underlying theme in the usual way. Instead of focusing on the philosophy of this holiday, Allen creates a narrative of simultaneous actions that intertwines the story of three sisters and their respective husbands or boyfriends. At the beginning of the story, there is no community, neither among the characters nor in the narrative structure. Allen creates a community through the structure by tying the three love stories together and using the Thanksgiving holiday three times.

The structure is as follows: the story begins with a Thanksgiving dinner attended by all the characters, accompanied by bad partners. Then the narrative fractures into a series of alternating montages centered on the six individuals. In the middle of the story, all the characters gather again for Thanksgiving; this time, most of them are with new partners... but they are still not the right ones. The narrative fractures again into many simultaneous threads, with the different characters engaging in their respective activities. The story ends with all the characters reunited for the third time around a Thanksgiving dinner, but this time it is a true community, each now with the right partner. The narrative and the holiday become one. These characters do not talk about Thanksgiving; they live it.

The Single Day

The single day is another temporal technique that has very specific consequences when used in a story. The first of these consequences is to create simultaneous movement while maintaining narrative dynamism. Instead of showing a single character throughout his development—the linear approach adopted by most authors—several characters are presented acting simultaneously, here and now, on this day. But the passage of hours forces the story to move forward and gives it a sense of compression.

If the story is set over twelve hours, that is, over a day or a night, a funnel effect is created. The audience understands that not only will each of the different narrative lines be resolved at the end of the twelve hours, but also that the sense of urgency will increase as the deadline approaches. "American Graffiti," "Ferris Bueller's Day Off," and "Smiles of a Summer Night" are based on this method.

If the story is set over twenty-four hours, the sense of urgency is diminished and the sense of circularity is increased. Whatever may have happened, we return to the beginning, everything becomes as before, and everything starts again. Some authors use this sense of circularity to further highlight the transformation. This technique consists of showing that if most things have remained the same, the two or three things that have changed over the twenty-four hours are the most important. This method is the hidden foundation of stories as different as Joyce's "Ulysses" or "Groundhog Day." (The TV series "24" reverses this technique by extending the twenty-four hours over an entire TV season to increase suspense and compress the plot.)

You'll notice that this circular twenty-four-hour day has dramatic consequences very similar to those of the four seasons. It is not surprising that these two techniques are often linked to comedy, a genre that tends to be circular, emphasizing society as opposed to the individual, and ending with a form of communion or marriage. Circular time techniques are also associated with myth, which is based on the circularity of space. In many myths, the hero leaves home, embarks on a journey, and returns home to discover what was already within him.

Eugene O'Neill uses the single day technique in "Long Day's Journey Into Night." But unlike Joyce's "Ulysses," which covers almost twenty-four hours and evokes the positive qualities of circularity, "Long Day's Journey Into Night" takes place over eighteen hours, starting in the morning and ending at nightfall. This tends to give the story a narrative line of decline—from hope to despair—with family members becoming increasingly detestable and the mother sinking into madness.

The second major consequence of using the single day technique is to highlight the everyday aspect of the drama being told. Rather than cutting out the dead times and showing only the big dramatic moments, all the small events and boring details that make up the life of Mr. Everyman are presented (as in "One Day in the Life of Ivan Denisovich"). The idea of this approach is to show that drama is just as important, if not more so, for the little guy as for the king.

The Perfect Day

The perfect day is a variant of the single day. It is a temporal version of the utopian world, and as such, it is almost always used to structure a part of the story, not the story itself. The idea of the perfect day is that everything is in harmony, which limits the duration of its use, because too much time spent without conflict would be the death of the story.

The perfect day technique is generally linked to a common activity taking place over a day or a night of twelve hours. The common activity is a determining element of this technique. Associating it with a particular moment in time, such as dawn or dusk, intensifies the feeling that everything works well together because harmony is rooted in a natural rhythm. The screenwriters of "Witness" understood this very well and proved it by linking the perfect day to the construction of a barn by the Amish community and the love at first sight between the two main characters.

The Countdown
The countdown technique, or stopwatch, involves directly telling the audience that the action must be completed within a defined time frame. It is widely used in action stories (Speed), thrillers (Outbreak), crime comedies (especially those where characters plan a heist, like Ocean’s Eleven), and suicide mission stories (The Guns of Navarone, The Dirty Dozen). The advantage of the countdown is that it provides the story with intense narrative dynamism and a sustained pace… but at the expense of its texture and subtlety. The countdown also tends to create a funnel effect even faster than that of the twelve-hour day, and it is for this reason that it is often used by authors seeking to give an action story an epic scope. This technique allows for the presentation of hundreds of characters acting simultaneously in the greatest urgency without stopping the narrative dynamism. In stories of this type – The Hunt for Red October, for example – the countdown is generally associated with a point in space towards which all actors and forces must converge.

The countdown technique can also prove very effective in comedic travel stories, although it is less commonly used there. Any travel story is a fragmented zigzag narrative. The comedic travel story gives the story an even more fragmented aspect because the narrative dynamism stops at each comedic scene. Jokes and gags generally tend to divert the narrative dynamism; the story waits while a character is, in some way, humiliated or diminished. By telling the audience from the start that there is a deadline, you give them a straight line to hold onto throughout the meanders of the story. Rather than becoming impatient and wondering what will happen next, the audience relaxes and enjoys the comedic moments. This technique is used in comedic travel stories such as The Blues Brothers or Jacques Tati’s Trafic.

THE STORY UNIVERSE VIA STRUCTURE

Now that you know some of the techniques that allow the development of the story universe over time, you must connect this world to the hero's development at each stage of the story. The arc – for example, from enslavement to freedom – gives you a general idea of how the universe of your story should transform. But you must now detail this development through the structure of the story. It is the structure that allows you to express your theme without a moralizing tone. It also allows you to present the audience with a richly detailed story without losing its narrative dynamism.

But how to do it? In short, you need to create seven visual stages. Each key stage of the narrative structure tends to have a unique sub-world within the global arena of the story. You will notice the enormous advantage this gives you: the story universe gains texture while transforming alongside the hero. These stages must be linked to the other main physical elements of the world, namely natural settings, man-made spaces, technology, and time. This is how you create perfect orchestration between the world and the story.

Here are the structural stages that tend to have a unique sub-world (“apparent defeat/temporary freedom” and “meeting with death” are not part of the seven key structural stages):

• Weakness and need

• Desire

• Opponent

• Apparent defeat/temporary freedom

• Meeting with death

• Final confrontation

• Freedom or enslavement

• Weakness and need At the beginning of the story, a sub-world is presented that is a physical manifestation of the hero's weakness or fear.

• Desire This is the sub-world in which the hero expresses their goal.

• Opponent The opponent (or opponents) lives or works in a unique place that expresses their power and ability to attack the hero's weakness. This opponent's world must also be an extreme version of the hero's world of enslavement.

• Apparent defeat/temporary freedom The apparent defeat is the moment when the hero mistakenly thinks they have lost and the opponent has won (we will discuss this in more detail in chapter 8). The hero's world of apparent defeat is generally the smallest “space” at this stage of the story. The forces of defeat and enslavement literally press on the hero.

The rare heroes who die or end up enslaved at the end of the story often experience a moment of temporary freedom when most other heroes experience their apparent defeat. This moment usually takes the form of a utopia occurring in a place that would be the ideal location for the main character if only they realized it in time.

• Meeting with death During this stage (which we will also study in chapter 8), the hero travels to the Underworld or, in more modern stories, suddenly feels they are going to die. They must confront their mortality in a place that represents decline, aging, or death.

• Final confrontation The confrontation must take place in the most confined place in the entire story. The physical compression creates a kind of funnel or pressure cooker effect that allows the final conflict to gradually reach its climax, until it explodes.

• Freedom or enslavement The world completes its detailed development by becoming a place of freedom, death, or enslavement. Again, this specific place must physically represent the end of the character's maturation or decline.

Here are some examples that will help you better understand how the seven visual stages work and how they can be linked to the four major elements – natural settings, man-made spaces, technology, and time – of the story universe (indicated in italics).

STAR WARS

(George Lucas, 1977)

The global world and the arena of the story are space.

• Weakness and need, desire A desert and wild landscape. In this barren world, where it is difficult to cultivate the land, Luke feels trapped. “I’ll never get out of here,” he complains. The event that triggers Luke's desire is a hologram – a miniature – of Princess Leia calling for help.

• Opponent The Death Star. Fantasy allows the use of abstract forms and transforms them into real objects. Here, the opponent's sub-world, the Death Star, is a giant sphere. Inside, Darth Vader interrogates Princess Leia. Then the commanders of the Death Star learn that the emperor has dissolved the last remnants of the republic, and Darth Vader shows them the deadly power of the Force.

• Apparent defeat and meeting with death A trash compactor that closes with a monster underwater. Combining “apparent defeat” and “meeting with death,” screenwriter George Lucas placed the characters in water with a deadly creature beneath the surface. This room is not only the smallest space observed in the story up to this point. It is a compactor, which generates a feeling of shrinking space and time.

• Final confrontation Trench. To appear realistic, an aerial battle should take place in an open space that would allow pilots to maneuver. But Lucas understood that the best confrontations always occur in confined spaces. He decided that his hero would fly his ship into a long trench, flanked by two walls, and placed the point of culmination of the hero's desire, the weak point where the Death Star can be destroyed, at the end of the trench. And as if all this were not enough, he wanted Luke to be pursued by his main opponent, Darth Vader. Luke fires, and this tiny target becomes the point of convergence for the entire film. An epic that covers the entire universe finally converges to a single point.

• Freedom The hall of heroes. The success of the warriors is celebrated in a grand hall where all the other warriors publicly express their admiration.

THE WILD BUNCH

(original story by Walon Green and Roy N. Sickner, screenplay by Walon Green and Sam Peckinpah, 1969)

The story is based on a linear journey through desert territories, a setting that becomes increasingly arid. The story also places the characters in a society undergoing a fundamental change, moving from the world of the village to that of the city. The new technology, manifested in the form of automobiles and machine guns, has arrived, and the Bunch does not know how to adapt to this new world.

• Problem A town. The story opens with soldiers entering a town in the American Southwest. But it is a dystopian town, as the soldiers are actually outlaws, and the lawmen seeking to capture them are worse than they are. The two groups engage in a fight during which many inhabitants die. The Wild Bunch enters the town to rob the bank, but they have been betrayed by one of their members. Many of them will not make it out alive.

• Weakness and need An empty bar. After the massacre, the members of the Bunch start fighting among themselves in an empty bar until their leader, Pike, gives them an ultimatum: stick together or die. Their problem worsens when the members of the Bunch discover that the silver coins they stole from the bank are worthless.

• Desire A campfire. Lying by a campfire, Pike confides to his second-in-command, Dutch, his desire: he wants to pull off one last big job before retiring. Dutch immediately points out the emptiness of this desire by asking, “Retire to do what?” This line foreshadows the development of the entire story, which is a passage from enslavement to death through even worse enslavement.

• Temporary freedom Under the trees. Although the general development of The Wild Bunch corresponds to a passage from enslavement to death, the screenwriters used the technique of the utopian place in the middle of the story. The Bunch makes a stop in the Mexican village where one of its members was born. It is the only and unique place of conviviality in the entire story. Under the trees, children play. It is in this Arcadian place that the outcasts of the Bunch should live. But they leave, and die.

• Meeting with death A bridge. Again, this stage takes place in the smallest space observed up to this point, that is, a bridge. If the members of the Bunch manage to cross to the other side, they will be free, at least temporarily. If they do not succeed, they will die. The screenwriters added to the technique of shrinking space that of shrinking time: on the bridge, the dynamite is already lit when the members of the Bunch are stuck trying to cross.

• Final confrontation The arenas of Mapache. A confrontation so large and violent should almost inevitably take place in a vast open space. But the screenwriters of this film understood that a good confrontation could only occur between walls, in a space as restricted as possible, to achieve maximum compression. The last four members of the Bunch enter a coliseum, filled with hundreds of opponents. When the pressure cooker explodes, it is one of the greatest battles in cinema history.

• Enslavement or death A wind-swept ghost town. The story does not end with the death of the main characters. The entire town is destroyed. To increase the sense of devastation, the screenwriters added an element: the wind.

MEET ME IN ST. LOUIS

(novel by Sally Benson, screenplay by Irving Brecher and Fred F. Finklehoffe, 1944)

The general arena of the story is small-town America; it is centered on a single large house. By setting their story at the turn of the century, the screenwriters placed the characters in a society transitioning from the small town to the big city. The narrative structure is based on the four seasons and uses the classic link between changing seasons and the rise and fall of a family.

• Freedom Summer in the warm house. The opening scene presents us with a utopian world, a perfect balance between territory, populations, and technology. Horse-drawn carriages and automobiles coexist on a tree-lined avenue. A little boy rides a bicycle towards the large gabled house, and we enter it with him, starting with the warmest and most convivial of all rooms: the kitchen. The screenwriters built the sense of community – a utopia within the house – by having one of the family’s daughters sing the title song (Meet Me in St. Louis) as she climbs the stairs. This technique allows them to establish the foundations of the musical, show the audience the details of the main space of the story, and introduce most of the secondary characters.

The little girl then passes the song, like a baton in a relay, to her grandfather, who heads to another part of the house. This technique tends to reinforce the sense of community, by introducing us to more characters, but also by showing us that it is a large family, of three generations, living under the same roof. After introducing the secondary characters, the main song, and the nooks and crannies of the warm house, the screenwriters position us at the window, where we meet the heroine, Esther, who, with her lovely voice, sings the title song as she climbs the porch steps.

In harmony with the utopian world, the heroine, Esther, is happy at the beginning of the story. She has no weakness, need, or problem yet, but she is vulnerable to attack.

• Weakness and need, problem, and opponent Autumn in the terrifying house. In the next season, autumn, the warm house has taken on a terrifying air. What is certain is that the house and the season are in harmony with Halloween, the holiday that pays homage to the dead. It is also the beginning of the family’s decline, which crumbles, ironically due to the possible departure of the two marriageable daughters but also because the opponent, the father, has decided that the family should leave the small town of St. Louis to settle in the big city of New York.

The screenwriters use Halloween to extend their critique to society itself, beyond the limits of this family. The two little girls preparing to go trick-or-treating spread rumors about one of their neighbors, who, according to them, poisons cats. Later, the youngest, Tootie, falsely claims that Esther’s boyfriend abused her. This is the dark side of life in a small town, where lies and rumors can destroy anyone in an instant.

• Apparent defeat Winter in the sinister house. With winter, the family reaches its lowest point. The bags are packed, and they are about to leave. Esther sings a sad song to Tootie; she hopes for a happier Christmas the following year: “Someday, if fate allows, we’ll be together again. In the meantime, we’ll have to make do as we can.” This family community is about to disintegrate and die.

• New freedom Spring in the warm house. Like any musical, the story ends when the heroes overcome the crisis – the father decides that the family must stay in St. Louis. The family community is reborn in the spring. There are not one, but two weddings, and the family, now even larger, joyfully heads to the World’s Fair. This fair is a new sub-world, a temporary utopia and a vision of America’s future in miniature designed to show this family, and the audience, that individual prospects can exist without destroying the community, “right here, in our garden.”

IT’S A WONDERFUL LIFE

(novella, The Greatest Gift, by Philip Van Doren Stern, screenplay by Francis Goodrich, Albert Hackett, and Frank Capra, 1946)

This elaborate social fantasy, which represents one of the best examples of connection between the story and its universe, is designed to allow the audience to see, and compare in great detail, two different versions of the same town. This small town is a miniature of America, and its two versions are based on different sets of values but both central to American life.

The arena is Bedford Falls, a lively small town with low-rise buildings, where you can wave to a friend passing by on the street. The Christmas holiday is one of the foundations of the story, even though it follows more the philosophy of Easter by making the “death” and rebirth of its hero the main basis of its structure.

• Weakness and need Night, Bedford Falls seen from the sky. The story begins with an omniscient narrator speaking in the third person (an angel) and is then carried by a real character, the angel Clarence. Clarence has a weakness: he does not yet have his wings. By helping George, he will be able to fulfill his need. George’s weakness and problem are that his despair has led him to the brink of suicide. This setting is designed to allow the audience to see a concise version of George’s past and to place side by side the two different versions of the town.

The sub-world of these two weaknesses, Clarence’s and George’s, is a divine vision of the arena: the town seen from the sky. The night is a physical manifestation of the religious elements of the story.

• Desire George’s warm childhood home and the deserted house where he makes a wish with Mary. After college, George lives in the domestic bustle of the family home with his father, mother, brother, and Annie, the maid. His father is a benevolent man, and there is much love between him and his son. But George is eager to leave the oppressive small town. He explains his goal to his father: “You know… what I’ve always talked to you about – building things… designing new buildings – creating modern cities.” This scene puts the structural stage of the story and the visual sub-world associated with it in conflict (in general, the sub-world corresponds to the stage). The warm house shows what a loving family can look like. But George’s intense desire to leave evokes the oppression of the small-town world, especially those controlled by tyrants.

George expresses his desire again when he returns home with Mary after falling into the pool at the dance. The two characters observe an old abandoned house on a hill – the terrifying house – which, for George, is the very symbol of all the negative aspects of small-town life. He throws a rock at it and says to Mary: “I’m going to shake the dust of this crummy little town off my feet and see the world… then I’m going to build things.” Of course, he ends up living in this house, which his wife tries to make comfortable and warm. But in his mind, this house is haunted; it is his tomb.

• Opponent The bank and Potter’s office. Henry Potter is “the richest and vilest man in the whole county.” When Clarence first sees him, driving his splendid horse-drawn carriage, he asks: “Who is that? A king?” Potter is the enemy of George and his building loan business, as these are the only two things preventing him from owning the entire town. It is from his lair, his bank, that Potter exercises his control over the town.

• Apparent defeat Bedford Falls Bridge. George experiences his apparent defeat when he must face the shame of bankruptcy due to Uncle Billy, who lost $8,000. George walks to the middle of the bridge in the snow and wind. In this narrow passageway, he decides to end his life.

• Meeting with death Pottersville, the opponent’s dystopian town. The angel Clarence shows George what the town would have been like if he had never lived and thus had not been able to counter Potter’s influence. Potter believes in business, money, power, and the enslavement of the average man. George thus begins his long journey through the Underworld of Pottersville, a perfect representation of Potter’s values.

The detail of this sub-world, written in the script, is grand, and the entire sequence takes place during George’s wandering. The main street is a lineup of bars, nightclubs, liquor stores, and pool halls resonating with dissonant jazzy music (it should be noted that this vision of the world may appeal to some of us). As stated in the script, “the wise and quiet little town has become essentially a frontier village.”

Unlike Bedford Falls, Potter’s town is devoid of a sense of community. No one recognizes George, and no one knows anyone. And, more importantly, all the secondary characters, who have been presented in great detail before, have become their worst possible version. The contrast with their previous selves is staggering but credible. Ernie, the cab driver, could live a dark version of his life. Mr. Gower, the pharmacist, could be a bum. George’s mother could be a nasty woman running a boarding house. This suggests that each of us is a range of possibilities and that the best or worst of us depends on the world we live in and the values we believe in.

George completes his journey in Pottersville – and his long meeting with death – in a cemetery, in the snow, at night. He sees his brother’s grave and narrowly escapes bullets fired by a policeman. He then returns to his starting point, the bridge, the transition place where he was about to end his life.
• Liberty Bedford Falls, the hero's utopian town. When George discovers he is alive, he experiences the intense feeling of liberation that comes from realizing the value of his own life, and even more, what he has managed to accomplish as a human being. Realizations of this kind are always very profound. In a moment of intense but enlightening irony, he happily walks down the main street of the town that, just a few hours earlier, had almost driven him to suicide. The town remains the same, but the simple street lined with trees and family businesses has become a winter wonderland. This town that George considered boring, he now sees as a utopia, because it is a community whose members care for each other. The old cold house, recently haunted and suffocating, has become warm, because it is where his family and the people who love him live, and this house is soon filled with all the secondary characters whose lives he has improved and who are now happy to return the favor.

It's a Wonderful Life offers a very close alignment between the story and its visual world. Unlike the grand sensational worlds of fantasies such as The Lord of the Rings or the "Harry Potter" series, the visual techniques of this film rely on the setting of mid-century middle-class suburban America (Big is a more recent example). It's a Wonderful Life is an excellent social fantasy. It stands on the same level as those of Twain or Dickens, from which it was indeed inspired.

It is perfectly possible to borrow from other authors, but it must be done playfully. The references should be light. Those who know them will appreciate it. Those who don't will enjoy the texture they add to the story. In It's a Wonderful Life, the angel who saves George is named Clarence, a name that recalls the ally in Twain's A Connecticut Yankee in King Arthur's Court. Clarence is reading The Adventures of Tom Sawyer when he is called to act. And of course, the story can be considered an American version of Dickens' A Christmas Carol, mixed with a strong dose of David Copperfield.

Note that you can draw inspiration from many things, including the guiding principle of your story. But in this case, you must nevertheless modify the original idea to make it unique. Your audience will appreciate this masterstroke, even if they are not fully aware of it. It's a Wonderful Life is not the story of a grumpy old American who would see Christmas past, present, and future in New York City. It's the story of an average American whose life is laid bare and who sees what his hometown would have become if he had never been born. This is an excellent modification of the guiding principle of A Christmas Carol. You may be surprised to learn that It's a Wonderful Life did not meet with success at the time of its release. Although it is very sentimental, this social satire was perhaps too dark for the general public of its time. But over time, the excellence of the film, particularly in the way it connects characters and the universe of the narrative, has eventually won the hearts of the crowds.

SUNSET BOULEVARD

(Charles Brackett, Billy Wilder, and D. M. Marshman Jr, 1950)

Sunset Boulevard is the scathing satire of a modern kingdom whose sovereigns are movie stars. These kings and queens live and die by selling beauty. This film particularly appeals to those who know the art of storytelling—not only because its main character is the modern storyteller, a screenwriter, but also because its visual world evokes all sorts of forms of stories and references. We can only present below a few of the narrative universe techniques present in this brilliant screenplay.

The global world of the story is Hollywood, which the screenwriters depict as a kingdom populated by courtiers and a crowd of laborious serfs. By using a voice-over narrator who is himself a screenwriter, the film's authors have given themselves the opportunity to make all sorts of literary connections with the narrative universe.

• Problem An apartment in Hollywood. Screenwriter Joe Gillis, who lacks work and money, lives in a decrepit apartment. He is also part of the "factory" of Hollywood screenwriters, and "churns out two stories a week." His problems worsen when two men arrive at his home to repossess his car.

• Weakness and need, adversary The decrepit villa and its pool. Upon seeing Norma Desmond's decrepit villa—the terrifying house—for the first time, Joe thinks this secret underworld has just saved him. He will be able to hide his car, rewrite Norma's horrible script, and make a lot of money. But he has just entered the underworld of the adversary, a world from which he will never escape. This world will trap him by feeding his major weakness, greed.

Here is how Joe, the screenwriter, describes this world:

It was a big white elephantine house. The kind of building that movie lunatics had built in the Roaring Twenties. Neglected houses always look sad. And this one was more than neglected. It was like that old woman in Great Expectations, Miss Havisham, with her rotting wedding dress, and her torn veil, who held a grudge against the whole world because she had been jilted.

As he takes refuge in the guest house, Joe passes by vines and brambles, just like the Prince in Sleeping Beauty. Through the window, he observes the empty pool full of rats. Symbols of death and sleep are everywhere in this world.

• Adversary, and apparent defeat The revitalized house, Joe captured at the pool. This fairy tale world, with its haunted house, brambles, and Sleeping Beauty, is also the home of a vampire. As Joe sinks deeper into the trap of the easy life, Norma and the house come back to life. The pool is now clean and filled with water, and when Joe emerges from it, Norma, invigorated by this new blood, dries the young man she has acquired with a towel, as if he were her baby.

• Final confrontation, death Gunshots at the pool. The confrontation is brief and unbalanced: Norma shoots Joe when he tries to leave her. He falls into the pool. The vampire has finally killed him.

• Enslavement of the adversary Norma on the staircase descending into madness. With such a human adversary, Sunset Boulevard could not stop at the hero's death. The adversary literally descends into madness. She has lost all ability to distinguish dream from reality and is both her character—"Down there, they're waiting for the princess"—and an actress playing in a new Hollywood film. In front of the journalists' cameras, Norma descends the grand staircase of the "palace" in a deep sleep from which no prince will ever awaken her.

ULYSSES

(James Joyce, 1922)

One might initially feel some reluctance to use Joyce's Ulysses to study storytelling techniques, precisely because many people consider it the greatest novel of the 20th century. Its incredible complexity might seem too difficult to grasp for the mere mortal authors that we are, and its intentionally obscure references and techniques might appear as totally unsuitable models for those who wish to write popular stories in the form of film or TV scripts, novels, or plays.

Nothing could be further from the truth. While Joyce was undoubtedly, by nature, incredibly gifted for writing, he was also one of the best-trained authors in history. And if he chose to use this training to write with a complexity that you may want to avoid, it can be said that, for all sorts of legitimate reasons, the techniques he used can be universally applied to any story, regardless of its mode of expression.

Ulysses is the novelist's novel. Its second main character, Stephen, is a man striving to become a great writer. Joyce used a wider and more elaborate range of techniques than any that have ever been used in all the works ever written (except, perhaps, Joyce's Finnegans Wake, but since no one has really read it from beginning to end, it can be said that it doesn't count). In many ways, Joyce challenged other writers, as if to say: "Can you understand what I've done, and can you do it too?" Why not try to take up his challenge?

The story being a modern version of The Odyssey, it is a combination of myth, comedy, and drama. The global arena is the city of Dublin, but ironically the story is primarily set not in a house but "on the road." Like in any myth, the main hero, Leopold Bloom, embarks on a journey and returns home. But since it is a comic myth, or a "parody of an epic," no learning seems to emerge upon the hero's return.

Like many other highly elaborate stories, Ulysses takes place at the turn of the century, a time of societal change, transitioning from a small town to a big city. Dublin contains many elements of the small town, but also many elements of the big city—even the modern and oppressive city. From the start, we are plunged into the guilt that is so common in stories set in small towns: Stephen has a roommate who reproaches him for not praying at his own mother's deathbed.

The main hero, Bloom, is both the ordinary hero of the big city and the loser of the modern and oppressive big city. If Ulysses is a frustrated warrior, Bloom is a frustrated Everyman. He is Charlie Chaplin's tramp, Charles Schulz's Charlie Brown, Seinfeld's George Costanza. He is also a timid cuckold who knows his wife has a lover but does nothing to end the situation. In many ways, Joyce's narrative universe is not built on the usual combination of elements. If Dublin, for example, is an oppressive city, it is not because of overwhelming technology, nor the enslavement of the future, but because of the shackles of the past, particularly the British Empire and the Catholic Church.

In addition to using the myth of The Odyssey and societal change, Joyce built the structure of his story on the technique of the twenty-four-hour day. Circular time aligns with the circular space of myth and comedy, allowing Joyce to better define the ordinary quality of his hero and to compare and highlight the actions of a vast network of characters in the city.

Joyce also uses the twenty-four-hour day to establish the opposition of characters between his main hero and his secondary hero. The first three sections of the story, which follow the steps of the secondary hero, Stephen, take place between 8 a.m. and about noon. Then Joyce returns to the starting point, 8 a.m., to follow the steps of his main hero, Bloom. This temporal comparison constantly pushes the reader to imagine what these two men are doing at roughly the same time, and Joyce establishes a number of parallels between them to help the reader compare them.

Joyce was the creator of several techniques for depicting the secondary characters of his story's world. Since much of his theme relates to the enslaving nature of this world, he assigned many secondary characters a specific weakness and need. These are generally variations on the theme of their overly close ties to the Catholic Church, as well as the domination exerted over them by England. Or these characters have too much faith in the heroes of Ireland's past and its comfortable but ultimately debilitating stereotypes.

The network of characters in Ulysses is among the most detailed in the history of storytelling. Alongside the key fictional characters, there are a number of "real" people who lived in Dublin at the time the story takes place, that is, in 1904. And among these "real" people, there are many fictional characters already used by Joyce in other stories (particularly in his collection of short stories, Dubliners). All this tends to give the narrative universe a rich realistic texture, deeply rooted in history, as each of these real or imagined characters has a detailed character and story already defined, whether or not readers are familiar with them.

Joyce is extremely skilled at connecting key structural stages to the visual sub-worlds of the story. By modeling a modern journey through the city on Ulysses' travels, Joyce was able to create identifiable sub-worlds within an amorphous city. This technique also allowed him, despite the complexity of the story, to imbue each sub-world with the essence of one or two of the main structural stages. The reader thus feels more at ease in the ebb and flow of this great epic, and despite the complex turn of events, the psychological and moral lines of the two main characters are always highlighted.

Below is a brief summary of the major structural stages of the story, along with the sections of The Odyssey on which they are based (in parentheses) and the sub-worlds of Dublin in which they take place (in italics).

• Weakness and need, problem, adversary, and specter of Stephen (Telemachus) The Martello tower. It is 8 a.m. in an apartment in the Martello tower overlooking the beach of Dublin Bay. Stephen Dedalus is a troubled young man. He has just returned from Paris, where he was trying to become a writer, because of his mother's death. He is disoriented and doubts himself. He also feels terribly guilty for having refused to fulfill his mother's last wish, which was for him to pray for her. Like Ulysses' son, Telemachus, he wonders who his real father is and where he is. His roommate, Buck Mulligan, who appears to be his friend but is actually his enemy, reproaches him for refusing to pray for his mother.

This tower-house, which Joyce parallels with Hamlet's castle, is a prison for the sensitive Stephen, who shares it with Mulligan, the tyrant, and the haughty Englishman, Haines. Although Stephen pays the rent, he lets Mulligan borrow the apartment key.

• Weakness and need, problem, and specter of Stephen (Nestor) Deasy's school. While he wishes to become a writer, Stephen is forced to teach, for a meager salary, in a boys' school. The classroom, with its noisy and cunning students, depresses him and reminds him of the specters of his past. For the aspiring artist that Stephen is, this school is a trap.

• Weakness and need, problem, and specter of Stephen (Proteus) Sandymount beach. Stephen walks along the beach, where he sees images of birth and death, as well as a three-masted ship that reminds him of the crucifixion. He struggles to distinguish between reality and appearances, between what he must become and what others want to make of him. Again, he questions the identity of his real father.

• Weakness and need, problem, and desire of Bloom (Calypso) The kitchen and the butcher shop. At 8 a.m., Leopold Bloom is preparing breakfast for his wife, Molly, who is still asleep. Ulysses was enslaved by a woman, Calypso, for seven years. Bloom is enslaved by his wife. But it is an enslavement he has imposed on himself. Strange and isolated, Bloom has become somewhat estranged from Molly, both sexually and emotionally. He needs to be deeply accepted and loved.

In the kitchen and the butcher shop, Bloom shows his attraction to carnal pleasures, that is, cooking, women, and sex. Like Stephen, Bloom leaves his house without taking his key.

• Weakness and need, problem, and desire of Bloom (The Lotus-Eaters) A street on the way to the post office and the pharmacy. Bloom would prefer to dodge his problems or, like the Lotus-Eaters, forget them completely. Like Stephen, Bloom is passive and idle. Throughout the story, he will experience many futile desires that lead nowhere. At the post office, he regrets his correspondence with a woman named Martha, but on the other hand, he does not intend to go beyond this exchange of words and consummate this relationship. At the pharmacy, the world of medicinal drugs, Bloom's desire is to escape and overcome his loneliness.

• Adversaries, and specters (Hades) Car ride through the city streets to the cemetery. Bloom joins men he considers friends and takes part in a funeral procession. But these men treat him as a stranger. They cross paths with Blazes Boylan. Bloom knows that this man will sleep with his wife later in the day. Like Ulysses in the Underworld, Bloom remembers his father's suicide and the death of his little boy, Rudy, ten years earlier.

• Desire, and adversaries (Aeolus) Newspaper office. During one of his adventures, Ulysses is pushed by favorable winds towards his home when one of his companions opens the bag containing the contrary winds that Aeolus, the god of the wind, had carefully sealed.

Bloom, the modern traveler, sells advertising newspapers. At his office, he tries to make a sale, but just as he is about to succeed, he fails because of his boss. He is also forced to listen to a band of braggarts who seek to humiliate him and make ill-advised comments about Ireland's supposedly glorious past.

• Narrative universe, adversary, and specter (Lestrygonians) The streets of Dublin, the Burton Hotel restaurant, Davy Byrne's pub, the National Museum. This miniature odyssey (and Joyce's work contains many miniatures) is a depiction of Bloom walking through the heart of Dublin, with many details about the inhabitants and daily events of this world.

At the Burton Hotel, Bloom is disgusted by the disgusting way some patrons eat; he is forced to leave. As Bloom undertakes a journey and as he is a man who seeks to avoid confrontation, his main adversary, Boylan, is not materially present. There is no direct conflict. But Boylan is always on Bloom's mind. At Davy Byrne's pub, Bloom looks at the clock and realizes that Molly and his enemy's romantic rendezvous will take place two hours later.

At the end of this section, Bloom sees Boylan in the street. He slips into the museum to avoid speaking to him but must then pretend to be interested in the buttocks of Greek goddess statues to avoid being caught.

• Adversaries, and Stephen's realization; Bloom's adversary (Charybdis and Scylla) The National Library. At the library, a place of the mind, the theoretical and artistic Stephen presents his theories on Shakespeare to Dublin's intellectual elite. But like Bloom, Stephen is an uninvited stranger. Buck Mulligan arrives and mocks him again. Stephen has an important realization: the gap between him and Mulligan is an abyss, and he will now stop considering him a friend.

At the library, Bloom also has problems with Shakespeare and his supporters. Mulligan saw him slip into the museum and mocks the deep interest he seemed to have in the buttocks of the goddesses.

• Narrative universe (Wandering Rocks) The streets of Dublin. The Wandering Rocks section is the entirety of Ulysses' narrative universe in miniature, at the very heart of the work. Joyce takes the time to define, sometimes comically and sometimes sadly, a number of secondary characters in the city on the path of their own daily odyssey.

• Weaknesses and need, adversary, apparent defeat of Bloom (Sirens) The bar of the Ormond Hotel. Like the sirens who seduce sailors and lead them to death by singing, two barmaids tease Bloom at the bar of the Ormond Hotel. The sentimental Irish songs he hears there hurt him because they remind him of his lost son and his problems with Molly. Bloom knows the exact moment when Blazes Boylan will enter his home, and he knows it is approaching. Bloom has reached the lowest point of his fall, highlighting his loneliness and deep sense of alienation.

• Adversary (Cyclops) Barney Kiernan's pub. In Barney Kiernan's pub, Bloom stands up to the "Citizen," an Irish nationalist. Ironically, Bloom knows that at this very moment, Boylan, his main adversary, is sleeping with his wife. But even during this short phase, one of the most heroic in the story, Bloom struggles to hide his weaknesses. He comes across as a "Mr. Know-it-all," an annoying braggart giving lessons.

The bar where Bloom faces one of his greatest adversaries, the "Citizen," resembles a cave. And as the section progresses, this place seems increasingly dark, violent, and filled with hatred.

• Adversary, and narrative dynamism (Nausicaa) Sandymount beach. On the very beach where Stephen walked a few hours earlier, Bloom sees a pretty woman whose physical charms tempt him so much that he ends up masturbating. But this is just another false ally, and this moment is just another digression, a diversion designed to prevent Bloom from reconnecting with his wife.

• Narrative dynamism and Bloom's realization; Stephen's adversary (Helios' herds) The maternity ward of the National Hospital, Burke's pub, the streets of Dublin. Bloom goes to the hospital to see Mrs. Purefoy, who has been trying to give birth to her baby for three days.

Stephen has been drinking with friends, and at Burke's pub, he continues to waste his money buying drinks he can't afford. He fights with Mulligan, injures his hand, and then goes to a brothel.

Bloom begins to take an interest in Stephen and decides to stay with him to watch over him. Bloom, a passive and idle man, who had so far been driven only by small desires mostly unfulfilled, now has a true narrative dynamism, which is to find a son. It will be Stephen, the son of his friend.
• Adversary, awareness, and moral decision of Stephen; narrative dynamism and moral decision of Bloom (Circe) The brothel. In the Circe section (which corresponds to the Odyssey where men are turned into pigs), Stephen, drunk, enters a brothel. His mother appears to him in a hallucination and tries to increase his guilt to make him return to the church. Stephen refuses this way of life and breaks a chandelier with his cane (his sword), finally freeing himself from the past that has held him prisoner for so long.

Bloom enters the brothel to fetch Stephen with intense determination. He defends Stephen against the madam, Bella Cohen, who tries to swindle the young man by demanding exorbitant compensation for the chandelier. Ironically, it is by relying on blackmail that Bloom accomplishes his most moral action of the day: he threatens Bella to publicly reveal that she used prostitution to send her son to Oxford.

• Limited awareness, and second moral decision of both men (Eumaeus) The Fitzharris café. The two men head to a small café. Since his awareness at the brothel, Stephen knows what he must do with his future. He lends money to a man and tells him that his teaching position will soon be available.

In the café, Bloom and Stephen have a long discussion on many subjects. But even if they experience a moment of communion, they are ultimately too different for their friendship to survive the night. Bloom is too practical, too philistine for Stephen's extremely artistic and theoretical personality.

Bloom's narrative dynamism changes again as he wonders whether he is capable of returning to Molly, of going back home. Although he fears his wife's anger, he decides to take Stephen with him: "You can lean on me," he tells him. If Ulysses is more complex psychologically and morally than most other stories, it is partly because Bloom's moral decision is not entirely altruistic. Bloom thinks Stephen can help him write an advertisement. He also believes the young man can provide him with material for a story he wants to write and that he can benefit from his excess sensitivity.

• Thematic revelation (Ithaca) Bloom's kitchen and bedroom. The new "father" and "son" share another moment of communion while drinking chocolate in Bloom's kitchen, the very place where the "enslaved" Bloom of the previous morning was preparing Molly's breakfast. Stephen goes home, and Bloom goes to bed. Using a technique borrowed from catechism of questions and answers to tell the story, Joyce elevates Ulysses above its few characters, towards a cosmic perspective, a thematic revelation, just as he did at the end of his short story The Dead. Although the two men have experienced a short but intense moment of communion, when Stephen leaves, Bloom feels "the cold of interstellar space."

• Weakness and need, problem, partial awareness, and moral decision of Molly (Penelope) Bloom and Molly's bed. In her bed, Molly recounts the story of Ulysses from her point of view, but her journey takes place only in her mind. She expresses her deep loneliness and her feeling of being unloved by her husband. She is also fully aware of Bloom's many weaknesses and needs. In the marital bed, while her husband is now beside her (though head-to-foot), she recalls the affair she had with Blazes Boylan earlier in the day.

But ultimately, Molly is the woman of "yes." The feeling that Bloom and Molly's love can be reborn comes from the fact that she considers preparing her husband's breakfast the next morning and serving him eggs. She also remembers Bloom when, deeply in love, she agreed to marry him and made him a "caraway seed cake." In this great circular journey that ends with a return home, there is a sense that a "remarriage" between Bloom and Molly is possible.
The foundations of this goal are established when Harry and the other first-year students gather in the Great Hall, where they learn the school rules and are sorted into one of the four houses by the Sorting Hat. You will notice that this scene brings together all the episodes of a myth, unfolding over an amorphous school year, and places them on a single, measurable line. The line of desire begins in the Great Hall where all the students are gathered, and it ends in this same hall, where all the students applaud Harry and his friends who have enabled their house to win the victory.

3. Line of desire for the second half of the story: Solve the mystery of the Philosopher's Stone under the trapdoor.

It is the desire to win the school cup that guides the entire school year. But many other episodic matters must be resolved, especially since this novel is the first in a series. Rowling must introduce many characters, explain the laws of magic, and provide many details to the world of her story, particularly by explaining the rules of Quidditch. A second, more specific desire thus becomes necessary.

When Harry, Ron, and Hermione accidentally find themselves on the forbidden floor and discover the trapdoor guarded by the three-headed dog, they come to the desire that will allow this complex story to converge to a precise point. The Philosopher's Stone then becomes a detective story, the form that undoubtedly has the clearest and strongest backbone of all.

• Opponents The suburban house, the classrooms, the stadium, the toilets. Harry must face his first opponents, his uncle, Vernon, his aunt, Petunia, and his cousin, Dudley, in his own home. Like Cinderella, he must do all the chores, and he is forced to live in a tiny room under the stairs. Harry's recurring opponent is Draco Malfoy, with whom he competes in several of his classes. As a member of Gryffindor house, Harry must beat Draco's house, Slytherin, in the Quidditch match held in the stadium. Harry and his friends fight against the giant troll in the girls' toilets.

• Opponent, and apparent defeat The Forbidden Forest. Lord Voldemort is Harry's long-term opponent. Hidden behind the scenes, he is the most powerful of all. Rowling, in this first novel of a series of seven, had to face a difficult narrative problem to solve. Since she had to maintain this opposition over seven books, and since Harry was only eleven years old in the first of them, Voldemort had to start the story in a very weakened state. Thus, in the Philosopher's Stone, Voldemort struggles to stay alive and must use the mind and body of Professor Quirrell.

But Voldemort and his underworlds remain dangerous. The Forbidden Forest is filled with deadly plants and animals, and Harry and the other students can easily get lost there. Harry enters the terrifying forest at night and comes across the vampiric Lord Voldemort drinking the blood of a unicorn. Despite his weakened state, Voldemort is powerful enough to kill. Harry will only be saved by the last-minute intervention of a centaur.

• Opponent, and confrontation The Underworld of Hogwarts (trapdoor, Devil's Snare, enclosed room). Harry, Ron, and Hermione go to the third floor to find the Philosopher's Stone. But after defeating the three-headed dog (which evokes Cerberus, the dog of Hades), they fall into the trapdoor and must fight against the deadly roots of the Devil's Snare. They are in the Underworld of Hogwarts, Voldemort's other underworld. And they must win the violent confrontation of the wizard's chess game, abstract but deadly.

Harry's confrontation with Voldemort takes place in a closed room – a confined space. The room is located at the top of a long flight of stairs, giving the impression that it is at the base of a whirlwind.

Harry faces Voldemort and Professor Quirrell alone, and as he tries to escape, Quirrell encircles him with a ring of fire. Voldemort attacks Harry's major weakness – his desire to live with his parents, whom he never knew – by promising the young boy to bring his family back to life if he gives him the stone.

• Realization The burning room, the infirmary. Under the extreme attacks of Voldemort and Professor Quirrell, Harry takes a stand for good. As he regains strength in the infirmary, Professor Dumbledore tells him that his body is literally imbued with love and protected by love. His skin burned the evil Quirrell and killed him thanks to the love his mother showed when she sacrificed her life to save his.

• New balance The station. With the school year over, the students are preparing to return to the ordinary world. But Harry is now armed with a photo album that Hagrid gave him, showing him in the loving arms of his parents, whom he never knew.
Mario Puzo connected his hero to God, but with the aim of highlighting a very different aspect of the divine. Puzo's God is the Father who controls his world and dispenses justice. But he is a vengeful god. He is a man-god with dictatorial power beyond the reach of ordinary mortals. Puzo also created a symbolic opposition within this character by establishing a link between God and the devil. The parallel between the usual opposites of the sacred and the profane is a fundamental element of this character and the entire story.

THE PHILADELPHIA STORY

(play by Philip Barry, screenplay by Donald Ogden Stewart, 1940)

The writer Philip Barry connected his heroine, Tracy Lord, to aristocracy, but also to the concept of a goddess. In addition to her last name, which gives her a noble aspect, Tracy Lord has a nickname invented by her father and ex-husband: "The Bronze Goddess." These symbolic signs tend to both lower and elevate her. The story is based on the following question: Will Tracy succumb to the worst traits of "divinity"—her cold, haughty, inhuman, and ruthless side—or will she choose her better aspects—a greatness of spirit that will, ironically, allow her to become more human and benevolent?

Divine heroes can also be found in The Matrix (Neo = Jesus), Cool Hand Luke (Luke = Jesus), and A Tale of Two Cities (Sydney Carton = Jesus).

ANIMAL SYMBOLISM

A STREETCAR NAMED DESIRE

(Tennessee Williams, 1947)

In A Streetcar Named Desire, Tennessee Williams identifies his characters with animals, which tends to demean them, but also shows that their behavior is driven by biological laws. Stanley is compared to a pig, a bull, a monkey, a dog, and a wolf to highlight his essentially greedy, brutal, and masculine nature. Blanche evokes a moth, a bird, fragile and frightened. Throughout the story, Williams repeats these symbols in various forms. And ultimately, the wolf ends up devouring the bird.

BATMAN, SPIDER-MAN, TARZAN, CROCODILE DUNDEE

Comics can be considered modern versions of myths. It is therefore not surprising that their characters are identified with animals from the start. It is the most metaphorical and "biggest" symbol one can find. The name, physique, and clothing of Batman, Spider-Man, and Tarzan, the ape-man, all tend to draw attention to the connection between these characters and the animals they are associated with. These characters do not merely have certain traits reminiscent of animal behavior that subtly but powerfully affect them, like Stanley Kowalski. They are animal men. Fundamentally divided characters, half-men, half-animals. The cruelty of the natural state in which man finds himself forces them to turn to an animal to benefit from its specific powers and fight for justice. But all this comes at a cost: they must suffer from uncontrollable internal division and insurmountable alienation.

Identifying a character with an animal often results in very popular stories, as they present a form of enlargement (but not too much, to avoid making the story boring). Swinging from tree to tree (Tarzan) or from skyscraper to skyscraper (Spider-Man), or exerting power over the animal kingdom (Crocodile Dundee), are dreams deeply rooted in the human mind.

Other stories that use the symbol of the animal man include Dances with Wolves, Dracula, The Wolfman, and The Silence of the Lambs.

MECHANICAL SYMBOLISM

Connecting a man to a machine is another common way to create a symbolic character. Machine characters, or robot men, are generally endowed with mechanical, and therefore superhuman, strength, but they are also insensitive beings incapable of compassion. This technique is generally used in horror or science fiction stories, where excessive symbols are part of the form and are therefore well accepted. When good authors repeat these symbols throughout the story, they do not add details, as is done for most other symbolic characters. They create an inversion. At the end of the story, the machine man turns out to be the most human of all the characters, while the human character has acted like an animal or a machine.

FRANKENSTEIN OR THE MODERN PROMETHEUS

(novel by Mary Shelley, 1818, play by Peggy Webling, screenplay by John L. Balderston and Francis Edward Faragoh, 1931)

The first to connect a character and a machine was Mary Shelley with Frankenstein. Her human character, at the beginning of the story, is Dr. Frankenstein. But this man is quickly elevated to the status of a god when he decides to create life. And he will create the machine man, the monster who, being constructed from different parts, lacks the fluidity of movement of human beings. The third protagonist, the hunchback, is the symbolic intermediary character, the subhuman excluded and considered a monster by the human community, but who nevertheless works for Dr. Frankenstein. You will notice that these characters are defined and contrasted using simple but clear types. As the story progresses, it is precisely because he is considered an inferior being that he must be chained, burned, and then made to disappear, that the monster rebels and seeks revenge on his cold and inhuman divine father.

Other stories that use the machine character technique include Blade Runner (the replicants), Terminator (Terminator), 2001: A Space Odyssey (HAL), and The Wizard of Oz (the Tin Man and the Scarecrow).

OTHER SYMBOLISM

THE SUN ALSO RISES

(Ernest Hemingway, 1926)

The Sun Also Rises is a classic example of creating a symbolic character without using metaphorical character types, god, animal, or machine. Hemingway created a symbolic opposition within his hero Jake Barnes by presenting him as an integral, strong, and confident man, but who became impotent due to a war injury. The combination of strength and impotence creates a character whose main characteristic is being lost. Jake is therefore an ironic man, moving from one sensual moment to another, but unable to function at this basic level. As a man who is not a man, he is a completely realistic character who also comes to represent an entire generation of drifting men.

TECHNIQUE: THE SYMBOLIC NAME

To connect symbol and character, you can also use the technique of translating the essential characteristic of the character through their name. A master of this technique, Charles Dickens created names whose images and sounds immediately identify the essential nature of his characters. Ebenezer Scrooge, for example, is unmistakably a man who loves money and would do anything to have it. Uriah Heep may hide behind the serious facade of Uriah, but his fundamentally obsequious nature is revealed in the "Heep." We know that Tiny Tim is the quintessential good boy long before he utters the phrase: "God bless us, everyone."

Vladimir Nabokov noted that this technique had become much less frequent in post-19th-century fiction. This is probably because it risks drawing attention by being too overtly thematic.

However, when well-chosen, the symbolic name can be a wonderful tool. But it is a technique that generally works better when writing a comedy, a genre that features type characters.

Here are some characters invited to a party given by Gatsby in The Great Gatsby. Note that Fitzgerald often lists names that evoke a failed attempt to integrate into American aristocracy: Mr. and Mrs. O.R.P. Schraeder and the Stonewall Jackson Abrams of Georgia; Mrs. Ulysses Swett. He then ruthlessly explains who these people really are and what they have become.

Then, from East Egg came Mr. and Mrs. Chester Becker and the Leech, as well as a man named Bunsen, whom I had known at Yale, and Dr. Webster Civet, who had nearly drowned last summer in Maine. And there were also the Hornbeam and Mr. and Mrs. Willie Voltaire. [...] From a little further on the Island came the Cheadle and Mr. and Mrs. O.R.P. Schraeder, as well as the Stonewall Jackson Abrams of Georgia, the Fishguard and Mr. and Mrs. Ripley Snell. Snell had come three days before his stay in the penitentiary; dead drunk on the gravel driveway, he had his right hand crushed by Mrs. Ulysses Swett's car.

Another technique using symbolic character names involves mixing "real" characters and fictional characters, as is the case in Ragtime, The Wind and the Lion, Underworld, Carter Beats the Devil, and The Plot Against America. The historical characters in these works are in no way "real." But their legends have given them an iconic, and sometimes even divine, character in the reader's mind. They have thus become the gods and mythical heroes of a nation. Their names possess a prefabricated power, like a flag, which the author can use or not.

TECHNIQUE: SYMBOL CONNECTED TO A CHARACTER'S TRANSFORMATION

One of the most advanced techniques is to use a symbol that accompanies the character's transformation: the author chooses a symbol that represents what they want the character to become once they have undergone their transformation.

To use this technique, you must focus on the key scenes at the beginning and end of the story, then associate a symbol with the hero when you create their weakness and need. Finally, you must repeat this symbol at the moment of the character's transformation, but with slight variations from the first time you introduced it.

THE GODFATHER

(novel by Mario Puzo, screenplay by Mario Puzo and Francis Ford Coppola, 1972)

This technique is brilliantly used in the film The Godfather. The opening scene shows a typical experience of what a Godfather is: a man comes to Vito Corleone to ask for justice. This scene is fundamentally a negotiation, and, in the end, the man and the Godfather have reached an agreement. The Godfather's last line before the end of the scene is: "Someday, and that day may never come, I will call upon you to do a service for me." This line, which sums up the negotiation, subtly suggests that a Faustian pact has just been made and that the Godfather is the devil.

The screenwriters evoked the devil symbol once again towards the end of the story, when Michael, the new Godfather, attends the baptism of his nephew while his emissaries kill the heads of the five mafia families of New York. According to the baptism ritual, the priest asks Michael: "Do you renounce Satan?" And Michael replies: "Yes, I renounce him," at the very moment he becomes Satan through his actions. Michael then promises to protect the child of whom he literally becomes the godfather, even though, as the Godfather, he will have the child's father killed right after the baptism.

This confrontation scene should normally be followed by a scene of awareness. But since Michael has become diabolical, the screenwriters deliberately deprived him of awareness. This awareness is experienced by Kay, the Godfather's wife: from the next room, Kay observes Michael's emissaries gathering around him to congratulate him on his "ascension," and then the door leading to the new king of Hell is closed in her face.

You will notice the subtlety with which the symbol is applied to the first scene: no character uses the word "devil." It is through an ingenious construction of the scene that the screenwriters attached this symbol to the hero: the word "Godfather" is spoken at the end, just before the last line that vaguely suggests a Faustian pact. And it is thanks to the subtlety with which the symbol is introduced, and not despite it, that this technique has such a strong dramatic impact on the audience.

SYMBOLIC THEMES

The next step in creating the network of symbols is to find a symbol that encompasses moral debates in their entirety. This technique tends to produce a particularly intense concentration of meaning. It is therefore highly risky. Executed without subtlety, it makes the story ridiculously moralistic.

To create a symbolic theme, you need to find an image or object that represents a series of actions that harm others in some way. Or, better yet, find an image or object that represents two series of actions—two moral sequences—that are in conflict with each other.

THE SCARLET LETTER

(Nathaniel Hawthorne, 1850)

Hawthorne is a master of the symbolic theme. The scarlet A seems at first to represent the very simple moral argument that condemns adultery. It is only as the story progresses that we understand that this symbol actually represents two opposing moral arguments: the absolute, inflexible, and hypocritical argument that publicly condemns Hester, and the much more flexible and sincere morality in which Hester and her lover have lived privately.

BEAU GESTE

(novel by Percival Christopher Wren, screenplay by Robert Carson, 1939)

This story of three brothers who enlist in the French Foreign Legion illustrates a very important rule of the symbolic theme technique: this technique works best when used through the plot. At the beginning of the story, the three brothers are children playing "King Arthur." While hiding in armor, the eldest brother hears about a family jewel, a sapphire called the "Blue Water." A few years later, he steals this jewel and joins the Foreign Legion to save his aunt's and family's reputation. The knight's armor thus comes to symbolize a noble and selfless act, the beautiful gesture at the heart of the story's theme. By embedding this symbol in the plot, the screenwriters allowed the connection between the symbol and the theme to evolve and develop throughout the story instead of striking the audience with a single brutal blow.

THE GREAT GATSBY

(F. Scott Fitzgerald, 1925)

The Great Gatsby is the masterpiece of an author who was a master at attaching symbols to his themes. Fitzgerald created a network composed of three main symbols to crystallize a thematic sequence. These three symbols are the green light, the billboard in front of the wasteland, and the "fresh, green breast of the new world." The moral sequence unfolds as follows:

1. The green light represents modern America. But the original American dream has been perverted by the pursuit of material wealth and the golden girl who is only desirable because she is presented in a pretty package.

2. The billboard in front of the wasteland is the symbol of America hidden beneath the material surface, completely exhausted, and the mechanical waste created by materialistic America. The machine has devoured the garden.

3. The "fresh, green breast of the new world" represents the natural world of America, at the time of its discovery and endowed with immense potential, promising a new way of living, a second chance in the Garden of Eden.

Note that this symbolic sequence is not presented in chronological order, but in structural order. Fitzgerald introduces the "fresh, green breast of the new world" on the very last page of his novel. And this is a brilliant choice, because the rich nature and immense potential of the new world are made intensely real by the striking contrast with what men have done to this new world. And this contrast occurs at the very end of the story, after Nick's awareness. In this way, from a structural point of view, the symbol, and what it refers to, explodes in the audience's mind, like a striking thematic revelation. The technique is executed here masterfully. This is also what it means to create a work of art.

SYMBOLS OF THE STORY'S UNIVERSE

We discussed in Chapter 6 the many techniques that can be used to create the story's universe. Some of these techniques, like the miniature, are also techniques related to the symbol. In fact, the most important function of the symbol is probably to encompass an entire world, or a set of forces, in a single, easily understandable image.

Natural worlds such as the island, the mountain, the forest, and the ocean have inherent symbolic power. But if you want to amplify or modify the meaning that the audience generally associates with them, you can attach other symbols to them. For example, you can imbue these places with magical powers. This is the technique used for Prospero's island (The Tempest), Circe's island (The Odyssey), the forest in A Midsummer Night's Dream, Arden in As You Like It, the Forbidden Forest in the "Harry Potter" series, and the Lothlórien forest in The Lord of the Rings. Strictly speaking, magic is not a symbol in itself, but a set of forces of a different order on which the functioning of this world relies. That being said, creating a magical place produces the same effect as applying a symbol: concentrating meaning and charging the world with a field of forces that captures the audience's imagination.

You can create symbols that express this set of supernatural forces. Let's take the example of Moonstruck to better understand how.

MOONSTRUCK

(John Patrick Shanley, 1987)

John Patrick Shanley makes the moon a physical manifestation of the notion of destiny. This technique is particularly useful in the context of a love story, a genre where the important thing is not the individuals but the love they feel for each other. The audience must feel that it is a great love and that it would be tragic if it could not flourish and last. One way to create this feeling in the audience's mind is to show that love is necessary, that it is a matter of destiny, of forces far beyond these two mere mortals who are the main characters. To connect these characters—Loretta and Ronny—to the moon, Shanley begins, from the start of the story, by presenting Loretta as someone unlucky in love. This tends to create the feeling that higher forces are acting on her. Loretta's grandfather then explains to a group of old men that it is the moon that brings the woman to the man. At a dinner, Raymond, Loretta's uncle, recounts how the young woman's father, Cosmo, courted her mother, Rose: on a full moon night, Raymond woke up and saw through the window Cosmo, who, in the street, was watching Rose's room.

Shanley then uses the technique of cross-cutting to place the entire family under the power of the moon and establish a link between the moon and love. Several images follow one another quickly: Rose observes the full moon; Loretta and Ronny, after their first night together, go to the window to contemplate it in turn; and Raymond wakes up and tells his wife that Cosmo's moon is back. This prompts these two elderly characters, married for a long time, to make love. The sequence ends with the grandfather, and his dogs, howling at the moon hovering over the big city. The moon thus becomes the great matrix of love, bathing the entire city in its light and fairy dust.

You can also create a symbol when writing a story in which the world moves from one stage of society to another, for example from village to city. Social forces being extremely complex, creating a symbol, which tends to make these forces realistic, coherent, and understandable, can be very useful.

SHE WORE A YELLOW RIBBON

(book by James Warner Bellah, screenplay by Frank Nugent and Laurence Stallings, 1949)

The story presents the last working days of a captain of the American cavalry about to retire at an isolated outpost in the West around 1876. The end of the captain's professional life is paralleled with the end of the frontier (the world of villages) and the warrior values it embodied. To highlight this change, screenwriters Frank Nugent and Laurence Stallings relied on the symbol of the bison. A few days before the captain's departure, a grumpy old sergeant celebrates his own retirement in a saloon. And he says to the bartender: "That's it, the good old days are over... You don't know when the buffalo are coming back? You haven't heard about it?" But the audience knows that the buffalo will never return, nor will men like the sergeant and the captain, who belong to the "good old days."

ONCE UPON A TIME IN THE WEST

(original story by Dario Argento, Bernardo Bertolucci, and Sergio Leone, screenplay by Sergio Leone and Sergio Donati, 1968)

This great western opera opens with the murder of a man and his children in an isolated house. The woman he has just married arrives on the scene and discovers that she is a widow and the owner of a seemingly worthless house in the middle of the American desert. As she rummages through her late husband's belongings, she discovers a toy representing a city. This city-toy is both a miniature and a symbol of the future, a model of the city the deceased was thinking of when the railroad finally arrived at his doorstep.

CINEMA PARADISO

(original story by Giuseppe Tornatore, screenplay by Giuseppe Tornatore and Vanna Paoli, 1989)

The cinema of the title is both the symbol of the entire story and the symbol of the world. It is a cocoon where people gather to experience the magic of cinema, and thus create their community. But as the small town evolves and grows, the cinema declines, deteriorates, and ends up being replaced by a parking lot. It is the death of utopia and the death of the community, which has also disintegrated. This cinema is a good illustration of the symbol's ability to concentrate meaning and move an audience to tears.

THE MATRIX

(Andy Wachowski and Larry Wachowski, 1999)

and

NETWORK

(Paddy Chayefsky, 1976)
When you set your story in as vast and complex a framework as a society or an institution, the symbol becomes almost indispensable if you want to reach a wide audience. Both Matrix and Network owe their success to the symbols that represent their stories and the social worlds in which they unfold. The terms "matrix" and "network" both evoke unity but also a web that can trap and enslave. These symbols tell viewers they are entering a complex world made up of many forces, some of which will remain hidden. The audience thus knows it is useless to try to understand everything immediately, that the story will contain many surprising twists.

SYMBOLIC ACTIONS

Each action is normally part of a broader sequence of actions contained within the plot. Each action is like a car of a long train embodying the competition between hero and adversary for the goal. When you make an action symbolic, you connect it to another action or object and thus give it more meaning. Note that when you make an action symbolic, you somewhat highlight it from the sequence of the plot. You draw attention to it, telling the audience: "This action is particularly important; it expresses the theme or one of the characters in miniature." So be cautious.

WUTHERING HEIGHTS
