(novel by Emily Brontë, 1847, screenplay by Charles MacArthur and Ben Hecht, 1939)

When Heathcliff pretends to fight the black knight for Cathy in their "castle" on the moor, he expresses their world of imaginary romance and Cathy's desire to live in a world of wealth and nobility. Heathcliff also stages, in miniature, the entire story: his confrontation against the well-born Linton for Cathy's hand.

WITNESS

(Earl W. Wallace and William Kelley, based on an original story by William Kelley, 1985)

By participating in the construction of a barn and exchanging glances with Rachel, John signals his desire to leave the violent world of the police and create a romantic bond in a peaceful community.

A TALE OF TWO CITIES

(Charles Dickens, 1859)

Like Christ on the cross, Sydney Carton sacrifices his life for another by being guillotined. "It is a far, far better thing that I do, than I have ever done; it is a far, far better rest that I go to than I have ever known."

GUNGA DIN

(poem by Rudyard Kipling, story by Ben Hecht and Charles MacArthur, screenplay by Joel Sayre and Fred Guiol, 1939)

The Indian "coolie" Gunga Din desperately wants to join the regiment of the three British soldiers he admires. During the final confrontation, when his comrade-in-arms is wounded and captured, Din blows his bugle, putting himself in mortal danger but saving the regiment from falling into a trap.

SYMBOLIC OBJECTS

Symbolic objects rarely exist alone in a story because, alone, such an object would struggle to refer to something else. However, a network of objects connected by a form of overarching principle can offer a deep and complex configuration of meaning, which usually tends to support the theme.

If you wish to create a network of symbolic objects, start by returning to the guiding principle of your story. The guiding principle is indeed the cement that can allow you to bring together the different symbolic objects to form a unified whole. And in this way, each object will not only refer to another object but also to the entire set of symbolic objects in the story.

You can create a network of symbolic objects in any story, but you should know that these networks are more easily identifiable in certain genres, namely myth, horror, and western. These genres have given birth to so many stories that their rules have been perfectly defined. And this includes objects, which have been used so often that they have become immediately identifiable metaphors. These objects are prefabricated symbols whose meaning the audience immediately understands consciously or unconsciously.

Let's now examine the networks of symbolic objects from stories that can be considered the best representatives of these highly metaphorical genres.

The Myth Symbol Network

Myth is the oldest form of story and, to this day, the most popular. The myths of ancient Greece, which are one of the pillars of Western thought, are allegorical and metaphorical, and you must understand how they work if you wish to use them as a basis for your own story.

Myth always presents two levels of being: divine and human. Do not make the classic mistake of thinking that this model corresponds solely to the Greeks' idea of how the world worked. The two levels of these stories do not serve to express belief in gods who would govern human life. The gods represent what in man allows him to achieve excellence or enlightenment. The "gods" are actually an ingenious psychological model in which a network of characters represents distinctive traits and ways of acting that can serve as models or deterrents.

In addition to this set of highly symbolic characters, myth uses a clearly imposed set of symbolic objects. When these stories were told, the audience knew that these symbols always represented something else and knew exactly what they meant. Storytellers achieved their goal by juxtaposing these key symbols throughout the story.

What is absolutely essential to understand about these metaphorical symbols is that they also represent something at the heart of the hero. Below are some of the key symbols of myth and the meaning they likely had for the ancient audience. Obviously, despite their highly metaphorical nature, these symbols do not have completely determined meanings; symbols always retain a certain ambiguity.

• Journey: The path of life

• Labyrinth: Confusion in the search for the path to enlightenment

• Garden: Harmony with the laws of nature; harmony with oneself and others

• Tree: Tree of life

• Animals (horses, birds, snakes): Models on the path to enlightenment or the Underworld

• Ladder: Steps of enlightenment

• Underworld: Unexplored region of the self, world of the dead

• Talisman (sword, bow, shield, armor): Good deed

THE ODYSSEY

(Homer)

The Odyssey is, in our view, the most artistic and influential Greek myth in the entire history of storytelling. And Homer's use of symbolic objects is one of the reasons for this success. To better understand the techniques related to symbolism in this work, we must start by returning, as always, to the characters.

The first thing to note about the characters is that Homer has moved from the powerful warrior who fights to the death (The Iliad) to the cunning warrior who seeks to return home and live. Odysseus is an excellent fighter, but he is a much better seeker, thinker (manipulator), and lover.

This change in the hero's personality leads to a change in symbolic theme: from matriarchy to patriarchy. The Odyssey is not a story in which the king must die and the mother remain; Odysseus returns and reclaims his throne. As in most great stories, the hero undergoes a transformation: when he returns home, Odysseus is the same man as at the beginning, but greater. At least, that is what his moral decision suggests: by returning home, Odysseus chooses mortality over immortality.

Among the central oppositions of symbolic characters, the most important is undoubtedly that of man vs. woman. Unlike Odysseus, who learns by traveling, Penelope stays put and learns by dreaming. She also makes decisions based on her dreams.

Homer constructs the network of symbolic objects in The Odyssey based on the characters and theme. This is why this network includes only male objects: axe, mast, oar, and bow. For the characters, each of these objects represents a version of the right path to follow and the right action to take. These symbols contrast with the tree that supports the marital bed of Odysseus and Penelope. This tree is the tree of life and represents the idea that marriage is organic: it flourishes or withers. When a man ventures too far or too long in his quest for glory (the warrior's ultimate value), marriage and life itself die.

The Horror Symbol Network

Horror is a genre based on the fear that the inhuman will enter the human community. It explores what lies beyond the boundaries of civilized life—boundaries separating the living from the dead, the rational from the irrational, the moral from the immoral—with the almost inevitable result of destruction. The horror story poses the most fundamental question of all: what is human and what is inhuman? This is why it is very often associated with religion. In America and Europe, the religious basis of horror stories is Christianity. Consequently, the network of characters and symbols in such stories is almost completely determined by Christian cosmology.

In most horror stories, the hero is passive, and the main adversary, who drives the action, is the devil or one of his emissaries. The devil is the embodiment of evil, the bad father, who will lead humans to eternal damnation if not stopped in time. In such stories, the moral debate is always reduced to its simplest binary expression: the fight of good against evil.

The symbol network therefore also begins with a binary opposition, and the visual and symbolic expression of good against evil is light against darkness. The first symbol, on the side of light, is of course the cross, which has the power to repel Satan himself. The symbols of darkness are often linked to animals. In pre-Christian myths, animals such as the horse, deer, bull, ram, and serpent were symbols of ideals that could lead man to perform a good deed and achieve a higher form of self. In Christian symbolism, these animals represent diabolical action. This is why the devil has horns. Animals such as the wolf, monkey, bat, and serpent represent the disappearance of sanctions, the passion that devours the body, and the path of evil. And these symbols exert all their power in darkness.

DRACULA

(novel by Bram Stoker, play by Hamilton Deane and John L. Balderston, screenplay by Garrett Fort, 1931)

The vampire Dracula, an eternal being, is the ultimate creature of the night. He lives off the blood of men he kills or poisons to make them his slaves. He sleeps in a coffin and would burn and die if exposed to sunlight.

Vampires are extremely sensual. They gaze longingly at the bare necks of their victims and eventually succumb to their intense desire to bite that neck and suck the blood. In vampire stories like Dracula, sex equals death, and the blurring of the boundary between life and death leads to a fate worse than death itself: living in endless purgatory, wandering forever in the dark night.

Dracula has the power to transform into a bat or a wolf, and he usually lives in ruins populated by rats. As a count, a member of the aristocracy, he is a typically European character. Count Dracula is the representative of an aging and corrupt aristocracy that parasitizes the rest of society.

Dracula is extremely powerful at night. But he can be stopped by someone who knows his secret. He shrinks when he sees a crucifix and burns when sprinkled with holy water.

Among other classic horror stories that play on this network of symbols are The Exorcist and The Omen. Carrie is based on the same network but reverses its meaning. There, Christian symbols are associated with bigotry and stupidity, and Carrie kills her evangelical mother by teleporting a crucifix straight into her heart.

The Western Symbol Network

The western is the last form of the great creation myths because the American frontier was the last habitable frontier on earth. This form of story is America's national myth and has been written and rewritten thousands of times. This is why its symbol network is highly metaphorical. The western is the story of millions of individuals traveling west, taming the wild and building towns. These individuals are led by a solitary warrior hero who has the power to defeat the barbarians and secure the land so that pioneers can establish their villages. Like Moses, this warrior can lead his people to the Promised Land but cannot enter it himself. He is doomed to remain single and alone, to travel forever in the wild, until death, his own or that of nature.

The western reached its peak between 1880 and 1960. This genre has therefore always dealt with a time and place that were already in the past. But it is important to remember that as a creation myth, the western has also always been a vision of the future, a stage of national development that Americans collectively wanted to embody, even if it was set in the past, and therefore could not be created for good.

The key ideas of the western are the conquest of the land, the annihilation or transformation of "inferior" or "barbaric" races, the spread of Christianity and civilization, the transformation of nature into wealth, and the creation of the American nation. The general guiding principle of the western is as follows: the entire process of human history is repeated on the blank slate of America's wild and virgin nature, thus becoming for men the last chance to regain the lost paradise.

Every national story is a religious story that relies on the definition of certain rituals and values and depends on the intensity with which people believe in it. It is therefore not surprising that such a national and religious story generates such a highly metaphorical network of symbols.

The first symbol in the western network is the rider. He is both hunter and warrior and represents the very embodiment of warrior culture. He also owes some of his distinctive signs to the English national myth of King Arthur. He is a knight by nature, an ordinary man of pure and noble character who adheres to a moral code based on courtesy and justice (known as the Code of the West).

The western hero does not wear armor, but he always carries the second great symbol of the network: his six-shooter. The six-shooter represents mechanical force; it is a "sword" of justice whose power is multiplied. Due to his code of honor and the values of his warrior culture, the cowboy never draws his weapon first. And he must always administer justice in the open street, where everyone can see him.

Like the horror story, the western is always an expression of the binary values of good and evil, signaled by the third major symbol of the network: the hat. The hero wears a white hat; the villain a black hat.

The fourth symbol of the western is the badge, which itself takes the form of another symbol, the star. The western hero is always the champion of good, sometimes at his own expense, because his violence often ostracizes him. He may temporarily join the community when he becomes an official lawman. And he enforces the law not only on the wild world outside but also on the one at the heart of each person.

The last major symbol of the western is the fence. It is always a wooden fence, thin and fragile, representing the weak control that the new civilization exerts over the wild world of nature and the savagery of human nature.

This network of symbols is used with great finesse in The Searchers, Stagecoach, Red River, and the most schematic and metaphorical of all westerns, Shane.

SHANE

(novel by Jack Schaefer, screenplay by A. B. Guthrie Jr and Jack Sher, 1953)

Due to its thematic aspect, Shane presents an easily identifiable network of symbols, but these symbols draw so much attention from viewers that they always feel like they are "watching a classic western." This is the main risk when using highly metaphorical symbols.

That being said, Shane takes the mythical form of the western and pushes it to the extreme of its logic. The story is about a mysterious stranger who, when first seen, is already on his way. He descends from the mountain on horseback, makes a stop, then returns to the mountain. This film is part of a subgenre we call "traveler angel stories." This subgenre includes some westerns, but also detective stories (the Hercule Poirot stories), comedies (Crocodile Dundee, Amélie, Chocolat, Good Morning Vietnam), and musicals (Mary Poppins, The Music Man). Traveler angel stories feature a hero who enters a community in crisis, helps its members solve problems, then leaves to help another community. In its western version, the traveler angel story features Shane, a warrior traveler angel who fights other warriors (the ranchers) to secure the land so that farmers and villagers can build their homes.

Shane also includes a highly symbolic network of characters: the angelic hero vs. the evil bandit; the farmer family head (named Joseph) vs. the cruel, single, graying rancher; the ideal woman and mother (named Marian); and the child, a boy who idolizes the man who is so skilled with a revolver. These abstract characters are endowed with almost no individual characteristics. Shane, for example, is haunted by a specter from his past related to guns, but it is never explained. The result is that the characters are at best very seductive metaphors.

All the standard symbols of the western are there, in their purest form. The revolver is crucial in all westerns. But in Shane, it is placed at the heart of the theme. The film poses a question that allows each character to be judged: do you have the courage to use this weapon? The ranchers hate the farmers because they erect fences. The farmers fight against the ranchers to build their town, their courts, and their churches. Shane wears light buckskin clothing; the evil bandit is dressed in black. The farmers buy tools and building materials in a store. But this store has a door that opens onto the saloon where the ranchers drink, fight, and kill. When in the store, Shane tries to change his life and start a family, but he gets drawn into the saloon and returns to his former life as a solitary warrior highly skilled in shooting.

All this does not mean that Shane is a bad story. If this film has a certain power, it is precisely because of its network of symbols so pure and clear. There are no frills. But it is also for this reason that the film seems schematic and its moral debate appears to be based on a one-sided moral philosophy, as is very often the case in religious stories.

TECHNIQUE: INVERTING THE SYMBOL NETWORK

The great weakness of a prefabricated metaphorical symbol network is that it is so obvious and predictable that the story becomes a kind of schema for the audience, rather than a living body. But this weakness offers an excellent opportunity. You can indeed use the audience's knowledge of the genre and its symbol network to invert the codes. This technique involves using all the symbols of the network and modifying them to give them a meaning very different from the audience's expectations. This tends to force the reader or viewer to rethink all their preconceived ideas. You can do this with any form of story whose symbols are well known. When working in a specific genre like myth, horror, or western, this is called "subverting the genre."

JOHN MCCABE

(novel by Edmund Naughton, screenplay by Robert Altman and Brian McKay, 1971)

John McCabe is an excellent film with a brilliant screenplay. Much of this success comes from the use of the technique of inverting the classic symbols of the western. This inversion arises from an extension of the traditional western theme. Instead of presenting characters who bring civilization to the wild West, John McCabe is about an entrepreneur who builds a town in the wild West but is destroyed by the business world.

The inversion of symbols begins with the main character. McCabe is a gambler and a dandy who makes his fortune by opening a brothel. It is through the money from sex that he manages to create a community in the wild. The second main character, McCabe's great love, is a madam who smokes opium.

The visual sub-worlds also invert the classic symbols. The town is not the usual geometric grid with clapboard facades set in the arid plains of the Southwest. It is a lineup of makeshift dwellings, of wood and tent, embedded in the lush and rainy forest of the Northwest. It is not a community whose members bustle under the benevolent gaze of a marshal but a fragmented and half-built town, whose inhabitants, isolated and nonchalant individuals, scrutinize all strangers with suspicion.
The key symbolic action of the western is the demonstration of strength, and this too is undermined. The classic demonstration of strength takes place in the middle of the main street, where all the town's inhabitants can observe it. The cowboy hero waits for the villain to draw first, still manages to defeat him, and reaffirms the power of good, law, and order for the entire community. In McCabe, the hero, who is anything but a lawman, is chased throughout the town by three killers during a blinding snowstorm. None of the town's inhabitants see McCabe or care that he is risking his life for the town's good. They are all busy extinguishing the flames of a fire that has broken out in a church that no one attends.

McCabe also alters the meaning of the symbolic objects of the classic western. The law does not exist. The church is empty. During the demonstration of strength, one of the killers hides behind a building and shoots McCabe with a rifle. McCabe, who, despite appearances, is not dead, retaliates by shooting the villain between the eyes with a derringer (a weapon reserved for women in classic westerns!) that he had hidden. Instead of the leather pants and wide-brimmed white hat of the cowboy, McCabe wears an East Coast suit and a bowler hat.

By choosing to undermine the genre, McCabe offers us excellent techniques for creating something new from old metaphorical symbols. It is an excellent lesson in writing and a reference in American cinema.

Examples of Symbol Networks

The best way to learn the techniques of the symbol network is to observe them in application in works. By examining these different stories, you will notice that these techniques also apply to a wide range of forms.

EXCALIBUR

(novel, Le Morte d’Arthur, by Thomas Malory, screenplay by Rospo Pallenberg and John Boorman, 1981)

If we consider the western as the national myth of the United States, then the legend of King Arthur is the national myth of England. Its power and appeal are so vast that it has influenced hundreds of thousands of other stories throughout the West. And it is for this sole reason that we, modern storytellers, must understand how its network of symbols works. As always, we will start with the symbolic characters.

King Arthur is not simply a man and not simply a king. He is the modern centaur, the metal horseman. And as such, he can be considered the first superman, the Man of Steel, the male pushed to his extreme. He is the embodiment of the warrior culture par excellence. He represents courage, strength, good deeds, and justice rendered by public combat. Ironically, despite this extreme masculinity, he respects the code of chivalry that places women on a pedestal of absolute purity. This transforms the entire female gender into a symbol divided according to the Christian binary opposition of the Madonna and the whore.

King Arthur also symbolizes the modern leader in conflict. In Camelot, he creates a perfect community based on purity, a community immediately destroyed when his wife falls in love with the best and purest of his knights. The conflict between love and duty is one of the great moral oppositions of storytelling, and King Arthur embodies it perfectly.

Arthur's ally, Merlin, is the quintessential wizard-mentor. This character is a remnant of the pre-Christian vision of magic, and as such, he represents the knowledge of the hidden forces of nature. Merlin is the ultimate artisan-artist of nature and human nature, proof that human nature finds its roots in nature itself. His spells and advice are always based on a deep understanding of the unique needs and desires of the individual he faces.

Arthur's adversaries possess a symbolic character that hundreds of writers have drawn inspiration from over the centuries. His son, Mordred, is the evil child whose very name evokes death. Mordred's ally is his mother, the fairy Morgana, an evil witch.

The knights, like Arthur, are supermen. They are above ordinary mortals not only because of their warrior talents but also because of their pure character and greatness of soul. They respect the code of chivalry and seek the Holy Grail, through which they can enter the Kingdom of God. And on their quest, they behave like the Good Samaritan by helping those in need and proving the purity of their hearts through their good deeds.

Excalibur, along with many other versions of the legend of King Arthur, is filled with symbolic worlds and objects. The first symbolic place is Camelot, the utopian community whose members have repressed their human desire for individual glory for the tranquility and happiness of all. This place takes on an even more symbolic dimension with the Round Table, which is the republic of the great. Around this table, all the knights have an equal place alongside their king.

Excalibur is the name of another determining symbolic object of the legend of King Arthur: the sword. It is the virile symbol of just action, and only the legitimate king with a pure heart can pull it from the stone and wield it to form the ideal community.

The symbols of the story of King Arthur have permeated our culture and can be found in stories as diverse as Star Wars, The Lord of the Rings, Hope and Glory, A Connecticut Yankee in King Arthur's Court, Fisher King, and hundreds of American westerns. If you wish to use the symbolism of King Arthur, be sure to modify its meaning to make it original and unique to your story.

USUAL SUSPECTS

(Christopher McQuarrie, 1995)

Usual Suspects is a unique story in which the main character creates, as the story unfolds, his own symbolic character using the techniques we discussed earlier. The aptly named Verbal appears to be a small-time crook and ally, while he is actually both the hero, an expert criminal (the main adversary), and a storyteller. By telling the customs inspector what happened, he constructs a terrifying and cruel character named Keyser Söze. He attaches the symbol of the devil to this character, so much so that Keyser Söze is endowed with mythical power and the mere mention of his name is enough to strike terror. At the end of the story, the audience understands that Verbal is Keyser Söze, and that he is an expert criminal partly because he is an expert storyteller. Usual Suspects is an example of excellent storytelling supported by top-level symbol creation.

STAR WARS

(George Lucas, 1977)

If Star Wars was so popular, it is largely because this film is based on the technique of the symbolic theme. This seemingly simple fantasy adventure story has a very strong theme, concentrated in the symbol of the lightsaber. In this technologically advanced world where people travel at the speed of light, both the hero and the adversary fight with a saber. Obviously, this is not realistic. But it is realistic enough in this world for the saber to become an object capable of carrying a thematic charge. The lightsaber symbolizes the samurai's code of training and conduct that can be used for good or evil. The importance of this symbolic object and the theme it represents in the worldwide success of Star Wars cannot be overstated.

FORREST GUMP

(Novel by Winston Groom, screenplay by Eric Roth, 1994)

The authors of Forrest Gump used two objects to represent the theme of their story: the feather and the box of chocolates. At first glance, one might criticize the choice of these symbols that so obviously evoke the theme of the story. In this everyday world, a feather floats down from the sky and lands at Forrest's feet. Of course, the feather represents Forrest's freedom of spirit and his open and relaxed way of life. The box of chocolates is an even more obvious symbol. Forrest states: "My mama always said, 'Life is like a box of chocolates. You never know what you're gonna get.'" It is an openly thematic statement about the right way to behave in life connected to a metaphor.

But these two symbols attached to the themes of the story work much better than they seem at first glance. And the reasons for this success are instructive. First, Forrest Gump is a form of myth connected to a drama, and the story spans nearly forty years. Thus, like the feather, the narrative zigzags through space and time without taking an apparent direction, except for the general line of History with a capital H. Then, its hero is a simple character who thinks in the form of easy-to-remember platitudes. A "normal" character who would declare right away that life is like a box of chocolates would seem verbose. But simple Forrest is charmed by this pretty intuition, which comes from his beloved mama, and the audience is charmed with him.

ULYSSES

(James Joyce, 1922)

Joyce pushed the idea of the storyteller as both magician, symbol creator, and enigma creator further than any other author. This has advantages but also disadvantages, notably shifting the reader's reaction from emotion to pure intellect. When you present literally thousands of subtle, even obscure symbols in a thousand complex ways, you force the reader to become a scientist of storytelling or a determined literary detective willing to step back as much as possible to understand how this puzzle was constructed by some god. Like Citizen Kane (though for different reasons), Joyce's Ulysses is a story admired for its techniques but difficult to love. Let us therefore study the symbolic techniques used in it.

Symbols of the Story and Symbolic Characters

Joyce builds his network of symbolic characters by starting to superimpose the characters of The Odyssey, the story of Christ, and Hamlet onto his narrative. He adds to these references to major character networks, references to real people, and emblematic characters from Ireland's past. This strategy has a number of advantages. First, it connects the characters to the theme: Joyce attempts to create a natural or humanist religion from the actions of his characters. His ordinary characters – Bloom, Stephen, and Molly – take on a heroic, even divine aspect, not just because of their actions, but through their constant references to other characters such as Ulysses, Jesus, and Hamlet.

This technique also allows Joyce to place the characters of Ulysses within a great cultural tradition while showing them rebelling against this tradition and detaching themselves as unique individuals. This is exactly the character development line Stephen strives to follow throughout the story. Oppressed by his Catholic upbringing and England's domination over Ireland, but not seeking to destroy all spirituality, Stephen is in search of a path that could allow him to be himself and a true artist.

The other advantage of references to characters from other stories is that it provides Joyce with a signaling network of characters that extends throughout the novel. This is extremely useful when writing a story as long and complex as this one. In addition to providing a guiding principle, the character signaling allowed Joyce to assess how his main characters change throughout the story by referring to these same symbolic characters – Ulysses, Christ, Hamlet – in different ways.

Symbolic Actions and Objects

Joyce applies these same techniques of symbolic character to the objects and actions of the story. He constantly compares the actions of Bloom, Stephen, and Molly to those of Ulysses, Telemachus, and Penelope, and the effect on the reader is both heroic and ironic. Bloom defeats the Cyclops and then escapes from the dark cavern of a bar. Stephen is haunted by the ghost of his mother just like Ulysses, who meets his mother in Hades, and Hamlet, who is visited by the specter of his father. Like Penelope, Molly stays at home, but unlike the loyal Penelope, she becomes famous for her infidelity.

The symbolic objects present in Ulysses form a vast network of "sacred" things in Joyce's naturalistic and everyday "religion." Both Stephen and Bloom leave their homes without taking their keys. Stephen broke his glasses the day before. But while his physical sight is diminished, he gains the opportunity to become a visionary, to form his artist's vision during this one-day journey. An advertisement for "Plum's canned meat" – "A home without it is not really a home" – evokes the absence of the sacred act of sex between Bloom and his wife, and the harm it has caused to their home. Wielding his cane like a sword, Stephen breaks the chandelier of the brothel and frees himself from the past that holds him like a prison. Bloom considers the Catholic host as a lollipop given to believers, but he and Stephen experience a real moment of communion when they have coffee together, then a chocolate in Bloom's house.

CREATING SYMBOLS – WRITING EXERCISE NO. 6

• Story Symbol Is there a symbol that could express your premise, the key twists of your story, its central theme, or the entire structure? Review your premise, theme, and the one-sentence description of your story's universe. Then describe in one sentence the main symbols of your story.

• Symbolic Characters Determine the symbols you will attach to your hero and other characters in your story. To do this, follow these steps:

1. Consider the entire network of characters before creating the symbol for each character.

2. Start with the opposition between the hero and his main adversary.

3. Define a specific aspect of the character or an emotion you want this character to evoke in your audience.

4. Consider creating an opposition of symbols within the character.

5. Repeat the symbol, associating it with the character in question, several times throughout the story.

6. Slightly modify the details of this symbol each time it repeats in the story.

• Types of Characters Consider connecting one or more of your characters to a particular type of character, god, animal, or machine.

• Symbol of Character Transformation Is there a symbol you could connect to your hero's transformation? If so, examine the opening scenes in which you expressed your hero's weakness and need, as well as the ending scenes that correspond to his realization.

• Symbolic Theme Look for a symbol that could encompass the theme of your story. This symbol should represent a series of actions with moral consequences, or better yet, two series of moral actions that are in conflict.

• Symbolic World Determine the symbols you wish to attach to the various elements of the story's universe, i.e., natural settings, man-made spaces, technology, and time.

• Symbolic Actions Does your story have one or more actions that deserve symbolic treatment? Determine the symbol you can attach to each of them to make them stand out.

• Symbolic Objects Create a network of symbolic objects based on the guiding principle of your story. Ensure that each object aligns with the theme. Then determine the objects you wish to give more meaning to.

• Development of Symbols Make a chart and note how each of the symbols you use evolves throughout the story.

To better understand how to put these techniques into practice, let's look at The Lord of the Rings.

THE LORD OF THE RINGS

(J. R. R. Tolkien, 1954-1955)

The Lord of the Rings is nothing less than a modern cosmology and mythology of England. This story brings together myth, legend, and romance as well as references to stories and symbols from Greek and Norse mythology, Christianity, fairy tales, the legend of King Arthur, and the wandering knight. The Lord of the Rings is an allegorical story in the sense that, as Tolkien said, it can be easily applied to the modern world and era. "Allegorical" means, among other things, that the characters, worlds, actions, and objects are, by necessity, highly metaphorical. This does not mean they are not unique or created by the author. It means that these symbols are references echoing earlier symbols, often deeply embedded in the readers' minds.

• Story Symbol The story's symbol is evoked in its very title. The ring is an object of unlimited power that everyone seeks to obtain. Whoever possesses it becomes a lord with divine powers. But this lord will inevitably be destructive. The ring is a great temptation that can lead people away from their moral and happy lives. And its seductive power is endless.

• Symbolic Characters The main strength of this incredibly textured story is its rich network of symbolic characters. It is not simply man vs man, man vs animal, or man vs machine. These characters are defined and differentiated through the opposition of good and evil, by their levels of power (god, wizard, man, Hobbit), and by the species to which they belong (man, elf, dwarf, Orc, goblin, Ent, and ghost). Myth works through character types, and this is one of the reasons it presents an epic scope but little subtlety in depicting people. Here, on the contrary, with this complex and rich network of character types, Tolkien and his audience have their cake and eat it too. This is an important lesson for any author using symbolic characters, especially in stories based on myths.

In Tolkien's character oppositions, good is symbolized by Gandalf and Sam, the self-sacrificing characters; by the warrior-king Aragorn who can both heal and kill; and by those who are one with nature and have become experts in self-mastery rather than mastery over others: Galadriel and Tom Bombadil. Tolkien's hero is not the great warrior but the little "man," the Hobbit Frodo Baggins, who, through his greatness of spirit, becomes the most heroic character of all. Like Leopold Bloom in Joyce's Ulysses, Frodo is a new type of mythical hero, defined not by the strength of his weapons but by the depth of his humanity.

The adversaries are also endowed with great symbolic power. Morgoth is the original evil character who precedes this story and is part of the past Tolkien created for The Lord of the Rings. Like Mordred in the legend of King Arthur, Maugrim in The Chronicles of Narnia, and Voldemort in the "Harry Potter" series (English writers love to name their villains with names containing the syllable "mor," probably because this syllable evokes the French word "mort"), Morgoth evokes for the reader the first anti-God, Satan, and his name and actions are associated with death. Sauron is the main adversary in The Lord of the Rings; he is evil because he seeks absolute power and wishes to use it to destroy Middle-earth. Saruman is a turncoat character: a wizard sent to fight Sauron, he is corrupted by the lure of absolute power. The other adversaries – Gollum, the Nazgûl, the Orcs, Shelob the spider, and the Balrog – are different symbolic expressions of envy, hatred, violence, and destruction.

• Symbolic Theme As always in good stories (and as in any allegory), all elements are based on the thematic line and oppositions. In Tolkien, this translates into a Christian-inspired thematic structure that highlights the battle of good against evil. Evil is defined here by the taste and use of power. Good comes from caring for living things, and its highest expression is sacrifice, especially giving one's own life to save another's.

• Symbolic Worlds The visual sub-worlds of The Lord of the Rings are as rich and symbolic as its network of characters. These worlds are both natural and supernatural. And the man-made spaces themselves are imbued with the natural environment, of which they are an extension. Like the characters, these symbolic sub-worlds are placed in opposition. In the world of the forest, there is the magnificent and harmonious Lothlórien and the forest of the tree-beings that are the Ents vs the evil Mirkwood. The forest worlds, the good, are also placed in opposition to the mountain world, the residence of the forces of evil. Sauron rules from his lair on the mountain, Mordor, located behind the massive gate of Morannon (more "mor"). It is on the Misty Mountains that the caves of Moria are found, and the heroes will descend into these "Hells." Frodo crosses the Dead Marshes, a cemetery for those fallen in battle.

The "human" communities are also an expression of this natural symbolism. Just as Lothlórien is a utopia built among the trees, Rivendell is a utopia built among water and plants. The Shire, home of the Hobbits, is a village erected within an agricultural and domesticated world. These communities contrast with the mountain fortresses of Mordor, Isengard, and Helm's Deep, founded on raw power.

• Symbolic Objects The Lord of the Rings is a story based on the quest for symbolic objects and their possession, and these objects are often drawn from the earth or forged in fire. The most important of these is, of course, the One Ring that Sauron forged in the fire of a volcano, Mount Doom. This ring symbolizes the desire for false values and absolute power, and whoever wears it will inevitably and completely become evil and corrupt. There is also another circular symbol: the Eye of Sauron, which can see everything from the top of the Dark Tower and aids Sauron in his quest for the ring.
Like Excalibur, Andúril, whose name literally means "the Flame of the West," is the sword of good deeds that can only be wielded by the rightful heir to the throne. While Excalibur is embedded in a stone, Andúril was broken and must be reforged for Aragorn to defeat the forces of evil and reclaim the throne. Aragorn is an irreplaceable warrior-king thanks to his mastery of the plant Athelas, which has the power to heal. Like Achilles, Aragorn is a highly skilled fighter, but also a being in harmony with nature and a representative of life.

Of course, these are just a few of the symbols used by Tolkien in the epic The Lord of the Rings. By studying them carefully, you will learn to master most of the techniques for creating symbols.

1. The original title is One Flew Over the Cuckoo’s Nest. The French translation, almost literal, could not convey the pun on the term cuckoo, which means both "coucou," the bird, and "fool" or "crazy" (Translator's Note).

2. As in French, the term network can refer to both a web and the broadcasting stations and relays of radio or television (Translator's Note).

3. Alien primarily means "stranger," and by extension, "extraterrestrial" (Translator's Note).

4. Steerforth could be literally translated as "Straight ahead" (Translator's Note).

5. The original title is The Godfather. This term, which indeed means "godfather," is composed of two words God and father. In the English language, the godfather is therefore the father given by God. (Translator's Note).

6. Scrooge means "miser" (Translator's Note).

7. Heep means "itching powder" (Translator's Note).

8. "Little Tim" (Translator's Note).

9. "Matrix" and "Network" (Translator's Note).

10. In French in the text (Translator's Note).

– 8 –

The Plot

Of all the major skills essential to storytelling, the plot is the most underestimated. Most authors understand that characters and dialogue play a decisive role in the story, even if they don't always know how to write them correctly. But when they think about the plot, they just tell themselves they'll think about it at the right time—and of course, that moment never comes.

Creating the plot involves interweaving the characters and their actions throughout the story. It is therefore an eminently complex task. The plot must be extremely detailed, and yet its different parts must form a whole. A problem with just one of the plot's events can be enough to make the story collapse like a house of cards.

It's not surprising that plot creation techniques—such as the "three-act structure"—that don't take into account the story as a whole or the various detailed threads of the narrative, fail miserably. Authors who rely on the old techniques of the three-act structure always complain about having problems with the second act. This is because the techniques they use to create their plot are fundamentally flawed. The mechanical and simplistic techniques of the three-act structure fail to provide an accurate map that would show how to weave a plot to get out of the difficult middle section of the story.

If many authors underestimate the importance of the plot, it's because they have a misconception of it. Often it is believed that the plot is the same as the story or that the plot simply follows the actions of a hero seeking to achieve a goal. Or that the plot is the way the story is told.

The story is much broader than the plot. The story is composed of all the subsystems of its body—the premise, the characters, the moral debate, the universe, the symbols, the plot, the scenes, and the dialogues—that work together. The story is "a complex of multifaceted forms and meanings where the narrative line [the plot] is just one aspect among many."

The plot weaves beneath the surface the various lines of actions, or sets of events, so that the story unfolds gradually from its beginning to its end through its middle. To be more precise, the plot follows the complex dance of the hero and all his adversaries as they fight for the same goal. It combines the events and the way they are revealed to the audience.

KEY POINT: Your plot depends on how you hide and reveal information. Creating a plot is "masterfully managing suspense and mystery, artfully guiding the reader in a highly elaborate space [...] always filled with signs to decipher and threatened, until the end, with being misinterpreted."

THE ORGANIC PLOT

A plot describes a sequence of events: this happens, then that, and then that. But a simple sequence of events is not a good plot. Because there is no design, no guiding principle that tells you which events should be told and the order in which they should be. A good plot is always organic. This means in particular:

• An organic plot presents the actions that lead to the transformation of the hero, or explains why this transformation is impossible.

• Each of the events is causally correlated with the others.

• Each event is essential.

• Each action is proportionate in its length and rhythm.

• The events of the plot seem to naturally emanate from the main character, rather than being imposed on him by the author. Imposed plots seem mechanical, the workings of the story being too apparent. The characters seem emptied of their content and humanity, and look like puppets or pawns. A plot naturally emanating from the hero is not simply a plot concocted by the hero. It is a plot that is in line with his desire and his ability to develop a... plot!

• The sequence of events has a unity, gives the impression of a whole. As Edgar Allan Poe said, in a good plot "no part can be moved, under penalty of collapsing the whole."

TYPES OF PLOT

The idea of an organic plot is difficult to grasp, but much less so to apply. This is partly because creating a plot always involves a contradiction. The plot is something that is shaped by creating actions and events from nothing and then connecting them in a certain order. And yet, the events of this plot must appear as necessary steps that develop on their own.

Historically, one can say that, in general, in the plot, we have moved from emphasizing action to emphasizing understanding (action and understanding being the two "legs" that allow any story to move forward). The earliest plots, which relied on the form of myth, presented a main character undertaking a series of heroic actions encouraging the audience to imitate him. Later, the plot, relying on an expanded version of the detective story, began to present a hero to an audience who did not know what was happening or had difficulty grasping everything, his task being to determine the truth about the various events and characters.

Let's now study some of the main types of plot to understand the different means that can be implemented to create a sequence of events and an organic plan.

The Journey Plot

The first plot creation strategy comes from myth tellers, whose main technique is the journey. With this form of plot, the hero undertakes a journey during which he successively encounters a number of adversaries. After defeating them one by one, he returns home. The journey is supposed to be organic (1) because a single person creates the narrative line and (2) because the journey provides a physical manifestation of the character's transformation. Each time the hero defeats one of his adversaries, he may experience a mini-transformation. But he only experiences his great transformation (his realization) when he returns home to discover what he already had within himself; that is, what he "had in his belly."

The problem with the journey plot is that it generally fails to fully achieve its organic potential. First, the hero, most often, does not experience the slightest transformation when he defeats each of his adversaries. After each victory, he simply continues on his way. So each confrontation only repeats the same high point and gives the impression of being episodic, not organic.

The other reason the journey plot rarely becomes organic is that the hero covers so much space and time during his journey. Due to the extension and many twists of the story, the author has a hard time bringing back later the characters the hero meets in the first part of the story, and doing so naturally and credibly.

Over the years, authors, painfully aware of the inherent problems of the journey plot, have tried various techniques to solve them. In The History of Tom Jones, for example, which is a comedic travel story, Henry Fielding relies on two major structural corrections. First, at the beginning of the story, he hides the true identity of the hero, as well as that of a few other characters. This allows him, later, to return to these already familiar characters to explore them more deeply. Fielding applies the twist technique, also called the "discovery" technique, to the journey plot.

Second, Fielding brings back most of the characters present at the beginning of the story on the hero's journey by sending them on a journey themselves, whose destination is the same as Tom's. This creates a funnel effect and allows Tom to multiply encounters with the same characters throughout the story.

The difficulty of creating an organic plot using the journey appears clearly in Mark Twain's Adventures of Huckleberry Finn. Twain had the brilliant idea of the raft, a miniature floating island, on which he can place Huck, and a second recurring character, Jim. But this vehicle is too small: Huck and Jim have no recurring adversary; and simply meet a succession of strangers "on the road." Another difficulty: with his main character drifting along the Mississippi, Twain doesn't know how to bring the plot to a natural end. He therefore arbitrarily ends the journey and uses a deus ex machina to get out of it. Tom Sawyer has no reason to reappear, except to bring the plot back to its comedic roots, simply applying a nice coat of varnish before putting up the "End" sign. Even Mark Twain shouldn't be allowed to get away with that...

The Three Unities Plot

The second major strategy for creating an organic plot comes from Antiquity and Greek playwrights such as Aeschylus, Sophocles, and Euripides. Their essential technique is based on what Aristotle called the unity of time, place, and action. With this technique, the story must take place in twenty-four hours, in a single location, and cover only one action, or "essential action." The plot is organic because all actions come from the hero, over a very short development time. Note that this technique solves the major problem of the journey plot by making the adversaries characters the hero knows and who are present throughout the story.

The problem with the three unities is that if the plot becomes organic, it is not consistent enough. The very short time frame limits the number and power of twists. Twists correspond to the "understanding" part of the plot (as opposed to action), and they determine the complexity level of the plot. The brevity of these stories implies that the hero knows his adversaries too well. They may have started plotting something before the story begins, but once it starts, their possibilities for concealment are limited.

Therefore, the three unities plot generally only allows enough time, adversaries, and complexity of action for one major twist. Oedipus (the world's first detective story), for example, learns that he killed his father and slept with his mother. It is undeniably a great twist. But for the plot to be consistent, it must be peppered throughout the story with multiple twists.

The Twist Plot

The third type of plot is what we can call the twist plot. With this technique, the hero usually stays in one place, but it is not necessarily as restricted as that required by the unity of place. The story can, for example, take place in a large city. And the twist plot almost always covers a longer period than that allowed by the unity of time. The story can even unfold over several years (if it spans several decades, you are probably writing a saga, a type of plot more akin to the journey plot).

The key technique of the twist plot is that the hero is familiar with his adversaries, but he and the audience are unaware of many things about them. Moreover, these adversaries excel at manipulating to get what they want. The result of this combination is a plot full of twists, or surprises, for both the hero and the audience.

Note the main difference between the journey plot and the twist plot: with the journey plot, surprise is limited because the hero quickly gets rid of many adversaries. The twist plot limits the number of adversaries and hides as much as possible about them. The twists multiply the plot by diving beneath the surface of the story.

Well executed, the twist plot is organic because the adversary is the character most capable of attacking the hero's major weakness, and the twists are the scenes where the hero and the audience learn how these attacks occurred. The hero must then overcome his weakness and change, or be destroyed.

The twist plot is very popular because it offers the maximum surprise, which delights the audience. It is also called "big plot," not only because it contains many surprises, but also because these surprises are often shocking. Although the twist plot is still extremely popular today—especially in detective stories and thrillers—it can be said that it had its heyday in the 19th century with authors such as Dumas (The Count of Monte Cristo, The Three Musketeers) and Dickens. Unsurprisingly, the 19th century is also the golden age of novels like Portrait of a Lady where extremely powerful villains hatch diabolical plots to achieve their ends.

Dickens was a master of the twist plot and his talent has probably never been equaled. But if Dickens is considered one of the greatest storytellers of all time, it is partly because he often combined the twist plot and the journey plot to give his story a broader dimension. Needless to say, this requires immense talent for plot creation, as these two approaches are in many ways opposed. In the journey plot, the hero encounters a wide range of society representatives but quickly leaves them behind. In the twist plot, the hero meets only a handful of people but gets to know them in depth.

The Anti-Plot

If the 19th century was the era of the super plot, the 20th century, at least in "serious" fiction, was the era of the anti-plot. In stories as different as Joyce's Ulysses, Last Year at Marienbad, L'Avventura, Waiting for Godot, The Cherry Orchard, and The Catcher in the Rye, there is a kind of disdain for the plot, as if it were a kind of magical sacrifice to be made to the audience in order to fully devote oneself to character work. As Northrop Frye said, "one can read a novel or watch a play to see 'how it feels.' But once one knows what it gives, and the charm has ceased to act, one tends to forget the continuity, the very element of the play or novel that allowed us to take part in it."

If one attempted to summarize the plot of some of these stories, it would give something like this: The Catcher in the Rye is the story of a teenager wandering around New York for two days. In The Cherry Orchard, a family arrives on their land, waits for it to be auctioned off, and leaves. L'Avventura is a detective film in which no crime is committed or solved.

I am quite sure that many 20th-century authors were not so much rebelling against the plot as against the "big" plot, whose sensational twists shocked the reader so much that they swept everything away. What I call anti-plot is rather a wide range of techniques designed by these authors to make the plot organic by having it express the subtleties of the characters. Point of view, change of narrator, branching narrative structure, and non-chronological time are techniques that play on the plot by changing the way the story is told with the ultimate goal of presenting a more complex vision of the human character.

If these techniques can give the story a fragmented aspect, they are not necessarily inorganic. Multiple points of view sometimes evoke a collage, a montage, and a dislocation of the character, but they also give a sense of vitality and produce a flow of sensations. If these experiences contribute to the development of the character and the audience's idea of the character, they can be considered organic and ultimately satisfying.

Digressions—very common in anti-plots—are a form of simultaneous actions and sometimes flashbacks. They are organic if, and only if, they come from the character himself. The Life and Opinions of Tristram Shandy, Gentleman, for example, the anti-plot novel par excellence, has often been criticized for its endless digressions. What readers did not understand is that Tristram Shandy is not a story whose plot is interrupted by digressions but a story of digressions interrupted by what seems to be a main plot. The main character, Tristram, is essentially a man who digresses. Therefore, the way the story is told is a perfectly organic expression of the hero himself.

Reverse narration, like Harold Pinter's Betrayal, a play whose scenes are presented in reverse chronological order, is a variant of the anti-plot. Reverse narration tends to highlight the organic unfolding of the story by emphasizing the cause-and-effect link between scenes. This link is normally hidden beneath the surface of the story, each scene seeming to naturally follow the previous one. But if the story is told in reverse, the audience is forced to become aware of the link connecting the scenes. They understand that what just happened had to result from the previous event, which itself results from a previous event.

The Genre Plot

While "serious" authors were working to diminish the plot, popular authors, especially screenwriters and novelists, were striving to give it even more importance through genre. Genres are types of stories whose characters, themes, universes, and plots are predetermined. Genre plots are usually big plots made of twists so staggering that they sometimes completely overturn the story. But of course, these big plots lose some of their power by the very fact that they are predetermined. In a genre story, the audience generally knows what will happen, and only the details can still surprise them.

If these various genre plots seem organically connected to their main characters, it is simply because they have been written and rewritten to excess. They are thus devoid of any frills. But these genre plots lack a fundamental element of the organic plot. Indeed, they were not written specifically for their characters. They are literally generic, and therefore mechanical. In some genres, such as farce and heist stories, this mechanical aspect is pushed to the extreme, so much so that the plot comes to have the complexity and precision of a Swiss watch—but no character.

The Multiple Thread Plot

The most recent plot strategy, that of multiple threads, was first invented for novels and cinema, but really took off on TV in soap operas, starting with the flagship series Hill Street Blues. With this strategy, each story, or weekly episode, includes between three and five plot threads. Each plot thread is led by a distinct character within a group, usually an organization such as a police station, hospital, or law firm. And the author constantly switches from one thread to another, in cross-cutting. When this strategy is poorly implemented, the different threads have nothing to do with each other, and the cross-cutting is used only to force the audience's attention and speed up the story's pace. When this strategy is well implemented, each of the threads is a variation on a theme, and the transition from one thread to another creates a shock of understanding when two scenes are juxtaposed.

The multiple thread plot is clearly a much more simultaneous form of storytelling, emphasizing the group, or mini-society, and the comparison between characters. But this does not mean that this plot strategy can never be organic. The multiple approach simply changes the unit of development, shifting it from the individual hero to the group. When all the threads are variations of the same theme, the audience more easily grasps what makes our common humanity, which can be just as revealing and moving as observing the development of a single person.

CREATING AN ORGANIC PLOT
Now that you are familiar with some of the main plot strategies, the big question is: how do you create an organic plot for your characters? Here’s how to proceed:

1. Re-examine your guiding principle, which is the organic seed of your story. The plot must ultimately be the fruit of this principle, down to the smallest detail.

2. Immerse yourself again in your thematic line, that is, the moral debate you wish to establish, summarized in one sentence. The plot must also be a detailed manifestation of this line.

3. If you have created a symbolic line for your entire story, your plot, in general, should also express this line. Here, you must find how your sequence of symbols can be expressed through the actions of the hero and his adversary (the plot).

4. Determine whether or not you want to use a narrator. This choice will have a significant impact on how you tell the audience what is happening and, consequently, on the structuring of your plot.

5. Detail the narrative structure using the twenty-two structural steps of any good story (we will discuss this in a moment). This will provide you with most of the highlights (i.e., major actions or events) of your plot and will guarantee you—as much as a technique can—an organic plot.

6. Decide if you want your story to adhere to one or more genres. If so, you must add the specific highlights of these genres to your plot by placing them in the right place and modifying them so that your plot does not become predictable.

You should decide on the presence or absence of a narrator in your story before using the twenty-two building blocks to define your plot. That being said, we will proceed in reverse and start by explaining what these powerful and sophisticated tools that are the twenty-two steps of the narrative structure correspond to, as it is the simplest way to understand them.

THE TWENTY-TWO STEPS OF THE NARRATIVE STRUCTURE

The twenty-two building blocks of any great story are the essential structural steps or events for the deployment of an organic plot. We have already mentioned the seven key structural steps in Chapter 3. But these seven steps correspond to the beginning and the end of the story. The other fifteen are essentially located in the middle of the story, which is where most stories fail.

Due to its broad and detailed nature, the twenty-two-step narrative technique is the most useful of all. The steps show you how to create an organic plot, regardless of the length or genre of your story. They also constitute an indispensable set of tools for any rewriting. If these twenty-two steps are so useful, it is primarily because they never tell you what to write, as ready-made formulas or genres do. They show you the most dramatic way to tell your story to an audience. They provide you with an extremely precise map of your entire plot, allowing you to construct your story gradually, from beginning to end, avoiding the infamous "soft belly," dead and fragmented, in the middle, which causes so much trouble for many authors. Here is the list of these twenty-two steps:

1. Awareness, need, and desire

2. Ghost and story world

3. Weakness and need

4. Inciting event

5. Desire

6. Ally(ies)

7. Opponent / mystery

8. False ally

9. First revelation and decision: modification of desire and motivations

10. Plan

11. Opponent’s plan and main counterattack

12. Narrative drive

13. Attack by an ally

14. Apparent defeat

15. Second revelation and decision: obsessive drive, modification of desire and motivations

16. Revelation to the audience

17. Third revelation and decision

18. Narrow gate, Scylla and Charybdis, and meeting with death

19. Final confrontation

20. Hero’s awareness

21. Moral decision

22. New equilibrium

At first glance, you might think these steps will hinder your creativity and lead to a mechanical rather than organic story. This idea is linked to a fear that affects many writers, that of excessive planning. But writers who refuse any form of planning try to write their story as it comes to them and often end up with a big mess. Using the twenty-two steps actually avoids these two extremes and increases creativity. These steps should not be considered a ready-made formula for good writing. They should be seen as the scaffolding you need to achieve something truly creative and to ensure that everything will work as the story unfolds organically.

Similarly, it is useless to fixate on the number twenty-two. A story can have fewer than twenty-two steps. It all depends on its type and length. You should think of the story as an accordion. It is only limited in its contraction capabilities. But it must always be composed of a minimum of seven steps to remain organic. You will notice that even thirty-second commercials, when they are good, follow these seven steps.

But the longer the story, the more structural steps it requires. A short story or a sitcom, for example, must stick to the seven major steps due to the limited time allotted to the story they tell. A film, an hour-long series episode, or a short novel will have at least twenty-two steps (unless it is a multi-thread drama, in which case each thread will follow the seven steps). A long novel, with all its twists and surprises, can contain far more than twenty-two structural steps. In David Copperfield, for example, there are no fewer than sixty twists.

By studying the twenty-two steps in depth, you will see that they are actually several systems of the story body combined to weave a single narrative line. These steps blend the character network, the moral debate, the story world, and the series of events that make up the plot. They represent the detailed choreography that pits the hero against his opponents as he tries to achieve a goal and solve a more existential problem. In practice, these twenty-two steps ensure that it is indeed your hero who leads the plot.

The table below represents the twenty-two steps divided into three categories, or sub-systems of the story. Remember that each step can be the expression of several sub-systems. Narrative drive, for example, which corresponds to all the actions taken by the hero to achieve the goal, is a step that primarily belongs to the plot. But it is also a step during which the hero commits a number of immoral acts in order to win. And therefore, it also belongs to the moral debate.

The description of the twenty-two steps below will help you understand how to use them to create your plot. After each step explanation, we will illustrate our points with examples from two films, Casablanca and Tootsie. These films represent two different genres—the love story and the comedy—and were written forty years apart. Yet, their organic plots both rely on the twenty-two steps, developing gradually from their beginning to their end.

Never forget that these steps are powerful tools, but they are not set in stone. You must therefore be very flexible when using them. Every good story goes through these steps in a slightly different order than the one presented below. You must therefore find the order that best suits the plot and characters you have created yourself.

1. Awareness, need, and desire

Awareness, need, and desire represent the entire transformation your hero will undergo throughout the story. A combination of steps 20, 3, and 5, this framework provides you with the structure of your hero’s "journey." Remember that in Chapter 4, we explained that you had to start with the culmination of the hero’s development, that is, with awareness, then go back to the beginning to find his weakness and need, as well as his desire. You must proceed in the same way to create the plot.

By starting with the framework of the story—from awareness, to weakness, to need, and desire—you determine the culmination of the plot. This way, all the steps you go through afterward will automatically lead you to where you want to go.

At this stage of plot development, you must ask yourself the following questions and be as precise as possible in your answers:

• What will my hero learn at the end?

• What does he know at the beginning? No character is a blank slate at the beginning of the story. A character always believes something.

• What is he wrong about at the beginning? Your hero cannot learn something at the end of the story if he is not wrong about something at the beginning.

CASABLANCA

• Awareness Rick understands that he cannot withdraw from the fight for freedom because of a broken heart.

• Psychological need Overcome his bitterness towards Ilsa, find a reason to live, and renew his faith in his ideals.

• Moral need Stop being interested only in himself at the expense of others.

• Desire Get Ilsa back.

• Initial error Rick considers himself a dead, immobile man. He is no longer interested in world affairs.

TOOTSIE

• Awareness Michael understands that he has treated women as sexual objects and that he has not been a worthy man.

• Psychological need Overcome his arrogance towards women and, in love, learn to give and receive sincerely.

• Moral need Stop lying and using women to get what he wants.

• Desire Julie, one of the actresses in the series.

• Initial error Michael finds his behavior perfectly normal and thinks he is right to lie to women.

2. Ghost and story world

Step 1 allowed us to establish the framework of the story. From step 2, we will work on the structural steps in the order they appear in a typical story. Remember, however, that the number and order of steps may differ depending on the particularity of the story you want to tell.

Ghost

You have probably heard the word "backstory." This word refers to all the events that occurred in the hero’s life before the story being told. We rarely use the term "backstory" because it is too broad to be useful. The audience is not interested in all the events that occurred in the hero’s life. They are interested in the essential events. That is why the term "ghost" is more appropriate.

There are two types of ghosts. The first, which is the most common, is a past event that continues to haunt the hero in the present. The ghost is an open wound that is often the source of the hero’s moral and psychological weakness. The ghost allows for the psychological development of the hero to begin before the start of the story. The ghost is therefore a major element of the story’s foundations.

We can also consider this first type of ghost as the hero’s internal opponent. It is the great fear that prevents him from acting. Structurally, the ghost acts as a counter-desire. The hero’s desire pushes him forward; the ghost pulls him back. Henrik Ibsen, who placed great importance on the ghost in his plays, spoke of "sailing with a corpse in the hold."

HAMLET

(William Shakespeare, 1601)

Shakespeare was a writer who knew the value of a ghost. Before the first page of the story, Hamlet’s uncle murdered his father, the king, and then married his mother. And as if all this didn’t make enough ghosts, Shakespeare introduces in the first pages of the play the actual ghost of the king, who asks Hamlet to avenge him. Hamlet says: "The time is out of joint. O cursed spite, that ever I was born to set it right!"

IT’S A WONDERFUL LIFE

(novel, The Greatest Gift, by Philip Van Doren Stern, screenplay by Francis Goodrich, Albert Hackett, and Frank Capra, 1946)

George Bailey’s desire is to see the world and build things. But his ghost—his fear of what the tyrant Potter would do to his family and friends if he left—holds him back.

The other type of ghost, rarer, is the story in which the ghost is not conceivable because the hero lives in a paradisiacal world. Instead of starting the story in bondage—partly because of his ghost—the hero is initially free. But an attack soon changes the order of things. Meet Me in St. Louis and The Deer Hunter are good illustrations of this type of ghost.

We must warn you of one thing here: you must not "over-write" the exposition at the beginning of the story. Many authors, from the first page, try to tell the audience everything about their hero, including how and why the ghost haunts him. This mass of information only distracts the audience from the story. Instead, try to hide as much information as possible about the hero, including the details of his ghost. The reader or viewer will guess that you are hiding something and will literally enter your story. They will say: "There is something fishy, and I want to understand what it is."

The events constituting the ghost are rarely mentioned in the early scenes. It is much more common for another character to expose and explain the hero’s ghost during the first third of the story. (On rare occasions, the ghost is exposed during awareness, that is, towards the end of the story. But this is generally a bad idea because the ghost—the power of the past—then dominates the story and continues to pull everything backward.)

Story world

Just like the ghost, the story world is present from the very beginning of the story. It is the place where the hero lives. This world, which includes an "arena," natural settings, atmospheric conditions, man-made spaces, technology, and its own time, represents one of the main ways to define the hero and other characters. Conversely, these characters and their values define the story world. (For more details, refer to Chapter 6, "The Story World.")

KEY POINT: The story world must express your hero’s personality. It must show his weaknesses, needs, desires, and obstacles.

KEY POINT: If, at the beginning of your story, your hero is, in some way, enslaved, the story world must be enslaving and must highlight or exacerbate his major weakness.

You place your hero in the story world from the very first page. But understand that several of the steps we present here will have their own sub-world.

People often think that when writing neither fantasy nor science fiction, they can quickly sketch the world to focus more on the hero’s line of desire. Nothing could be further from the truth. Whatever type of story you are writing, you must create a unique and detailed world. The audience loves to project themselves into a special world. If you take care of the story world, readers or viewers will not want to leave it, and they will return to it again and again.

CASABLANCA

• Ghost Rick fought against the fascists in Spain and sold weapons to the Ethiopians so they could defeat the Italians. The reasons for his departure from America remain obscure. Rick is haunted by the memory of Ilsa, who left him in Paris.

• Story world The screenwriters of Casablanca spent a lot of time detailing a very complex story world from the beginning of the film. Using a map (a miniature), a voice-over narrator presents the masses of refugees fleeing Nazi-occupied Europe to reach the distant desert base of Casablanca in North Africa. Instead of quickly moving to the main character’s desire, the film lingers on the refugees, all seeking a visa to leave Casablanca and head to free Portugal or America. It is a community of world citizens imprisoned like animals in a pen.

The screenwriters continued to detail the story world through a scene in which the Nazi major Strasser meets the French police chief, Captain Renault, at the airport. Casablanca is a confused world mixing political powers, a world of limbo: the city is theoretically governed by Vichy France, but the real power lies in the hands of the Nazi occupier.

Within the arena of Casablanca, Rick has created a small island of power in his bar and casino, Rick’s Café Américain. He is depicted as a king surrounded by his court. It is a world where all the secondary characters play clearly defined roles. Moreover, part of the pleasure the audience derives from this film comes from how all these characters seem to feel comfortable in this hierarchy. Ironically, this film about freedom fighters is, in a sense, very anti-democratic.

The bar is also a venal place, a perfect representation of Rick’s cynicism and selfishness.

TOOTSIE

• Ghost Michael is not haunted by any specific past event. But he has a reputation for being impossible to manage, which is why he can no longer find work.

• Story world From the start, Michael is immersed in the world of comedy and the New York entertainment industry. It is a world that values appearances, fame, and money. This system is extremely hierarchical, with a few rare star actors at its top who grab all the good roles and a mass of unknowns fighting at the bottom of the ladder for roles and having to work as waiters to pay their rent. Michael’s life consists of teaching the art of comedy, multiplying auditions, and arguing with directors about how the role should be played.

Once Michael, disguised as Dorothy, gets a role in a soap opera, the story focuses on the world of television series. It is a theater totally dominated by commerce, where actors play a silly melodramatic scene at record speed to quickly move on to another. It is also a sexist world dominated by an arrogant director who orders all the women on set.

In Michael’s world, the man-made spaces are the small apartments of hardworking actors and the television studio where the series is filmed. The studio is a place of acting, a place of pretense, perfect for a man trying to pass as a woman. The tools of this world are the tools of the acting profession: voice, body, hair, makeup, and costume. The screenwriters created a good parallel between the makeup Michael uses to play his role in the series and the makeup Michael uses to play his role as a woman in front of the camera and off-screen.

The superficial and sexist world of the soap opera expresses and exacerbates Michael’s major weakness: he is a sexist man willing to lie and betray others’ trust to get a role.

3. Weakness and need

• Weakness The hero has one or more flaws so significant that they ruin his life. Weakness comes in two different forms: it can be psychological or moral. But these two categories are not exclusive; a character can very well combine both.

Every weakness is psychological. In some way, the person is deeply wounded. A weakness also has a moral aspect when it leads the character to hurt others as well. A character with a moral weakness always has a negative impact on at least one other character.

KEY POINT: Many authors think they have given their hero a moral weakness when it is actually only psychological. For the weakness you have given your hero to be moral, he must hurt at least one other character at the beginning of the story.

• Need The need is what the hero must accomplish to improve his life. It almost always involves overcoming his weakness towards the end of the story.

• Problem The problem is the crisis the hero faces at the very beginning of the story. The hero is aware of the crisis but does not know how to solve it. The problem is usually an extension of the hero’s weakness, and it is designed to quickly present this weakness to the audience. Although it must be present at the beginning of the story, the problem is far less important than the weakness and need.

CASABLANCA

Rick seems to want nothing and need nothing. But that’s because he hides his need. He appears stronger than others, self-satisfied. If his cynical nature may suggest he is a deeply troubled man, he remains the master of his world. He runs his club like a kind of benevolent dictator. He is also a man who controls women.
