And he is a man of extreme contradictions: while he now appears cynical, bitter, and sometimes immoral, he was, not so long ago, a freedom fighter.
What is original in this film is that the main character, who is nevertheless someone who controls his world, begins the story by observing and remaining passive. Rick is a man with a lot of power and a long history, but he has chosen to stop doing good and retreat to one of the most remote corners of the world, Casablanca—and deep within himself. Rick is a lion locked in a cage he created himself.

• Weakness Rick is cynical, passive, selfish, and has lost his illusions.

• Psychological need To overcome his bitterness towards Ilsa, Rick must find a reason to live and renew his faith in his ideals.

• Moral need Stop being interested only in himself at the expense of others.

• Problem Rick is trapped in Casablanca and trapped in his world of bitterness.

TOOTSIE

• Weakness Michael is selfish, arrogant, and a liar.

• Psychological need Overcome his arrogance towards women and, in love, learn to give and receive honestly.

• Moral need Stop lying and using women to get what he wants.

• Problem Michael can't find roles.

Openings

The spectrum, the universe of the story, the weakness, the need, and the problem are the different elements that make up the opening of your story, which is decisive. There are three types of structural openings that establish these elements.

The "community" start

The main character lives in a paradisiacal world where territory, populations, and technology are in perfect harmony. Therefore, the hero has no spectrum. He is happy, has only minor problems, or no problems at all, but is also vulnerable to attack. This attack, which will come either from outside or inside, does not take long to occur. Meet Me in St. Louis and The Deer Hunter are good representatives of this warm and friendly type of opening.

The fast start

The classic opening, designed to hold the reader's attention from the first pages, is composed of a number of structural elements. The hero has a strong spectrum. He lives in a world of servitude, has several significant weaknesses, as well as both a psychological and moral need, and he must face one or more problems. This type of opening is found in most good stories.

The slow start

The slow start is not an opening in which the author has omitted to include some of the structural steps of the fast start. It is the opening of stories whose hero is aimless.

For it must not be forgotten that there are people who have no goal. But the pace of the stories that talk about them is extremely slow. As the hero's awareness corresponds to the awareness of his true desire (which allows him to set a goal), the first three-quarters of the story have no objective, and the narrative therefore has no narrative line. Very few stories have been able to overcome this enormous structural obstacle. On the Waterfront and Rebel Without a Cause are good examples of success.

4. Inciting Event

This is an event from the outside that leads the hero to set a goal and act.

The inciting event is a small step, but it nevertheless has an important function: it connects need and desire. At the beginning of the story, when the foundations of weakness and need are established, the hero is, in one way or another, paralyzed. Therefore, there must be an external event that pushes him out of his paralysis and into action.

KEY POINT: To find a good inciting event, keep in mind the expression "out of the frying pan into the fire."

A good inciting event is one that makes your hero think he has just overcome the crisis he has been facing since the beginning of the story. But in reality, because of this event, the hero has just gotten himself into the worst mess of his life.

In Sunset Boulevard, for example, Joe is a screenwriter who can't find work. Two men come to repossess his car, and he flees. Suddenly, his tire blows out (inciting event). Joe turns into Norma Desmond's driveway and thinks he's out of trouble. What he doesn't know is that he's just fallen into a trap he will never escape.

CASABLANCA

Ilsa and Laszlo come to find Rick. They are the characters from the outside who will push Rick out of his stable but unhappy position.

TOOTSIE

George, Michael's agent, explains to him that no one wants to hire him anymore because of his terrible character. This is what pushes Michael to disguise himself as a woman and audition for the soap opera.

5. Desire

Desire is the hero's specific goal. It is the backbone of the plot. When we talked about the seven steps in chapter 3, we said that to create a good story, there must be a goal that is specific and present over a large part of the narrative. Here we must give you another piece of advice: at the start, the goal must be put on hold.

The story is built by gradually increasing the importance of desire. If you start with too strong a desire, you won't be able to increase its intensity, and the plot will seem flat and repetitive. You must therefore start with a desire that is weak enough to be able to move forward.

When you build desire throughout the story, be careful not to create a new desire. You must increase the intensity and stakes of the original desire.

CASABLANCA

Rick desires Ilsa. But since it's a love story, this desire is dulled because Ilsa is also Rick's first adversary. Since Rick resents her for leaving him in Paris, he first seeks to hurt her.

Rick's desire for Ilsa being frustrated, the story focuses on another character's desire: Laszlo's, who wants to obtain exit visas for himself and his wife. But the screenwriters have defined Rick's desire clearly and early enough, which tends to ease the audience's impatience when Laszlo's actions are presented because they know Rick's desire will soon take over. The wait actually allows the desire to "simmer."

Towards the end of the story, Rick begins to feel a second desire in contradiction with the first: he wants to help Ilsa and Laszlo escape. If he had felt this contradictory desire earlier, the story would have had two backbones. But this desire arriving near the end and remaining hidden until the last moment becomes both a discovery and part of Rick's realization.

TOOTSIE

Initially, Michael wants to get a role. But this desire is very quickly fulfilled. The goal that truly serves as the backbone of the film is Michael's desire for Julie, one of the actresses in the series.

TECHNIQUE: THE DIFFERENT DEGREES OF DESIRE

Part of the success of your story depends on the degree of desire you give your hero. A desire that remains weak throughout the story diminishes the hero, and, as for the plot, makes any complexity literally impossible. The weakest degree of desire, for example, is survival. The hero is attacked and seeks to escape. This lowers him to the animal stage. The plot of escape stories tends to simply repeat the same highlights of the escape.

Below are the different degrees of some of the classic lines of desire, from weakest to strongest:

1. Survive (escape)

2. Take revenge

3. Win the fight

4. Accomplish something

5. Explore a world

6. Catch a criminal

7. Discover the truth

8. Win love

9. Restore justice and freedom

10. Save the republic

11. Save the world

6. Ally(ies)

Once the hero is equipped with a line of desire, he usually needs to make one or more allies who will help him defeat the adversary and achieve his goal. An ally cannot simply be the spokesperson for the hero's opinions (although this function is important, especially in theater, film, and television). The ally is a key element of the character network and represents one of the main ways to define the hero.

KEY POINT: Consider giving the ally their own line of desire. You have relatively little time to define this character. The quickest way to convince the audience that they are watching a complete person is to give this character a goal. In The Wizard of Oz, for example, the Scarecrow wants a brain.

KEY POINT: Never make the ally a more interesting character than the hero. Remember the rule we stated when we talked about the premise: you must always write the story of the most interesting character. If your ally is more interesting than your hero, you need to rethink the story so that the ally becomes the hero.

CASABLANCA

Rick's allies are the various characters who play a role in the bar: Carl, the professor turned waiter; Sacha, the Russian bartender; Émile, the croupier; Abdul, the bouncer; and Rick's sidekick, Sam, the piano player.

TOOTSIE

Jeff, Michael's roommate, is writing a play, Return to the Love Canal, and Michael wants it to be staged so he can play a role in it.

TECHNIQUE: THE SUBPLOT

In chapter 4 (Characters), we said that the subplot had a precise definition and function: it serves to compare how the hero and another character approach two situations that are generally identical.

Remember the two key rules of the subplot:

1. The subplot can only exist if it impacts the main plot. If the subplot does not play a role in the main plot, you end up with two simultaneous stories that may be clinically interesting but will tend to make the main plot too long. To connect the subplot to the main plot, make sure it generally joins it towards the end. In Hamlet, for example, the secondary character, Laertes, allies with Hamlet's main adversary, Claudius, and duels with Hamlet in the confrontation scene.

2. The secondary character is generally not an ally. The secondary character and the ally fulfill two distinct functions in the story. The ally helps the hero in the main plot. The secondary character leads a different plot from the main plot, but similar in some aspects, allowing for a comparison between the two.

Most American films today combine several genres, but few have true subplots. The subplot tends to broaden the story, and most American screenwriters are too concerned with speed to worry about that. The films where subplots are most often found are love stories, as this genre tends to generate thin main plots. In Moonstruck, for example, there are two subplots, one involving the heroine's father and the other her mother. The main plot and the subplots all deal with the problem of fidelity.

If the subplot is not part of the twenty-two steps, it's because it is rarely used and is a plot in itself with its own structure. But it remains an excellent technique to improve characters and theme and to flesh out the story. On the other hand, the subplot tends to slow down the line of desire—the narrative momentum. You must therefore determine what is most important to you.

If you decide to create a subplot, be aware that you will only have enough time for the seven steps. And know that if you can't go through all of them, your story will seem incomplete and incongruous. Due to the limited time you have, you should also try to introduce your subplot as early as possible in your story.

7. Adversary / Mystery

The adversary is the character who seeks to prevent the hero from achieving his goal. The relationship between this character and the hero is the most important of all the relationships in the story. If you establish the foundations of this opposition adequately, your plot will unfold as it should. But know that if you fail, no rewriting will ever save your story.

The best adversary is the one who is necessary: the character most able to attack the hero's major weakness. He thus pushes the hero to overcome his major weakness and develop or annihilates him. Go back to chapter 4 (Characters) to study again the different elements necessary for any good adversary.

Here are the two main reasons why the adversary and the mystery are closely linked:

1. A mysterious adversary is much more difficult to defeat. In most stories, the hero's only task is to defeat the adversary. In good stories, the hero's mission is divided into two parts: unmask the adversary, then defeat him. This makes the hero's task twice as difficult and makes his success a much greater accomplishment.

Hamlet, for example, does not know if the king really killed his father, as it is a ghost who revealed this information to him. Othello does not know that Iago wants to destroy him. Lear wonders which of his daughters really loves him.

2. In certain types of stories, such as detective stories and thrillers, the mystery is necessary to compensate for the absence of the adversary. Since in detective stories the adversary remains hidden until the end, the audience needs something to replace the ongoing conflict between the hero and the adversary. In this type of story, the mystery must be introduced at the point where the main adversary usually appears.

Before introducing your main adversary, ask yourself the following questions:

• Who is trying to prevent the hero from getting what he wants and why?

• What does the adversary want? He must be in competition with the hero for the goal.

• What are the adversary's values and how do they differ from those of the hero? Most authors never ask themselves this question, and it is a big mistake. A story without a conflict of values, like a story without a conflict of characters, cannot be built.

CASABLANCA

Since Casablanca is fundamentally a love story, Rick's first adversary is the one he loves, Ilsa Lund. This mysterious woman did not tell Rick that she was, and still is, married to Victor Laszlo. Rick's second adversary is the other suitor, Laszlo, the great man who has impressed half the world. The two men hate the Nazis, but they represent two very different versions of the great man. Laszlo's greatness is political and social while Rick's greatness is personal.

Major Strasser and the Nazis provide the external opposition and danger that elevate the love story to a higher level. Strasser is in no way mysterious, but he doesn't need to be: in Casablanca, he is all-powerful.

TOOTSIE

Since Tootsie's structure relies on the form of farce (as well as romantic comedy), the film does not use the mysterious adversary technique. Farce is a form that has more adversaries than any other and works on the rhythm of the attacks they lead. Here is how Michael's main adversaries attack his weakness:

• Julie forces him to reflect on how he behaves with women.

• Ron, the arrogant director, didn't want Dorothy (Michael) for the role and is therefore unpleasant with her.

• Les, Julie's father, unknowingly reveals to Michael the consequences of his dishonesty when he is seduced by Dorothy.

• John, one of the actors in the series, also makes unwanted advances to Dorothy.

TECHNIQUE: THE ICEBERG ADVERSARY

It is extremely important to make the adversary mysterious, regardless of the story you are writing. Think of the adversary as an iceberg. A piece of the iceberg is visible. But its larger part is hidden beneath the surface, and it is by far the most dangerous. There are four techniques that can help you make the opposition as dangerous as possible:

1. Create a hierarchy of adversaries with a number of alliances. All adversaries are linked to each other; they work together to defeat the hero. The main adversary is at the top of the pyramid, with the others under his power. (Refer to the section of chapter 4 that deals with the four-corner opposition. You will find an illustration of this technique at the end of this chapter, where the example of The Godfather is developed.)

2. Hide this hierarchy from the hero and the audience, and hide the true agenda (desire) of each adversary.

3. Reveal all this information gradually and at an increasingly fast pace. This means there must be more twists at the end of the story. As we have seen, it is the way information is revealed to the hero and the audience that makes the plot.

4. Have the hero face an obvious adversary at the beginning of the story. Then, as the conflict intensifies, the hero must discover attacks coming from a stronger and hidden opposition, or from the part of the adversary that has been concealed.

8. False Ally

The false ally is a character who appears to be an ally of the hero but is actually an adversary or an emissary of the main adversary.

The plot is driven by twists-revelations, which come from the steps the hero takes in discovering the true power of the opposition. Every time the hero discovers something new about his adversary—a revelation—the plot "bounces," much to the audience's delight. The false ally increases the power of the adversary because he represents hidden opposition. He forces the hero and the audience to dig beneath the surface of the iceberg and discover what the hero is really fighting against.

If the false ally is so important, it's also because he is inherently complex. This character often undergoes a fascinating transformation during the story. By pretending to be the hero's ally, the false ally ends up feeling like an ally. He becomes torn by a dilemma: he works for the adversary but wants the hero to win.

The false ally is usually introduced after the main adversary, but not necessarily. If the adversary has devised a plan to defeat the hero before the story begins, the false ally must be introduced first.

CASABLANCA

While always being sympathetic and charming with Rick, Captain Renault protects himself by working for the Nazis. Renault is much more open in his opposition than the vast majority of false allies who work undercover. At the very end of the story, Renault makes a 180-degree turn to become a true ally of Rick. It is one of the best twists in the story and a good example of the power of storytelling that comes from character reversals, turning from allies to adversaries or from adversaries to allies.

TOOTSIE

Sandy is also not the usual false ally who deceives the hero and the audience from the beginning of the story. Initially, she is an actress who is friends with Michael. She becomes a false ally when Michael disguises himself as a woman to get the role she also wants. When she catches Michael trying on her clothes, he only increases her disappointment by pretending he has fallen in love with her.

9. First Twist-Revelation and Decision: Modification of Desire and Motivations

At this point in the story, the hero makes a discovery: he discovers new and surprising information. This information forces him to make a decision and take a new direction. It can also lead him to clarify his desire and motivations. Motivations are the reasons that drive the hero to desire the goal. The four events that constitute the twist-revelation, the decision, the modification of desire, and that of motivations must occur at the same time.

Twists are key elements of the plot. However, they are absent from most stories. In many ways, the quality of your plot comes from the quality of your twists. Keep these techniques in mind:

1. The best twists are those that concern the adversary. The new information discovered by the hero about him tends to intensify the conflict. They are also the ones that have the most impact on the outcome of the plot.

2. The modified desire must be a deviation from the original desire; it must not be a break from it. Think of the modified desire as a river that changes course. If, at this point, you give your hero a completely different desire from the first, you start a new story. You must clarify and intensify the original line of desire.

3. Each twist-revelation must be explosive and stronger than the one that preceded it. If it is not important information, the story will remain flat. And each twist-revelation must build on the one that preceded it. The plot must literally "thicken." Think of the twists-revelations as the gears of a car. With each gear change, the car (the story) accelerates, and, by the last gear, it is at full speed. The audience doesn't understand how they got to such speed, but what is certain is that they are having a good time.

If the intensity of the twists does not gradually increase, the plot will remain flat or even decline. And that is what must be avoided at all costs.

Note that, in recent years, Hollywood has become more aware of the importance of plot, which makes the trust screenwriters place in the three-act structure even more dangerous. Remember that this structure requires two or three twists-revelations. Besides being completely wrong, this advice can only provide you with a shabby plot that will have no chance of breaking into the professional film world. Today, the average American film has between seven and ten twists. Some types of films, including detective stories and thrillers, have even more. Set aside the three-act structure and learn advanced plot creation techniques. You will put all the chances on your side.

CASABLANCA

• Twist-revelation Ilsa shows up at Rick's bar late at night.

• Decision Rick decides to hurt her deeply.
• Modified Desire Before Ilsa's arrival, Rick simply wanted to run his bar, make money, and have peace. Now, he wants Ilsa to suffer as much as he does.

• Modified Motivations She deserves it because she broke his heart in Paris.

TOOTSIE

• Twist-Revelation Michael discovers he has real power when "Dorothy" acts like a diva at the soap opera audition and dismisses Ron, the director.

• Decision Michael, as Dorothy, decides to behave like a powerful woman who gets straight to the point.

• Modified Desire No. Michael still wants the role.

• Modified Motivations He now knows how to get the role his way.

TECHNIQUE: ADDITIONAL TWISTS-REVELATIONS

The more twists-revelations there are, the richer and more complex your plot will be. A twist-revelation occurs whenever the hero or the audience learns new information.

KEY POINT: The twist-revelation must be significant enough to push the hero to make a decision and change the course of their actions.

TOOTSIE

• Twist-Revelation Michael discovers he is attracted to Julie, one of the actresses in the series.

• Decision Michael decides to befriend Julie.

• Modified Desire Michael wants Julie.

• Modified Motivations He is falling in love with her.

10. Plan

The plan is the set of directives or strategies the hero will use to defeat their adversary and achieve the goal.

KEY POINT: Be careful not to let the hero simply follow their plan. Your plot would become predictable and your hero superficial. In almost all good stories, the hero's initial plan fails. At this point in the story, the adversary is still too strong for the hero, who must think hard to develop a better strategy considering the adversary's strengths and weapons.

CASABLANCA

Rick's initial plan to win back Ilsa is both aggressive and passive: he knows she will return to him, and he tells her bluntly. His main plan, which he develops relatively late in the story, is to use Ugarte's exit visas to help Ilsa and Laszlo escape the Nazis. The advantage of this late plan is that it provides rapid and stunning twists at the end of the story.

TOOTSIE

Michael's plan is to remain disguised as a woman while convincing Julie to break up with her fiancé, Ron. He must also fend off advances from Les and John without them realizing he is actually a man. And he must deceive Sandy into believing he loves her and lying to her about the soap opera role.

TECHNIQUE: TRAINING

Most heroes are already trained to do what they need to succeed. Their failure in the first part of the plot comes from an omission: they haven't introspected and confronted their weakness.

But training plays an important role in certain genres, where it often represents the most popular part of the plot. It is very common in sports and war stories (including suicide missions like The Dirty Dozen) and heist stories (like Ocean's Eleven). Training is usually placed right after the plan and before the main lines of action and conflict.

11. Adversary's Plan and Main Counterattack

The adversary, like the hero, has a plan consisting of several steps to achieve the goal. They develop a strategy and begin executing a series of attacks aimed at the hero. We cannot stress enough the importance of this step, yet most writers completely overlook it.

As we have already explained, the plot largely relies on twists. To create twists, you must hide the means used by the adversary to attack the hero. You must therefore create a detailed plan for the adversary, including as many secret attacks as possible. Each of these attacks, when it hits the hero, will constitute a new twist.

KEY POINT: The more complex your adversary's plan and the better you hide it, the more interesting your plot will be.

CASABLANCA

• Adversary's Plan Ilsa tries to convince Rick that she had good reasons to leave him and that Laszlo must flee Casablanca. Major Strasser's plan is to pressure Captain Renault to keep Laszlo in Casablanca and intimidate anyone, including Rick, who might try to help him escape.

• Main Counterattack When Rick refuses Laszlo's offer to buy the letters, Ilsa comes to him and threatens him with a weapon. Strasser's main attack occurs after Laszlo inflames the French in the bar by having the orchestra play La Marseillaise. Strasser orders the bar closed and tells Ilsa that if she doesn't return to occupied France with Laszlo, he will either be imprisoned or killed. Later that night, he pushes Captain Renault to arrest Laszlo.

TOOTSIE

As it is a romantic comedy and a farce, each of Michael-Dorothy's adversaries has a plan based on what they think the character is. The plot is ingeniously constructed on escalating attacks from the adversaries: Dorothy must share her room and bed with Julie, Dorothy must take care of Julie's crying baby, Julie mistakenly thinks Dorothy is a lesbian, Les and John court Dorothy, and Sandy is furious with Michael for lying to her.

This cyclone effect is one of the pleasures of the farce form, but Tootsie gives it a strong emotional impact, an impact that most farces lack. Michael's gender changes play on the audience's feelings and tend to unsettle them more quickly and intensely. It's excellent writing.

12. Narrative Dynamism

Dynamism corresponds to the series of actions undertaken by the hero to defeat the adversary and win. Representing what is usually the largest section of the plot, these actions begin with the hero's plan (step 10) and continue until the apparent defeat (step 14).

During this period, the adversary is generally too strong for the hero, who tends to lose the competition. The hero is therefore desperate and often commits immoral actions to win (these immoral acts are part of the story's moral debate; see chapter 5).

KEY POINT: During this period, your plot must develop, not repeat. In other words, you must radically change your hero's actions. Do not repeat the same highlights (the same actions or events) over and over.

In a love story, for example, two characters falling in love might go to the beach, then to the movies, then to a park, and finally to a restaurant. These are indeed four different actions, but they correspond to the same highlight of the plot. The plot repeats, it does not develop.

For the plot to develop, your hero must react to the new information they have learned about the adversary (i.e., again, to twists) and adapt their strategy and course of actions accordingly.

CASABLANCA

The main characteristic of Rick's dynamism is that it is postponed. This is not a flaw in the script. It stems from Rick's character, his weakness, and his desire. Rick is paralyzed by bitterness and thinks nothing has value in the world. He wants Ilsa, but Ilsa is his adversary and is with another man. Thus, at the beginning and middle of the story, Rick talks to Ilsa but does not actively seek to win her back. In fact, he initially dismisses her.

This postponement of desire, while required by Rick's character, is not without consequences: it generates dead times that tend to diminish the audience's interest. Laszlo seeking exit visas from Ferrari, Laszlo at the police station, Laszlo seeking exit visas from Rick, Laszlo with Ilsa, Laszlo fleeing the underground meeting, all these constitute deviations from the hero's dynamism.

But the postponement of desire also has two major advantages. First, the writers relied on Laszlo's actions to build the epic and political aspect of the story. Although these actions have nothing to do with the hero's dynamism, they are necessary for this story because they give the ultimate twist and Rick's final decision a global scope.

Secondly, by waiting so long to show Rick embarking on his quest, the writers gave themselves the advantage of having climaxes and twists-revelations follow one another rapidly.

When Ilsa enters Rick's room and declares her love, Rick finally acts, and the story ignites. Of course, the great irony is that if Rick suddenly multiplies actions, it is precisely to ensure he does not bring Ilsa back to him. The change in motivations and goal of the main character – who initially wanted to win back Ilsa and now wants to help her escape with Laszlo – occurs just after Rick has begun his quest for Ilsa. Moreover, the most exciting part of this last quarter of the film is the uncertainty surrounding the goal Rick seeks to achieve.

KEY POINT: This uncertainty regarding two goals only works because it exists over a short period of time and is part of the great twist in the final confrontation.

• Steps of Dynamism

1. Rick remembers the moments he spent with Ilsa in Paris.

2. Rick calls Ilsa a tramp when she returns to his café.

3. Rick tries to reconcile with Ilsa at the market, but she rejects him.

4. Rick refuses to return the transit letters to Renault.

5. After seeing Ilsa, Rick helps the Bulgarian couple find enough money to pay Renault.

6. Rick refuses to give the letters to Laszlo. He tells him to ask Ilsa to explain her refusal.

7. Rick refuses to give the letters to Ilsa, who confesses she still loves him.

8. Rick tells Ilsa he will help Laszlo, and only Laszlo, escape.

9. Rick asks Carl to sneak Ilsa out of the bar while he talks to Laszlo, who is then arrested.

TOOTSIE

• Steps of Dynamism

1. Michael buys women's clothes and explains to Jeff how hard it is to be a woman.

2. He lies to Sandy about his new source of income.

3. He practices doing his own makeup and hair.

4. He improvises to avoid kissing a man.

5. He befriends Julie.

6. He lies to Sandy, saying he is sick.

7. He invites Sandy again.

8. He helps April rehearse.

9. He helps Julie rehearse and asks her why she puts up with Ron.

10. He lies to Sandy when he arrives late for their date.

11. He improvises lines to make Dorothy a stronger woman.

12. He improvises lines with Julie.

13. He asks George to help him find more interesting roles now that he knows how to act like a real woman.

14. Michael, as a man, makes advances to Julie, but she rejects him.

15. As Dorothy, he asks Ron to stop calling him "Tootsie."

16. He lies to Sandy to go to the countryside with Julie.

17. At the farm, he falls in love with Julie.

18. The producer tells Michael he wants to renew Dorothy's contract.

13. Attack from an Ally

At one point in the narrative dynamism, the hero loses the fight against the adversary and despairs. When he begins to act immorally to regain the upper hand, the ally confronts him.

At this moment, the ally becomes the hero's conscience. They essentially say, "I'm trying to help you achieve your goal, but you're going about it the wrong way." Generally, the hero tries to defend himself and refuses to accept the ally's criticism (for more details on writing moral dialogue, refer to chapter 10, "Scene Construction and Symphonic Dialogues").

The ally's attack elevates the story to its second stage of conflict (the first being the hero vs. the opposition). This attack increases the pressure on the hero and forces him to start questioning his values and actions.

CASABLANCA

• Ally's Critique Rick is criticized not by one of his allies but by his first adversary, Ilsa. At the market, she reproaches him for no longer being the man she knew in Paris. When Rick makes advances, she tells him she was married to Laszlo before meeting him.

• Hero's Justification Rick does not try to justify his words and simply says he was drunk the night before.

TOOTSIE

• Ally's Critique When Michael pretends to be sick to get rid of Sandy and go to the countryside with Julie, Jeff asks him how long he plans to keep lying to people.

• Hero's Justification Michael replies that it's better to lie to a woman than to hurt her by telling the truth.

14. Apparent Defeat

At one point in the narrative dynamism, the hero loses the fight against the adversary. Around two-thirds or three-quarters of the story, the hero experiences an apparent defeat. He thinks he has lost the competition for the goal and that the adversary has won. He has reached his lowest point.

The apparent defeat is an important punctuation of the narrative structure because it corresponds to the moment when the hero hits rock bottom. It increases the dramatic aspect of the story by forcing the hero to rise again to eventually win. Just as a sports event is more interesting when the team you support comes back to win, a story is more interesting when the audience's beloved hero rises from what appears to be certain defeat.

KEY POINT: The apparent defeat is not a small temporary failure. It must truly be a devastating experience for the hero. And the audience must truly believe the hero is finished.

KEY POINT: Your story should only include one apparent defeat. While the hero can experience several failures, the story can only include one moment where everything seems completely lost. Otherwise, it would lack coherence and dramatic power. To understand the difference, think of a car speeding down a hill and, one: hitting two or three big speed bumps or, two: crashing into a brick wall.

CASABLANCA

Rick's apparent defeat occurs quite early in the narrative dynamism when Ilsa visits him after the bar closes at night. Drunk, he remembers their affair in Paris and its terrible ending, the moment Ilsa didn't show up at the train station. As she tries to explain what happened, he bitterly attacks her and sends her away.

TOOTSIE

George explains to Michael that he cannot break the contract binding him to the soap opera. He must continue living this nightmare, disguised as a woman.

Apparent Victory

In stories that end with the hero's death or total subjugation, the apparent defeat stage is replaced by the apparent victory stage. The hero reaches the peak of their success or power before starting a downward slope. This moment often corresponds to when the hero enters a sub-world of temporary freedom (see chapter 6, "The Story's Universe"). This apparent victory stage appears, for example, in Goodfellas when the characters successfully rob Lufthansa. They think they've pulled off the heist of the century. But in reality, this success marks the beginning of a process that will end in the death and destruction of all the characters.

15. Second Twist-Revelation and Decision: Obsessive Dynamism, Modified Desire and Motivations

Just after his apparent defeat, the hero almost always makes another major discovery. If this were not the case, his defeat would not be apparent but real, and the story would be over. At this precise moment, the hero obtains new information proving that victory is still possible. And he decides to resume the fight and relaunch the quest for the goal.

This important twist-revelation has a galvanizing effect on the hero. If, previously, he desired the goal (desire and narrative dynamism), he is now obsessed with the goal. And he would literally do anything to obtain it.

In short, at this point in the plot, the hero becomes tyrannical in his quest for the goal. You will notice that while he is strengthened by the new information he has obtained, he also tends to continue his moral decline, begun at the start of the narrative dynamism (this is one of the stages of the story's moral debate).

The second twist-revelation also pushes the hero to modify his desire and motivations. Again, the story takes a new direction. Ensure that these five elements – twist-revelation, decision, obsession, modified desire, and motivations – are all present. If not, the pressure drops, and the plot becomes weak.

CASABLANCA

• Twist-Revelation Ilsa tells Rick she was married to Laszlo before meeting him, explaining why she stood him up in Paris.

• Decision Rick seems to make no decision, but he tells Renault that if anyone touches the letters, he will kill them.

• Modified Desire Rick no longer desires to hurt Ilsa.

• Obsessive Dynamism Rick's first obsessive dynamism begins when Ilsa shows up at the club. Rick desperately seeks to hurt her to avenge the pain she caused him. This is another unique element of Casablanca. Rick starts the story with a much higher degree of passion and obsession than most heroes. But this high degree of desire leads somewhere, as at the end of the story, Rick leaves to help other fighters save the world.

It should also be noted that Rick only appears to become more immoral as the story progresses. In reality, he has decided to help Ilsa and Laszlo escape together, and he is determined to succeed.

• Modified Motivations Rick has forgiven Ilsa.

TOOTSIE

• Twist-Revelation The soap opera producer tells Dorothy he wants her to sign another one-year contract.

• Decision Michael decides to ask George to help him break the contract.

• Modified Desire Michael wants to end this charade and get closer to Julie.

• Obsessive Dynamism Michael is determined to leave Dorothy behind.

• Modified Motivations Michael feels guilty about how decently Julie and Les have treated him.

Additional Twist-Revelation

• Twist-Revelation Les makes advances to Dorothy.

• Decision Dorothy leaves Les in the bar.

• Modified Desire Michael wants to stop misleading Les.

• Modified Motivations No. Michael still feels guilty.

You will notice that Michael's moral decline tends to increase, despite his guilt and desire to end his difficult situation. The longer he maintains the charade, the more he harms those around him.

16. Revelation for the Audience

This revelation corresponds to the moment when the audience – but not the hero – learns new information. This information usually concerns the true identity of the false ally. The audience realizes that the character they thought was the hero's friend is actually their enemy.

But whatever the nature of the information learned by the audience, this revelation plays an important role in the story for three main reasons:

1. It's a boost that revitalizes what is often a rather slow section of the plot.

2. It shows the audience the true power of the opposition.

3. It allows the audience to observe certain hidden elements of the plot staged dramatically and visually.

You will notice that the revelation for the audience marks a major turning point in the relationship between the hero and the audience. In most stories (farce being a notable exception), until this point, the audience made discoveries at the same time as the hero. This tended to create a direct relationship – an identity – between the hero and the audience.

But with this revelation, for the first time, the audience learns something before the hero. This tends to create a distance and place the audience in a position of superiority over the hero. And this is essential for many reasons, the main one being that it allows the audience to take a step back and see the entire process of the hero's transformation (which will reach its climax with the hero's realization).

CASABLANCA

Gun in hand, Rick forces Renault to call the airport control tower. But the audience sees that the captain is actually calling Major Strasser.

TOOTSIE

This step does not occur in Tootsie, mainly because Michael deceives the other characters. Since he is fooling them, he controls the situation, and the audience learns things at the same time as he does.

17. Third Twist-Revelation and Decision

The hero discovers new information about the means to defeat the adversary. If the story includes a false ally, this step often corresponds to the moment when the hero discovers the true identity of this character (which has already been revealed to the audience in step 16).

One might think that upon discovering the extent of the opposition, the hero would want to withdraw from the conflict. But this new information, on the contrary, must make him stronger and more confident because he now knows what he is up against.

CASABLANCA

• Twist-Revelation Ilsa comes to ask Rick for the letters and confesses she still loves him.

• Decision Rick decides to give the transit letters to Laszlo and Ilsa but hides this decision from Ilsa and the audience.

• Modified Desire Rick wants to save Ilsa and Laszlo from the Nazis.

• Modified Motivations Rick knows Ilsa must leave with Laszlo and help him defend his cause.

TOOTSIE

• Twist-Revelation When Michael gives Sandy the chocolates Les had given Dorothy, Sandy calls him a liar and a hypocrite.

• Decision Michael goes to George to help him break his contract.

• Modified Desire No. Michael still wants to leave the soap opera.

• Modified Motivations No. He cannot continue lying to all these people.

Additional Twist-Revelation

• Twist-Revelation When Dorothy gives Julie a gift, she tells her she doesn't want to see her anymore to avoid giving her false hopes.

• Decision Michael decides to tell her the truth.
- Modified Desire: No. Michael still wants Julie.

- Modified Motivations: Michael is in love with Julie and realizes he cannot "get" her as long as he continues playing the role of Dorothy.

18. Narrow Gate, Caudine Forks, and Encounter with Death

Towards the end of the story, the conflict between the hero and the adversary intensifies to such an extent that the pressure on the hero becomes almost unbearable. He has less and less room to maneuver, and often, the space he must pass through literally narrows. Ultimately, he must squeeze through a narrow gate or a long passage (while being attacked from all sides).

This is also the moment when the main character encounters "death." In myths, the hero descends into the Underworld and glimpses his future in the world of the dead.

In more modern stories, the encounter with death is psychological. The hero suddenly becomes aware of his own mortality; life is fleeting, and his could end at any moment. One might think that this realization would tend to make him abandon the conflict, knowing now that it could prove fatal. But instead, it drives him to fight harder. The hero tells himself: "If I want to give meaning to my life, I must fight for what I believe in. And I will do it here and now." The encounter with death is thus the trial that generally triggers the confrontation.

The narrow gate, the Caudine Forks, and the encounter with death are the most mobile stages of all and are often found in another part of the plot. The hero, for example, may have an encounter with death during his apparent defeat. He may pass under the Caudine Forks during the final battle (see the trench in Star Wars or the tower in Vertigo), or even after this battle, as Terry Malloy does at the end of On the Waterfront.

CASABLANCA

This stage corresponds to the moment when Rick strives to reach the airport with Ilsa, Laszlo, and Renault, while Major Strasser tries to catch up with them.

TOOTSIE

Michael passes under the Caudine Forks and experiences an escalation of nightmares when he must watch Amy, Julie's baby who won't stop crying; then face the situation when Julie pushes him away after he tries to kiss her; then dance with Les, who has fallen in love with Dorothy; then get rid of John, the actor who is also attracted to Dorothy; and finally refute Sandy's accusations after he gave her the candies Les had given him.

19. Final Confrontation

The confrontation is the final conflict. It determines who, the hero or the adversary, achieves the goal (if either does). The grand violent conflict, though common, is the least interesting form of confrontation. Violent confrontations have a lot of flash but little meaning. The confrontation should be an opportunity to clearly show the audience what the characters are fighting for. The focus should not be on strength but on values and ideas.

The confrontation is the convergence point of the story. It brings together all the characters and various lines of action. It takes place in a very confined space, which increases the conflict and unbearable pressure.

The confrontation generally (but not always) corresponds to the moment when the hero fulfills his need and obtains the desired object. It is also the moment when he shares the most traits with his adversary. But in this similarity, the determining differences that oppose them become even clearer.

The confrontation is also the moment when the theme explodes in the audience's mind. In the conflict of values, readers or viewers understand for the first time what the best way to act and live is.

CASABLANCA

At the airport, Rick points a gun at Renault and tells Ilsa she must leave with Laszlo. He then tells Laszlo that Ilsa has been faithful to him. Laszlo and Ilsa board the plane. Major Strasser arrives and tries to stop the plane, but Rick shoots him.

TOOTSIE

During a live taping of an episode of the series, Michael improvises a complicated monologue in which he explains that his character is actually a man. Then he removes his disguise, shocking both the audience and the other soap opera actors. When Michael finishes, Julie hits him and leaves.

The final conflict between Michael and Julie is rather light (reduced to a slap). The grand conflict has been replaced by a grand twist-revelation: Michael removes his mask in front of the cast and crew, and in front of a national audience.

The writers had a brilliant idea here: the complex monologue improvised by Michael follows the path of women's liberation ideas that Michael himself has taken by putting himself in a woman's shoes.

20. Realization

The trial of confrontation usually pushes the hero to transform deeply. For the first time, he becomes aware of who he truly is. He sheds the mask he has lived with until now and sees, in a moving way, his true self. Facing the truth about himself can either destroy him—as in Oedipus Rex, Vertigo, or The Conversation—or make him stronger.

If the realization is not only psychological but also moral, the hero also learns how he should behave with others. To have maximum dramatic impact, a good realization must be sudden; it should be overwhelming for the hero, whether positive or negative; and it must be something new, something the hero was unaware of about himself until that precise moment.

The quality of your story largely depends on the quality of the realization. Everything leads to this point. Therefore, you must take special care in writing this stage. And avoid the following two pitfalls:

1. What your hero learns about himself must be something very important. You cannot settle for a few pretty words or platitudes about life. This leads to a second warning.

2. Your hero must not directly tell the audience what he has learned. This is the mark of bad writers (in Chapter 10, "Construction of Scenes and Symphonic Dialogues," we will explain how to use dialogue to express realization without a moralizing tone).

TECHNIQUE: THE DOUBLE REVERSAL

At the realization stage, you can use this technique, which consists of giving a realization to both the hero and the adversary. Each character learns something from the other, and instead of a single vision, the audience receives two different perspectives on the appropriate way to act and live in the world.

Here's how to create a double reversal:

1. Give the hero and his main adversary a weakness and a need (the hero's and adversary's weaknesses and needs do not have to be identical or even similar).

2. Make the adversary human: make him capable of learning and changing.

3. During the confrontation, or just after, give a realization to both the hero and the adversary.

4. Connect the two realizations: the hero must learn something from the adversary and the adversary must learn something from the hero.

5. Your moral viewpoint will correspond to the best of what both characters have learned.

CASABLANCA

- Psychological Realization: Rick reconnects with his idealism and gains a clear vision of who he truly is.

- Moral Realization: Rick realizes he must sacrifice himself to save Ilsa and Laszlo and that he must join the fight for freedom.

- Double Reversal: Renault announces that he too has become a patriot and will accompany Rick on his new path.

TOOTSIE

- Psychological Realization: Michael realizes he has never truly fallen in love because he has never looked beyond a woman's physical attributes.

- Moral Realization: He understands that he has suffered from his arrogance and disdain towards women, and that he has made the women around him suffer. He tells Julie that he has learned more about what makes a man by living in a woman's skin than he ever learned by living in a man's skin.

21. Moral Decision

Once the hero has understood, through his realization, how he should act, he must make a decision. The moral decision is the moment when the hero chooses between two ways of acting, each representing a set of values and a lifestyle that impacts others.

The moral decision is proof of what the hero has learned during the realization stage. By taking this action, the hero shows the audience what he has become.

CASABLANCA

Rick gives Laszlo the letters of transit, urges Ilsa to leave with him, and tells Laszlo that Ilsa loves him. Then he leaves to risk his life as a freedom fighter.

TOOTSIE

Michael sacrifices his job and apologizes to Julie and Les for lying to them.

TECHNIQUE: THEMATIC REVELATION

In Chapter 5, "The Moral Debate," we presented thematic revelation as a revelation that affects not the hero but the audience. The audience understands how people in general should live and behave. The thematic revelation allows the story to go beyond the limits of its simple characters to touch the audience in their own way of living.

Many authors prefer to leave aside this very elaborate technique, fearing to appear moralizing in the last moment they share with the audience. However, when well executed, the thematic revelation technique can have a profound impact.

KEY POINT: The trick is to draw the abstract and general from the concrete and particular of your characters. Try to find a specific action or gesture that could have a symbolic impact on the audience.

PLACES IN THE HEART

(Robert Benton, 1984)

A brilliant example of thematic revelation is found at the end of Places in the Heart, the story of a woman, played by Sally Field, in 1930s Midwest America, whose sheriff husband is accidentally killed by a drunken black boy. The boy is lynched by Ku Klux Klan men, who then expel a black man who had helped the widow manage her farm. In a subplot, a man has an affair with his best friend's wife.

The film's final scene takes place in a church. As the preacher talks about the power of love, the wronged woman takes her husband's hand for the first time since she learned of his affair, and he feels the irresistible power of forgiveness. People take communion, one after the other. Each time a person drinks the wine, the preacher says: "The peace of Christ." Each of the story's characters drinks the communion wine. And slowly, the audience has an astonishing thematic revelation. The banker, who was one of the heroine's adversaries, drinks. The black man, who was expelled—and who has long since disappeared from the story—also drinks. The character played by Sally Field drinks. Sitting behind her, her late husband drinks. And next to him, the black boy who killed him and paid for it with his own life also drinks. "The peace of Christ."

From a realistic portrait of the characters, the scene gradually becomes a moment of universal forgiveness, shared by the audience. Its impact is profound. Do not shy away from this excellent technique for fear of seeming pretentious. Take a chance. Do it. Tell a great story.

22. New Balance

Once the desire and need have been fulfilled (or not, which is tragic), things return to normal. But there is nevertheless a big difference from the beginning: thanks to his realization, the hero has risen to a higher level or fallen lower.

CASABLANCA

Rick has regained his idealism and sacrificed his love for a nobler cause.

TOOTSIE

Michael has learned to be honest and less selfish. Telling the truth has allowed him to reconcile with Julie and start a true love story.

The twenty-two steps are powerful tools that give you the ability to create a detailed and organic plot. But know that it takes practice to learn to master them. We therefore advise you to apply them to everything you can write and everything you can read, keeping in mind two essential points:

1. Stay flexible. The twenty-two steps have no clearly defined chronological order. They are not a ready-made formula that allows you to make your story conform. These steps are presented here in the order in which humans generally go through them to solve their problems. But every problem is unique. And every story is unique. Use the twenty-two steps as the framework for the organic unfolding of your unique story, dealing with unique characters trying to solve unique problems.

2. Be careful when changing the order of these steps. This second warning contradicts the first, but it also relies on the fact that these steps are presented in the order in which humans go through them to solve their problems. These twenty-two steps represent an organic order, the development of an individual unit. Thus, if you try to change this order too drastically to appear original or to be surprising, you risk creating a story that will seem false or artificial.

SEQUENCE OF TWISTS-REVELATIONS

Good authors know that twists-revelations are the keys to the plot. And that is why it is very important to take the time to separate these twists from the rest of the plot and consider them as individual units. Searching for the sequence of twists-revelations is one of the most useful narrative techniques for the author.

To determine if the sequence of twists-revelations is well constructed, the following criteria must be considered:

1. The sequence must be logical. The twists-revelations must appear in the order in which it is most likely that the hero has them.

2. The sequence must follow an ascending order in terms of intensity, each twist having to be stronger than the one that preceded it. This is not always possible, especially in long stories (it would go against logic). But care must nevertheless be taken to build the story in a generally ascending way, in order to gradually increase the dramatic tension.

3. The twists must also follow one another at an increasingly rapid pace. This also tends to increase the dramatic tension, the surprise effect being thus denser.

The most powerful of all twists is known as the reversal. This twist reverses the meaning of the entire story: the audience suddenly sees all the elements of the plot in a completely different light. In an instant, the entire reality has changed.

This technique is more common in detective stories and thrillers, which is not surprising. In The Sixth Sense, the reversal occurs when the audience discovers that the character played by Bruce Willis was actually dead for most of the story. In The Usual Suspects, the reversal occurs when the audience discovers that the humble Verbal invented the whole story and is actually the terrible adversary, Keyser Söze.

You will notice that in these two films the great reversal occurs at the end of the story. This has the advantage of leaving the audience in a state of shock. And that is the greatest reason for the tremendous success of these two films.

But this technique nevertheless requires some precautions. Because it can reduce the story to a mere vehicle for the plot, and very few stories can withstand such domination of the plot. O. Henry became known for using the reversal technique in his short stories (such as The Gift of the Magi). But these stories have also been criticized for their forced, artificial, and mechanical aspect.

Let's now study the sequences of twists-revelations in stories other than Casablanca and Tootsie.

ALIEN

(original story by Dan O'Bannon and Ronald Shusett, screenplay by Dan O'Bannon, 1979)

- Twist-Revelation No. 1: The crew members realize that the Alien uses the ventilation system to move around the ship.

- Decision: They decide to propel the Alien into the airlock, then into space.

- Modified Desire: Ripley and the others want to kill the Alien.

- Modified Motivations: If they don't kill the Alien, they will die.

- Twist-Revelation No. 2: Ripley learns from the computer, MOTHER, that the crew can be sacrificed in the name of science.

- Decision: Ripley decides to protest against Ash.

- Modified Desire: She wants to understand why this fact was hidden from the crew.

- Modified Motivations: She suspects Ash is not on the crew's side.

- Twist-Revelation No. 3: Ripley discovers that Ash is a robot who will not hesitate to kill her to protect the Alien.

- Decision: With Parker's help, Ripley attacks Ash and destroys him.

- Modified Desire: She wishes to eliminate all traitors and leave the ship.

- Obsessive Dynamic: She will oppose anything and anyone who tries to help the Alien and will kill without hesitation.

- Modified Motivations: No. Her motivation remains her own survival.

- Twist-Revelation No. 4: Once his head is revitalized, Ash, the robot, explains to Ripley that the Alien is a perfect organism, an amoral killing machine.

- Decision: Ripley orders Parker and Lambert to prepare for evacuation and immediate destruction of the ship.

- Modified Desire: Ripley still wants to kill the Alien, but this now involves destroying the ship.

- Modified Motivations: No.

- Revelation for the Audience: Throughout the story, the Alien remains a mysterious and terrifying force. In general, the audience learns the information at the same time as Ripley and the crew members, depriving them of a sense of superiority and increasing their fear.

- Twist-Revelation No. 5: Ripley discovers that the Alien has blocked her access to the shuttle.

- Decision: She hurries to cancel the self-destruct process.

- Modified Desire: Ripley does not want to die in the ship's explosion.

- Modified Motivations: No.

- Twist-Revelation No. 6: Ripley discovers that the Alien is hiding in the shuttle.

- Decision: She puts on a spacesuit and opens the shuttle to the vacuum of space.

- Modified Desire: No, Ripley still wants to kill the Alien.

- Modified Motivations: No.

Note the final twist-revelation, typical of horror films: the place of refuge is actually the most dangerous place of all.

BASIC INSTINCT

(Joe Eszterhas, 1992)

- Twist-Revelation No. 1: Nick discovers that a professor was killed while Catherine was teaching at Berkeley.

- Decision: Nick decides to follow Catherine.

- Modified Desire: Nick wants to solve the murder and bring Catherine down from her pedestal.

- Modified Motivations: Nick and the police thought until now that Catherine was innocent; they begin to suspect her.

- Twist-Revelation No. 2: Nick learns that Hazel, Catherine's friend, is a murderer, and that Catherine knew the murdered professor.

- Decision: He decides to continue following Catherine.

- Modified Desire: No.

- Modified Motivations: No.

- Twist-Revelation No. 3: Nick discovers that Catherine's parents died in an explosion.

- Decision: He thinks Catherine is a murderer and that he must arrest her.

- Modified Desire: No.

- Obsessive Dynamic: He will beat this brilliant criminal, even if it's the last thing he ever does (which is very likely).

- Modified Motivations: No.

- Twist-Revelation No. 4: Gus, Nick's partner, informs him that an internal affairs officer named Nilsen died leaving a large sum of money in the bank, as if he had been paid by someone.

- Decision: Rick does not make a specific decision regarding this information, but he decides nevertheless to discover the source of this money.

- Modified Desire: Nick wants to understand how Nilsen obtained all this money.

- Modified Motivations: No.

- Twist-Revelation No. 5: Nick discovers that his ex-mistress, Beth, changed her name, that Nilsen had a file on her, and that Beth's husband was killed.

- Decision: Nick decides to try to find evidence of Beth's guilt.

- Modified Desire: He wants to know if Beth committed these murders while trying to frame Catherine.

- Modified Motivations: No. He still wants to solve the case.

- Twist-Revelation No. 6: Gus informs Nick that Beth was Catherine's roommate in college, and her lover.

- Decision: Nick decides to go find Beth with Gus.

- Modified Desire: Nick still wants to solve the case, but now he is sure of Beth's guilt.

- Modified Motivations: No.

You will notice that in this crime thriller the twists-revelations are increasingly important and get closer and closer to the goal.

THEME OF THE TRAITOR AND THE HERO

(Jorge Luis Borges, 1956)

Borges is one of the few writers who has been able to use great twists-revelations, even in very short stories, without them dominating the fiction at the expense of characters, symbols, the story's universe, and theme. Borges's philosophy as a writer emphasizes learning, or exploration, as a means of escaping a personal and cosmic labyrinth. Borges's twists-revelations therefore have immense thematic power.

Theme of the Traitor and the Hero is a short story almost exclusively composed of twists-revelations. An unnamed storyteller explains that he is writing a story whose details have not yet been revealed to him. His narrator, Ryan, is the great-grandson of Kilpatrick, one of the greatest Irish heroes, a man who was assassinated in a theater on the eve of a victorious revolt.

- Twist-Revelation No. 1: While writing Kilpatrick's biography, Ryan discovers in the police investigation a number of troubling details, such as a letter advising Kilpatrick not to go to the theater, much like the letter that announced Julius Caesar's assassination.

- Twist-Revelation No. 2: Ryan understands that there is a secret form of time in which events and lines are repeated throughout history.
• Twist-revelation no. 3 Ryan learns that the words supposedly spoken by a beggar to Kilpatrick can be found in Macbeth.

• Twist-revelation no. 4 Ryan discovers that Kilpatrick's best friend had translated Shakespeare's plays into Gaelic.

• Twist-revelation no. 5 Ryan learns that Kilpatrick had ordered the execution of a traitor—whose identity is unknown—just days before his death, but this information doesn't seem to fit with Kilpatrick's good character.

• Twist-revelation no. 6 Kilpatrick had entrusted his friend Nolan with the mission to discover who the traitor was, and Nolan found out that the traitor was none other than Kilpatrick himself.

• Twist-revelation no. 7 Nolan devised a plan: Kilpatrick would be dramatically assassinated; by dying a hero, he would trigger the revolt. Kilpatrick agreed to play along.

• Twist-revelation no. 8 With very little time to implement his plan, Nolan borrowed elements from Shakespeare's plays to perfect his project and make it more convincing and dramatic.

• Twist-revelation no. 9 Since the Shakespearean elements are the least dramatic part of the plan, Ryan understands that Nolan used them so that the truth, and Kilpatrick's true identity, would one day be restored. Ryan, the author, is therefore part of Nolan's plan.

• Revelation for the public Ryan refuses to reveal this last discovery and publishes a book glorifying Kilpatrick.

THE NARRATOR

To use a narrator or not: that is the question. It’s one of the most important decisions you’ll have to make as an author. We introduce this topic in relation to the plot because a narrator can radically change the order in which it unfolds. But if you write an organic story, this choice will equally impact how you present your characters.

Therein lies the rub (to extend the Hamlet metaphor a bit further). The narrator technique is one of the most misused because most authors ignore its true value and implications.

The vast majority of stories—films, novels, or popular plays—do not have an identifiable narrator. They are linear stories told by an omniscient narrator. Someone tells the story, but the audience doesn’t know who it is and doesn’t care. Stories of this type almost always have a fast pace, a single strong desire line, and a "big" plot.

A narrator is a person who describes the hero's actions, either in the first person—if they speak about themselves—or in the third—if they speak about someone else. With an identifiable narrator, more complexity and subtlety are allowed. To be very clear, one could say that the narrator allows the hero's actions to be described and commented upon.

From the moment you identify the person telling the story, readers or viewers wonder: "Why is this person telling the story? And why does this story need to be told by a person, now, in front of me?" The narrator thus tends to draw attention to themselves and, at least initially, prompts the audience to take a step back from the story. This gives the author the advantage of detachment.

A narrator also allows the audience to hear the voice of the character telling the story. There are always great debates about the term "voice" as if it were the panacea for telling stories well. When we talk about allowing the audience to hear the character's voice, it's to truly enter the character's mind, at the very moment they speak. This mind is thus expressed in the most precise and unique way possible: what the character talks about and how they say it. If one can enter the character's mind, it means it is a real person, with biases and illusions, even if they themselves are unaware. This character may or may not try to tell the truth to the audience, but whatever truth comes out of their mouth will be highly subjective. These are not the words of God or an omniscient narrator. If we push this logic to the extreme, we can say that the narrator blurs, even destroys, the boundary between reality and illusion.

Another important implication of using a narrator is the notion of memory. Indeed, the narrator recounting what happened in the past immediately brings memory into play. From the moment readers or viewers hear that the story is a memory, they feel a sense of sadness and nostalgia, a regret about "what might have been." They also feel that the story is complete and that the narrator, with the hindsight that can only come at the end, tells it with a bit more wisdom.

Some authors play on these two points—the person directly telling the story to the audience, and presenting it as a memory—to better deceive the reader or viewer by leading them to think that what they are about to hear is reliable. The narrator essentially says: "I was there, I'm going to tell you what really happened. Trust me." For the audience, it's a tacit invitation not to trust and to explore the problem of truth as the story unfolds.

In addition to increasing the problem of truth, the narrator gives the author immense advantages. It helps establish an intimate connection between the character and the audience. It can also make characters more subtle and help distinguish them from one another. Moreover, using a narrator is often a sign of the transition from a hero who acts—generally a warrior—to a hero who creates—an artist. The act of narrating the story becomes the main point, and the path to "immortality" thus shifts from a hero undertaking glorious actions to a narrator who tells them.

As for plot construction, the narrator plays a liberating role. Since the actions of the plot are framed by a person's memories, one can set aside chronology and arrange the actions in the order that is most structurally significant. The narrator can also help the author link actions and events that span a long period of time or a vast space, especially when the hero undertakes a journey. As we have already mentioned, this type of plot often seems fragmented. But framed by a narrator's memories, the actions and events suddenly seem to regain unity, and the wide gaps separating the events of the story suddenly seem to disappear.

Before discussing the best techniques, we will present what must be avoided at all costs. First of all, the narrator should not be used as a mere frame. By this, we mean that the story cannot open with a narrator essentially saying: "I'm going to tell you a story." Then recounting the events of the plot in chronological order, and, at the end, saying: "That's what happened. It was quite an amazing story."

This type of scheme, although quite common, is worse than useless. Not only does it draw attention to the narrator for no reason, but it also fails to take advantage of any of its implications and strengths. The presence of the narrator seems to have the sole function of telling the audience that they should appreciate this story because it is told "artistically."

However, there are a number of techniques that allow for maximizing the possibilities offered by the choice of a narrator. If these techniques are so powerful, it's because they are inherent in the structure of a person who needs to tell a story and a story that needs to be told. But don't imagine that you have to use them all. Each story is different. Choose the techniques that best suit yours.

1. Understand that your narrator will probably be your main character

Whatever form of narration is used—first or third person—nine times out of ten, the true hero of the story is the narrator. And this for structural reasons. Telling the story is equivalent to going halfway through the awareness stage. At the beginning, the narrator looks to the past to try to understand the impact that their actions or those of others have had on them. By describing the actions of another or those they themselves undertook in the past, the narrator glimpses an external pattern of actions and gains a deep and personal thought that changes the course of their life in the present.

2. Introduce the narrator in a dramatic situation

When a fight, for example, has just taken place, or when an important decision must be made. This method allows the narrator to be placed in the story and thus creates suspense around their identity. It also tends to give the narrator's account a quick start.

• Sunset Boulevard: The narrator, Joe Gillis, has just been killed by his mistress, Norma Desmond.

• Raging Bull: The narrator is about to step into the ring, where he will deliberately lose the championship.

• The Usual Suspects: The narrator is probably the sole survivor of a massacre and is being interrogated by the police.

3. Give them a good reason to start telling the story

Rather than simply saying: "I'm going to tell you a story," the narrator must be personally motivated, in the present, by a problem in the story. And this story problem, their personal motivation, must be directly related to the reason that drives them to tell this story at this precise moment.

• Raging Bull: The narrator-hero is a corrupt boxer. He is about to deliberately lose the championship and therefore needs to understand, before the match begins, how he got there.

• The Usual Suspects: The policeman threatens Verbal with being killed if he refuses to talk.

• How Green Was My Valley: The narrator is devastated by the idea of having to leave his beloved valley. Before leaving, he too needs to understand how he got there.

4. Do not make them an omniscient narrator

An omniscient narrator has no dramatic interest in the present. They already know everything that happened and thus become a lifeless frame. On the contrary, the narrator must have a major weakness that they can only overcome by telling the story. The memory and narration of the story must represent a painful experience. Thus, the narrator has dramatic power and is personally interesting in the present, and the act of narrating the story becomes heroic in itself.

• Cinema Paradiso: The hero, Salvatore, is rich and famous, but also sad and unhappy. He has known many women but has never truly loved any. And he hasn't returned to his Sicilian hometown in thirty years. The news of the death of his old friend Alfredo prompts him to remember his childhood in this village where he swore never to return.

• The Shawshank Redemption: Red "Redding," sentenced to life in prison, has just been paroled. He was a man without hope who thought he needed the prison walls to survive. One day Andy arrived. Like every new prisoner, he had to go through the wringer. Red bet that Andy would be the first newcomer to scream that night. But Andy didn't make a sound.

• Heart of Darkness: This is the ultimate detective story where the "crime"—the "horror" of what Kurtz may have done or said—will never be known or solved. Part of the mystery lies in Marlow's true motivations, the reasons that drive him to tell his story over and over again. The answer he gives to Kurtz's "fiancée," when she asks what her beloved's last words were, may be considered a clue. Rather than reporting the last words Kurtz actually uttered before dying—"The horror! The horror!"—Marlow prefers to lie and respond: "The last word he spoke was—your name." Marlow regrets lying to her, giving her a simple answer, and provoking undue emotion in her. And it is his guilt that drives him to tell his story repeatedly, until it is perfect, even though Kurtz's experience, and the heart of darkness itself, will forever remain unknown.

5. Try to find an original structure rather than telling the story in chronological order

The way you tell the story (via the narrator) must be exceptional. Without this, the narrator is just a useless frame. By telling the story in an original way, you justify the use of the narrator, as if to say: this story is so exceptional that it can only be told by an exceptional narrator.

• It's a Wonderful Life: Two angels tell one of their "colleagues" about the past events in the life of a man who is about to commit suicide. The third angel then shows the man in question an alternative present: what the world would have looked like if he had never been born.

• The Usual Suspects: Several people have been murdered on a docked boat. Agent Kujan interrogates a crippled man named Verbal who tells him that the whole story began six weeks earlier when the police questioned six men about a robbery. The story alternates between Verbal's interrogation and the events he describes. After releasing Verbal, Kujan observes the bulletin board in the interrogation room and sees all the names Verbal used in his story. In the present, Verbal invented all the events of the "past." He is both the killer and the narrator.

6. The narrator can try to tell the story in different ways as they strive to discover and express the truth

The story is not a fixed thing, determined from the beginning. It is a dramatic debate between the author and their audience. The act of narrating the story and the act of listening to it, and silently questioning it, must partly determine the fate of the narrative.

The narrator allows this back-and-forth by leaving openings during moments when they hesitate on the best way to tell the story, allowing the audience to fill in the blanks. Despite the difficulties they encounter, the narrator gradually comes to understand the deep meaning of the events, and by exposing them to readers or viewers and involving them, they push them to understand the deep meaning of the events in their own lives.

• Heart of Darkness: This is an anti-narrator story that actually uses three to structurally show that the "true" story is so ambiguous that it can never be narrated. A sailor talks about a narrator (Marlow) who is telling his shipmates a story that was told to him by a man (Kurtz) whose last words, "The horror! The horror!" are never explained. We are literally faced with a mystery wrapped in an enigma, an infinite regression of meaning, as obscure as "the horror" itself.

Moreover, Marlow has told his story many times, as if trying each time to get closer to the truth, but always ending up failing. He explains that he goes up the river to discover the truth about Kurtz, but the closer he gets to him, the more things become obscure.

• The Life and Opinions of Tristram Shandy, Gentleman: Three hundred years earlier, this method, although applied to a comedy, had already been used in Tristram Shandy. The narrator of this novel tells a story in the first person that goes forward as well as backward. He addresses the reader directly, reproaching them for not reading correctly. And he complains when he has to explain something he would have preferred to keep for later.

7. Do not abandon the narrator's frame at the end of the story, but around its last quarter

If you close the narrator's frame at the very end of the story, the act of remembering and narrating the story may not have a dramatic or structural impact on the present. You need to leave room in the story for the act of recounting the narrator's own change.

• It's a Wonderful Life: Angel Clarence listens to the story of George's life until the moment he is about to end his life. This account of past events occupies about two-thirds of the story. In the last third, Clarence shows George an alternative reality and helps him change.

• Cinema Paradiso: The hero, Salvatore, learns of the death of his friend Alfredo. He thinks back to his childhood, much of which took place at Cinema Paradiso, where Alfredo was the projectionist. The memory ends when Salvatore leaves his hometown to make a name for himself in Rome. The story returns to the present. Salvatore returns to his hometown for the funeral and discovers that Cinema Paradiso has become a ruin. However, Alfredo has left him a gift: a film reel featuring all the great kissing scenes that the priest had ordered to be cut when Salvatore was little.

8. The act of telling the story can lead the narrator to an awareness

By reflecting on the past, the narrator has a great awareness of themselves in the present. Remember that the entire process of narration corresponds structurally to the awareness stage for the narrator. Telling the story is therefore a means for the hero-narrator to fulfill their need.

• The Great Gatsby: Nick says at the end: "That's my Middle West [...]. I'm part of all this, a bit grave because of the feeling those long winters left me [...]. After Gatsby's death, the East was haunted for me like that [...]. That's why when the blue smoke of brittle leaves was in the air and the wind began to stretch the damp clothes on the lines, I decided to go home."

• The Shawshank Redemption: Red learns to live with hope and freedom thanks to the inspiring example of his friend Andy.

• Goodfellas: This dark comedy uses first-person narration to emphasize the ironic fact that the hero has not had an awareness at the end of the story, even though it was perfectly clear that he should have had one.

9. Push the narrator to reflect on the immoral or destructive aspect that the act of telling the story may have, for themselves or others

This technique makes narration a moral problem in itself, interesting in the present from a dramatic point of view.
