• Copenhagen: This story is a true competition of narrators: three characters present a different version of the facts that occurred when they met during World War II to discuss building a nuclear bomb. Each story represents a different viewpoint on morality, and each character uses their own story to attack the morality of the other.

10. The act of telling the story can provoke a final dramatic event

This event often corresponds to the hero's moral decision.

The narration of the story must have consequences, the most dramatic being that the hero-narrator is forced to make a moral decision based on their awareness.

• The Great Gatsby: Nick decides to leave the moral decadence of New York to return to the Midwest.

• It's a Wonderful Life: George decides not to commit suicide and to reunite with his family.

• Raging Bull: The narrator-hero, after reflecting on the past, decides not to deliberately lose the fight.

• The Shawshank Redemption: Red decides not to give up after leaving prison like his friend Brooks did. He decides to live fully and join Andy, who has started a new life in Mexico.

11. Do not fall into the trap of believing that a character's death allows for knowing their true story

This is a very common trigger: the narrator claims that since the character is dead, it is finally possible to tell the truth about them. The scene of their death and last words is presented as the key that allows the truth to finally "be restored."

This is a very bad technique. It is not your death that allows you to better understand your life by finally seeing it as a whole. It is acting as if you were going to die that creates meaning by pushing you to make choices here and now. Giving meaning to your life is a continuous process.

Similarly, the narrator can use the character's death (their own or someone else's) to give the impression that, from now on, the story can finally be told and understood in its entirety. But meaning comes from the act of narrating the story, reflecting on the past, over and over, and each time, the "true" story is different. In accordance with Heisenberg's uncertainty principle, the narrator can know a meaning at a given moment, but never the meaning.

• Citizen Kane: The meaning of Kane's last word, "Rosebud," is not that it sums up the hero's life, but that it cannot.

• Heart of Darkness: Kurtz's last words—"The horror! The horror!"—do not make the enigma of life easier to understand. It is the final mystery of a larger mystery concerning the heart of darkness that exists in every human being, including the narrator, Marlow, who tells the story over and over in a vain attempt to finally discover the truth.

12. The deep theme of the story must relate to the truth and beauty of creativity, and not to the heroism of actions

By placing all actions within the framework of narration and emphasizing the importance of the narrator's difficulties in describing these actions, you make narration the essential action and the great achievement of the story.

• The Usual Suspects: Verbal is an expert criminal who has beaten or killed everyone who tried to stop him. But his greatest achievement—which is also the main reason for his success as a criminal—is the story he improvises that makes everyone think he is a weak and pathetic man.

• Gilgamesh: Gilgamesh is a great warrior. But when his friend and comrade-in-arms dies, he seeks immortality in vain. This immortality is ultimately achieved by telling his story.

• The Shawshank Redemption: The greatest gift Andy gave to his friend Red (the narrator) and the other prisoners is showing them that one could live with hope, style, and freedom, even in prison.

13. Beware of too many narrators
Despite all these advantages, the narrator also has weaknesses. His greatest flaw is that he places a frame between the story and the audience, which usually tends to diminish the emotional impact of the story. The more narrators there are, the more likely the reader or viewer is to take a step back, to the point where they may end up viewing the story with a cold and distant eye.

Among the stories that excel in the use of the narrator are Sunset Boulevard; The Conformist; American Beauty; The Usual Suspects; Goodfellas; The Shawshank Redemption; Forrest Gump; Presumed Innocent; The Magnificent Ambersons; Heart of Darkness; The Life and Opinions of Tristram Shandy, Gentleman; Copenhagen; Madame Bovary; Citizen Kane; How Green Was My Valley; Cinema Paradiso; Gilgamesh; The Great Gatsby; Life Is Beautiful; and Blood and Gold.

GENRES

Genre is another structural element that can impact the plot. A genre is a form of story, a particular type of story. In films, novels, and plays, most stories are based on at least one genre, but more often on a combination of two or three. It is therefore important to know which form of story, if any, you will use. Each genre includes predetermined plot highlights that you must include in your story to avoid disappointing the audience.

Genres are true subsystems of the story. Each genre goes through the universal stages of narrative structure, the seven key steps and the twenty-two steps, and executes them differently. It is perfectly possible to tell a great story without relying on any genre. But if you wish to use one, you must master how it executes the structural steps and know the particular way it handles characters, theme, the story world, and symbols. Then you must use these elements in an original way so that your story does not resemble all other stories in the genre, even if in many respects, it will resemble all other stories in the genre. The audience for genre stories likes to recognize the familiar framework of the form under a new skin that gives the story its freshness.

The details of the various genres are beyond the scope of this book, and we have already written extensively on this subject in other works. Each genre is very complex, and one must fully immerse oneself in one or two of them if one wishes to have a chance of using them effectively. The good news is that with a little practice, as all good authors know, one can perfectly learn to master them.

CREATE YOUR PLOT – WRITING EXERCISE NO. 7

• Guiding Principle and Plot Review the guiding principle and theme of your story. Ensure that your plot follows these two lines.

• Symbols and Plot If you are using a story of symbols, ensure that your plot is indeed an expression of it.

• Narrator Determine whether or not you wish to use a narrator. If so, choose a type of narrator and keep in mind the structural techniques that allow you to make the most of using this narrator.

• Twenty-two Steps Describe in detail the twenty-two steps of your story. Start with step no. 1, the plot framework, so that all other steps naturally fall into place.

• Sequence of Twists-Revelations Focus on the sequence of twists-revelations. Establish a list of the different twists independently of the other steps of the story. To make these twists as dramatic as possible, follow these guidelines:

1. Ensure that the sequence seems logical.

2. Try to make each twist more intense than the one before it.

3. Check that each twist forces your hero to modify their original desire in some way.

4. Keep in mind that the closer you get to the end of the story, the faster the twists should follow one another.

We will now dissect a well-known story, The Godfather, and determine its twenty-two steps so that you understand how they can add decisive details to the seven key structural steps we have already determined.

THE GODFATHER

(Novel by Mario Puzo, screenplay by Mario Puzo and Francis Ford Coppola, 1972)

• Hero Michael Corleone

1. AWARENESS, NEED, AND DESIRE

• Awareness Michael has no awareness. He has become a ruthless killer, but only his wife, Kay, notices his moral decline.

• Need Avoid becoming a ruthless killer.

• Desire Avenge the men who shot his father.

• Initial Error Michael thinks he is different from the other members of his family and that he is above criminal activity.

2. GHOST AND STORY WORLD

• Ghost Michael's ghost is not an event from the past but a family legacy of crime and murders that he deeply despises.

• Story World The story world is the mafia system of Michael's family. It is an extremely hierarchical world, run in a military fashion, with strict rules. The Godfather, the absolute ruler, dispenses justice as he sees fit, and the family uses murder to get what it wants. The workings of this world are exposed during the wedding of Michael's sister, to which all the characters in the story have been invited, including the hidden adversary, Barzini.

The national scope of the family's power is then exposed when a Hollywood producer refuses to do what the Godfather asked. When the man wakes up, he discovers that the head of his favorite horse has been cut off and placed on his bed.

3. WEAKNESS AND NEED

• Weaknesses Michael is young, inexperienced, overconfident, and has yet to prove himself.

• Psychological Need Michael must overcome his superiority complex and dogmatism.

• Moral Need He must avoid becoming ruthless, like all other Mafia leaders, while continuing to protect his family.

• Problem Members of a rival gang shoot Michael's father, the head of the family.

4. INCITING EVENT

The distance separating Michael from the rest of his family disappears when he reads that his father has been shot.

5. DESIRE

Avenge the men who shot his father, and thereby protect his family.

6. ALLY(IES)

Michael has a wide range of allies within his family: his father, Don Corleone; his brothers, Sonny and Fredo; Tom; Clemenza; and his wife, Kay.

7. OPPONENT / MYSTERY

Michael's first opponent is Sollozzo. However, his main opponent is the more powerful Barzini, who is actually the mastermind behind Sollozzo and seeks to annihilate the entire Corleone family. Michael and Barzini clash over the survival of the Corleone family and fight for control of organized crime in New York.

8. FALSE ALLY

Michael has an unusually large number of false allies, which tends to significantly flesh out the plot. There is the car driver when his father is shot; the Sicilian bodyguard, Fabrizio, who tries to kill him but only succeeds in blowing up the car with his wife inside; his brother-in-law, Carlo, who will cause Sonny's death; and Tessio, who eventually joins Barzini's camp.

9. FIRST TWIST-REVELATION AND DECISION: MODIFICATION OF DESIRE AND MOTIVATIONS

• Twist-Revelation The hospital where Michael's father is has no guard and is literally empty. Michael realizes that men are about to come and kill his father.

• Decision He decides to protect his father by moving his bed to another room, where he will stay to keep watch.

• Modified Desire Michael no longer seeks to distance himself from his family; he now wants to protect his father and save his family.

• Modified Motivations Michael deeply loves his family and will now put his competitive and success-driven nature at its service.

10. PLAN

Michael's first plan is to kill Sollozzo and his protector, the police captain. His second plan, towards the end of the story, is to kill all the heads of the other mafia families at once.

11. PLAN AND MAIN COUNTERATTACK OF THE OPPONENT

Michael's main opponent is Barzini. His plan is to use Sollozzo as a henchman to kill Don Corleone. Once Don Corleone is out of the picture, he bribes Carlo to lure Sonny into a trap and pays Michael's bodyguard in Sicily to kill him.

12. NARRATIVE DRIVE

• Stages of Drive

1. Clemenza explains to Michael how to kill Sollozzo and McCluskey.

2. At the restaurant, Michael shoots Sollozzo and McCluskey.

3. A quick montage shows newspaper articles.

4. Sonny and Tom argue because Sonny wants to kill old Tattaglia.

5. In Sicily, Michael sees a pretty girl by the roadside and tells his father he would like to meet her.

6. Michael meets Apollonia.

7. Sonny notices Connie has a black eye. In a street, he beats up her husband, Carlo.

8. Michael and Apollonia get married.

9. Tom refuses to give Kay's letter to Michael.

10. Michael teaches Apollonia to drive; he learns of Sonny's death.

• Additional Twist-Revelation On a Sicilian road, Michael sees a beautiful Italian woman.

• Decision He decides to meet her.

• Modified Desire He wants this young woman.

• Modified Motivations He falls in love.

13. ATTACK FROM AN ALLY

• Ally's Criticism When Michael returns from Sicily, Kay reproaches him for working for his father. She tells him he's not like that.

• Hero's Justification He promises her that his family will be completely "legit" in five years.

14. APPARENT DEFEAT

Michael's apparent defeat is a "double blow." He discovers that his brother has been murdered, and immediately after, sees his wife die in an explosion meant for him.

15. SECOND TWIST-REVELATION AND DECISION: OBSESSIONAL DRIVE, MODIFICATION OF DESIRE AND MOTIVATIONS

• Twist-Revelation Michael realizes his car has been rigged with explosives and his wife is about to start it.

• Decision He tries to stop his wife, but it's too late.

• Modified Desire Michael wants to return to his family.

• Obsessional Drive He is determined to avenge the men who killed his wife and brother.

• Modified Motivations He wants to make them pay for killing the people he loved.

16. REVELATION FOR THE AUDIENCE

The audience sees Luca Brasi, Don Corleone's most dangerous ally, being murdered during a meeting with Tattaglia and Sollozzo.

17. THIRD TWIST-REVELATION AND DECISION

• Twist-Revelation Michael discovers that Tessio has switched sides and that Barzini plans to assassinate him.

• Decision He decides to strike first.

• Modified Desire He wants to eliminate all his enemies at once.

• Modified Motivations He wants to win the war once and for all.

18. NARROW DOOR, CAUDINE FORKS, AND ENCOUNTER WITH DEATH

Michael, being a very skilled fighter who even manages to deceive the audience, will not pass through the narrow door or under the Caudine Forks before the final battle. His encounter with death is that of his wife dying in an explosion meant for him.

19. FINAL CONFRONTATION

The final confrontation is presented as an alternating montage between Michael's presence at his nephew's baptism and the murder of the heads of the five Mafia families. At the baptism, Michael says he believes in God. Clemenza shoots men coming out of an elevator with a shotgun. Moe Green is shot in the eye. Michael, in accordance with the baptismal liturgy, renounces Satan. Another man shoots one of the Mafia chiefs between the panes of a revolving door. Barzini is assassinated. Tom prepares to kill Tessio. Michael has Carlo strangled.

20. AWARENESS

• Psychological Awareness There is none. Michael still believes his sense of superiority and dogmatism are justified.

• Moral Awareness There is none. Michael has become a ruthless killer. The screenwriters used a very studied narrative structure technique by giving the awareness to the hero's wife, Kay, who sees what her husband has become as the door closes in her face.

21. MORAL DECISION

Michael's major moral decision occurs just before the confrontation when he decides to kill all his rivals, as well as his brother-in-law, after becoming the godfather of the latter's baby.

22. NEW EQUILIBRIUM

Michael has killed his enemies and "risen" to the position of Godfather. But from a moral standpoint, he has fallen and become "the devil himself." This man who once wanted nothing to do with his family's violence and crimes has become its leader and will kill anyone who tries to betray or hinder him.

QUESTIONS / ANSWERS

Q: What are the main mistakes authors make regarding their plot?

A: The biggest problem for most writers is that they don't know how to create an interconnected and growing plot.

The plot is the sequence of events through which the hero tries to defeat the opponent and achieve the goal. It is what makes the internal development of the character enjoyable for the audience. It is the trick that distinguishes your story, telling the audience that you are a true storyteller.

Q: What causes this difficulty in creating the plot?

A: If most writers struggle to create their plot, it's because they think tactically instead of strategically. Certainly, you need to know specific plot techniques, the tactics that allow you to create a moment of conflict or a scene that will pay off. But a good plot is a strategy, a pattern of interconnections that the author develops to constantly pressure the hero and relentlessly attack them.

When writers have plot problems, it's almost always because they lack this overall strategic vision. They view opposition tactically, scene by scene, little piece by little piece. They see opposition as a series of isolated obstacles the hero must overcome along a path.

But this is not surprising. Because it is not easy to explain how to create a complex and interconnected strategy. Those who teach writing, myself included, generally describe opposition in distinct units. The guidelines are usually focused on the moment of attack, which is important. But these close-ups on particular points tend to complicate the author's task when it comes to understanding how to create their plot.

Q: Can you give an example of this tactical approach?

A: One of the techniques I mentioned for comedy is to place the hero in their worst nightmare. Determine the worst thing that could happen to your character and force them to endure it. The idea is to batter the hero, torture them, put maximum pressure on them.

TOOTSIE

Earlier in this chapter, we studied different moments when Michael, the hero of Tootsie, is battered by various opponents. Each of these moments is a mini-nightmare for this particular hero, a macho actor so obsessed with finding work that he has come to disguise himself as a woman.

At the first audition, for example, the director, who is also macho, rejects Michael because he, or rather she, is not pretty enough for the role.

Michael falls for Julie, one of the actresses in the series, but he can't try to seduce her because to keep his role he must maintain his disguise.

Julie's father, Les, falls in love with Michael as a woman and declares his feelings.

These are all moments that are funny in themselves. But what distinguishes Tootsie is that the screenwriters have connected these individual attacks to a larger strategy, or plot, to put maximum pressure on the hero.

Q: Are there other technical means to create moments of conflict?

A: The other technique for creating individual moments of opposition is the one imagined by Trey Parker and Matt Stone, the creators of South Park and The Book of Mormon. Instead of bringing in the next plot element by thinking "Yes, and then...", the author should think "Yes, but..." The hero takes a step towards the goal but immediately encounters a new obstacle that diverts them from their path or sets them back.

These two techniques are useful for authors to understand the tactics of a moment or a striking plot element. But if they are not integrated into a broader strategy of interconnections, they can very well undermine your plot.

Q: How can the tactical approach undermine the plot?

A: By thinking in terms of individual moments of opposition, one can easily fall into the two greatest sins of plotting:

1. episodic plot

2. repetition of the same highlight.

An episodic plot is a plot where individual events stand out but are not connected and do not grow beneath the surface.

Repetition of the same highlight means that events are superficially different but fundamentally identical. Instead of plot development, you get plot repetition.

These errors can only come from a hero facing a series of unrelated obstacles, making it impossible to build a growing plot.

Q: At what exact point in the twenty-two steps can this problem occur?

A: When using the twenty-two steps, this difficulty in creating the plot is perceived from step 4, the inciting event, to step 19, the final confrontation. But the step where the problem becomes more evident and more common is step 12, the narrative drive, and step 15, the obsessional drive, where the hero undertakes a number of actions to achieve the goal. In narrative drive and obsessional drive, the problem stems notably from two sources:

1. The hero undertakes actions that do not respond to those of the opponent, and vice versa. In other words, there is no series of attacks and counterattacks between the hero and their opponents. A series of attacks and counterattacks can only occur if each character reacts to the other's actions.

2. The actions the hero takes to achieve the goal, although different in appearance, are fundamentally identical.

In a love story, for example, the hero and the loved one may meet on various dates – restaurant, museum, beach – which, although they take place in different locations, fundamentally correspond to the same plot event.

Q: How can the author think strategically about their plot?

A: To fully understand the strategy of the plot, one must start by looking at what the verb "to plot" means: to create a scheme or secret plan to achieve a goal. In a story, the plot is the scheme the author devises to make their hero go through the worst time of their life. But this scheme must remain a secret that neither the characters nor the audience should know.

To devise this scheme, and for the causal relationships between the events of the story to become very complex, you must understand that you are working within the vast system of character development. You cannot simply dismantle the system to try to understand its connections; you would only get spare parts.

Q: How to build this "vast" plot?

A: To build this vast plot, the author's scheme, two major sequences of causality must be intertwined.

1. The attack and counterattack between the hero and the opponents as they try to achieve the goal.

2. A sequence of revealing information through which the author explains, sometimes lying, who does what to whom.

These two sequences of causality – how the hero acts and what they learn – drive the evolution of the story. To weave these two sequences throughout the story to make it a vast plot, you must consider the plot as a complete network of conflicting actions and information.

In a way, one could say that every good plot resembles a detective story. The author has devised a complex but secret scheme. They deliberately play at revealing and concealing information from the characters and the reader. The author is the killer who leaves behind a trail of clues to mislead until the most dramatic moment. The reader or viewer, and at least one of the characters, are the detectives trying to discover the truth, the real connections, hidden beneath the structure the author has presented to them.

Q: What are the elements of a good plot?

A: There are many, as it is an ambitious project, but I think three stand out particularly:

1. A good plot is linked from the first sentence to the last, from the inciting event to the final action. But the way the plot is linked should not be clear to the reader until the last sentence, ideally, in any case, not until the very end of the story. This last sentence should contain a twist-revelation that, for the first time, exposes to the reader the true connections of the plot in its entirety.

By keeping this huge twist secret until the very end of the story, the author creates an explosion, an orgasm of understanding, which is both intellectually pleasing, the beauty of the construction inspiring admiration, and emotionally touching, by the transformation this insight produces on the characters' lives.

2. A good plot has a sequence of causal connections linking the characters' actions. Nothing is left to chance. It is the opposite of the episodic plot, which most often occurs when the hero goes on a journey during which individual events occur, but without being connected to each other, which tends to prevent growing development.
Moreover, the best journey plots, like good stories of wandering knights, are only seemingly random and episodic. As Erich Auerbach writes in "Mimesis," his fundamental work on narration, the knight encounters obstacles as if on an assembly line. The author specifically designs these obstacles for this particular character, relying on his greatest flaws, so that he is inexorably led, if capable, to evolve and gain enlightenment.

3. A good plot is not a plot for the sake of plot, a vast scheme aimed solely at deceiving the audience and showcasing the author's talent for creating puzzles. A good plot always serves the characters. It is a process of actions designed to create a process within the characters.

I would go so far as to say that you cannot create good characters without testing them with a good plot. It is only by facing the challenges of intense conflict and complex plot that the character can reach their best self or, in case of failure, present to the audience, through profound and emotional means, what their life could have been.

Q: Can you give some techniques for creating a good plot?

A: I will give you four:

TECHNIQUE 1: NARRATIVE DYNAMISM IN THE PLOT

Narrative dynamism, which is the basis of popular stories (page-turners), simply relies on a plot with maximum speed and urgency. It stems from four major narrative elements:

1. The hero trying to fulfill their desire.

2. The amount of conflict between the hero and all their adversaries.

3. The number of high points—twists or major actions—in the story.

4. The pace of the high points; in other words, their acceleration as the story approaches its end.

TECHNIQUE 2: ENRICHING THE PLOT BY ADDING REVERSIBLE CHARACTERS

Reversible characters are characters who change roles in the story. They can be false allies or false adversaries.

False ally: A character who seems to help the hero achieve their goal but is actually an enemy trying to prevent them.

False adversary: A character who seems to prevent the hero from achieving their goal but is actually a friend.

If these characters are so useful for enriching the plot, it is because every time one of them changes sides, it is a twist-revelation for the hero and/or the audience. To simplify: plot = twists-revelations. So, the more reversible characters you have, within reason, the more enriched your plot will be.

THE "HARRY POTTER" SERIES (novels by J. K. Rowling, with the first published in 1997)

To illustrate the importance of reversible characters in creating the plot, let's study the most popular stories of all time, those of Harry Potter. The challenge presented by their plot for the author, J. K. Rowling, was not insignificant: to create a plot that would extend over seven books!

One of the best techniques she used to meet this challenge was to include several reversible characters, the most remarkable being Severus Snape.

Snape constantly shifts from false adversary to false ally, and vice versa, throughout the seven novels.

Professor Quirrell - false ally

Sirius Black - false adversary

Peter Pettigrew - false ally

Mad-Eye Moody - false ally

TECHNIQUE 3: STARTING WITH THE ADVERSARY'S PLAN

I teach plot using what I believe to be the most powerful tools: the twenty-two steps of any good story. By using the twenty-two steps (the seven main ones, plus the fifteen that help you develop the middle of your story), you will inevitably get a good plot. Why? Because the twenty-two steps allow you to outline the overall strategy of your plot, consisting of the sequence of conflicts and the sequence of twists-revelations of your particular story.

Step 11, the adversary's plan, though little known, is one of the most important keys to a good plot. Just as the hero has a plan to defeat the adversary and achieve the goal, the adversary has a plan to defeat the hero and achieve the goal.

KEY POINT: The more developed the adversary's plan, the stronger the plot will be. Or more precisely, the more ingenious the adversary's plan, the stronger the plot will be.

This brings us to what we believe to be the best technique of all regarding plot: to determine the "vast" plot of the story, start by determining the adversary's plan.

It is often said that the hero must lead the action. This is generally true. But if, at the start, you try to build the plot based on this rule, you will likely end up with a very weak plot. Why? Because the hero, after a few active steps, will achieve the goal too easily, without surprise for the audience.

It is better to start by determining how the adversary will deploy their forces and use cunning to defeat the hero. This will provide you with various attacks that will push the hero back and force them to react. It will also provide you with various twists-revelations: the hero will discover, one by one, the traps the adversary has set for them.

Plot = sequence of conflicts + sequence of twists-revelations

TECHNIQUE 4: SEQUENCE OF CONFLICTS ATTACKS/COUNTER-ATTACKS

We mentioned earlier that the biggest problem authors face regarding plot construction is related to a mistake: tactical thinking, focused on the single obstacle the hero must overcome, rather than strategic thinking, encompassing the sequence of conflicts and the sequence of twists-revelations that are connected and crescendo throughout the story. Some authors make this mistake from the inciting event to the end of their story.

However, in general, this big plot problem occurs more in the middle of the story, during the narrative dynamism stage. Dynamism corresponds to the series of actions the hero undertakes to achieve the goal and, in terms of page quantity, it is the most important stage of the entire story.

KEY POINT: To avoid this problem, ensure that the adversary's attacks occur in response to the hero's actions, and vice versa.

You will thus obtain an interconnected and growing sequence of attacks/counter-attacks that will extend throughout the middle of the story. The hero and the adversaries are organically linked in a global conflict sequence, and the plot develops without repeating itself (thus avoiding the episodic plot or one that repeats the same high points).

Q: Why doesn't the "three-act structure" work for plot?

A: Proponents of the "three-act structure" think in terms of "plot points" that should appear two or three times in a feature film script. The result is a plot far, very far from the density and complexity required in the intrigue-heavy world of genre-based films.

Let's be clear about this: these two or three plot points are simply ridiculous. It's a beginner's method; those who use it have no chance of writing or selling a feature film.

By writing a script with only two or three plot points, you create all sorts of problems for your story. You deprive yourself of having a plot in the middle of your story, the place where it is most difficult but also most important to create intrigue. And by doing so, you place yourself far, very far outside the realm of professional writing.

If scriptwriters using the "three-act" structure have so many problems with plot, it's partly because the expression they rely on, the "plot point," is so broad that it almost becomes meaningless. It doesn't allow for an understanding of what actually happens when the plot "bounces."

Q: But then, what is this "plot point" they refer to?

A: A plot point is either a major attack or a twist. Once this definition is established, it's easy to understand that you need far more plot points than the two or three found in scripts written on a three-act structure. Let's focus for a moment on the twists:

KEY POINT: A densely plotted film contains, depending on its story and genre, between seven and ten major twists. A densely plotted novel contains three to five times more. "David Copperfield," for example, includes more than sixty twists.

Q: American films seem artificial because they have too many twists. Does too much plot kill the plot?

A: In theory, yes, but to write something popular, we should all try to have this problem.

The real reason why too many twists give some films an artificial aspect is that screenwriters do not derive the plot from the character. It's plot for the sake of plot. Screenwriters simply twist the hero's mind, and the audience's, over and over, to make the puzzle as complicated as possible. The characters are just pawns.

The audience ends up refusing to get involved in the story because they perceive the mechanics of the plot as well as the heavy hand of the author. But worse: too much plot—a plot that does not derive from the character—tends to annihilate emotion, not only in the characters but also in the spectators.

To write a good story, you must find a balance between plot and characters. Once again, a plot without character is a puzzle. A character without plot is a drug. You need no more and no less than the amount of plot your character deserves.

Let's now turn to popular storytelling. If we said that we should all try to have this problem of excess plot, it's because the vast majority of authors struggle to imagine even the thinnest of plots, that is, a plot with only two or three twists. In the context of the global storytelling market, increasingly plot-rich across all mediums, these authors have no chance.

Popular storytelling is almost exclusively genre-based. Genres are actually specialized plot forms, and they have been written, rewritten, and perfected for so long that they have become relatively dense plot forms. One tends to fit as much plot as possible into a genre story.

All genre stories are fundamentally artificial in some respects. And in some cases, like romantic comedy, extremely artificial. If you decide to embark on genre storytelling, you must therefore accept a certain artificiality. Notably, more twists than would occur in "real life."

But this is not an excuse to write genre stories that seem artificial. Quite the opposite. Your task is to transcend the genre so that the artificial form seems real, original, and organic, with a plot that appears to derive from the characters.

TECHNIQUE: THE SIMPLE SEVEN-STEP PLOT

You can and should determine the basic plot of your story by starting with the seven steps. The plot thus defined will constitute a sound structure on which you can build all the elements that make a good story. In other words, the seven steps provide you with a plot that is not only surprising but also has structural foundations that will have strong emotional repercussions on the characters and the audience.

But for a good plot, you will need much more than seven steps, especially in the middle of the story.

Q: How do you move from a good seven-step plot to a super twenty-two-step plot?

A: To achieve this, you need to carefully study the additional steps, notably the adversary's plan, narrative dynamism, apparent defeat, and twists-revelations. We have already discussed the adversary's plan and dynamism, which provide the sequence of conflicts. Let's now focus on the very important twists-revelations.

TECHNIQUE 5: TWISTS-REVELATIONS

During a twist-revelation, the hero obtains new information that prompts them to make a decision and change the course of their actions. We can talk about a twist-revelation or a surprise. Now, surprise is what the audience loves most in a story.

Coupled with a major attack, the twist-revelation corresponds to the moment when the plot "bounces."

KEY POINT: The twist-revelation is the most important element of a good plot and is the best way to increase narrative dynamism.

For a good plot, you will need more twists-revelations occurring at a faster pace as you approach the end of the story. This is another key to achieving strong narrative dynamism and creating the vortex, or whirlwind, where everything accelerates as the story approaches its end.

Q: How do you create twists?

A: Here are five tips:

1. Assign secrets to each of the major characters, especially all the adversaries.

2. Make the adversaries cunning, not only in the plan they implement but also in the way they attack.

KEY RULE: The more the adversaries use deception, the stronger the plot will be.

3. Include a number of false allies and false adversaries.

Every time one of them changes sides, it constitutes a twist.

4. The best twists are those concerning the actions taken by the adversary to defeat the hero. The adversary hides their attack. The hero and the audience say: "I thought they were using this tactic, but in fact, it's that one." This forces the hero to adapt and counterattack.

5. The most moving twists are those that occur when there is betrayal.

This is why false allies are more useful than false adversaries: the hero discovers that a close and trusted friend is actually working for the opposing side.

This results in a twist with maximum emotional impact.

THE GODFATHER

1. Sollozzo pretends to be the sole enemy of Don Corleone. Barzini pretends to be the Don's friend and conciliator.

2. Barzini pushes Sollozzo to launch an attack against Don Corleone. They bribe the Don's driver to assassinate him. They bribe the police to get rid of the guards watching his hospital room and try to finish their job. They bribe Michael's bodyguards to try to kill him by blowing up his car. They trap Luca Brasi and kill him. They pay Carlo to beat up Connie, a decoy that lures Sonny out of the den, allowing them to kill him. They get Tessio to arrange a meeting with Michael, during which they plan to assassinate him.

3. There are four false allies in the story: the Don's driver, Michael's Sicilian bodyguards, his brother-in-law Carlo, and one of the family's top lieutenants, Tessio.

4. Michael realizes that Sollozzo has gotten rid of the guards watching his father's hospital room just minutes before the assassins arrive to kill the Don.

5. Michael discovers that the family's loyal lieutenant, Tessio, wishes to help Barzini kill him.

6. Michael discovers that his own brother-in-law, Carlo, had Sonny assassinated.

TECHNIQUE 6: THE SEQUENCE OF TWISTS-REVELATIONS

A good plot consists of two causal sequences: the actions that the hero and the opponents undertake as they vie for the goal (the sequence of conflicts) and the twists-revelations that the hero and the audience discover during the confrontation.

Q: How do you create a strong sequence of twists-revelations?

A: To achieve this, you must go through these five steps:

1. Write the list of scenes, or list of events, for the entire story.

Describe each scene, or event, in one sentence.

Tip: This list should include the actions of the hero and the adversaries attacking each other, as well as all the twists-revelations.

2. Create a separate list for the twists-revelations.

This list will include only the twists-revelations. It will be your sequence of twists-revelations and will be highly useful to you.

3. Study your sequence of twists-revelations to determine if, in general, the movement is increasing.

It is not necessary for each twist-revelation to be more powerful than the one that preceded it. But, in general, there should be an increase.

4. Try to accelerate the pace of the twists-revelations as you approach the eye of the vortex at the end of your story.

5. Don't hesitate to experiment, that is, to change the order of the twists-revelations to determine what will have the most dramatic impact on the audience.

DEFENDING JACOB (Novel by William Landay, 2012)

Premise: A prosecutor investigates the murder of a 14-year-old boy, of which his own son, Jacob, is suspected.

Sequence of twists-revelations:

1. Revelation to the reader: After the events of the story, the prosecutor is questioned before a jury.

2. The prosecutor discovers that the murdered boy was bullying his son, Jacob.

3. Jacob is suspected of the murder.

4. Jacob displays a knife at school.

5. The prosecutor finds the knife and makes it disappear.

6. Jacob is not very popular.

7. Revelation to the reader: The prosecutor's father was convicted of murder.

8. On vacation, a girl Jacob knows disappears.

9. The prosecutor's wife—Jacob's mother—finds red stains on her son's shirt. She now knows he committed a murder.

THE GENRE

Q: What is the biggest challenge French authors face when trying to make a place for themselves in the global entertainment industry?

A: Most authors are unaware of what we consider the very first rule of the global entertainment industry. It does not buy or sell movie stars, screenwriters, or directors.

The business of the entertainment industry is the buying and selling of genres.

99.9% of all films, novels, TV series, video games, and webisodes belong to at least one genre and generally correspond to a combination of two, three, or even four genres.

Q: Why is this a specific problem for French authors?

A: For decades, French films have been trapped by the theory of auteur and art-house cinema, which could only lead to the idea that a film is built primarily on the director's point of view. This results in strange, self-centered dramas that interest no one other than the director's close circle. However, they do not result in good stories. And that's what the audience, not only French but also international, demands.

The key element that allows for storytelling with universal appeal is genre. But strangled by the theory of auteur and art-house cinema, most French screenwriters and writer-directors have despised genre storytelling, considered an American predictable matter addressing only the lowest common denominator. Refusing to make American films in French, they have worked on the margins of the genre so that their films have been neither good genre films nor good art-house films.

It has only been about fifteen years since the French entertainment industry realized it could not hope to compete in a global entertainment market driven not by the director's point of view but by the story, especially the genre story. Starting with thrillers and gangster stories, France has proven it can tell exciting, plot-driven genre stories that appeal to a wide audience worldwide while containing specifically French elements that distinguish them as artistic successes.

Q: What are the keys to mastering genre storytelling?

A: Genre is a specialized plot form, such as action, love, crime, or true story. In addition to being the basis of 99.9% of films, TV series, novels, and video games worldwide, genre has more impact than any other element on how you tell your story.

There are twelve major genres: action, comedy, suspense, crime, fantasy, horror, love, "masterpiece" (which, theoretically speaking, is not a true genre), memoirs/true story, myth, science fiction, and thriller. Each major genre has a unique set of narrative high points (events)—between eight and fifteen—that are used to chain the plot. Each genre also expresses a set of themes, a mini-philosophy about how to live well.

There are two main principles to follow to write a genre story that is not just a repetition of the story everyone tells in this form.

1. Mix genres that the audience is not used to seeing together.

"Inception," for example, combines science fiction and heist story.

2. Transcend the genre.

To transcend a genre, the author must use each of the specific high points of the form but reshape them to give them an aspect the audience has never seen before. The audience thus has the double pleasure of enjoying the narrative form they like and seeing it treated in a fresh and surprising way.

A PROPHET

Screenwriter-director Jacques Audiard is one of the first to have brought French cinema into genre storytelling, which now defines global entertainment. His technique? Transcend the form to benefit from the genre's popularity with a wide global audience, but also reshape this form to make it powerful and original.

As Jacques Audiard says, the stylization of the genre film is a way to accelerate the connection with the viewer. Here's the good guy, here's the bad guy. But once the viewer is on board, it's up to the filmmaker to be more subtle and push the boundaries of the genre.
A Prophet represents a significant advancement in French cinema, transitioning from difficult and slow films relying on the vision of their directors to fast-paced genre stories. With A Prophet, screenwriters Thomas Bidegain, Audiard, Abdel Raouf Dafri, and Nicolas Peufaillit found a way to transcend the false distinction between genre cinema and art-house cinema, thus creating a genre film that is, in itself, a true work of art.

Elaborate Plot and Genre Techniques in A Prophet

A Prophet is a transcendent gangster story that twists the rules of the highlights of this form and adds powerful dramatic elements that propel it to a higher level. Let's examine some of the techniques used in the script:

TECHNIQUE OF A PROPHET: TRAPPED IN A SYSTEM

The screenwriters use an advanced storytelling technique applied to the gangster story: the hero trapped in a system. The most advanced stories, in all mediums, place the individual within a vast society and pose this key question: how to live without being enslaved by a system?

In the opening scene, the hero of A Prophet, Malik, doesn't just enter prison; he enters a powerful and rigid system. From the first scenes, he gets a glimpse of this enslaving system and how it operates. Malik, in action and under the extreme threat of death, must reconstruct the hierarchy and rules of the system and determine how to play it before it kills him.

This technique is also used in another film that transcends the gangster narrative, The Godfather, although the character who enters the system here is not the hero. His function is to allow the screenwriters to begin exposing a complex system to the audience, but this explanation is emotional rather than intellectual, giving it a much stronger dramatic impact.

TECHNIQUE OF A PROPHET: THE DILEMMA

In the chapter on the seven steps, we explained that the screenwriters of A Prophet used the technique of postponing desire. The hero is unable to have an active goal in this harsh prison environment, and by postponing his desire, the authors allow the audience to experience the horror of the situation before being caught up in the action.

But with this technique, you risk losing some of your audience if you make them wait too long before starting the story. So the screenwriters immediately added another technique: the dilemma. To implement this technique, you must give the hero two contradictory goals: if he tries to win A, he will lose B, and if he tries to win B, he will lose A.

In the early scenes of A Prophet, the screenwriters move Malik from one dilemma to another, notably when the prison "boss" orders him to kill another inmate. Malik wants to survive, but he doesn't want to have another human's blood on his hands.

This technique reinforces the feeling of being trapped in the system for both the audience and the hero. It also has the advantage of providing the narrative dynamism of the story where the hero has no active goal.

TECHNIQUE OF A PROPHET: ADDING STORYLINES

At the beginning of A Prophet, Malik has no active desire and does not create the plot. But as he learns about the system and his power grows, he begins to aim for an active goal, which is to become the leader. And as the hero's goal strengthens, the screenwriters add other storylines, both inside and outside the prison.

Note that a reactive hero is not able to lead a plot. But by gaining knowledge and aiming for a goal, he gains the ability to create a plot, and the story becomes progressively more interesting. The hero and the audience follow the same path throughout the film, moving from one state to another:

1. Not understanding the system of enslavement and being a victim of it.

2. Understanding it, gradually controlling it, and ultimately, leading it.

Q: Can you give an example of another French film that transcends the genre?

A: Each major genre has a number of subgenres with their own sets of highlights (events) related to plot development.

"Traveling angel" stories deal with a perfect person who solves the problems of a community before moving on to another. It is an ancient form. It is found in the eternal stories of Santa Claus making his rounds, in classic detective stories like those of Sherlock Holmes, in classic westerns like Shane, and in some tragedies like Dead Poets Society.

But this story is more popular as a sub-form of comedy. Mary Poppins is the perfect example, as are Being There, Ferris Bueller's Day Off, Crocodile Dundee, and Good Morning Vietnam.

TECHNIQUE: HOW TO TRANSCEND THE TRAVELING ANGEL COMEDY GENRE

It is mainly the traveling angel comedy, as well as the thriller and gangster film, that have paved the way for the French movement towards genre storytelling. One could even say that over the past fifteen years, France has become a true world leader in creating this type of story.

Amélie, Welcome to the Sticks, and The Intouchables are excellent examples of this highly popular form. The Chorus is a traveling angel drama linked to music. All these films reveal the potential of French cinema, which can be highly competitive in the global market while maintaining excellent quality and a distinctively French style.

THE INTOUCHABLES

(screenplay by Olivier Nakache and Eric Toledano, 2011)

When you use a genre, you must find a way to make your story distinct from all those written in the same form. That is, to transcend the genre. To transcend the traveling angel comedy genre, the most important technique is to give the angel a moral weakness and need or, at the very least, a deep and genuine psychological weakness, which will be overcome by the end of the story. This way, the main character will not just be a perfect angel solving everyone else's problems; he will also have to overcome the weakness that ruins his life.

Most traveling angel comedies do not attribute a flaw to their angel, which tends to limit their emotional impact on the audience. If the French have become world leaders in this form of story, it is mainly because their main characters almost always have a deep flaw, as seen in Amélie or The Intouchables.

Let's now study the specific highlights of the traveling angel form and see how The Intouchables manages to both fit the genre and transcend it.

1. Start by establishing the community, not the hero.

2. Quickly but thoroughly expose the delicate situation in which the community and the people within it, who have particular weaknesses and problems, find themselves.

3. Introduce the traveling angel, perfect to some extent. The angel must always enter a unique arena.

The Intouchables twists the rules of this highlight by starting with the angel, not the community. And this is because the screenwriters gave him a moral flaw, a major structural element of a traveling angel story that transcends its genre. They needed to establish this weakness in the "angel" before presenting him actively solving the problems of the specific community mentioned.

Driss, the main character, returns to his aunt's house after spending six months in prison for theft. And he gives her a Fabergé egg he stole from Philippe when he applied for the job.

His aunt exclaims: "Do you think [...] I'm the biggest fool? Six months without seeing you, not a call, nothing. And you show up like a flower offering me a Kinder. Do you think it's with your schemes that I pay the rent? The groceries? [...] Idiot!" And she throws him out.

4. The angel must have a unique and amusing way of arriving.

Mary Poppins, for example, floats on a cloud.

Driss applies for the position of Philippe's caregiver, but he just wants a signature on a document to get his unemployment benefits. Philippe, in a wheelchair, asks him to come back the next day. Driss says goodbye with a "Stay seated."

5. The angel informs the characters that he will leave when he sees a sign.

This highlight is more common in traveling angel stories based on fantasy. It is therefore not used in The Intouchables.

6. Give the angel a good position in the community, but not one with too much responsibility.

Driss is Philippe's caregiver and home nurse.

7. Establish an opponent who is external to the community and an internal opponent who has responsibilities within the community. The internal opponent is often the father and the main adversary of the story. He is authoritarian and must learn to live.

In The Intouchables, Philippe is Driss's main adversary. He is a man of responsibilities, the "father" of the house. Traditionalist, sad, withdrawn, hopeless, he is simply trying to overcome his depressing life as a quadriplegic. He needs to learn not just to survive, but to live with joy, spirit, and love.

As the film focuses heavily on the love story between Driss and Philippe, it does not feature an external adversary.

In one of the best opposition scenes between the two protagonists, Philippe dictates a poetic letter to his pretty assistant, Magalie, addressed to Éléonore, a woman he has never met. Driss mocks Philippe's style: "Why go through all this mess? The sphinx, the daisies, the angels..." He turns to Magalie: "Are you interested in a guy who tells you that? [...] How long has it been going on? – Six months," replies Magalie. Driss insists that Philippe call Éléonore, before grabbing the last letter she wrote to him, which includes her phone number. "This means: call, I'll lick your ear," says Driss. He dials the number, and Philippe is forced to talk to her.

8. The angel must use cunning.

9. The angel has a series of realizations about the best ways to help the different people who have problems.

10. The angel solves the problems in this order: 1. those of the least important characters, 2. those of the external adversary, 3. those of the main (internal) adversary.

Driss confronts Philippe about the rudeness of Élisa, his spoiled daughter, towards all the household members. "She treats us all like dogs." Philippe agrees to talk to her.

Driss forces Élisa's boyfriend, Bastien, to apologize to her for breaking up and insulting her. And he orders Bastien to bring her croissants every day.

At Philippe's birthday, a classical orchestra plays music. Philippe tries to interest Driss in great music, but Driss responds that, for him, music is for dancing. Driss plays a funky Earth, Wind and Fire track and demonstrates a dance. "Come on, it's a birthday, let's dance!" he exclaims. The other people start dancing. Philippe laughs; he is clearly having a good time.

11. The realization occurs among the characters who have problems, especially the main (internal) adversary. They learn to have more self-esteem, to love, and to love life.

12. These realizations lead to marriages, and the community comes together to form a whole.

Driss takes Philippe to a luxurious seaside restaurant. He tells him: "I'm not staying for lunch, Philippe. In fact, you have a date." He returns the Fabergé egg he stole from him, asks him to kiss the young woman for him, and leaves. A pretty woman, Éléonore, sits across from Philippe and starts talking. Philippe seems delighted.

13. Once the angel has helped all the characters, especially the main internal adversary, solve their problems, he must see the sign and leave.

As Philippe talks with Éléonore, Driss walks away along the beach.

14. The community is saddened by the angel's departure, but its members have found love and know he will return one day.

1. Peter Brook, The Empty Space. Writings on Theatre, Le Seuil, 2014.

2. Peter Brooks, Reading for the Plot, Harvard University Press, 1992, p. 168.

3. Edgar Allan Poe, in a review of Night and Morning by Edward Bulwer Lytton, Graham’s Magazine, April 1841, pp. 197-202.

4. Northrop Frye, "The Road of Excess," in Bernice Slote (ed.), Myth and Symbol (Lincoln: University of Nebraska Press, 1963), p. 234.

5. Henrik Ibsen, "Letter in Verse," in Det nittende Aarhundrede, April-September 1875.

6. Translation by François-Victor Hugo (Translator's Note).

7. Twist and revelation-twist translate reveal or revelation in the original text. Realization translates self-revelation.

8. Translation by Victor Llona (Translator's Note).

– 9 –

The Weaving of Scenes

What makes Jane Austen and Charles Dickens such great authors, who still appeal to audiences in our world of ever-increasing technology and speed? The answer is simple: they are masters in the art of weaving scenes.

The word "scene" generally refers to a single action taking place in a single location at a given time. The scene is the basic unit of the story's progression, as experienced by the audience, here and now. The weaving of scenes is the organization of these units. Great authors create a tapestry-like weave, bringing a thread to the surface before plunging it below the weave to reappear later in the story.

The weaving of scenes, also known as the "scene list," "scene scheme," or "scene breakdown," is the final step before the full writing of the story or script. It is the list of all the scenes that you believe should be in the final story. This list also includes comments for each scene that corresponds to a structural step.

The weaving of scenes is an extremely important step in the writing process. Like the seven steps, the character network, and the sequence of twists-revelations, it is a way to see how the different elements of the story fit together beneath the surface.

The weaving of scenes is actually an extension of the plot. It is the plot, but more precise. The interest of the weaving of scenes is that it allows for a final look at the overall architecture of the story before writing it. Therefore, it should not be too detailed; this would tend to obscure the structure. Try to describe each scene in a single sentence. Here is an example of a description of four scenes from The Godfather:

• Michael saves the Don from an assassination attempt at the hospital.

• Michael accuses police captain McCluskey of working for Sollozzo. McCluskey hits him.

• Michael hints that he wants to eliminate the captain and Sollozzo.

• Clemenza explains to Michael how to kill them.

You will notice that only the essential action of each scene has been reported in the list. If you manage to describe each scene in one or two sentences, your list should only span a few pages. When a scene corresponds to a structural step (such as "desire," "plan," or "apparent defeat"), you should note it next to its description. Some scenes will have a comment, but many will not.

KEY POINT: Know that you will likely need to modify your scene weaving when you start writing the scenes one by one.

When writing a scene, you may discover that the essential action occurring in it is not what you thought. You can only be sure of this action by entering "into" the scene, that is, by writing it. Therefore, you must be very flexible. What is important at this stage of the process is to achieve an overall vision of the actions that you believe should be the main actions of the different scenes.

Know that the average American film has forty to seventy scenes. Novels generally have twice as many, or even much more (depending on their length and genre).

Your story may include subplots that, woven with the rest, will form the plot. If you have more than one subplot, assign each one a number. This will allow you to consider each subplot as a unit in itself and ensure that they build correctly.

Once your scene weaving is complete, determine if you need to make any of the following changes:

• Rearrange the order of the scenes Start by reflecting on the entire story. Then study the juxtapositions of each scene.

• Merge scenes Authors often create scenes solely because they think they can insert good lines into them. Whenever possible, merge scenes, ensuring that each scene corresponds to a single essential action.

• Cut or add scenes Always think about trimming. Remember that the pace of the story is not only related to the length of the scenes but also to the choice of scenes. Once you have removed the superfluous, you may feel that there are gaps between certain scenes that need to be filled with new ones. If so, add these new scenes to your list.

KEY POINT: The order of scenes should be dictated by structure, not chronology.

Most authors determine the order of scenes based on the chronological order of actions (scenes). This results in stories "padded" with unnecessary scenes. Instead, choose scenes by asking what they can contribute to the hero's development. If a scene contributes nothing to the hero's development, it is better to cut it.

With this technique, you ensure that each scene plays an essential role in the story and is in the right place. Most often, in the end, the scenes are in chronological order, but this is not necessarily the case.

KEY POINT: Pay special attention to the juxtaposition of scenes.

The juxtaposition of two scenes can be more important than the content of each scene, especially in television and cinema, where the transition from one scene to another is instantaneous. When studying juxtapositions, focus first on the contrast of content. Ask yourself how each scene comments on the scene that preceded it.

You can then look at the contrast of proportions and rhythms. Ask yourself if the length of each scene is appropriate to that of the scene that preceded it and well-proportioned in terms of importance.

The rule is simple: find the line and keep that line.

There are scenes—such as those of the subplot—that only serve to support the narrative line. Insert them and keep moving forward. But be aware that if you stray from the narrative line for too long, your story will eventually collapse.

There are many ways to give power to juxtapositions. The best of them, especially in cinema and television, is to juxtapose image and sound, that is, to separate these two means of communication to create a third meaning.

M

(Thea von Harbou and Fritz Lang, 1931)

A very good illustration of this technique is found in the great German film M: a child murderer buys a balloon for a little girl. In the next scene, a woman prepares dinner and calls her daughter, Elsie. As she continues to shout the little girl's name, the visual separates from the sound, and the audience sees an empty staircase, a group of buildings, Elsie's empty chair, and her plate and spoon on the kitchen table, all while hearing the increasingly desperate cries of the mother calling "Elsie!" The visual line ends with a shot showing a balloon caught in electrical wires before detaching and flying away. This contrast between the sound line and the visual line is the source of one of the most moving moments in cinema history.

The most common scene juxtaposition technique is undoubtedly cross-cutting. This technique allows for back-and-forth between two lines of action, or even more. Its consequences are twofold:

1. Cross-cutting creates suspense, especially when switching more and more quickly from one scene to another, for example, when a character must hurry to save another who is in danger.

2. It also allows for the comparison of two lines of action, two contents, and places them on an equal footing. This tends to broaden the scope of the theme. Each time you switch from one line of action to another, you move from a simple linear development (usually that of a single character) to the presentation of a deeper characteristic present throughout society.

An example of cross-cutting is found in the sequence of M where the story goes back and forth between a group of police officers and a group of criminals. Each group tries to determine how to catch the child murderer, and the cross-cutting shows the audience that these two types of people, generally considered opposites, are in many ways identical.

THE GODFATHER

(novel by Mario Puzo, screenplay by Mario Puzo and Francis Ford Coppola, 1972)

An even better example of cross-cutting is found in the confrontation scene of The Godfather. The challenge was to create a confrontation scene that expressed Michael's character, who has become the new Godfather. By alternately showing Michael's men assassinating the heads of the five mafia families, the screenwriters not only managed to bring a series of punch events to the plot but also to express Michael's new position as a sort of crime business leader. Michael does not kill these men himself; these are not crimes of passion. He hires crime experts through his "company."
This first cross-cutting is complemented by a second: the story alternates between the multiple murders and Michael, who renounces Satan to become the godfather of a child whose father he will soon assassinate. Through this second cross-cutting, the audience understands that Michael has become Satan at the very moment he rises to the position of Godfather.

We would now like to compare the weaving of scenes from an early draft of The Godfather to the final version. This will allow you to see how much a good juxtaposition of scenes—and in this case, sections—can contribute to the quality of a story. The key difference between these two weavings of scenes appears right after Michael kills Sollozzo and Captain McCluskey in the restaurant. In the first draft, you will notice that the writers grouped all the scenes related to Sonny's death and the end of the war between the families (underlined). Then they grouped all the scenes of Michael in Sicily, ending with the assassination of his wife (in italics).

THE GODFATHER: FIRST DRAFT

1. In a restaurant, Michael talks with Sollozzo and McCluskey; Michael pulls out his gun and shoots them.

2. Montage of newspaper articles.

3. Sonny gets out of a woman's bed and goes to his sister Connie's.

4. Sonny notices that Connie has a black eye.

5. In the street, Sonny beats up Carlo, Connie's husband.

6. Tom refuses to give Michael Kay's letter.

7. Don Corleone returns home after his stay in the hospital.

8. Tom explains to Don Corleone what happened; the Don is sad.

9. Sonny and Tom argue because Tom wants to have old Tattaglia killed.

10. A violent argument breaks out between Connie and Carlo; Connie calls home; Sonny is furious.

11. Sonny is assassinated at the toll booth.

12. Tom informs Don Corleone of Sonny's death; Don Corleone responds that war must be declared.

13. Don Corleone and Tom bring Sonny's body to the undertaker Bonasera.

14. Don Corleone makes peace with the other family heads.

15. Don Corleone learns that the leader is actually Barzini.

16. In Sicily, Michael sees a pretty girl by the roadside and tells his father he would like to meet her.

17. Michael meets Apollonia.

18. Michael and Apollonia get married.

19. Wedding night.

20. Michael teaches Apollonia to drive; he is informed of Sonny's death.

21. Michael's car explodes while Apollonia is driving.

This sequence of scenes posed several problems. The most dramatic and important scenes from a plot perspective—those of Sonny's death and the revelation about Barzini—appeared first. There was thus a big lull when the plot moved to Sicily. Moreover, the sequence of Michael in Sicily was relatively long and slow. The story came to a screeching halt, and the writers had enormous difficulty getting the "train" moving again at the end of this section. By presenting all the scenes with Apollonia together, the writers also tended to emphasize the sudden and somewhat implausible aspect of Michael's marriage to a Sicilian peasant. The dialogues try to gloss over this aspect by suggesting that Michael fell in love at first sight. But if the audience had seen all these scenes at once, this explanation would have remained unconvincing.

THE GODFATHER: FINAL VERSION

In the final version of the script, the writers overcame the potentially fatal weakness of their scene weaving by alternating Sonny's line with Michael's:

1. In a restaurant, Michael talks with Sollozzo and McCluskey; Michael pulls out his gun and shoots them.

2. Montage of newspaper articles.

3. Don Corleone returns home after his stay in the hospital.

4. Tom explains to Don Corleone what happened; the Don is sad.

5. Sonny and Tom argue because Tom wants to have old Tattaglia killed.

6. In Sicily, Michael sees a pretty girl by the roadside and tells his father he would like to meet her.

7. Michael meets Apollonia.

8. Sonny gets out of a woman's bed and goes to his sister Connie's.

9. Sonny notices that Connie has a black eye.

10. In the street, Sonny beats up Carlo, Connie's husband.

11. Michael and Apollonia get married.

12. Wedding night.

13. Tom refuses to give Michael Kay's letter.

14. A violent argument breaks out between Connie and Carlo; Connie calls home; Sonny is furious.

15. Sonny is assassinated at the toll booth.

16. Tom informs Don Corleone of Sonny's death; Don Corleone responds that war must be declared.

17. Don Corleone and Tom bring Sonny's body to the undertaker Bonasera.

18. Michael teaches Apollonia to drive; he is informed of Sonny's death.

19. Michael's car explodes while Apollonia is driving.

20. Don Corleone makes peace with the other family heads.

21. Don Corleone learns that the leader is actually Barzini.

Due to the cross-cutting, on screen, the slow Sicilian line seems less long, preventing it from killing the narrative momentum of the plot. Moreover, the two lines converge to the same point, which is the apparent defeat of the hero, the lowest point he reaches during the story (see chapter 8, "The Plot"), the moment when he learns of Sonny's death just before witnessing his wife's. This "pair of slaps" is highlighted by the next big revelation: it was actually Barzini who was behind the whole story. This revelation about the true adversary's identity propels the rest of the plot toward its astonishing conclusion.

Of all the techniques we have presented in this book, scene weaving is probably the easiest to understand when illustrated with examples. So let's start with a simple example, taken from the TV series ER, because good series always rely on weaving a fine tapestry where multiple narrative lines are juxtaposed.

WEAVING MULTIPLE THREAD PLOT SCENES

The plot of series of this type includes between three and five threads, each with its own hero. Due to the limited time allotted to narrating each of the stories (each episode lasting sixty minutes minus commercials, or forty-five minutes), no plot line of any episode can have much depth. The writers hope to compensate for this problem over the entire season, or even over the succession of different seasons.

KEY POINT: With multiple-thread weaving, the quality of the entire story comes first from the juxtaposition of the plot lines. The problem faced by the members of a mini-society is compared at the same time. The audience is thus presented in condensed form with the different solutions the main characters bring to a problem that is generally the same for all.

KEY POINT: When there are three to five threads, you cannot go through the twenty-two steps for any of the plots. However, each plot must meet the seven major structural steps. If one of them has fewer than seven steps, it will not be a complete story, and the audience will find it useless and boring.

KEY POINT: When there are multiple main characters and multiple plots, you must frame the entire story and maintain the narrative line by making the hero of one line the adversary of the other. This prevents the story from exploding even more, and from ending up with, for example, five heroes, five adversaries, myriads of secondary characters, etc.

If ER and many other TV series use the alternation of threads, it is because this technique gives the episodes a strong dramatic density. These stories have no downtime. The audience only sees the dramatic "punch" scenes of each plot. ER's creator, Michael Crichton, who is also Hollywood's greatest premise author, managed to combine the advantages of medical drama and the "action" genre. Crichton added to this mix a network of characters covering a wide range of social classes, races, ethnic origins, nationalities, and genders. And this combination proved very powerful and very popular.

"THE HESITATION WALTZ" (Jack Orman, 2000)

The episode we will study has five plot lines, each built on a number of previous episodes.

• Plot no. 1 Abby visits her mother, Maggie. It is known that she is bipolar and tends to stop taking her medication, then disappear for long periods.

• Plot no. 2 Dr. Elizabeth Corday is being sued: she allegedly botched one of her operations, causing her patient to be paralyzed.

• Plot no. 3 In a previous episode, Dr. Peter Benton's nephew was murdered by street gang members. The boy's girlfriend, Kynesha, shows up at the hospital, her face marked by blows.

• Plot no. 4 Dr. Mark Greene has hidden something from his girlfriend Elizabeth (Dr. Corday) and the other doctors. Today he will find out if his brain tumor is fatal.

• Plot no. 5 Due to his previous drug problems, Dr. Carter must undergo regular medical exams if he wishes to continue working at the hospital.

The first thing you notice in this episode is that the different plot lines have an underlying unity. They are variations on the same problem. And that's what makes the juxtaposition interesting. On a superficial level, most of these plots involve a character with drug problems. But, more importantly, the five plots talk about the consequences of lying and sincerity.

The strength of the weaving of "The Hesitation Waltz" relies on two principles of storytelling: each plot is a variation on the theme of truth and lies, and the five stories converge toward the most powerful realizations or twist-revelations that the main character and the plot are capable of receiving.

Enigma

1. Abby's mother, Maggie, who is bipolar, finds her daughter counting her medication. Abby wants to have her mother take a blood test to ensure she is taking her medication. Plot no. 1: weakness and need, adversary.

By studying this scene weaving in detail, you will notice that each plot line meets the seven steps. Each story is thus strong enough to stand on its own. Once these foundations are established, the writer can play with the juxtaposition of individual scenes from each plot.

Act 1

2. Dr. Greene reassures his girlfriend, Elizabeth, by telling her she did not commit negligence and that her deposition will be perfect. Elizabeth tells him to stop bumping his head against signposts when jogging. Plot no. 2: weakness and need; plot no. 4: problem and need.

3. At the hospital, Maggie begs Abby not to have her take a blood test, saying that both of them would feel worse. Abby reluctantly agrees. Plot no. 1: desire, adversary.

4. A woman named Stephanie is looking for Dr. Malucci. Maggie arrives running and says a young girl has been thrown from a car. Plot no. 3: weakness and need.

5. Dr. Cleo Finch, Abby, and Maggie help the injured girl. Abby asks her mother to leave. Plot no. 3: weakness and need.

6. Lawyer Bruce Resnick, the opposing counsel, is strangely friendly with Elizabeth when he meets her for her deposition. Plot no. 2: adversary.

7. Cleo tells Dr. Peter Benton that he should not take the patient because she is his late nephew's girlfriend. Dr. Benton refuses to listen. Plot no. 3: specter, desire.

8. Greene learns from his doctor that his tumor is inoperable. Plot no. 4: twist-revelation.

Act 2

9. Carter corrects a misdiagnosis by Greene. Greene reminds Carter that he must take his blood and urine tests due to his drug problem. Plot no. 5: weakness and need, adversary; plot no. 4: desire.

10. Peter, Cleo, and Abby try to determine if Kynesha was raped. She insists she just fought with a group of girls. Plot no. 3: adversary.

11. In her deposition, Elizabeth states that she had to operate on her former lover Peter's (Dr. Benton) nephew first and was upset by the boy's death when she operated on the opposing party's client. Plot no. 2: momentum, adversary.

12. Carter jokes while Abby draws his blood. Greene doesn't seem amused at all. Abby learns her mother had an incident in a clothing store. Plot no. 5: adversary. Plot no. 1: twist-revelation.

13. Kynesha refuses to reveal to Peter the identity of her attackers. Peter tells her that if his nephew was killed, it was because he came to see her. She explains that if the gang members killed him, it was actually because he was trying to get her out of the gang. Plot no. 3: twist-revelation.

14. Abby must help her mother to avoid a shoplifting charge. Plot no. 1: adversary

15. Greene reproaches Carter for not taking his medication, as stipulated in his contract. Carter responds that it's too much. Greene collapses and has a seizure. Plots no. 4 and 5: combine personal stories via opposition between doctors.

In scene 10 (plot no. 3), Kynesha arrives at the hospital. She has been beaten and possibly raped. Kynesha is the girlfriend of Peter's nephew, who died some time ago. In the next scene (11, plot no. 2), the lawyer asks Dr. Corday if she was affected by the young man's death when she operated on his client. Scene 10 (plot no. 3) is therefore an extension of the plot line to which scene 11 (plot no. 2) refers.

Act 3

16. Greene gets up and refuses to comply with Carter's request, who advises him to get a head scan. Plot no. 4: narrative momentum.

17. Lawyer Resnick states that Elizabeth operated on his client too quickly because she was in a hurry to get to a personal appointment. Plot no. 2: adversary.

Scenes 16 (plot no. 4), 17 (plot no. 2), and 18 (plot no. 1) all feature characters—Greene, Elizabeth, and Maggie—who lie to others and refuse to admit the importance of their problem.

18. Maggie insists she acted within her rights. Abby tells her mother she needs stitches. Plot no. 1: adversary.

19. Police officers ask Kynesha to reveal the identity of Peter's killer so they can arrest him. Kynesha refuses to speak. Plot no. 3: adversary.

20. Greene explains to Carter that he has a brain tumor and will probably no longer be able to work from today. Plot no. 5: twist-revelation.

21. Elizabeth's lawyer asks her to limit her answers to yes and no. Elizabeth responds that this amounts to hiding the truth. Plot no. 2: adversary.

In scene 20 (plot no. 5), Greene finally tells someone the truth about himself. This scene is immediately followed by scene 21 (plot no. 2) in which his girlfriend Elizabeth's lawyer asks her to hide the truth.

22. Maggie flirts with Abby's boyfriend, Dr. Kovac, while he stitches her wound. She is under the influence of drugs. Abby apologizes. Her mother verbally attacks her and then runs away. Kovac brings her back to the operating table while she screams at Abby not to do this to her. Plot no. 1: adversary.

The last scene, the dramatic convergence point of act 3, presents the terrible consequences of lying. At her workplace, Abby, belittled by her mother, experiences intense public humiliation.

Act 4

23. Elizabeth gets up to make the last part of her deposition and comes face to face with her patient waiting in a wheelchair. Her lawyer tells her not to be moved. Plot no. 2: twist-revelation, narrative momentum.

24. The hospital psychologist tells Abby she can admit her mother to her service if she wishes, but Abby doesn't care about her words. She leaves. Plot no. 2: narrative momentum.

In scene 23 (plot no. 2), which corresponds to the beginning of the final act, Elizabeth is confronted with the consequences of her negligence: the patient who sued her shows up at the deposition in a wheelchair.
