25. Peter puts Kynesha in a taxi, giving her recommendations on how to care for her injuries. Kynesha gives him the finger. Plot no. 3: adversary.

26. The opposing party's lawyer claims the anesthetist told Elizabeth that some cerebrospinal fluid could leak. She lies by maintaining that she conducted a thorough examination. Plot no. 2: confrontation, revelation for the audience.

27. Carter tells Abby that her mother is no longer there. Abby replies that she often disappears for four months before reappearing; "It comes and goes, it's our little waltz." Plots no. 5 and no. 1: combine personal stories by having one addict learn about another; Plot no. 1: realization.

28. Kynesha tells Peter that police officers came to her house and that gang members will now want to kill her. Peter puts her in his car. Plot no. 3: adversary.

As the story nears its end, confrontations and twists multiply, which is one of the greatest narrative advantages of the multiple-thread technique. In the confrontation scene of plot no. 2 (scene 26), during her deposition, Elizabeth makes her big moral decision: she lies. Then Abby, from plot no. 1, explains to Carter, who lied about his own drug problems in plot no. 5, that her mother and she are in a constant waltz of drugs, lies, and mutual meanness.

29. Elizabeth explains to Greene that the deposition went badly. She tells him she lied. Greene replies that she is not the only one. He explains that his headaches are not from a jogging accident. Plot no. 2: twist-revelation and realization.

In the penultimate scene of the episode, Greene and Elizabeth support each other to face a negative reality.

30. Abby gets out of the bed she shares with Kovac, turns on the bathroom sink faucet, and starts crying. Plot no. 1: former balance.

The last scene constitutes a brilliant dramatic twist for the first plot. By starting and ending with a scene from plot no. 1, the writer framed the entire episode and unified all the story's plot lines. Abby wakes up in the middle of the night and turns on the bathroom faucet to cry without waking her partner. For people who constantly dance the same waltz, things never change. We return to the former balance. For Abby, the realization about her mother and herself was tragic. The audience suddenly realizes that life is not a story where people necessarily change and flourish at the end. And this is painful. It's a very beautiful weaving of scenes.

WEAVING SCENES – WRITING EXERCISE NO. 8

• Scene List Make a list of all the scenes in your story. Try to describe each scene in one sentence.

• Twenty-two Steps Comment on all the scenes that correspond to one of the twenty-two steps. If your story has multiple plot lines, mark after each scene the number of the plot it corresponds to.

• Scene Order Study the order of the scenes. Ensure that the sequence of scenes is well constructed on structure, not chronology.

1. Cut unnecessary scenes.

2. Merge all the scenes you can.

3. Add scenes if you notice gaps in the story's development.

The best way to understand scene weaving is through practice, so we would like to change our usual end-of-chapter pattern by studying not one but several examples. Of course, each scene weaving is unique to its story. But precisely, by observing each of the examples, think about the different scene weaving problems that must be solved by the author depending on the story's particularity and its genre.

WEAVING SCENES OF THE DETECTIVE STORY

L.A. CONFIDENTIAL (novel by James Ellroy, screenplay by Brian Helgeland and Curtis Hanson, 1997)

L.A. Confidential presents one of the best scene weavings we have seen in recent years. It's a huge funnel that begins with three police heroes in the corrupt world of the Los Angeles Police Department. As the story progresses, the writers weave these three distinct threads together so that they form a single narrative line. The narrative momentum relies on the three heroes, who function among themselves as adversaries while searching for the killer, the funnel's convergence point.

These foundations allow the writers to compare, through cross-cutting, the three heroes and their different approaches to justice and crime-solving. They also allow them to create a series of twists that become increasingly dense as the funnel narrows and the story moves toward its convergence point.

In the following scene weaving, Bud White is designated as hero no. 1, Jack Vincennes as hero no. 2, Ed Exley as hero no. 3, and Captain Smith as the main adversary, although he appears to be an ally.

1. In voice-over, Sid Hudgens, editor-in-chief of the scandal magazine Hush-Hush, presents Los Angeles as a paradise but claims it's just an image. Beneath the surface, Mickey Cohen runs organized crime. Story universe.

In the opening scene, a voice-over narrator establishes the foundations of the story universe—the Los Angeles of the 1950s—and the essential thematic opposition on which this world rests—an apparent utopia that is corrupt beneath its surface.

2. Officer Bud White arrests a parolee who has just beaten his wife. Hero no. 1.

3. Sid agrees to pay Sergeant Jack Vincennes, technical advisor to the TV series Badge of Courage, to arrest an actor in possession of cannabis so he can photograph him. Hero no. 2: need, false ally.
4. Sergeant Ed Exley answers questions from a journalist who asks what it's like to start in the police force. Captain Dudley Smith implies that Ed doesn't have the guts to become a detective because he refuses to break the law to catch criminals. Ed insists he wants to become a detective lieutenant. Hero no. 3: desire, main adversary, false ally.

5. Bud is buying alcohol for the precinct's year-end party when he meets Lynn Bracken, a young woman with the looks of Veronica Lake. Hero no. 1: desire.

6. Outside, Bud beats up Leland Meeks, a former cop turned driver for Pierce Patchett. A bandaged woman resembling Rita Hayworth tells him she's fine. Bud's partner, Dick Stensland, says he's seen Meeks before but doesn't know him personally. Hero no. 1: adversary, story universe, ally.

7. Jack arrests actor Matt Reynolds and a girl, while Sid photographs the scene for Hush-Hush magazine.

8. While searching for cannabis in Matt's apartment, Jack discovers a card marked "Fleur de Lis." Sid writes his article and pays Jack. Hero no. 2: twist-revelation.

9. Stensland explains to other officers that if they're late, it's because Bud had to help a damsel in distress.

10. When Jack arrives with Matt and the girl, he gives Ed $10 for allowing the operation to proceed smoothly. Ed refuses. Hero no. 2 vs. hero no. 3: adversary.

11. Cops enter the precinct with Mexicans who had beaten other officers earlier in the evening. Drunk, the cops, led by Stensland, beat the Mexicans despite Ed's protests. Bud and Jack join them. Hero no. 1 and no. 2: adversary.

12. Bud refuses to testify about his colleagues' role in the fight. He is suspended from duty.

These early scenes lead to a turning point defining the three heroes and the corrupt world of the police. Ed is the only cop who refuses to beat the Mexicans (scene 11). In this scene and the following ones, Ed becomes an adversary to Bud and Jack (scenes 10-15).

13. Ed agrees to testify and suggests the chief corner Stensland and Bud. The chief promotes Ed to lieutenant. Ed explains how to get Jack to confirm his testimony. Hero no. 3: narrative drive, story world.

14. The chief threatens Jack with banning him from working on the series. Jack eventually agrees to testify.

15. Before testifying, Jack asks Ed what he got in exchange for his own testimony. He strongly advises him always to support his colleagues, especially Bud. Hero no. 2 and no. 3: adversary.

16. The captain returns Bud's badge and gun and asks him to join him for a special mission, a "muscle job" related to a homicide. Hero no. 1: desire.

17. Two of Cohen's henchmen are murdered in their car. Adversary's plan.

18. Cohen's dealer is shot in his house. Adversary's plan.

19. In an isolated hotel, the Victory Motel, Bud beats up a gangster, whom the captain tells it's time to leave town. Hero no. 1: narrative drive.

From scene 16 to scene 21, the story fragments into three lines presented in alternating montage. Bud has a new role as the captain's henchman; the hidden adversary kills several gangsters; and Jack discovers a clue that will ultimately lead to one of the two main adversaries.

20. While working on his new mission, Jack notices the fleur-de-lis sign on pornographic photographs circulating in the city. Hero no. 2: twist-revelation.

21. Jack tries in vain to find information about an organization called "Fleur de Lis." Sid claims he's never heard of it. Hero no. 2: narrative drive.

22. Stensland returns his gun and badge, says goodbye to his colleagues, and, as he leaves, kicks a box that lands at Ed's feet.

23. Stensland tells Bud he has a confidential date tonight but that they'll have a drink together during the week. Hero no. 3: adversary.

24. Alone in the precinct, Ed learns by phone that several murders have occurred in a bar, the Nite Owl. Hero no. 3: inciting event.

25. Ed goes to the crime scene and discovers a pile of bodies in the bathroom. Hero no. 3: desire.

26. The captain decides to take charge of the investigation and makes Ed his second. They discover that one of the victims is none other than Stensland. Hero no. 3: twist-revelation.

27. Bud goes to see Stensland's body in the morgue. Ed explains what seems to have happened.

28. A woman struggles to identify her daughter's body. She says she's changed a lot. Bud realizes the dead girl is Susan Lefferts, the girl who looked like Rita Hayworth in the car. Hero no. 1: twist-revelation.

29. The captain tells his men that three young black men were seen shooting and driving a brown car in the neighborhood the night of the murder. The chief tells the officers not to spare any means. False ally's plan; hero no. 1, 2, and 3: narrative drive.

30. Bud decides to investigate something else. Ed agrees to help Jack verify a hunch. Hero no. 1, 2, and 3: narrative drive.

31. Bud asks the liquor store owner for Susan's address. Hero no. 1: narrative drive.

32. Patchett tells Bud that if the girl's face in the morgue was marked, it's because she just had cosmetic surgery to look like Rita Hayworth. Susan was part of his stable of movie star lookalikes who rented out their services. Hero no. 1: twist-revelation.

33. Bud asks one of Lynn's clients, a city councilman, to leave. Lynn explains the deal she made with Patchett. Bud asks to see her again, then retracts his words. Hero no. 1: desire.

34. A black boxer, whose brother is in prison, explains to Jack and Ed where they can find a man driving a brown car. Hero no. 2 and 3: twist-revelation.

35. When Jack and Ed arrive, two detectives are already in front of the brown car. During the arrest, Ed asks the two other cops not to shoot the three black men.

36. The captain tells Ed that the cartridges found in the back of the brown car are identical to those from the crime scene. During the interrogation, Ed uses a sound recording system to let each of the three suspects hear the statements made by the others, making them talk. Hero no. 3: twist-revelation.

37. Ed manages to get one of the men to admit he hurt a girl, and Bud threatens to kill him if he doesn't give him the address. Hero no. 1 and 3: twist-revelation.

38. Bud enters the house first and discovers a girl lying on a bed. He shoots a black man in the chest, then puts a gun in his hands to make it look like he fired first. Hero no. 1: narrative drive.

39. Ed tells Bud he doesn't think the naked man was armed. Bud replies that he tried to hit him. The two cops learn that the Nite Owl suspects have escaped. Hero no. 1 vs. hero no. 3: opposition.

40. Ed looks at the interrogation transcript to find out where the suspects got their drugs. He asks one of the captain's men to help him. Hero no. 3: twist-revelation.

41. A shootout erupts. Ed is the only survivor. Hero no. 3: narrative drive.

42. The captain and other cops congratulate Ed and start calling him "Shotgun Ed."

In this section of the script, the writers avoid fragmenting the narrative line by focusing on the opposition between the heroes, Bud and Ed (scene 39). Ed searches for the escaped suspects. He is the only survivor of the shootout (scenes 40 and 41). This large section of the story ends with what seems to be the end of the narrative drive (scenes 42-44).

43. Ed is awarded a medal of honor.

44. Jack receives a warm welcome upon his return to the series set.

45. Lynn notices Bud watching her from his car.

46. The city councilman tells a man he won't vote for Patchett's project. The man shows the councilman a photo of him in bed with Lynn. Adversary's plan.

47. The city councilman announces he will vote for the project.

The writers then bring the adversary, Patchett, from the background to the forefront through several scenes revealing the extent of his influence over the city (scenes 46-49).

48. Patchett attends the Santa Monica freeway inauguration.

49. Patchett smiles as he watches Lynn flirt with a client at one of his receptions.

50. Bud is disgusted when the captain asks him to beat up another gangster at the Victory Motel. The captain watches him drive away.

51. Bud knocks on Lynn's door, and she lets him in. They kiss on the bed. Hero no. 1: narrative drive (second).

52. Sid pays Jack $50 to arrest the prosecutor in the act with young actor Matt Reynolds later in the evening. Matt asks Jack if they've met at a "Fleur de Lis" party. Sid and Jack promise Matt a role in the series if he agrees to have an affair with the prosecutor. Hero no. 2: narrative drive.

53. Bud and Lynn go to the movies.

54. Jack feels guilty and leaves the $50 bill Sid gave him on the bar. Hero no. 2: realization, moral decision.

55. Jack goes to the motel and discovers Matt is already dead. Hero no. 2: twist-revelation.

56. The rape victim tells Ed she lied about the actions of the three men. Hero no. 3: twist-revelation.

57. In bed, Lynn tells Bud she plans to return to her hometown in two years to open a clothing store. He tells her he got his scar trying to save his mother, whom his father beat to death. Bud wants to stop being the captain's muscle and work on homicide cases. He feels something is wrong with the Nite Owl case.

Lynn tells him he's insightful. Hero no. 1: ghost, desire (new).

58. Bud looks at the Nite Owl photos. He remembers that Stensland and Susan died there. Hero no. 1: twist-revelation.

59. Susan's mother identifies Stensland as her daughter's boyfriend. Bud finds something fishy and discovers Meeks' body under the house. Hero no. 1: twist-revelation.

60. Ed is concerned about the Nite Owl case and discovers Bud was also interested in it this morning. Hero no. 3: twist-revelation.

61. Ed learns from Susan's mother that Bud has already searched under the house. Hero no. 3: twist-revelation.

From this point, the story tightens around the heroes' search for the real killer. Initially, each investigates on their own, with their own reasons (linked to redemption) and using their own techniques (scenes 58-62).

62. Ed takes the body to the morgue and tells the staff not to talk about it.

63. Ed asks Jack to follow Bud because he doesn't trust any cop. He explains that he nicknamed the man who killed his father, a policeman, and got away with it "Rollo Tomasi." That's why he wanted to become a cop. Jack replies he doesn't remember why he wanted to become a cop. He agrees to help Ed with the Nite Owl case if Ed agrees to help him solve Matt's murder. Hero no. 2 and 3: ghost, desire, realization, moral decision.

64. Gangster Johnny Stompanato tells Bud that Meeks probably tried to run off with a load of cocaine he was entrusted with. Hero no. 1: twist-revelation.

65. Jack and Ed see Bud kissing Lynn in her apartment. Hero no. 2 and 3: twist-revelation.

66. Jack tells Ed he thinks all these cases are linked to "Fleur de Lis."

67. Ed tries to question Stompanato. He mistakes the real Lana Turner for a lookalike prostitute. Hero no. 2 and 3: twist-revelation.

68. Jack and Ed question Patchett about Matt and ask why Bud is having an affair with Lynn. But Patchett says nothing.

69. After Ed and Jack leave, Patchett calls Sid. Adversary's plan.

70. The coroner tells Jack the body is indeed Meeks'. Hero no. 2: twist-revelation.

71. Jack asks to see Meeks' file when he worked in vice.

72. Lynn tells Ed that if she loves Bud, it's because he's the opposite of him, Ed, the political animal who would ruin himself to solve a case. Ed starts kissing her. She moves so Sid can take good photos of them in bed. Hero no. 3: desire.

73. Jack goes to the captain's house. He remembers that a few years ago, the captain supervised a case where Stensland and Meeks investigated Patchett. The captain shoots Jack. Jack's last words are "Rollo Tomasi." Attack of the false ally; hero no. 2: twist-revelation.

74. The captain tells his team to relentlessly pursue Jack's killer. He questions Ed about a possible associate of Jack, Rollo Tomasi. False ally's plan; hero no. 3: twist-revelation.

75. The captain asks Bud to accompany him to the Victory Motel to help beat up the man who likely killed Jack.

76. The coroner explains to Ed that he told Jack the body was indeed Meeks', the former cop. Hero no. 3: twist-revelation.

As they took particular care in creating the story universe's foundations and crafted three seemingly distinct narrative lines at the beginning, the writers can now hit the audience with a series of twists. Ed and Jack's teamwork ends with the most surprising twist, which has a bombshell effect on the audience: the captain kills Jack (scene 73).

77. The captain interrogates Sid about Jack and Patchett while Bud beats him up. When Sid claims he photographed Lynn sleeping with a cop, Bud goes into a rage, tears the photos from him, and leaves. Attack of the false ally; hero no. 1: twist-revelation.

78. The captain finishes off Sid while he begs, saying they form a team with Patchett. Revelation for the audience.

79. Ed asks an employee to find the file listing people Meeks arrested when he worked in vice.

80. Lynn tells Bud she thought she was helping him by sleeping with Ed. Bud hits her. Hero no. 1: adversary.

81. Ed learns that Meeks and Stensland were under the captain's orders. Bud hits Ed. Ed points a gun at Bud, saying Meeks was killed over a heroin deal. Ed tells him Dudley's men must have framed the three black men, and somehow all these cases are linked to Patchett.

Bud and Ed continue searching separately until a mini-confrontation. Then they agree to work together (scene 81). This team will lead the rest of the story.

Hero no. 3: twist-revelation. Hero no. 1 vs. hero no. 3: opposition.

82. Ed tells the prosecutor he wants an investigation into the captain and Patchett. The prosecutor refuses. In the bathroom, Bud shaves his head and hangs him out the window. The prosecutor then admits the captain and Patchett take a cut from Cohen's rackets, but he can't prosecute them because they have compromising photos of him. Adversary; hero no. 1 and 3: twist-revelation.

83. Ed and Bud find Patchett dead next to a fake suicide letter. Hero no. 1 and 3: twist-revelation.

84. Ed asks the local police to escort Lynn to the station to protect her from the captain.

85. Lynn tells Ed she knows nothing about the captain.

86. Bud finds Sid dead in his office. He learns Ed has arranged to meet him at the Victory Motel. Hero no. 1: twist-revelation.

87. When Bud arrives, he realizes he and Ed have fallen into a trap. A shootout ensues, and Bud and Ed kill several of the captain's men. Bud falls into a hole in the floor. Ed is hit. Two men arrive to finish him off when Bud emerges from the floor and kills them. The captain shoots Bud twice. Ed calls the captain "Rollo Tomasi, the guy who gets away with it." Bud stabs the captain's leg. The captain shoots Bud again, but Ed points a shotgun at him. The captain promises Ed he'll make him chief detective if he doesn't kill him. Sirens are heard approaching. Ed shoots the captain in the back. Hero no. 1 and 3: twist-revelation, final confrontation; hero no. 3: realization, moral decision.

88. During the interrogation, Ed explains that the captain was behind the murders of Susan, Patchett, Sid, and Jack and that he worked with the Los Angeles mob. The prosecutor tells the police chief they might save the precinct's reputation by turning the captain into a hero. Ed says it would take more than one hero for that to work. Story universe.

89. The chief awards Ed a new medal. From afar, Lynn watches.

90. Ed thanks Bud, covered in bandages in the back seat of Lynn's car, who is leaving for her hometown. New balance, hero no. 1 and 3.

Ever the politician, Ed earns another medal from this murder (scene 89). He says goodbye to his radical opposite, Bud, the simple guy who goes to live in a small town with Lynn (scene 90).
1. Luke and Han patrol the icy planet of Hoth. An ice monster knocks Luke off his tauntaun and drags him into the ice. Problem.

2. Han returns to the rebel base. Chewbacca repairs the Millennium Falcon. Allies.

3. Han requests leave to pay the huge debt he owes to Jabba the Hutt. He says goodbye to Princess Leia. Allies.

4. Leia and Han argue about the real or imagined feelings they have for each other.

5. C-3PO and R2-D2 announce that Luke is still missing. Han asks for a situation report. Allies.

6. Despite the report, which announces very high levels of icing, Han insists on going to find Luke.

7. Luke escapes from the ice monster's lair.

8. In the rebel base, C-3PO and R2-D2 worry about Luke.

9. Luke struggles to survive in the literally freezing cold. Han searches for him. Encounter with death.

10. Leia reluctantly agrees to close the base doors. Chewbacca and the droids worry about Han and Luke.

11. Obi-Wan Kenobi explains to Luke that he must train with Yoda. Han arrives and saves Luke. Inciting event.

12. In small planes, some rebels set out to find Luke and Han and eventually locate them.

13. Luke thanks Han for saving his life. Han and Leia resume their lovers' quarrels.

14. The general reports that a strange signal has just been detected from a new probe. Han decides to investigate.

15. Han and Chewbacca destroy the Imperial probe. The general decides to evacuate the planet. Twist-revelation.

16. Darth Vader learns of these new facts. He orders the invasion of the planet Hoth. Opponent.

17. Han and Chewbacca repair the Millennium Falcon. Luke says goodbye to them.

18. The rebel general learns that Imperial forces are approaching. He deploys an energy shield for protection.

19. Vader kills a hesitant admiral and orders a ground attack on Hoth. Opponent's plan and attack.

20. Imperial forces attack the rebel base. Luke and his team of pilots defend themselves. Confrontation.

21. Han and Chewbacca argue while repairing the Millennium Falcon. C-3PO says goodbye to R2-D2, who will accompany Luke.

22. Luke's plane crashes. Luke manages to escape the Imperial walker just before it destroys his plane. Confrontation.

23. Han orders Leia to board the last transport ship before it leaves. Imperial forces enter the base.

24. Luke blows up an Imperial walker while another walker destroys the main power generator.

25. Han, Leia, and C-3PO can no longer access the transport ship. They run towards the Millennium Falcon.

26. Vader and the Imperial forces enter the rebel base. The Millennium Falcon takes off.

27. Luke and R2-D2 flee Hoth. Luke tells R2-D2 they are heading to Dagobah. Desire.

28. Pursued by TIE fighters, Han unsuccessfully tries to activate the hyperdrive. He pilots the Millennium Falcon into an asteroid field.

29. Luke lands in the desolate, barren, and swampy lands of Dagobah. Plan.

30. Vader orders the Imperial fleet to follow the Millennium Falcon into the asteroid field.

31. C-3PO tries to repair the hyperdrive function. Han and Leia continue their lovers' quarrels.

32. Yoda meets Luke but hides his true identity. He promises to lead him to Yoda. Ally.

33. C-3PO finally discovers the hyperdrive function problem. Han and Leia end up kissing.

34. The Emperor announces that Luke Skywalker is the new man to kill. Vader vows to turn Luke to the "dark side." Opponent's plan.

35. Yoda reveals to Luke that he is the Jedi Master. He worries about Luke's impatience and lack of motivation. Twist-revelation.

36. TIE fighters search for the Millennium Falcon in the asteroid field.

37. Han, Leia, and Chewbacca look for a place to land. Han narrowly avoids a giant serpent. Twist-revelation, opponent.

38. Luke trains with Yoda in the swamps. Luke leaves Yoda to face a strange challenge of the Force. Need, narrative drive.

39. Luke enters a cave and fights against the specter of Darth Vader. He manages to cut off the specter's head and sees his own face instead. Need, twist-revelation.

40. Vader asks bounty hunters to search for the Millennium Falcon. The admiral informs him that it has been found.

41. TIE fighters push the Millennium Falcon out of the asteroid field. Han pilots the Millennium Falcon straight towards the star cruiser.

42. The admiral watches the Millennium Falcon head towards the cruiser. The radar operator loses the Millennium Falcon's signal on his screen.

43. Luke continues his training. He fails to lift the X-Wing from the swamp. Yoda does it with ease. Apparent defeat.

44. Vader kills the new admiral for his mistake and replaces him with another.

45. The Millennium Falcon hides in the star cruiser's garbage chute. Han decides to scout the mining colony of Lando Calrissian.

46. Luke has a vision of Han and Leia in danger in a city in the clouds. He wants to save them. Twist-revelation.

47. Han struggles to land in Lando's colony. Leia worries about the troubled past Han shares with Lando.

48. Lando welcomes Han and the others. The two men discuss their turbulent past. A stormtrooper secretly shoots C-3PO. False ally.

49. Yoda and Kenobi plead with Luke not to stop his training. Luke promises to return after saving his friends. Attack by an ally.

50. The Millennium Falcon is almost repaired. Leia worries about C-3PO's disappearance.

51. Chewbacca finds C-3PO in a pile of garbage. Lando flirts with Leia.

52. Lando explains his plan to Han and Leia. He leads the couple to Darth Vader.

53. Luke heads to the mining colony. Narrative drive.

54. In a prison cell, Chewbacca repairs C-3PO.

55. Vader promises to hand Han's body to the bounty hunter. Lando complains about the changes made to their agreement. Opponent's plan and attack.

56. Lando explains the arrangement to Han and Leia. Han punches Lando. Lando replies that he did what he could.

57. Vader inspects a carbonite freezing chamber designed for Luke. He states he will test it on Han first. Opponent's plan.

58. Luke approaches the colony.

59. Vader prepares to freeze Han. Leia tells Han she loves him. Han survives the freezing process. Opponent's attack.

60. Luke fights stormtroopers. Leia warns Luke it's a trap. Luke explores a secret passage.

61. Luke finds Vader in the freezing chamber. They fight with their lightsabers. Confrontation.

62. Lando's allies free Leia, Chewbacca, and C-3PO. Lando tries to explain himself. They all rush to save Han.

63. The bounty hunter loads Han's body into his spaceship and leaves. The rebels fight against the Empire's soldiers.

64. Luke and Vader continue their battle. Luke flees the freezing chamber. The pressurized air sucks him into a ventilation shaft. Confrontation.

65. Lando and the others head to the Millennium Falcon. Lando orders the city's evacuation. They escape aboard the Millennium Falcon.

66. Luke fights Vader in the ventilation shaft gallery. Vader reveals he is Luke's father. Luke rejects the "dark side" and falls. Final confrontation and realization.

67. Leia senses that Luke needs help. Chewbacca turns back, and they manage to save Luke. TIE fighters approach.

68. The admiral claims he has deactivated the Millennium Falcon's hyperdrive. Vader prepares to intercept the Millennium Falcon.

69. Luke wonders why Kenobi never told him about his father. R2-D2 repairs the hyperdrive function. The Millennium Falcon escapes.

70. Vader watches the Millennium Falcon disappear.

71. Lando and Chewbacca promise to save Han from Jabba the Hutt. Luke, Leia, and the droids watch them leave. New balance.
60. At the airport, the opening voice-over is repeated, but this time it's Alex's heart that's broken. Mélanie advises him to return to his life of lies and brief affairs. Their father was right: he runs away when things get tough. He defends himself and leaves.

61. Alex takes a taxi but has forgotten his wallet. He has to run away.

62. As she walks up the aisle, Juliette asks her father how much he paid. He tells her Alex refused the money. He tells her there's a car outside with the keys in the ignition. She runs away.

63. As her car gets stuck, she runs. Alex runs toward her. They meet on the road. He tells her he hates everything she loves. He doesn't know if he's good enough for her, but he needs to see her every day. She kisses him.

64. Sophie tells Jonathan she doesn't understand how Juliette could do this to him. She tells him she's available.

65. Juliette's father tells the thugs that Alex must not know that he actually owed the money to himself.

She will then explain her specter, which is not related to romantic relationships: when she was in college, she followed a rock star's tour and missed her mother's funeral.

There is a true sense of initiation between the two main characters as they share their most painful memories, and despite the scam (of which only Alex, its author, is aware), a genuine intimacy develops between them.

As the film approaches its end, Alex takes the scam to an even higher level: he wants to "work without a net." He decides to focus on Juliette's psychological need. An action film highlight is also introduced here: the hero is at his best when improvising.

66. Marc tries to take over Alex's job by seducing women. He fails and asks Mélanie to critique his work.

Scene 44:

Highlight 12: The confrontation must take place in a public place where everyone is disoriented and everyone mistakes each other's identities.

This is not the case here. The scam continues until the end of the story, and the two main characters do not have a final confrontation scene in front of a group of witnesses. The screenwriters have therefore reversed this highlight. In scene 44, the "confrontation" is actually the couple's first date and first dance, which mimics the loved one's favorite movie, Dirty Dancing.

By the first dance of a love story, we mean the moment when future lovers dance, either physically or verbally, and where the intimacy between them reaches a new level. It's love expressed through action.

In a romantic comedy, the first dance usually occurs at the beginning of the middle section. It's often the best scene in the love story. But when it happens so early, its power is sometimes diminished.

The first dance is the best highlight in Heartbreaker, and thanks to the film's unique structure that pushes it to the end of the story, this highlight has an exceptional dramatic and emotional impact. You will also notice that the hero is still in the logic of the scam, which thus reaches new heights.

Key point: Delaying the first dance and the first kiss until the climax of the story is a brilliant maneuver, which also reveals the power that can be obtained by twisting the rules of genre highlights and transcending the form.

Scene 60:

Highlight 13: During the psychological realization, each understands that they are in love with the other and that they are not chained by their past. At the airport, Alex repeats the opening sequence in voice-over, but this time it's his heart that's broken. His sister tells him: "It's true what Dad said: as soon as it gets difficult, you run away."

This is Alex's psychological realization. Confronted by his sister, who throws his initial weakness in his face, he responds: "I'm not running away!" And he runs to try to win the heart of the love of his life before she marries another.

It is in scene 62 that Juliette has her realization. As she walks up the aisle with her father, her "perfect" fiancé waiting just a few meters away, she has a stunning realization when her father whispers to her that Alex refused the money he was supposed to give him.

Her realization? I'm in love with Alex and he sacrificed himself for me. So she runs away to be with the man she truly loves. This highlight echoes the end of a classic American romantic comedy, It Happened One Night.

Scene 63:

Highlight 14: In the moral realization, the hero and the loved one must both understand that they have been selfish and undertake a moral action. This is the "double-reversal" during which each learns from the other and evolves.

Ideally, each of the two main characters should sacrifice for the other the strongest symbol of their selfishness.

During his moral realization, Alex gives up Juliette's father's money and confesses the truth to Juliette: he hates everything she loves. He doesn't know if he's good enough for her – he doesn't own a private jet or an apartment and sleeps in his office – but he needs to see her every day. This line echoes the end of When Harry Met Sally.

Juliette's moral realization? She apparently gives up her fortune and forgives him for his scam by kissing him.

This moment represents a minor weakness in the script: she has no trouble forgiving him for lying to her and almost ruining what she thought was her marriage to the ideal man.

Scene 63:

Highlight 15: The story ends with a wedding.

In Heartbreaker, the two characters run toward each other and form a union, a sort of marriage, with their first kiss.

Key point: The first kiss of the two main characters occurs in the last scene. As they did for the first dance highlight, the authors delayed this kiss to place it literally at the end of the film. This first kiss, which is already a major event, thus produces an even stronger emotional impact.

– 10 –

Construction of scenes and symphonic dialogues

Scenes are the framework of action. Through description and dialogue, all elements of the premise, structure, characters, moral debate, story universe, symbols, plot, and scene weaving are transformed into a story that will be experienced by the audience. It is with the construction of scenes that the story is brought to life.

The word "scene" refers to a single action taking place in a single location at a given time. But what are scenes composed of? How do they work?

A scene is a mini-story. This means that a good scene must go through six of the seven structural steps, the exception being the realization, which is reserved for the end of the story. In most scenes, the realization step is generally replaced by something else: a twist, a surprise, or a discovery.

BUILDING A SCENE

When you build a scene, you must always set two goals:

• Determine its place in the overall development of the story and the function it fulfills.

• Make it a mini-story.

These two goals must necessarily be achieved, with the arc of the hero's overall development remaining a priority.

KEY POINT: Think of the scene as a triangle pointing downward.

The beginning of the scene should summarize its entire content, then the scene should converge to a precise point, with the most important word or phrase being spoken last:

Now let's study the steps you should ideally go through to build a scene. Ask yourself the following questions:

1. Position in the character arc: What place does this scene occupy in the character's development (also known as the character arc) and what function does it fulfill?

2. Problems: What problems need to be resolved in this scene, or what goals need to be accomplished?

3. Strategy: What strategy can be used to solve these problems?

4. Desire: Which character's desire will lead this scene (it can be the hero or another character)? What do they desire? This desire will provide the scene with its backbone.

5. Outcome: What is the outcome of this character's desire? By determining the end in advance, you will build the entire scene with confidence that you are heading toward this point.

The outcome of the desire also coincides with the tip of the inverted triangle, the moment when the most important word or phrase in the scene appears. This combination of desire fulfillment and key word or phrase produces a K.O. that also pushes the audience toward the next scene.

6. Opponent: Determine who opposes the hero's desire and why the characters are fighting.

7. Plan: The character with the desire has a plan that should allow them to achieve their goal. Within a scene, characters can use two types of plans: the direct plan or the indirect plan.

With a direct plan, the character with a goal openly and directly defines what they wish to achieve. With an indirect plan, they pretend to want one thing while actually wanting something else. The opponent has one of two reactions: they understand the deception and manipulate the hero, or they are fooled and end up giving the character exactly what they wanted to obtain.

There is a simple rule to determine which kind of plan the character should use: the direct plan increases the intensity of the conflict and separates the characters. The indirect plan, at first, decreases the intensity of the conflict and brings the characters together, but it can later also increase the conflict when the deception is revealed.

Note that the term "plan" here refers to how the character attempts to achieve their goal in the scene, not in the entire story.

8. Conflict: Build the conflict gradually until its outcome: the rupture or the appeasement.

9. Twist or revelation: From time to time, the characters or the audience (or both) are surprised by what happens before their eyes. Or a character reveals something to another in an aside. This is a kind of realization on the scale of the scene, but it is not definitive and may even prove to be wrong.

You will notice that many writers, thinking they are making their story more "realistic," start their scenes very early and develop them slowly until the main conflict. This does not make the scenes more realistic; it makes them boring.

KEY POINT: Start your scenes as late as possible, but without skipping any of the key structural steps you need.

COMPLEX SCENES OR SUBTEXT SCENES

A subtext scene is generally defined as a scene in which the characters do not say what they really want. This definition may be true, but it does not tell you how to write this type of scene.

The first thing to understand about subtext is that popular wisdom is wrong: subtext is not always the best way to write a scene. In general, subtext characters do not say what they think or really want because they are afraid, because it would be painful, or because they are simply embarrassed. If you want a scene with maximum conflict, do not use subtext. But of course, if subtext is a technique suited to your characters and their scenes, you should absolutely not hesitate to use it.

The subtext scene relies on two structural elements: desire and plan. To achieve maximum subtext, the following techniques can be used:

• Assign several characters in the scene a secret desire. The different desires must conflict with each other. For example, A is secretly in love with B, who is secretly in love with C.

• Ensure that all characters with a secret desire use an indirect plan to achieve their ends. These characters say something, but they actually want something else. They may try to deceive others, but they may also use a ruse that they themselves find crude while hoping that the artifice will have enough charm to allow them to obtain what they want.

DIALOGUES

Once your scenes are constructed, you must use description and dialogue to write them. The delicate art of description goes beyond the scope of a work on fiction. This is not the case with dialogues.

Dialogues are among the most misunderstood writing tools. Many writers are mistaken about the function they fulfill in the story: they ask them to serve as scaffolding for the narrative, to play the role of narrative structure. And the result is dialogues that seem artificial, forced, and false.

But the worst mistake about dialogues is the extreme opposite: rather than asking too much of the dialogues, thinking they should resemble real conversations.

KEY POINT: The dialogues of a story are not the conversations of life. They are composed of highly selective language that gives them a realistic air.

KEY POINT: Good dialogues are always smarter, wittier, more metaphorical, and better argued than real-life conversations.

The lines of the least intelligent and least educated character cannot fall below the threshold of intelligibility. And even when a character is wrong, they are wrong more eloquently than in "real life."

Like the symbol, dialogue is a technique of the small. Added to the top of the whole constituted by structure, characters, theme, story universe, symbols, plot, and scene weaving, it is the most subtle of narrative tools. But it nevertheless carries enormous power.

Dialogue is better understood when compared to music. Like music, dialogue is a form of communication based on rhythms and tones. And like music, dialogue is of better quality when it mixes a number of "tracks." The problem with most authors is that they write their dialogues on only one track, the "melody." Their dialogues only explain what is happening in the story. Single-track dialogues are the hallmark of mediocre writers.

Good dialogue is not a melody; it is a symphony, played on three major tracks. These three tracks are narrative dialogue, moral dialogue, and key words or phrases.

Track 1: Narrative Dialogue – Melody

Narrative dialogue, like melody in music, is the story expressed through speech. These are words that talk about what the characters are doing. We tend to think of dialogue as the opposite of action. "Actions speak louder than words," it is often said. But speech is a form of action. Narrative dialogue is used when characters talk about the main line of action. And dialogue can, for short periods of time, carry the story on its own.

Narrative dialogue is written in the same way as a scene is constructed:

• Character no. 1, who is the main character of the scene (but not necessarily the hero of the story), formulates their desire. As an author, you must know the outcome of this desire, which will give you the line (the backbone) to which all the lines of the scene will cling.

• Character no. 2 criticizes this desire.

• Character no. 1 reacts with lines based on a direct or indirect plan.

• As the scene progresses, the conversation becomes more and more heated. It ends with lines that express anger or appeasement.

There is an elaborate technique that consists of moving, in a scene, from lines about action to lines about being. In other words, moving from lines that deal with what the characters do to lines that deal with what the characters are. When the scene reaches its hottest point, one of the characters says: "You are..." And they add what they think of the other character. For example, "You are a liar" or "You are pathetic," or even "You are a genius."

You will notice that this transition gives the scene immediate depth, as the characters suddenly start talking about how their actions define what they are deeply. The character defining the other is not necessarily right. But their simple statement leads the reader or viewer to form a clearer idea of what they themselves think of the characters at this point in the story. This technique constitutes a kind of realization on the scale of the scene and often requires lines that relate to the characters' values (see "Track 2: Moral Dialogue"). The transition from action to being is absent from most scenes; it is generally found only in key scenes. Let's now study an example from a scene in The Verdict.

THE VERDICT

(novel by Barry C. Reed, 1980; screenplay by David Mamet, 1982)

In this scene, Mr. Doneghy, the victim's brother-in-law, reproaches attorney Frank Galvin for refusing a settlement without consulting him first. We are about halfway through the scene:

INT. COURTHOUSE HALLWAY – DAY

DONEGHY

[...] Four years... My wife cried so much she can't sleep anymore. For what they did to her sister.

GALVIN

Listen, I swear I wouldn't... I wouldn't have refused their offer if I didn't think I would win the case.

DONEGHY

What does it matter what you think? I'm a working man and what I want is to get my wife out of this town. We hired you, we give you a percentage. And what do I learn from the other side? That you were offered two hundred thousand dollars!

GALVIN

I'm sure to win. I'm going before the jurors with a solid case, I have... I have a famous doctor as a medical expert. You're going to get what, five or six times what...

DONEGHY

You big shots, you're all the same. The doctors, the hospital or you, it's always: "See what I'm doing for you?" And then when the shot misses, it's: "But we did the best we could, we're really sorry." And poor people like us, we live with your mistakes for the rest of our lives.

Track 2: Moral Dialogue – Harmony

Moral dialogue deals with right and wrong actions, and also with values, or what makes life valuable. It's the equivalent of harmony in music, as it provides depth and texture to the melodic line and broadens its scope. In other words, moral lines do not deal with the events of the story. They deal with the characters' attitudes toward these events.

Here's how a moral dialogue should unfold:

• Character no. 1 proposes or exposes an action.

• Character no. 2 opposes this action by explaining that it will hurt others.

• The characters attack and defend, each arguing in favor of their position.

In moral dialogue, characters invariably express their values, tastes, and preferences. Remember that a character's values are actually the expression of a deeper idea about what the right way to live should be. Moral dialogue, at its most advanced stage, allows for the comparison through debate not only of two actions but also of two ways of life.

Track 3: Key Words, Lines, Repetitions, and Sounds – Refrains, Variations, and Leitmotifs

Key words, lines, repetitions, and sounds represent the third track of dialogue. These are words that have the potential to carry a particular meaning from a symbolic or thematic point of view, much like a symphony will use certain instruments, such as the triangle, here and there to highlight a particular moment in a piece. To build this meaning, you must ensure that your character pronounces the word or words in question much more often than necessary. Repetition, especially when done in multiple contexts, has a cumulative effect on the audience.

A repetition is a line that appears several times throughout the story. Each time it is used, it takes on a particular meaning until it becomes a sort of slogan of the story. Repetition primarily serves to express the theme. Among classic repetitions, we can cite: "Round up the usual suspects," "I stick my neck out for nobody," and "Here's looking at you, kid" (Casablanca); "What we've got here is a failure to communicate" (Cool Hand Luke); "May the Force be with you" (Star Wars); "If you build it, he will come" (Field of Dreams); and the two great repetitions from The Godfather: "I'm gonna make him an offer he can't refuse" and "It's just business, it's not personal."

A good example of repetition use can be found in Butch Cassidy and the Sundance Kid. When the line is first spoken, it has no particular meaning. Butch and Sundance, who have just robbed a train, see a posse in the distance. "Who are those guys?" Butch asks. A little later, as the posse gets closer, Sundance repeats the question, this time with a hint of despair. As the story progresses, it becomes clear that Butch and Sundance's main mission is to determine the identity of "those guys." Because they are not the usual posses that the heroes have such ease in eluding. These lawmen, hired by Eastern industrialists, are Western stars that Butch, Sundance, and the audience never even meet. But if Butch and Sundance don't find out in time who those guys are, they will die.

SCENES

Let's now see how certain types of scenes can both apply and modify the basic principles of scene construction and symphonic dialogue.

The opening
The opening scene establishes the foundations for all the characters and actions of the story, which is probably why it is so difficult to write. As the first scene of the inverted triangle that is the story as a whole, it must create a framework that defines the scope of the narrative. The first scene generally tells the audience about the subject of the story. But it can also be a mini-story in itself, with characters and actions that dramatically produce a highly dynamic opening.

This is why it can be useful to think of the first scene as a small inverted triangle within the large inverted triangle that is the story:

As it provides the framework that defines the story, the opening scene also suggests the major thematic structures—of identity and opposition—that the author will weave throughout the story. But to prevent the scene from becoming theoretical or moralizing, these major structures must always be anchored in very specific characters.

To understand how the principles of the opening scene work, they must be seen in practice, which is why we will now analyze the first two scenes of Butch Cassidy and the Sundance Kid.

BUTCH CASSIDY AND THE SUNDANCE KID

(William Goldman, 1969)

The first two scenes of Butch Cassidy and the Sundance Kid constitute one of the greatest openings in cinema history. The construction of the scenes and the dialogues by screenwriter William Goldman not only attract the audience's attention in a very pleasing way but also establish the foundations of the thematic structures and oppositions that will determine the entire story.

Scene 1: Butch at the Bank

In the first scene, a man (whose identity is still unknown to the audience) carefully observes a bank that is closing for the night.

• Position in the character arc This is the opening scene of the story and the first look at the main character, Butch. It is also step 1 of the hero's journey: a robber in the Wild West who will eventually die.

• Problems

1. Introduce the universe of the narrative, notably the outlaws in a Wild West on the verge of disappearing.

2. Introduce the main character, who is part of a pair in a friendship story.

3. Hint that the hero, like the West itself, is getting old and is also about to disappear.

• Strategy

1. Create a typical Butch and Sundance experience that will introduce the key thematic structures.

2. Present in a single scene the essential process of the entire story: a whole world closing in.

3. Do it in a light and amusing way while hinting at a darker aspect and future.

4. Show a man considering robbing a bank but finding that this task was much easier to accomplish in "the good old days."

5. Trick the viewer by omitting to reveal the man's identity. By forcing them to gradually realize that he is actually a robber scouting the place, the screenwriters made the final joke even more amusing and also presented the hero as a con artist and a man with a lot of charm.

• Desire Butch wants to scout the bank for a robbery.

• Outcome He discovers that the bank's security is much more elaborate than he thought and that the bank is closing.

• Adversaries The guard and the bank itself.

• Plan Butch uses a ruse, pretending to be interested in the building's aesthetics.

• Conflict The bank, like a living thing, closes in on Butch.

• Twist or revelation The man observing the bank is scouting it for a robbery.

• Moral debate and values Aesthetics vs practicality. Of course, the comedy comes from this idea of aesthetics applied to the bank, especially by a man who wants to rob it. But the function of this opposition is not solely comedic. It is actually the central value opposition of the story. The universe of the narrative is a world that is becoming increasingly practical, but Butch and Sundance are, first and foremost, men of style, in love with a way of life that is rapidly disappearing.

• Key words and images The bars lowering, the countdown ending, the light fading, the space closing in.

The dialogues point to the central line of the scene, with the key phrase and word coming last: "It's a small price to pay for such beauty." What is noteworthy is that this line corresponds to the moment of the main character's identity reveal: this man is a con artist (a bank robber) whose mode of action is speech. The line has two opposing meanings. On one hand, the man mocks the bank's aesthetics; he wants to rob it. On the other, this line truly defines the character's identity: he is a man of style, and it is this quality that will kill him.

Scene 2: Sundance Playing Poker

In this scene, a man named Macon accuses another of cheating at cards. Macon tells the man to return his money and leave. But the cheater turns out to be the famous Sundance Kid, and Macon nearly loses his life.

• Position in the character arc This scene marks Sundance's starting position in his character arc, that of a robber who will eventually die. It also tends to provide more details on the previous presentation of Butch's character.

• Problems

1. Introduce the second main character of this friendship story and show how he differs from the hero.

2. Show the two friends in action; and above all, show that they are a team.

• Strategy Goldman created a second prototype scene whose impact on the plot is nil. Its sole function is to clearly define the characters very concisely.

1. In contrast to the first scene, the second defines the characters through conflict and crisis, as crisis tends to immediately clarify the essence.

2. The second scene defines first and foremost Sundance, but also Butch, who is seen in action and can be compared to his partner.

3. This scene presents the two men working as a team, somewhat like musicians. Sundance creates the conflict; Butch tries to calm it. Sundance is a taciturn man; Butch, the classic con artist, is charming.

4. To create a crisis scene, Goldman starts with a strong moment of classic Western intrigue, poker, but overturns the preconstructed expectations this implies for the audience. He replaces the usual show of strength with the ridiculous scene of a man defending his honor when accused of cheating. And Goldman overturns the classic scene again, making his character go from the ridiculous to a greatness that surpasses all Western heroes.

5. Goldman's key strategy is to deceive the audience about Sundance's identity at the very moment Sundance is deceiving his opponent. We will return to this shortly.

• Desire Macon wants to take all of Sundance's money and kick him out of the saloon after stripping him.

• Outcome Macon is humiliated but realizes he made the right choice when Sundance shows what he is capable of when he has a weapon in hand.

• Adversaries Sundance, then Butch.

• Plan Macon does not use deception. He directly tells Sundance that if he refuses to leave, he will kill him.

• Conflict Macon and Sundance argue over the game, and the conflict escalates to the point where both men draw their weapons, with one of them facing death. Butch then tries to calm the conflict by negotiating a deal, but he fails.

• Twist or revelation Goldman has entirely constructed this scene around twists. Note that he conceals information to deceive the audience along with Macon. At first glance, Sundance appears to be in a position of weakness, a weakness he exacerbates when he childishly insists he didn't cheat. And Sundance weakens himself further in the audience's eyes when Butch reminds him that he's getting old, that he's on the decline.

So, when the tables turn abruptly, Sundance makes a strong impression on the audience. The spectators understand that he is an action hero by the way he handles his weapon at the end of the scene. But the true proof of his greatness is his ability to deceive the audience and his willingness to act as if he could lose. Sundance is truly very strong.

• Moral debate and values This situation is an extreme example of warrior culture: the public display of strength, competition based on physical abilities and courage, the importance of a man's name and reputation. Butch would never engage in such things; he comes from a more affluent social background than Sundance. He just wants everyone to stay alive and move on.

• Key words and images Aging, time closing in on the characters—but not completely yet.

The lines of the show of strength are very concise, sometimes limited to one sentence per character, which tends to give the impression that these fighters are exchanging words like blows. But most importantly, the dialogues are highly stylized and witty, with the precision of rhythm and timing of a stand-up routine. Even Sundance, the man of action, proves to be a master of verbal conciseness. When Macon asks him, "What's the secret of your success?" he simply replies, "Prayer." Sundance's first line is just two words, and his stylish insolence perfectly defines the character.

You will notice that in the second section of the scene, we shift to a conflict opposing Sundance to Butch. These friends are so close that they argue even when facing a life-threatening situation. Butch's lines are also concise and stylish, but they also highlight Butch's diplomatic qualities, as well as one of the story's major themes: aging and the difficulties of adaptation that come with it.

The heart of the scene plays on the absurdity of the solution Butch and Sundance have invented together to get out of this extremely dangerous situation. Although he appears to be in a position of weakness, Sundance says, "If he invites us to stay, we'll leave." Strangely, Butch reports this proposal to Macon but tries to soften the humiliation by asking, "How about asking us to stick around a bit?" and adding, "You don't have to mean what you say, you know." In addition to showing the audience, by stylishly modifying a typical Western situation, what each of them is capable of, Butch and Sundance reveal that they form a great team. A great comedic team.

After this long preamble, Butch delivers the central line: "I can't help you, Sundance." Again, you will notice that Goldman placed the key word, "Sundance," at the end of the line. Suddenly, the characters' status shifts, the terrifying Macon is terrified, and the comedic teamwork of Butch and Sundance comes to an end. Macon says, "Stick around a bit, why not?" and Butch, always courteous and considerate, replies, "Thanks, but we have to go."

This scene ends with an obvious trap: Macon asks Sundance if he's really as good as they say, and Sundance responds with a demonstration of his remarkable physical skills, confirming through action what the audience had already guessed through words. But again, you will notice that the thematic key line of the story comes last, forming the final point of the triangle of this opening scene and evoking the final point of the film itself. Butch says, "What did I tell you?—on the decline." This undeniably sarcastic comment is at odds with the performance Sundance just executed and the verbal ruse that allowed the two characters to deceive Macon and the audience. It is only later, with hindsight, that the audience will understand that these two characters are indeed on the decline, but they don't know it, and this ignorance will be the cause of their death. These scenes are truly brilliantly written.

TECHNIQUE: THE FIRST SENTENCE

The opening sentence takes the principles of the opening scene and condenses them to the extreme. It is the most general statement of the story and frames the subject of the narrative. But at the same time, it must have a certain dramatic power, have a dynamic effect. We will now study three classic examples of opening sentences. We have added to these opening sentences some of the sentences that follow to help you better understand how the sentence fits into the author's overall strategy for the scene and the entire story.

PRIDE AND PREJUDICE

(Jane Austen, 1813)

• Position in the character arc Before even the heroine, there is the universe of the narrative—a world where women seek to marry.

• Problems

1. Jane Austen must make the reader understand that this is a comedy.

2. She must give some indication of the universe of the narrative and its rules of operation.

3. She must let the audience know that the story will be narrated from a woman's point of view.

• Strategy Start with a seemingly serious sentence that seems to state a fact and an act of universal altruism but is actually an opinion on an act that is purely self-interested. The content of the first sentence tells the reader that the story will deal with marriage, how women and their families seek good matches, and the essential connection, in this world, between marriage and money.

After presenting the general arena of the story comically in the first sentence, the author focuses on the particular family that will apply the principles of the opening throughout the story. You will notice that these opening sentences do not present an ounce of superfluousness.

It is a truth universally acknowledged that a single man in possession of a good fortune must be in want of a wife, and, however little known the feelings or views of such a man may be on his first entering a neighbourhood, this truth is so well fixed in the minds of the surrounding families, that he is considered as the rightful property of some one or other of their daughters.

"Do you know, my dear, that Netherfield Park is let at last?" said Mrs. Bennet to her husband one day.

Mr. Bennet replied that he had not.

"But it is," returned she; "for Mrs. Long has just been here, and she told me all about it."

Mr. Bennet made no answer.

"Do not you want to know who has taken it?" cried his wife impatiently.

"You want to tell me, and I have no objection to hearing it."

This was invitation enough.

DAVID COPPERFIELD

(Charles Dickens, 1849-1850)

• Position in the character arc Through the use of the narrator, the author creates a hero who is at the end of the arc but speaks of the very beginning of that arc. The hero will therefore be very young at first but will have a certain wisdom.

• Problems

1. When telling the story of a man's life, where do you start and where do you end?

2. How do you explain to the reader the type of story you are going to tell them?

• Strategy Use first-person narration. Have the narrator say, in the chapter title: "I Am Born." Four little words. But with incredible force. This chapter title is actually the opening sentence of the book. The narrator plants the flag of his own life. "I am important, and this is going to be a great story," he tells us. This narrator also indicates that he is about to tell us a coming-of-age story that takes the form of a myth, starting with the hero's birth. It is a very ambitious story.

After this brief but powerful sentence, Dickens adds: "Whether I shall turn out to be the hero of my own life, or whether that station will be held by anybody else, these pages must show." Immediately, he makes the audience understand that the hero thinks in terms of story (he is, after all, a writer) and seeks to fully realize the potential of his life. He then returns to the precise moment of his birth, which is extremely presumptuous. But if he does so, it is because this event contains a dramatic element: the hero was born as the clock struck midnight.

Dickens's opening strategy has one last consequence: the reader settles comfortably into the story. The author tells them: "I am going to take you on a long but fascinating journey. So sit back, relax, and let me guide you through this world. You won't regret it."

I AM BORN

Whether I shall turn out to be the hero of my own life, or whether that station will be held by anybody else, these pages must show. To begin my life with the beginning of my life, I record that I was born (as I have been informed and believe) on a Friday, at twelve o'clock at night. It was remarked that the clock began to strike, and I began to cry, simultaneously.

In consideration of the day and hour of my birth, it was declared by the nurse, and by some sage women in the neighbourhood who had taken a lively interest in me several months before there was any possibility of our becoming personally acquainted, first, that I was destined to be unlucky in life; and secondly, that I was privileged to see ghosts and spirits; both these gifts inevitably attaching, as they believed, to all unlucky infants of either gender, born towards the small hours on a Friday night.

THE CATCHER IN THE RYE

(J. D. Salinger, 1951)

• Position in the character arc In a sanatorium, Holden Caulfield recalls events that happened the previous year. He is therefore quite close to the end of his development, but he lacks the final insight that will come to him once he has remembered the story and told it.

• Problems

1. He must determine when to start this story that concerns him and define what this story should include.

2. He wants to explain to the reader what he really is through the way he will tell his own story, not just by what he will say about himself.

3. He must express the theme and basic values that will guide the story and the character.

• Strategy

1. Write in the first person, thus putting the reader in the hero's mind and telling them that it is a coming-of-age story. But since the hero tells the story from a sanatorium and speaks in "bad boy" language, the reader understands that what they are about to read is actually the opposite of the classic coming-of-age story.

2. Surprise the reader by creating a narrator who is aggressive towards them. Warn the reader, directly and bluntly, that they will not read a sweet and superficial childhood story and that Holden will not "kiss up" to them to gain their sympathy. The consequence of all this is that the narrator will be brutally honest. In other words, telling the truth as he saw it is a moral imperative for him.

3. Write a long, rambling sentence whose form will express the hero's personality and the nature of the plot.

4. Immediately reference David Copperfield and express some disdain for this defining version of the 19th-century coming-of-age story. The audience thus understands that everything the narrator is about to say will be the opposite of David Copperfield. Instead of a grand plot and a great journey, they will be presented with a small plot, even an anti-plot, and a small journey. This allusion also suggests an ambition: the author implies that he is about to write a coming-of-age story for the 20th century that will be as good as the best coming-of-age stories of the 19th century.

But most importantly, the reader understands that the value that will guide the hero and dictate the narration of the story will be sincerity. Expect real characters, real emotions, and real transformations, if any occur.

If you really want to hear about it, the first thing you'll probably want to know is where I was born, and what my lousy childhood was like, and how
As this film is a social fantasy, it is not just about a debate between two people arguing over personal issues. What is at stake is how an entire society should live. This dialogue is therefore also political. We do not mean political ideas concerning a specific issue; those ideas would soon become obsolete. We are talking about human politics, the relationship between populations and their leaders. What is particularly brilliant here is the extremely emotional and personal language the screenwriters have given to this overall vision. They focused on a single action—the closure of the loan company—and personalized it by associating it with the death of the hero's father.

You will notice that, except for a short exchange in the middle, the scene is composed exclusively of two monologues. These monologues are quite long and break with Hollywood's conventional wisdom, which requires short lines and quick back-and-forth exchanges. But each character needs time to build the defense of their way of life. If the screenwriters had not rooted this debate in a personal dispute between two characters who despise each other, this scene could have been a political philosophy essay.

• Position in the character arc With his father's death, George has just experienced his first disappointment regarding his desire (to travel the world and build things) and sacrifices himself for the first time for his family and friends. He is now about to go to college to fulfill his dreams.

• Problem The screenwriters must construct a fight that involves the values on which the town and America itself must rely. And they must do so without appearing preachy.

• Strategy

1. Have the hero and the main adversary debate the future of an institution—the loan company—on which the entire town is founded, as well as the man who built this institution but is now dead.

2. Focus the entire philosophical debate on one word, "rich," in the last sentence of the hero's monologue.

• Desire Potter wants to close the loan company.

• Outcome He fails because George prevents him.

• Adversary George.

• Plan Potter directly states he wants to close the company, and George directly opposes him.

• Conflict The conflict intensifies when Potter shifts from the institution to Potter's father in his remarks.

• Twist or revelation Young George is able to stand up to the man who intimidates the entire town.

• Moral debate and values The exchange between the two men amounts to an in-depth examination as it is a classic example of conflicting values. You will notice that these two monologues are extremely well constructed. These men develop very precise arguments that represent two opposing systems from a political and philosophical point of view.

Potter's arguments and values

1. There is a great distinction between businessmen and men of high ideals.

2. Lacking common sense, ideals can ruin an entire town. This argument suggests to the audience that the town itself is the battlefield and that the essential question of the film will be: what lifestyle will make this battlefield, this world, a more pleasant place to live?

3. Potter then cites a specific example, that of Ernie Bishop, the friendly taxi driver, already known and liked by the audience. The viewer has seen that Ernie is not the type of crook, but Potter claims that if he got a loan to build his house, it is only because he is friends with George.

4. The result of this type of arrangement, according to Potter, is that the saving proletarians become bitter and idle scoundrels. This argument reveals Potter's sinister value system: America is a class society, and Potter feels perfectly comfortable in his role as leader of the lower class. With this argument, the dialogue may go a bit too far: Potter becomes not only the typical patriarch but also the evil capitalist.

5. Potter ends up attacking the very symbols of what George is: the dreams and personal, friendly contacts that make the small town a pleasant place to live.

George's arguments and values

KEY POINT: To strengthen George's arguments, the screenwriters put them in the mouth of his father a few scenes earlier. At that time, George had expressed a view opposed to his father's. This strategy tends to make George's eloquence in this scene both more credible and more poignant.

1. George starts brilliantly by conceding a point to Potter: his father was not a businessman, and he himself is not very interested in his business, which does not bring in much.

2. He then moves on to an argument that is primarily in favor of his father: he was a selfless man, although this quality did not allow George and Harry to go to college.

3. Then he attacks Potter on his own turf, business. He tells him that his father helped people out of the slums of the town, and these people thus became better citizens and better consumers, capable of contributing to the wealth and well-being of the entire community.

4. And he pushes the argument even further by pleading for the heroism of the weak. The people Potter calls "idle scoundrels" are those who work, who pay, and who die the most for the community; they give themselves body and soul. And if the community is to be the place that allows people to thrive, no one can be treated as a member of a lower class.

5. George ends with the most important of all his arguments, that of the inalienable rights of human beings. His father treated each person as a human being, as an end in itself, whereas Potter treats people like cattle, like soulless animals that can be led wherever one wants. In other words, Potter treats human beings as means to his end, namely making more and more money.

KEY POINT: While presenting the most general argument—the rights of the ordinary man—the screenwriters converge towards a very personal plan with the key line and word that come at the end.

If Potter does all this, it is, according to George, because he is "a bitter and twisted old man." This line plays a decisive role in the film not only because it describes Potter but also because bitterness is one of the most obvious characteristics of George's character.

Then comes the last line, the culmination of the scene: "Well, [my father] died a hundred times richer than you will ever be!" The word "rich" has two different meanings. The most obvious—having a lot of money—qualifies Potter. But the deeper one—who gives and receives a lot from others—defines George.

• Key word "Rich."

INT. BAILEY'S OFFICE AT THE LOAN COMPANY – DAY

POTTER

Peter Bailey had no business sense. That's what led him to the grave. Oh, I don't want to speak ill of him. May he rest in peace because he was a hardworking man with high ideals, as they say. But an ideal without an ounce of common sense can ruin this town.

(He grabs a paper on the desk.)

Let's consider the advance made here to Ernie Bishop. You know him, a loafer who stays in his taxi all day without thinking and doing nothing useful. I recently learned that the bank refused him any advance. So he came here, and our company is building him a house on credit worth more than five thousand dollars. Why?

George is at the office door, holding his coat and files, ready to leave.

GEORGE

I studied this case, Mr. Potter. You have all the papers concerning it. His salary, his insurance. And I personally guarantee his honesty.

POTTER

(Sarcastically)

Is he one of your friends?

GEORGE

Yes.

POTTER

Perfect. You see, if you play ball with a simple company employee, they build you a house. Ha ha ha! But where does it lead? To flatter a clientele of idlers and lazy people instead of promoting a worthy class. And all this only because of a few dangerous dreamers like Peter Bailey who fill their heads with a lot of impossible and grotesque ideas. So I say...

George puts down his coat and approaches the desk, furious at what Potter just said about his father.

GEORGE

Just a moment. Please listen, Mr. Potter. You're right when you say my father wasn't a man who cared about money. Why did he ever think of founding this loan and construction company? I couldn't tell you. But I will never tolerate anyone raising a doubt about his honesty, because throughout his life, because throughout the time he ran this business with Uncle Billy, he always refused to think of himself. Isn't that right, Uncle Billy? He wanted to send Harry to college, he never could save the necessary money. But he pulled a number of his fellow citizens out of your slums, Mr. Potter. So you all have the business habit here. Are they worse citizens? And don't they pay regularly? You said earlier... You said these unfortunate people should wait before having a house built, before living in a place worthy of a human being. Allow me, but wait for what? Wait until their children have grown up and left them? Do you know how long it takes a worker to save five thousand dollars? Remember, Mr. Potter... remember that all these loafers you talked about are the most numerous to work, to spend their money in our community. Do you really think these poor people don't have the right to live in houses with at least two rooms? I know my father had a different view. To my father, they were men! To you, who don't see their pride, they are just beasts unworthy of interest. Well, my father died a hundred times richer than you will ever be!

SHADOW OF A DOUBT

(original story by Gordon McDonell, screenplay by Thornton Wilder, Sally Benson, and Alma Reville)

Shadow of a Doubt is probably one of the best thriller scripts ever written. The film tells the story of Uncle Charlie, a man who comes to visit his sister in a small American town. His niece, young Charlie, initially admires him deeply but gradually comes to believe he might be the serial killer known as "The Merry Widow Murderer."

Thornton Wilder's script is a model because it blends drama techniques with the thriller genre, thus transcending this form. A good illustration of this approach is found in the famous scene where Uncle Charlie justifies his murders on a moral level. A lesser screenwriter would have made the killer opaque, a monster who needed no justification due to his fundamentally monstrous nature. But the story would then have been limited to being the chronicle of a killing machine.

Wilder, on the contrary, chose to give the killer detailed and understandable moral arguments, which makes him even more terrifying. Uncle Charlie attacks the dark side of American life—the greed and the fact that the vast majority of people have not achieved the American dream—that most Americans themselves prefer to sweep under the rug.

• Position in the character arc The adversary does not have his own character arc in the story. But this scene occurs at a crucial moment in the heroine's development. Young Charlie was already very suspicious of the uncle she once revered. This moment corresponds to the shift from her former attraction to her new repulsion for the adversary. And she despairs of understanding how this happened.

• Problem How to make the adversary hint at the motives for his murders without stating or admitting them clearly?

• Strategy Gather the whole family around the dinner table so that the justification takes place within the family and appears as part of the everyday and normal life of Americans. Push Uncle Charlie's sister, Mrs. Newton, to ask her brother to give a speech at her women's club to provide him with a good reason to rant against elderly women. Then horribly dissolve this mundane world to plunge into terror.

• Desire Uncle Charlie wants to justify his disgust for women, especially elderly women, in front of his niece, whom he also seeks to frighten.

• Outcome He realizes he has gone too far.

• Adversary His niece, young Charlie.

• Plan Uncle Charlie uses an indirect plan that involves philosophizing about city women in general, allowing him both to maintain his cover and to connect with the only person at the table who understands him.
