• Conflict Despite young Charlie's single counterattack, the conflict builds mainly through the escalation of hatred towards women that emerges from Uncle Charlie's speech.

• Twist or revelation The good Uncle Charlie thinks most elderly women are no better than animals to be led to the slaughterhouse.

• Moral debate and values Uncle Charlie's moral arguments are simply terrifying. He begins by calling elderly women useless. Then he reduces them to sensual beasts devouring money. He ends by explaining that it is morally right to end the suffering of these too fat and too old animals. The values in opposition here are utility and humanity vs. money, sensuality, uselessness, and animals.

• Key words Money, women, useless, greedy, animals.

If this dialogue is so frightening, it is because it is both trivial and atrocious. It starts with the everyday life of couples and arrives at the idea that women can be compared to animals. You will notice that the key line, the last one, is in the form of a question. Uncle Charlie does not go straight to the point; he does not state that all women of a certain age should be put down. He asks his niece what should be done, and, because of the power of his terrible logic, young Charlie seems to have a hard time reaching a different conclusion than he does.

The excellence of the construction of this scene and its dialogues also comes from the comic commentary Wilder placed at the end. Uncle Charlie's older sister, Mrs. Newton, is completely unaware of what her brother is really saying. She thus brings the scene back to its origins, the speech Uncle Charlie will have to give before the women's club, and the audience understands that it's a bit like letting the wolf into the sheepfold. Moreover, Uncle Charlie's very maternal older sister has already chosen a nice widow for her brother.

INT. DINING ROOM – NIGHT

Uncle Charlie is now pouring wine into glasses. He acts meticulously and speaks in a relaxed manner.

UNCLE CHARLIE

What kind of audience is it?

MRS. NEWTON

Oh, women like myself. Mostly busy with their homes.

Uncle Charlie starts speaking, and it feels like his words come from the depths of his resentment.

UNCLE CHARLIE

In this kind of small town, women are active. In big cities, it's different. They are full of elderly women whose husbands have passed away. Husbands who spent their lives amassing capital but worked themselves to death. And at their death, of course, the money goes to their wives... to their weeping widows. And what do these widows do with this hard-earned money? They are found in the lounges of the best hotels in useless flocks. Eating their fortune, devouring it. Engaged in bridge games late into the night... and reeking of money. Proud to show off their jewelry for lack of anything else. Horrible, made-up, faded, fat, and greedy.

He is suddenly interrupted by young Charlie's voice, off-screen.

YOUNG CHARLIE'S VOICE

But we're not going to kill them for that, they are human beings!

He looks in her direction as if she just woke him up.

UNCLE CHARLIE

Do you think so? Do you think they are still human? Haven't they become some kind of impotent larvae, huh? And what do you do with an animal that has become too old and impotent?

(He suddenly calms down.)

(He laughs.)

Oh, but I feel like I'm already giving my little speech.

Young Charlie hastily grabs her fork. She lowers her eyes. We hear Mrs. Newton say:

MRS. NEWTON

Well, for heaven's sake, don't talk about women like that in front of my club. You'd get lynched quickly! Really, what an idea!

(She teases him.)

Especially since that charming Mrs. Potter will be there too. She asked about you, you know?

The monologue

The monologue is a particularly useful technique for the craftsman that is the author. Dialogue allows the writer to draw truth and emotion from the ordeal that is the conflict between characters. The monologue allows him to draw truth and emotion from a conflict that is within the character himself.

A monologue is a mini-story that unfolds in the mind of a character. It is another form of miniature, a summary of what the character is, his main struggle, and the process he goes through throughout the story. You can use it to present in detail to the audience the depths of a character's mind. Or to show the intensity of the pain he feels deep inside.

To write a good monologue, you must first and foremost tell a whole story, which means, as always, going through the seven narrative steps and placing a key word or phrase at the end.

THE VERDICT

David Mamet used a monologue to conclude the confrontation scene in The Verdict. Since this monologue is included in the plea the hero presents to a jury, Mamet did not have to justify its use through a "realistic" intermediary, as seen in most American films. If this monologue is extremely well-written, it is not because it tells a complete story. It is because it tells two stories: the journey of the woman the hero defends and the hero's own life journey.

• Position in the character arc Frank has already had his awakening. We are here at the final stage of the arc: by winning the trial, Frank proves he has indeed had an awakening.

• Problem How to summarize the plea while giving it maximum dramatic impact?

• Strategy Develop the plea and the moral judgment request made to the jury by secretly describing Frank's personal development.

• Desire Frank wants to convince the jury to restore justice.

• Outcome He realizes that each juror is a human being who wishes for justice to be done.

• Adversaries All the rich and powerful who crush and weaken us.

• Plan His plan is to speak from the heart and make justice a matter of sincerity.

• Conflict This monologue presents a man struggling to understand and do what is right while asking the jurors to act in the same way as him.

• Twist or revelation The viewer realizes that Frank is not just talking about the case but also about himself.

• Moral debate and values Frank's moral argument in favor of justice is a true seven-step story. It starts with people feeling lost, feeling like powerless victims (weakness). These people want to do the right thing (desire), despite the rich and powerful who constantly belittle them (opposition). When you become aware of your power (plan), when you believe in yourself (awakening), it is possible to act justly (moral decision, final confrontation, new balance).

• Key words Justice, believe.

GALVIN

Well... you know there are so many times when we are lost. We say, "God, please tell us what is right, tell us what is true." And there is no justice, the rich win, the poor are powerless. We end up being tired of hearing lies. And we end up playing dead. We are dead, we come to see ourselves... as victims... and we become victims. We become... we become weak. We doubt ourselves, we doubt our beliefs. We doubt our institutions. And we doubt the law. But today you are the law. You are the law. Not a law book, not the judges, nor the marble statue of justice, or the court ceremonial. These are just symbols of our desire to be just. But they are... they are actually a prayer. I mean a fervent and fearful prayer. In my religion, it is said: "Act as if you had faith, and then faith will be granted to you." If... if we want to have faith in justice, we just need to believe in ourselves and act justly. And I believe there is justice in our hearts.

Watch this film if you want to see what a great actor can do with a beautifully written monologue.

The end

Chekhov said that the last ninety seconds of the play were the most important of all. It is true that the final scene is the convergence point of the entire story. Sometimes, this scene includes one of the last plot twists in the form of an awakening. But generally, everything related to the plot has already been dealt with. And the final scene becomes, like the opening scene, a miniature of the entire story. The author presents the thematic structures one last time, and the audience realizes that these particular characters just introduced to them are part of a larger world. In short, the audience has a thematic revelation.

To write a great ending, you must understand that the final scene will be the tip of the inverted triangle that is the story, and that it will itself be an inverted triangle, with the key word or phrase—of the scene and the story—coming last:

The final scene must be the ultimate expression of the funnel effect we have already talked about: at the end, the key word or line creates a huge explosion in the audience's mind and continues to resonate long after the story concludes.
Let's now study some great final scenes. This will help us better understand how dialogue and scene construction work at this decisive moment in the story.

THE SUN ALSO RISES

(Ernest Hemingway, 1926)

This story follows the wanderings of a group of friends traveling across Europe, as well as a particular character who cannot live with the woman he loves due to a war injury that left him impotent. As it is a great impossible love, the characters are drawn into a whirlwind to a point where life is just a succession of movements toward sensation. They are idle people, aware of the trap they have fallen into, but unable to escape it.

The final scene is prototypical of the characters' actions. After dining, Jake and Lady Brett Ashley hit the road again. Someone drives them somewhere in a taxi. As the scene converges toward its conclusion, Brett utters a line typical of her character: "Oh, Jake, we could have been so happy together." This worldly, even disposable line is also the symbol of the entire story. What could have been a great romantic tragedy has been reduced to "a good time."

Jake then delivers a line typical of his character: "It's nice to think so." Cursed not only by his injury but also by a sensitivity that allows him to have illusions and be aware of them, Jake is damned for eternity.

Downstairs, we crossed the ground floor hall to go out into the street. A boy went to fetch a taxi. The air was warm and bright. Up the street, there was a small square with trees and grass where taxis waited. A taxi arrived with the boy on the running board. I tipped him and told the driver where we wanted to go. Then, I settled next to Brett. The driver started. I leaned back in the car. Brett moved closer to me. We were sitting very close to each other. I put my arm around her, and she nestled comfortably against me. The air was hot and bright, and the houses were a stark white. We turned onto the Gran Via.

– Oh, Jake, said Brett, we could have been so happy together!

Ahead of us, a khaki-clad officer was directing traffic from atop his horse. He raised his baton. The taxi slowed down abruptly, pressing Brett against me.

– Yes, I said. It's nice to think so.

THE SEVEN SAMURAI

(Akira Kurosawa, Shinobu Hashimoto, and Hideo Oguni, 1954)

In The Seven Samurai, the screenwriter's technical mastery reaches the level of a masterpiece. The screenplay of this film is among the best ever written, and it masterfully and literally executes all the techniques presented in this book. The final scene leaves the audience devastated, yet pleasantly surprised that such greatness is possible in human beings.

In this story, seven samurai have gathered around altruism and love for their warrior art to protect villagers from marauding bandits. Katsushiro, a young apprentice samurai, has fallen in love with Shino, a peasant girl. The battle is over; the samurai and villagers have won. But on the hill, four of the great warriors lie in their graves. And Shino has turned her back on the young warrior and joined the other farmers to plant what will be the next harvest.

Alongside Shichiroji, the other surviving samurai, Kanbei, the leader of the samurai, watches as a witness to Katsushiro's heartbreak, the farmers planting new life, and the graves of his four comrades on the hill. And he has a final realization: although victorious, the samurai have lost, and their entire way of life is disappearing. The deep differences that separated people, and which had temporarily vanished, are back, and the heroism of the dead warriors was as brief as a breath of wind.

Taken out of context, this scene might seem like a poorly formulated realization. But for many reasons, it cannot be considered this way. First, it is set after an epic battle in which seven samurai defeated forty bandits solely to save a few farmers who were strangers to them. It is therefore an enormous emotional upheaval. Furthermore, this scene is an important unveiling, and it is placed at the very end of the story, much like the shocking twists that mark the end of The Sixth Sense and The Usual Suspects. Finally, it is a thematic revelation through which the hero glimpses the death of an entire social world, a world that is, in many ways, magnificent.

EXT. VILLAGE – DAY

Kanbei lowers his head and looks at the ground. He takes a few steps toward the camera, then stops and turns to look at the rice fields. Then he returns to stand next to Shichiroji.

KANBEI

It's another lost battle.

Shichiroji is surprised. He looks at Kanbei questioningly.

KANBEI

It's the farmers who are the real winners. Not us.

Kanbei moves away from the camera and looks up; Shichiroji does the same. The camera slides along the slope of the burial hill, leaving the two samurai behind to focus on the four mounds of earth silhouetted against the sky. The music of the samurai covers that of the planters as the wind blows dust over the mounds of earth.

THE GREAT GATSBY

(F. Scott Fitzgerald, 1925)

The Great Gatsby is rightly famous for its ending. Gatsby is dead. Nick has realized the emptiness of his quest for success in the big city and has decided to return to the Midwest. He takes one last look at this wealthy enclave on the East Coast.

Fitzgerald's ending deserves close study. Through Nick, he tells us that the great mansions are closed for the season. This is a specific fact that, in the story, signifies the end of the false utopia of the grand and rich parties that died with Gatsby. Then Fitzgerald makes a leap in time while broadening the scope of his story when Nick imagines the island as it was at the beginning of America, a virgin paradise, full of potential, "the green breast of a new world" and "the last and greatest of all human dreams." This creates a sad comparison with the island as it is today, where the true desires of real people like Gatsby, Daisy, and Tom have turned the lush forests into false idols – the grand houses and flamboyant, vain parties.

From this general comparison, Fitzgerald refocuses on a specific point, a person, Gatsby, again, whose desire pointed, like a laser, toward the green light at the end of Daisy's dock. Gatsby is this vain dreamer who, like a mythical hero, does not realize that he already had everything he needed at the beginning, in the "dark fields" of the Midwest he left behind.

As Fitzgerald approaches the tip of the triangle, the end of the scene and the story, he evokes the symbol of this false desire, the green light. Unlike many stories that end artificially with the fulfillment of the hero's desire, The Great Gatsby ends with the unfulfilled desire, the effort that redoubles as our human goal recedes into space. The last sentence is a thematic revelation that sums up the entire story: "So we beat on, boats against the current, borne back ceaselessly into the past."

Most of the waterfront mansions were already closed, and there were hardly any lights except those, uncertain and moving, of a ferryboat across the Sound. And as the moon rose higher, the useless mansions began to fade until, by degrees, I had the impression of being on the ancient island that had once flowered in the eyes of Dutch sailors – the green breast of a new world, its vanished trees, the trees that had given way to Gatsby's mansion, had for a time whispered to the last and greatest of all human dreams; for a fleeting enchanted moment, man must have held his breath in the presence of this continent, compelled into an aesthetic contemplation he neither understood nor desired, face to face for the last time in history with something commensurate to his capacity for wonder.

And, sitting there, reflecting on the old unknown world, I thought of Gatsby's wonder when he first picked out the green light at the end of Daisy's dock. He had come a long way to this blue lawn, and his dream must have seemed so close that he could hardly fail to grasp it. He did not know that it was already behind him, somewhere in that vast obscurity beyond the city, where the dark fields of the republic rolled on under the night.

Gatsby believed in the green light, the orgastic future that year by year recedes before us. It eluded us then, but that's no matter – tomorrow we will run faster, stretch out our arms farther... And one fine morning...

So we beat on, boats against the current, borne back ceaselessly into the past.

BUTCH CASSIDY AND THE SUNDANCE KID

(William Goldman, 1969)

If the film Butch Cassidy and the Sundance Kid has one of the best openings in cinema history, it also has one of the best endings. And in many ways, one can say that the final scene is a reflection of the first two scenes.

• Position in the character arc The tragic thing about these two immediately likable characters is that they cannot change. They cannot learn. The new world arrives too quickly for them. They are doomed to die.

• Problem How to create an ending that expresses the essential qualities of the heroes and presents the consequences of their inability to adapt?

• Strategy As in the first scene, the characters find themselves in a confined space that closes in on them. As in the second scene, the characters face a crisis that defines them: in this scene, by the way they face death with extreme confidence – sure they can get out of it. And Butch has already planned their next stop. Butch is always the one with ideas, while Sundance remains the one who must get them out of the problems resulting from Butch's mistakes.

Again, Goldman highlights the team's excellence when Butch runs to get ammunition while Sundance covers him. If Sundance was impressive when he shot Macon's gun to make it fall, he is literally stunning when he draws at record speed to shoot all the policemen within reach. But what makes the audience love this team is its comedic aspect. The incessant and amusing quarrels of the two characters, present from the beginning with the excitable Butch and the quietly skeptical Sundance, show the audience once again that this is a perfectly matched pair.

But Goldman introduces a new contrast here that expresses the main theme and the lack of transformation in the characters: these two men do not understand the new world that is arriving. Goldman alternates their comedic quarrel about Butch's latest idea to dodge the future – Australia – with the arrival of what seems to be the entire Bolivian army. The extreme contrast between what the heroes know and what the audience knows highlights a fact whose foundations were established from the beginning of the story: Butch and Sundance see no further than the end of their small personal world. Although they are very likable, they are not as smart as they seem.

This contrast carries a final realization that touches not the heroes, but the audience: even supermen must die. And it's very sad.

Again, the last line is the key line of the scene and the story. When Butch asks Sundance if he has seen their arch-enemy, Lefors, around and Sundance replies no, Butch declares: "Good. For a moment there, I thought we were in trouble."

MASTERPIECES OF SCENE CONSTRUCTION

I would like to take one last look at dialogue techniques and scene construction by studying two great films, Casablanca and The Godfather. These films are masterpieces of the art of storytelling, and their dialogues and scene construction are brilliant. Since, for writing scenes, much of your success depends on your ability to place each scene in the character's development arc, I would like to study scenes from the beginning and end of these two films.

CASABLANCA

(play, Everybody Comes to Rick’s, by Murray Burnett and Joan Alison, screenplay by Julius J. Epstein, Philip G. Epstein, and Howard Koch, 1942)

First scene between Rick and Louis

In this scene, which occurs quite early in the story's development, Rick and police captain Louis Renault chat cheerfully before the arrival of Major Strasser and the arrest of Ugarte.

• Position in the character arc This is the first stage of the development of the relationship between Rick and Louis, a relationship that will lead to their mutual redemption and the formation of a "couple" in the last scene of the story.

This scene helps to understand why you should always start constructing a scene by determining its place in the character arc. Since it is not the first scene of the film, it feels like a step among others in the flow of the story's unfolding. You need to know the outcome of Rick's arc – becoming a freedom fighter and befriending Louis – to understand that this is an opening stage that plays a decisive role in the arc.

• Problems

1. Show the audience that Louis is as witty as Rick and can become an ideal friend for him at the end of the story.

2. Show that Louis's moral need is just as important as Rick's.

3. Provide more information about Rick's ghost, especially information that reveals that this cynical and tough man was once not only good but also heroic.

• Strategy

1. Have Louis question Rick under the pretext that he is trying to arrest Laszlo and that it is part of his job, and have Rick reveal information about his past. This is an excellent way to introduce the main character's exposition without seeming awkward. Moreover, Rick's insistence that he was well paid for this mission prevents him from appearing too sentimental or idealistic.

2. Have Rick and Louis bet on Laszlo's future. This gives the two men a common line of desire and allows them to present the cynical and selfish character they share: both turn the quest of a freedom fighter seeking to defeat the Nazis into a trivial gambling game.

3. Introduce information about Laszlo and Ilsa so that both can arrive preceded by their reputation.

4. Provide more information about the complex power relations between Louis, the French police captain, and the Nazi, Major Strasser.

• Desire Louis wants to learn more about Rick's past. Then he seeks to convince Rick not to help Laszlo escape.

• Outcome Rick claims he is only interested in Laszlo's fate to the extent that it can bring him money through the bet.

• Adversary Rick is Louis's adversary.

• Plan Louis asks Rick very clear questions about his past and advises him quite directly not to get too close to Laszlo.

• Conflict Rick and Louis disagree on Laszlo's fate, but Rick defuses the conflict by turning these differences into a bet.

• Twist or revelation Laszlo, the great freedom fighter, a character we have not yet met, has arrived in town with a remarkable woman. Moreover, Rick, the tough cynic, was himself a freedom fighter a few years earlier.

• Moral debate and values This exchange is rather an immoral debate. For their bet, the two men wonder if Laszlo will manage to escape, not if he should escape. Besides, Rick maintains that he will not help Laszlo and that he did not act for moral reasons when he fought for the "good" side in Ethiopia and Spain. Rick also says he thinks Laszlo will get an exit visa and leave his companion in Casablanca.

The opposition of values is very clear in this scene: it's money and self-interest vs. romance and altruistic fighting for good.

• Key words Romantic, sentimental.

The lines of the two characters are very stylized and witty in this scene. Louis doesn't just ask Rick about the ghost of his past. He asks: "Did you run off with the church funds? Did you run off with a senator's wife? I'd like to think you killed a man. It's the romantic in me." Rick doesn't just tell him to mind his own business. He says: "I came to Casablanca for the waters." And when Louis reminds him that Casablanca is in the middle of a desert, Rick replies: "I was misinformed."

INT. RICK'S CAFE AMERICAIN OFFICE – NIGHT

RICK

Alfred! Since he escaped from their concentration camp, the Nazis have been searching for him all over Europe.

RENAULT

Today they have him.

RICK

I'll bet you twenty thousand francs they won't.

RENAULT

Is your offer serious?

RICK

I just lost a hundred thousand. I want to get even.

RENAULT

Let's say ten thousand. I'm just a poor civil servant. [...] He may be clever, but he still needs a visa. And even two.

RICK

Why two?

RENAULT

He's traveling with a woman.

RICK

He'll leave her behind.

RENAULT

I bet he won't. I've seen her. If he didn't leave her in Marseille or Oran, I'm sure he won't leave her in Casablanca.

RICK

He may not be as romantic as you think. [...] And what gave you the impression that I might help this person escape?

RENAULT

For the simple reason that beneath your cold and cynical exterior, you're a great sentimentalist, Rick.

Final scene with Rick and Louis

The final scene of Casablanca is one of the most famous in cinema history. Rick has sacrificed his love for Ilsa and deliberately sent her away so she can support her husband, Victor Laszlo. He must then face his former adversary, who is also his stylistic equal.

• Position in the character arc

1. It is the culmination of the process by which Rick commits and becomes a patriot and freedom fighter.

2. Structurally, this scene is a double reversal, marked by the transformation of two characters, Louis and Rick.

3. It is also the culmination of Rick's relationship with Louis, with the two men ending up forming a "couple" of friends.

• Problems

1. How to give this final scene a powerful dramatic impact?

2. How to present the transformation of two characters in a way that is both credible and not too boring?

• Strategy

1. Wait until the very end of the story to reveal to the audience Louis's transformation and present the creation of this new team of friends.

2. Use the double reversal so that Rick and his equal both have a realization, but emphasize maintaining their pragmatic opportunism. What makes this scene great is the return to the bet, which allows both men to undergo a significant moral transformation while retaining their tough aspect. This avoids falling into sentimentality.

• Desire Louis wants to join Rick in his fight and start a friendly relationship.

• Outcome Rick warmly welcomes him.

• Adversaries It seems for a while that Rick and Louis might still be adversaries, especially on the issue of the bet and Rick's escape. But Louis eventually eases the conflict.

• Plan Louis hides his true intentions by pretending he might still oppose Rick on the issue of his exit visa or the bet.

• Conflict Rick negotiates with Louis for his exit visa, and Louis negotiates with Rick for the sum he owes him. But Louis ends this bargaining with a decision that will birth a great friendship.

• Twist or revelation Louis doesn't want to arrest Rick; he wants to join him. But it will cost Rick the 10,000 francs he just won.

• Moral debate and values The two men accept the idea that it's time to become patriots. But without forgetting money matters.

• Key words Patriot, friendship.

The final scene converges toward a precise point of the scene and the story: friendship. Rick has lost his great love, but he ends the story with an excellent friend who is his equal. The scene is designed to lead to the great twist: Louis's stylish way of joining Rick in his moral action. The dialogue is sharper and more sophisticated than any other. But what makes it even better is that it is impromptu.

The last thing to note about this dialogue is that it is both extremely witty and dense. The screenwriters managed to summarize enormous movements of the story in a few lines, and the impact on the audience is immense. Rick performs his noble action. There is an exchange of lines, and Louis performs his noble action by discarding the Vichy water. Louis proposes an arrangement for Rick's escape. Three short sentences. Rick brings up the bet again. Three short sentences. Louis mixes the escape with the bet, one sentence. Rick understands what happened. And the last sentence speaks of eternal friendship. This series of associations produces a great upheaval at the very end of the film's final scene. The screenwriters clearly understood how to apply Chekhov's rule regarding the last ninety seconds of the story.

EXT. AIRPORT – NIGHT

RENAULT

Ah, well done, my dear, you're not only a sentimentalist but also a patriot.

[...] Renault throws the Vichy water into the trash. Rick and Renault watch the plane take off and then disappear into the fog. They leave together.

RENAULT

You might do well to leave Casablanca for a while. You could join De Gaulle in Brazzaville. And who knows, I might give you a hand.

RICK

And a good safe-conduct. Anyway, it doesn't change our conventions. We had a bet of ten thousand francs, you owe me.

RENAULT

And those ten thousand francs will cover part of our expenses.

RICK

Our expenses?

RENAULT

Uh-huh.

RICK

Ah! Yes, I understand. You mean a new life begins for both of us.

THE GODFATHER

(Novel by Mario Puzo, screenplay by Mario Puzo and Francis Ford Coppola, 1972)
To understand how the screenwriters of The Godfather constructed the scenes and wrote the dialogues for this great film, we must start with an overview of the story. Here is how we could very concisely summarize the strategy or narrative process they sought to implement in the film:

1. The transfer of power from one king to the next.

2. Three sons, each endowed with different qualities, attempting to become king.

3. A family counterattacking to ensure its survival and to win.

Let us now examine some of the major thematic structures that the screenwriters wanted to follow in this story. First come the identifications, that is, the elements of the story that we normally conceive as distinct from one another, but that the screenwriters wanted to present as identical on a deeper level. The three most important are:

• The mafia family likened to a business

• The mafia family likened to a military organization

• The profane likened to the sacred and the sacred likened to the profane: “God” likened to the devil

We must then focus on the major structures of opposition, the key elements that the screenwriters wanted to contrast and place in conflict. The major structures of opposition are:

• Family vs law

• Family and vengeance vs American justice

• Immigrant America vs WASP America and the American elite

• Men vs women

If we were to write the scenes of this film, the last step we would go through would be the clarification of values and symbols, or key words, that come into conflict throughout the story. It is only by studying the story as a whole that one can determine which objects or images play a central or organic role. They can then be highlighted through repetition (track 3). In The Godfather, these values and symbols are divided into two main groups: honor, family, business, appearances, and crime vs freedom, country, and moral and legal action.

Opening Scene

The average screenwriter would have started The Godfather with a plot scene to give this great violent story a fast-paced start. He would have exclusively composed the scene of narrative dialogues (track 1) to give an even faster start to the plot. But Mario Puzo and Francis Ford Coppola are not average screenwriters. Guided by the principle of the inverted triangle of the story and the scene, they created a typical experience that frames the entire story while converging towards a single point at the end of the scene:

• Position in the character arc As this story follows the fall of a king and the rise of his successor, the opening scene does not mark the starting point of the new king's (Michael) journey. The story begins with the current king (Don Corleone) and the first scene presents his actions, as well as those of his successor.

• Problems When writing the story of a “king” in a democracy, there are many problems to solve in the opening scene:

1. Introduce the Godfather, and show what a Godfather does.

2. Begin to present the unique operating system of the Mafia, including the power hierarchy and the rules that organize it.

3. Announce the epic scope of this story so that the viewer immediately becomes aware of one of the main thematic points: the world of this family is not a ghetto he could look down upon but a world that symbolizes the nation.

4. Present some of the identifications and oppositions that the screenwriters will weave throughout the story.

• Strategy

1. Start with a typical experience of the Godfather, who acts as a judge and exercises absolute power over his surroundings.

2. Place the essential scene of the Godfather in a larger and more complex world – a wedding – where all the characters who are part of the system are gathered and where the central element that constitutes the family is highlighted.

• Desire Bonasera wants the Don to kill the boys who beat his daughter. In this world, Bonasera is a truly minor character. But he knows absolutely nothing about the system on which the Mafia is based. He is therefore the spectator. The screenwriters used him to lead the scene so that the audience learns at the same time as he does how this system works and understands what it feels like to enter this world. Moreover, the full name of this character, Amerigo Bonasera, could be translated as “Good evening, America.”

• Outcome Bonasera is trapped by the Don.

• Opponent Don Corleone.

• Plan Bonasera uses a direct plan: he asks the Don to kill the two boys, then he asks how much he wants to be paid. This direct approach results in a negative response.

Eager to enlist another person in his network, the Don uses an indirect plan: he pushes Bonasera to feel guilty for the way he has behaved with him in the past.

• Conflict The Don, unhappy with the various offenses Bonasera would have committed against him and continues to commit, refuses to accede to his request. But it should be noted that the construction of the conflict is limited in this scene, as the Don is all-powerful and Bonasera is not fooled.

• Twist or revelation The Don and Bonasera reach an agreement, but the audience understands that Bonasera has just made a pact with the devil.

• Moral debate and values Bonasera asks the Don to kill the two boys who beat his daughter. The Don replies that it would not be fair. Then he cleverly turns the moral argument against Bonasera, explaining that he would have offended and disrespected him.

• Key words Respect, friend, justice, Godfather.

The opening scene of The Godfather shows us that great dialogues are not just melodic; they are also symphonic. If the scene had been composed solely of narrative dialogues, it would have been twice as short and would have lost nine-tenths of its quality. But the screenwriters wrote the dialogue on three simultaneous tracks, and this scene is a masterpiece.

The scene ends with Bonasera uttering the word “Godfather” at the very moment he falls into the trap of the Faustian pact. It begins with these words, which frame the story: “I believe in America.” This is a value, and this value tells the viewer two things: he is about to experience an epic, and this story will deal with the means of achieving success.

The scene begins with a monologue delivered in a place that is practically devoid of details. With his monologue, Bonasera does not just tell the sad story of his daughter; his words are full of values and key words such as “freedom,” “honor,” and “justice.” Don Corleone responds by formulating a small moral attack, which puts Bonasera on the defensive. Then Don Corleone, acting as Godfather-judge, delivers his sentence.

There is a quick exchange of lines, with the characters disagreeing on moral issues, notably on the nature of justice. Then Bonasera, who represents the spectator, makes a mistake, as he does not know the rules of the system. He does not know how people are paid here.

A twist then occurs, and it is now the Don who leads the scene. He develops moral arguments, overflowing with values such as respect, friendship, and loyalty, designed to make Bonasera his slave. The Don pretends to simply want to make Bonasera his friend, but Bonasera understands the true goal of the Don's indirect plan. He lowers his head and utters the key word of the scene: “Godfather.” This word is followed by the last and most important line of the scene. The Godfather says: “One day, and that day may never come, I will call upon you to do a service for me.”

This line has the same form as the pact the devil made with Faust. The Godfather and the devil merge. The “sacred” equals the profane. End of the scene!

Let us now study the opening scene of The Godfather in its framework, with its three threads – narrative dialogue, moral and values dialogue, and key phrases and words – distinct, yet all pointing towards the eye of the vortex. We will thus see how the determining scenic techniques of symphonic dialogues and vortex combine to create one of the best opening scenes in the history of cinema.

INT. DAY: DON'S OFFICE (SUMMER 1945)

The Paramount logo is presented austerely on a black background. There is a moment of suspense and then these simple words in white letters:

THE GODFATHER

These words are still displayed when we hear: “I believe in America.”

The opening line of the dialogue falls under track 2, and probably track 3. The words “I believe” refer to a value. And the word “America” is probably a key word, although it cannot be stated with certainty since it has not yet been repeated. However, as it is the name of the country, there is little chance of being wrong.

It is on this opening line that the screenwriters plant the flag of the epic. The classic definition of the epic is that the fate of a nation is determined by the actions of a single man or family. The authors directly inform the audience that this story will deal with America. And that it will be a dark vision of America.

Suddenly, we see in close-up AMERIGO BONASERA, a man in his sixties dressed in a black suit who seems to be in the grip of violent emotion.

BONASERA

America has made my fortune.

As he speaks, the SHOT begins to widen imperceptibly.

BONASERA

I raised my daughter as a true American.

This line, which evokes a value, falls under track 2, but also track 3, in the repetition of the evocation of America.

I told her: you are free,

Again, tracks 2 and 3. Freedom is a value and, if the term is repeated, it becomes a key word.

but above all you must never dishonor your family.

Track 2: the value of never dishonoring one's family. And probably key words, for “honor” and “family.” Honor and family are the two most important values of the gangster story genre, and in this film, they are introduced from the start.

She had a little flirt,

This line marks the beginning of track 1, the narrative dialogue that describes what happened to Bonasera's daughter and why he came to see the Don.

not an Italian.

Back to track 2, value.

Often, they went to the movies, she came home very late, without me protesting. Two months ago, they went for a drive with a friend of her flirt. They forced her to drink whiskey. And then, they tried to take advantage of my daughter. She resisted;

These lines all fall under narrative dialogue.

she saved her honor.

Back to track 2, and repetition of the key word, “honor.”

Then they beat her like an animal. When I saw her at the hospital, her nose was broken, and her jaw fractured. It was held together with wire. She couldn't even cry because it hurt so much.

He now has difficulty speaking; he sobs.

BONASERA

[…] So, I went to the police like a true American.

Value, and repetition of the key word, “America.”

They put these two dogs on trial. The judge, he first sentences them to three years in prison, and then he grants them a suspended sentence. A suspended sentence! They walked free that very day! I looked at the court like a poor fool, and these two bandits, they smiled at me. So I said to my wife: “For justice, we must go to Don Corleone.”

This last line is rich in meaning. It introduces the value of justice, as well as the probable key words that are “justice” and “Godfather.” It also introduces the fundamental thematic opposition of the story: American legal justice vs the Godfather's personal justice.

BONASERA

[…] What do you want from me? Ask me anything, but give me what I ask of you!

DON CORLEONE

What do you ask?

BONASERA whispers in the DON's ear.

Bonasera uses a direct plan, and we quickly understand that he has asked the Don to kill the boys. As is usually the case with direct plans, the answer is no.

DON CORLEONE

No. That I cannot do.

BONASERA

I will pay you anything you ask.

The SHOT is now wide, and we can see Don Corleone's office in his house. The blinds are closed, the room is dark with shadowy areas. We see BONASERA over DON CORLEONE's shoulder. TOM HAGEN, sitting near a small table, examines documents, and SONNY CORLEONE gets up impatiently to join his father at the crossroad while sipping a glass of wine. We HEAR music, as well as the laughter and voices of many people outside.

This paragraph introduces the main characters in the power hierarchy of this mafia family. The last sentence evokes the broader universe of the story, but is in direct contrast with Bonasera sobbing in the room. Something is wrong, but we do not know what it is.

DON CORLEONE

We have known each other for years, but this is the first time you have come to me for advice or a favor. I don't even remember the last cup of coffee I came to have at your house... yet my wife is the godmother of your only child.

Don Corleone immediately goes on the attack. We are on track 2, the moral dialogue. The attack is not violent, but the Don wants to make Bonasera understand that he has not felt treated with friendship and respect, two values he will mention later.

The following monologue is a harsh moral attack led by Don Corleone against Bonasera for his poor values and behavior.

DON CORLEONE

You have rejected my friendship.

Key word: “friend.”

[…] You had paradise in America.

Key word: “America.”

[…] The police, the courts to protect you. You didn't need a friend.

Key word: “friend.”

Today, you come to me crying and say: “Don Corleone, give me justice!”

Key word: “justice,” but now redefined as personal justice.

Do I see a little respect? Do I feel a little friendship? Does it occur to you to call me Godfather?

Key word: “respect,” “friendship,” “Godfather.”

No, you come to me on the day my daughter Connie is getting married and you offer to pay me for a murder.

We now understand what the laughter and voices outside correspond to. This scene, in the Don's personal court of justice, takes place within the larger community moment of a wedding.

BONASERA

What I ask is justice.

DON CORLEONE

That would not be justice, your daughter is still alive.

This quick exchange falls under track 2, a moral debate on the nature of true justice, obviously close to that of the Old Testament.

BONASERA

I want to see them suffer as much as she suffers. How much must I pay?

HAGEN and SONNY both react.

This general comment actually means that they react badly, as Bonasera has just made a big mistake. But he does not know it, and neither do we, the spectators. Bonasera asks for a service and knows he will have to pay for it. What he does not know is that the currency of this system is not money, but favors owed.

Since we are Bonasera, we make the same mistake as he does. And we feel the same embarrassment when we realize we have made this mistake. This is the powerful effect obtained by the emotional, not intellectual, explanation of the rules of the mafia system. Entering the lion's den is simply terrifying.

DON CORLEONE

Bonasera, Bonasera. What have I ever done to make you treat me so disrespectfully? Had you come to me as a friend, then the scum who harmed your daughter would already be suffering. And if, by chance, a man like you had an enemy to complain about,

Key words and values: “friendship,” “respect.”

then his enemy would become mine. And he would fear you.

Slowly, Bonasera bows his head and murmurs:

BONASERA

Be my friend.

In appearance, the Don demands a friend-to-friend relationship, that is, an equal-to-equal relationship. But Bonasera is not fooled. He knows that what the Don is really asking for is a master-to-slave relationship. So he bows his head and repeats the key word, “friend,” but which must now be understood with its new meaning: “your servant.”

Here is a perfect illustration of the vortex technique within a scene. This complete exercise on justice, with its contrast between American legal justice and personal justice, spirals down to the key word and key phrase of the scene. This is the eye of the vortex, presenting the typical experience of the Godfather with maximum dramatic power.

BONASERA

Godfather.

DON CORLEONE

Good, one day, and that day may never come, I will call upon you to do a service for me.

The key word “Godfather” is immediately followed by a line that is vaguely familiar and vaguely unsettling, although we do not yet know why. It is a line from “Faust,” also found in many fairy tales. Bonasera is satisfied to have obtained a form of justice from the Godfather, even if it is not what he asked for. He therefore does not understand that he has just made a pact with the devil.

Key word followed by a key phrase: “Godfather” = devil. Maximum dramatic impact, and we exit the scene.

This is a brilliantly written scene. And its quality comes from the use of the three tracks of dialogue and the eye of the vortex.

Final Scene

This end, which is the tip of the inverted triangle of the story, is both a “trial” – Connie accuses Michael of murder – and a coronation. This final scene echoes the opening. We move from the typical experience of the Godfather ending in a diabolical pact to the coronation of a new demonic king.

• Position in the character arc Michael is accused of being a murderer by his sister while completing his rise by becoming the new Godfather. Michael also reaches a kind of culmination in his marriage to Kay, the mistakes he has made being irreparable.

• Problems How to make a moral argument against Michael without making him accept it.

• Strategy

1. Put this argument in the mouth of Connie, a woman, who is moreover hysterical, and thus discredit it in Michael's eyes.

2. Do not attribute the realization to Michael but to Kay. And do not base this realization on Connie's words but on what Kay sees in her husband.

• Desire Connie wants to accuse Michael of Carlo's murder.

• Outcome The door closes on Kay's face.

• Opponents Michael, Kay.

• Plan Connie uses a direct plan: she accuses Michael of her husband's murder in front of everyone.

• Conflict The conflict starts at an intense degree then dissipates at the end.

• Twist or revelation Michael lies to Kay, but Kay sees what Michael has become.

• Moral debate and values Connie claims that Michael is a heartless murderer who does not care about her. Michael simply refutes her accusations by suggesting that she is sick or hysterical and needs to see a doctor. Then he denies it to Kay.

• Key words Godfather, emperor, murderer.

INT. DAY: DON'S OFFICE

Connie rushes into the house where Michael and Kay are and enters the office.

CONNIE

Michael! You bastard! You killed my husband. You waited for dad to be dead to do whatever you want! You killed Carlo! You think it's because of him that Sonny died but everyone believes it. You never thought of me. You don't care about me.

(She cries.)

What am I going to do now?

KAY

Connie…

CONNIE

Why do you think he kept Carlo at home? I'm telling you it's because he had decided to kill him, that's all. To think he's the godfather of our son. But you're a bastard! A murderer! Do you want to know how many guys he had killed with Carlo? It's on the front page! Look at the newspapers! That's your husband.

MICHAEL

Take her upstairs. Call a doctor.

The bodyguard gently but firmly grabs Connie by the arms and takes her away.

Kay is in shock, staring at Michael in astonishment. He feels her gaze on him.

MICHAEL

She's hysterical. Hysterical...

Kay does not take her eyes off him.

KAY

Michael, is it true?

MICHAEL

Don't ask me about my business.

KAY

Is it true?

MICHAEL

No questions about my business! […] Okay, this is the only time... the only time I'll let you ask me about my business.

She looks him straight in the eyes. He holds her gaze, so intensely that we know he will tell her the truth.

KAY

Is it true? Is it…

MICHAEL (AFTER A LONG MOMENT)

No.

Relieved, she throws herself into his arms. He embraces her and then kisses her.

KAY

I think we really need a drink.

INT. DAY: DON'S KITCHEN

Kay goes into the kitchen to prepare the drinks. Through the open door, she sees Clemenza, Neri, and Rocco Lampone enter the office with their bodyguards.

She watches with curiosity as Michael, at ease, a hand on his hip like a Roman emperor, receives them. The caporegimes stand before him.

Clemenza takes Michael's hand and kisses it.

CLEMENZA

Don Corleone…

The smile fades from Kay's face as she observes what her husband has become.

WRITING SCENES – WRITING EXERCISE NO. 9

• Character transformation Before writing any scene, you must formulate your hero's transformation in a single sentence.

• Scene construction When constructing a scene, ask yourself the following questions:

1. What position does this scene occupy in your hero's arc and how does this scene lead him to the next stage of his development?

2. What problems do you need to solve and what do you need to accomplish in this scene?

3. What strategy will you use to achieve this?

4. Which character's desire will drive the scene? Remember that it does not necessarily have to be the hero of the story.

5. What does the character's desire lead to in this scene?

6. Who seeks to prevent the character from achieving his goal?

7. What type of plan – direct or indirect – will your character use to achieve his goal?

8. Will the scene end at the peak of the conflict or will there be a resolution?

9. Will this scene include a twist or a revelation?

10. At the end of the scene, will one of the characters make a comment on the deep nature of another?
• Scenes without dialogue Initially, try writing scenes without dialogue. Let the characters' actions tell the story. This way, you'll have a "clay" that you can mold and refine endlessly.

• Writing dialogues

1. Narrative dialogue: Rewrite each scene using only narrative dialogue (Track 1), meaning dialogue concerning the characters' actions in the plot.

2. Moral dialogue: Add moral dialogue to each scene (Track 2), meaning arguments that define the characters' actions as good or bad or comments on the characters' values (what they believe in).

3. Keywords: Rewrite the scenes again, highlighting key words, phrases, repetitions, and sounds (Track 3), meaning objects, images, values, or ideas that play an essential role in your story's theme.

When writing the three tracks of your dialogues, imagine you are creating a portrait of someone. You start by sketching the outline of the face (narrative dialogue). Then you add the play of light and shadow that gives depth to the face (moral dialogue). And you finish by drawing the finest lines and details that make this face unique (keywords).

• Unique voices Ensure that each character has a unique way of expressing themselves.

1. Translation by Charlotte Pressoir and V. Leconte (Translator's note).

2. Translation by P. Lorain (Translator's note).

3. Translation by Annie Saumont (Translator's note).

4. Translation by Victor Llona, Bibliothèque électronique du Québec.

– 11 –

The Neverending Story

A great story is immortal. And this is not a platitude or a tautology. A great story continues to affect the audience long after it has been told for the first time. It literally continues to tell itself. But how can a story become a living thing that will never die?

A neverending story is not a story that is so interesting that it becomes unforgettable. You can only create a neverending story by using certain techniques related to narrative structure. But before presenting these techniques, we must examine the opposite of the neverending story: the story whose life and power are cut short by an artificial ending. There are generally three types of artificial endings: the premature ending, the arbitrary ending, and the closed ending.

A premature ending can be due to many causes, including early realization. Once the hero has had their great realization, their development is complete, and everything that happens afterward is uninteresting from a plot perspective. The second cause of a premature ending is the hero succeeding too quickly. If they are then given a new desire, a new story begins. And the premature ending can also be due to an action undertaken by the hero that is not credible because it is not organically suited to the character's personality. When characters, especially the hero, are forced to act implausibly, the audience is immediately and brutally pulled out of the story, with the "gears" appearing on the surface. The audience understands that if the character undertakes this action, it is because you need them to (mechanical) and not because they need to (organic).

An arbitrary ending is an ending that occurs for no apparent reason. Arbitrary endings are almost always due to a non-organic plot. The plot does not follow the development of a single entity, whether it is a single main character or a social unit. If nothing develops, the audience does not feel that something will come to fruition or conclude. A classic example of this type of ending is found in The Adventures of Huckleberry Finn. Twain follows Huck's development, but due to the journey plot he uses, his hero literally ends up in a dead end. Twain is forced to rely on coincidence and deus ex machina to finish his story, disappointing all those who found the rest of the narrative particularly brilliant.

The most common type of artificial ending is the closed ending. The hero achieves their goal, has a simple realization, and lives in a new balance where everything is calm. These three structural elements give the audience the feeling that the story is complete and that the system has closed. But this is false. Desire never stops. Balance is temporary. Realization is never simple, and it cannot guarantee the hero will live happily ever after. A great story, always being a living thing, cannot have a more definitive and certain ending than any other part of the story.

But how can you create this sense of breath, pulse, and perpetual evolution that endures long after the last word has been read or the last image has been seen? To find out, we must return to where we began, to the essential definition of the story, which is a structure in time. The story is an organic unit that develops over time, and it must continue to develop long after the audience has stopped watching it.

Since the story is always a whole, and the end is found in the beginning, a good story always ends by signaling to the audience to return to the beginning to experience it again. The story is an endless circle—a Möbius strip—that is always different because the audience is always rethinking it in light of what has just happened.

To create a neverending story, the simplest way is through the plot, by ending the story with a twist. This technique involves creating an apparent balance, which is immediately destroyed with one last surprise. This twist leads the audience to rethink all the characters and actions that led them to this point. Like a detective reading the same clues but seeing a very different reality, the reader or viewer hurries to mentally return to the beginning of the story and reshuffle the cards to reach a new combination.

This technique is masterfully executed in The Sixth Sense when the audience discovers that the character played by Bruce Willis has been dead since the beginning of the story. Its implementation is even more stunning in The Usual Suspects, when the weak narrator leaves the police station and transforms before our eyes into a terrible adversary he himself created, Keyser Söze.

The twist-turnaround, though spectacular, remains a limited means of creating a neverending story. Because it only offers you a new cycle with the audience. The readers or viewers hadn't thought of it at the beginning. But now they know. There will be no more surprises. By using this technique, you don't get so much a neverending story as a story told twice.

Some authors say that you cannot create a neverending story with a plot that is too powerful, a plot that dominates the other narrative elements too much. Because even plots that end with a great twist leave the audience feeling that all the shutters of the house are now closed. The key has been turned; the puzzle is complete; the case is closed.

To tell a story that always seems different, it is not necessary to kill the plot. But you must use each of the systems of the story's body. By weaving a complex tapestry composed of characters, plot, theme, symbols, scenes, and dialogues, you allow readers or viewers to retell the story endlessly. They will have to rethink so many narrative elements that the possibilities for combinations will become limitless, and the story will live forever. Here are just a few methods you can use in your story to make it an infinite tapestry:

• The hero fails and does not fulfill their desire, but the other characters manifest a new desire at the end of the story. This prevents the narrative from closing and shows the audience that desire, even vain or unattainable, never dies ("I want, therefore I am").

• Have an adversary or secondary character undergo an astonishing transformation. This technique can lead the audience to revisit the story by considering this character as the true hero.

• Place a large number of details in the background of the story's universe, details that can, when the story is repeated, come to the forefront.

• Add elements of texture—to the characters, the moral debate, the symbols, the plot, and the story's universe—that become much more interesting once the plot's surprises and the hero's transformation have been seen.

• Create a relationship between the narrator and the other characters that will completely change meaning once the audience is aware of the plot. The unreliable narrator is just one way among many to achieve this.

• Make the moral debate ambiguous, or do not reveal what the hero decides to do when faced with the final moral choice. As soon as you go beyond the stage of simple moral debate—good versus evil—you force the audience to reevaluate the hero, the adversaries, and all the secondary characters to determine what constitutes a good or bad action. By hiding the final choice, you compel readers or viewers to question the hero's actions again and to ponder this choice in the context of their own lives.

The main problem we faced in this work was to present a practical poetics—the storytelling techniques found in all forms of stories. This involved explaining how to create a complex living story that develops in the minds of readers or viewers and never dies. It also meant overcoming what appeared to be a dreadful contradiction: telling a story that has universal appeal but is also completely original.

Our solution was to present you with the secret workings of the story world. We wanted you to discover the dramatic code—the way human beings evolve and change throughout their lives—in all its splendor and complexity. This book presents many techniques to express the dramatic code in a powerful and original story. If you are serious, you will never stop studying and practicing them.

But technical mastery is not enough. Allow me to end with one final revelation: you are the neverending story. If you want to tell the great story, the neverending story, you must, like your hero, personally face the seven steps. And you must do it every time you write a new story. I have tried to provide you with the plan: the strategies, tactics, and techniques that will help you achieve your goal, fulfill your needs, and create an unlimited stock of realizations. Mastering the art of storytelling is not an easy task. But if you learn the technique and make your life a great story, your ability to tell fabulous tales will astonish you.

If you truly know how to read—and I have no doubt about that—you are no longer the person you were when you started this book. Now that you have read it once, if I may... well, you know what you have to do.

Acknowledgments

This book could never have existed without my agent, Noah Lukeman, and my editor, Denise Oswald, who, along with her colleagues, provided me with the first-class treatment for which Farrar, Straus and Giroux are famous.

Many people gave me very useful advice about this work and the writing process, especially Tim Truby, Patty Meyer, Bob Ellis, Alex Kustanovich, and Leslie Lehr, whom I thank.

This book has also been greatly enriched by my former students, whose enthusiasm for writing pushed me to seek to clearly explain how to put narrative theories into practice.

I would like to especially thank Kaaren Kitchell, Anna Waterhouse, Dawna Kemper, and Cassandra Lane, who, despite their busy schedules as authors, took the time to give me hundreds of suggestions to improve this work. A writer primarily needs good readers, and they are the best of all.

A big thank you to Ollivier Pourriol, a true friend, for making this beautiful new edition possible with his knowledge of storytelling, his wise advice, and his passionate commitment. Thanks to Muriel Levet for her translation, and to Yann Raynal for his tireless pursuit of excellence. I am also extremely grateful to Elsa Lafon for offering a new home to this book at Michel Lafon, where I know we can reach new readers and, among them, the authors of tomorrow.

Finally, I would like to thank the screenwriters, novelists, and playwrights whose well-written stories have inspired me to explore the anatomy of fiction. These amazing, wonderful authors, who offer us all an invaluable gift, are the true stars of this book.
